Some Investors Buy Commodities As Inflation Hedge
Some Investors Buy Commodities As Inflation Hedge
By Terin Miller
Dow Jones Newswires
487 words
2 September 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- While the Federal Reserve continues to warn about the prospect of deflation, some investors
have been buying oil, precious metals and grains as a hedge against what they see as looming inflation.
Loose monetary and fiscal policy and the widening U.S. budget deficit have persuaded these investors there
is a heightened inflation risk.
Even a top Iranian official, Hojat Ghanimi Fard, executive director for international affairs at National Iranian
Oil Co., said in an interview last week that oil and gold, "as opposed to more volatile stocks," have caught
investors' attention.
Investors are quietly buying the more active components of the Commodity Research Bureau's index of
commodities, said Leonard Kaplan, president of Prospector Asset Management, a money-management and
commodity brokerage firm in Evanston, Ill.
"I have clients that are interested in hedging inflation by buying the CRB," Mr. Kaplan said. "However, the
CRB contract is basically untradable. It has no liquidity, and no volume. So what they've done is basically
thrown money at certain components of it."
The hedging activity has grown in "the last couple months," Mr. Kaplan added.
Large speculators have been piling into energy and gold. Large noncommercial participants at the Comex
division of the New York Mercantile Exchange held their biggest net long gold position -- or collective bet on
higher prices -- in more than five years as of Aug. 19, according to the Commodity Futures Trading
Commission. Gold prices climbed to $358.80 a troy ounce from lows around $357.20 before settling around
$357.30 by the end of the reporting period.
Mr. Kaplan points out that the recent rally in gold prices occurred against the headwind of a strengthening
dollar, which indicates inflation-hedging at work. Typically, the two markets move in opposite directions, as
gold is priced in dollars so that a higher dollar deters buyers.
Investors have also been moving into oil futures on the theory that inflation will raise the price of all
commodities, and oil is one of the most heavily traded commodities in the world, Mr. Kaplan said.
Speculative positions in Nymex light, sweet crude oil reached the highest level in nearly two years in the
week of Aug. 5, though they have since declined. Prices have stayed at high prices of $30 to $32 since late
June.
"Fund managers have been playing the long side of those markets," agreed Tim Evans, senior energy analyst
at research firm IFR Pegasus. But, he warned, investors expecting inflation may be in for a long wait.
"They may be right that the current fiscal policy of the U.S., the budget deficits both current and projected
essentially imply the government issuing paper, which tends to result in inflation over the longer term. But
the difficulty is that they may have to wait quite awhile for that inflation to actually materialize."

Wal-Mart Increases Sales Forecast --- Despite Blackout, Retailer May Post Biggest Gain In More Than a Year
Wal-Mart Increases Sales Forecast --- Despite Blackout, Retailer May Post Biggest Gain In More Than
a Year
By Russell Gold
610 words
26 August 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Wal-Mart Stores Inc. boosted its August sales-growth forecast and said it may post its largest monthly sales
gain in more than a year, a sign that consumer spending continues to accelerate despite setbacks such as
the blackout that struck much of the Northeast and Midwest two weeks ago.
The world's largest retailer said sales at stores open more than a year should increase 4% to 6%, somewhat
better than its previous estimate of 3% to 5%. Although modest, the sales gains would be the largest such
increase for the retailing giant in more than a year.
The improved forecast is the latest evidence that a nascent economic recovery is under way. Although
battered by terrorism, war and economic uncertainty following the collapse of the Internet bubble, consumer
spending has been a mainstay of the weak U.S. economy, fueled most recently by low interest rates that
sparked a home-refinancing boom.
Wal-Mart attributed the improved forecast to a greater-than-expected number of shoppers and larger average
purchases, bolstered by a cut in tax withholding and tax-refund checks of as much as $400 a child.
"People seem to be more inclined to shop right now," said Tom Williams, a spokesman for the Bentonville,
Ark., discount retailer.
The projection also put to rest fears that the widespread power outage in the Northeast and Midwest, which
forced more than 200 of the company's Wal-Mart and Sam's Club stores to close temporarily, would damp
consumer spending. The East Coast "was the strongest region" during the week ended Aug. 22, which
included the end of the blackout, the company said.
Earlier this month, Wal-Mart said its same-store sales for August could come in at the high end of its range.
Analysts thought the tax checks might also give it a boost.
A few weeks ago, Wal-Mart estimated customers were spending about 15% of their tax-refund checks, less
than the 25% spent from one-time refund checks sent in 2001. Some economists, armed with more recent
data, say taxpayers are actually splurging more this time around, largely because they are more confident
about the future.
"It looks like consumers are spending at a much more rapid pace then they did with the 2001 tax rebate," said
Diane Swonk, chief economist at Bank One Corp. "That's not surprising. It's a much better economy."
Wal-Mart's sales figures look particularly good because they are being compared with relatively soft sales last
year, said Daniel Barry, a senior retail analyst at Merrill Lynch. At the time, a sluggish economy and
unseasonably hot weather kept shoppers at home. "Now that the economy appears to be picking up, the
actual acceleration in sales could be very strong," he said.
A bigger question, however, is whether the improvement in consumer sentiment will persist, particularly now
that rising interest rates have crimped the refinancing wave. Economists expect the consumer-spending
effects of the refinancing boom, which peaked in June, will begin to fade by September.
Wal-Mart, a component of the Dow Jones Industrial Average, has reported several signs that consumer
spending is improving. Earlier this month, it said sales of back-to-school items were robust. It also noted that
consumers weren't coming to its stores as quickly after receiving their paychecks, a sign that consumers were
less financially strapped.
Wal-Mart shares were up 70 cents, or 1.2%, at $59.10 in 4 p.m. composite trading on the New York Stock
Exchange.

Going Global: Gold Rush Shows Metal Keeps Luster --- Some Expect Strong Gains But Continuing Volatility During the Next Decade
Going Global: Gold Rush Shows Metal Keeps Luster --- Some Expect Strong Gains But Continuing
Volatility During the Next Decade
By Michael R. Sesit
928 words
22 August 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C10
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
In the 1540s, Spanish conquistador Francisco Vasquez de Coronado led a small army through what is now
the American Southwest in search of seven cities of gold, only to find pueblos made of sun-baked clay.
Nearly five centuries later, global investors are bidding up the price of bullion and gold-mining shares so
aggressively you would think they are chasing Coronado's gold. Indeed, a growing number of money
managers, strategists and other gurus are proclaiming the cusp of a new bull market in bullion that some
contend could drive the price up hundreds of dollars over the next decade.
The price of gold has jumped 45%, $365.60 an ounce as of Wednesday, from a 22-year low in the first
quarter of 2001. It is up 19% in the past 12 months; and at one point in February, gold traded as high as
$389 an ounce, a seven-year peak. It fell back $5 to $360.60 yesterday.
But that is nothing compared with gold-mining stocks, which are often regarded as a proxy for gold itself. In
the past year, the Standard & Poor's gold subindex is up 53%, nearly 10 times the 5.4% rise in the S&P
500-stock index. So far this year, Ireland's tiny Celtic Resources Holdings PLC, with assets in the former
Soviet Union, has risen well more than threefold. Golden Star Resources Ltd. of the U.S., with assets mainly
in Ghana, has risen 91%. Canada's Bema Gold Corp. is up 67%. the U.S.'s Newmont Mining Corp., the
world's biggest mining concern, has jumped 36%.
One force behind the rising prices is traders and investors seeking to hedge against the weak dollar, which,
despite its recent rebound, many analysts predict will keep falling. Because gold is scarce, it is believed it will
keep its value.
Other factors include receding gold supplies, geopolitical uncertainties, still low -- even negative
inflation-adjusted -- interest rates, financial-market volatility, suspicions China will convert more of its rising
foreign-exchange reserves to gold, and mining-industry consolidation.
Gold has found converts among people burned by the stock-market bubble. "We've seen interest from
investors and speculators who are turning to hard assets after disappointing experiences with investing in
other markets over the last few years," says John Reade, a metals analyst at UBS in London. "Most recently,
though, we've seen more broad-based buying of commodities as speculators seek exposure to the unfolding
economic recovery."
He forecasts that gold will average $375 an ounce next year -- but trade in a range of $330 to $420 an
ounce. More optimistic, Frank Lucas, managing director of Loeb Aron &Co., a London corporate- finance firm
specializing in natural resources, sees it averaging $450 an ounce.
A growing cadre of professionals also likes bullion for the long run. Equating today's gold price to where the
Standard &Poor's 500-stock index was in the early-to-mid 1980s, David Fuller, global strategist at Stockcube
Research in London, says, "Gold is in the very early stages of a secular bull market" -- albeit one marked by
extended periods of range trading and extreme volatility.
"There will be a few quarters when you could easily lose 30% of your money," says Hugh Hendry, a partner
at Odey Asset Management in London, who invests in gold-mining shares. "But it's the only marketplace
where I can see making 10 times my money over the next five or six years."
In 10 to 15 years, Mr. Fuller forecasts gold will sell for a "minimum" of $1,500 to $2,000 an ounce and
appreciate a similar amount against most other currencies. At the peak of its speculative bubble in early
1980, gold traded at $850 an ounce.
Noting how central banks -- led by the U.S. Federal Reserve -- are cutting interest rates, printing money and
weakening their currencies to get their economies growing, Mr. Fuller says, "A consequence of those policies

--- Page 9 ---
Page 9 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
is a massive increase of the supply of fiat [paper] money." And history, he argues, shows that paper money
doesn't retain its purchasing power. Gold does, he and others contend.
Messrs. Fuller, Hendry and Lucas like gold not because they see Armageddon around the corner, but
because it is a value play at a time when other assets, especially currencies, are being debased.
Other Commodities Markets:
GASOLINE: Gasoline futures on the New York Mercantile Exchange rose sharply, fueled by fears of a severe
gasoline shortage in the waning days of the heavy U.S. driving season. Learning of the high premium paid
over the Nymex futures price in the New York Harbor spot market for reformulated gasoline, arbitrage players
bought up futures and pushed the spot premium even higher, said Tony Rosado of Zone Energy. The
September contract rose 9.57 cents a gallon to $1.099 -- the biggest gain for a nonexpiring contract in 19
years.
LUMBER: The September lumber contract on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange closed up the
$10-per-1,000-board-feet exchange-imposed daily price limit at $321.70 amid active commercial buying
because of a solid and rising cash market. Certain large producers in British Columbia increased prices
sharply or announced closures because of log shortages and forest fires.
---
Terin Miller of Dow Jones Newswires and Lester Aldrich of OsterDowJones contributed to this article.

Small-Stock Focus: RealNetworks Falls as HealthSouth, Dynegy Rise
Small-Stock Focus: RealNetworks Falls as HealthSouth, Dynegy Rise
By Cheryl Winokur Munk
Dow Jones Newswires
831 words
13 August 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Small-cap stocks outpaced the broader market yesterday, as investors took comfort in the
Federal Reserve's decision to keep interest rates unchanged.
"Small-cap stocks continue to lead the way up," said Cary Nordan, equity analyst at BB&T Asset
Management.
One company he likes is RealNetworks, which makes software for streaming video and competes with
Microsoft's Windows media player. Mr. Nordan said he expects the small-cap company to continue to
improve revenue and earnings and emerge as a dominant player.
Shares fell seven cents, or 1.2%, to $5.92 yesterday, after rising 13% a day earlier on word of a deal to
provide multimedia content to Sprint wireless customers.
Shares of HealthSouth jumped 60 cents, or 36%, to 2.25, after the scandal-ridden hospital chain attempting
to avoid bankruptcy said it has paid $117 million or all past due interest currently outstanding to its lenders
and noteholders.
Entertainment company Marvel Enterprises advanced on the New York Stock Exchange after a strong
second-quarter report that beat analysts' expectations. The company also raised its full-year guidance,
sending shares up 2.85, or 15%, to 21.50.
Energy company Dynegy (NYSE) gained following its $1.625 billion debt restructuring and some analyst
upgrades. The Houston company's shares rose 20 cents, or 6.7%, to 3.17.
"If you believe the economy's really going to improve as we do, you want to own companies in the small-cap
space," Mr. Nordan said.
The Russell 2000 Index of small-capitalization stocks gained 7.68 points, or 1.67%, to 466.95, while the
Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600 Index rose 3.63, or 1.6%, to 230.17.
The Nasdaq Composite Index of large and small stocks pushed up 25.50, or 1.53%, to 1687.01.
Internet service providers did particularly well, with CNET Networks gaining 45 cents, or 7.8%, to 6.23.
EarthLink rose 18 cents, or 2.6%, to 7.18.
Heavy machinery companies also showed strong performance on formidable earnings from large-cap Deere
&Co. Among small-caps, AGCO rose 99 cents, or 5.5%, to 19.13 on the NYSE.
Precious metals fell, with Meridian Gold (NYSE) shedding 22 cents, or 1.6%, to 13.71. Nonferrous metals
were pulled down by large-cap names in the group, but the small-caps forged ahead. Olin rose 15 cents, or
0.9%, to 16.76 on the NYSE and RTI International Metals gained 21 cents, or 2.1%, to 10.09, also on the
NYSE.
California Independent Bancorp advanced after announcing a deal to be acquired by Humboldt Bancorp in a
transaction valued at about $80 million, or $35.50 per California Independent share. California Independent
rose 5.81, or 20%, to 34.71, while Humboldt fell 57 cents, or 3.6%, to 15.33.
Extendicare, the Canadian operator of long-term care and assisted-living facilities, rose after reporting a $10
million increase in second-quarter net earnings to $12.1 million, or 18 cents a share. Merrill upped its rating to
buy from neutral. Shares gained 98 cents, or 28%, to 4.54.

--- Page 11 ---
Page 11 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mannatech advanced after the company showed earnings improvement in the second quarter. The Coppell,
Texas, developer of proprietary nutritional supplements and topical products rose 1.39, or 24%, to 7.17.
IXYS, Santa Clara, Calif., gained after returning to profitability in its most recent quarter. The maker of power
semiconductors and power modules earned $443,000, or a penny a share, in its fiscal first quarter, compared
with a net loss of $1.4 million, or five cents a share, a year earlier. Its shares rose 1.15, or 17.4%, to 7.75.
Overland Storage, San Diego, slipped after saying it expects its first quarter to come in below analysts'
expectations. Shares slid 3.85, or 21%, to 14.74.
Crosswave Communications fell after posting a wider first-quarter net loss. Nonetheless, the company said it
is still aiming for year-over-year growth and continues to make efforts to achieve profitability. Its shares fell
1.02, or 15%, to 5.59.
McDermott International, New Orleans, dropped a day after reporting a second-quarter net loss primarily on
asbestos costs related to a bankrupt unit and continuing losses from a marine construction project in
Argentina. The company also withdrew its 2003 guidance due to asbestos woes. Shares of the
energy-services company shed 1.26, or 23%, to 4.19 on the NYSE.
Reliant Resources (NYSE) fell after reporting disappointing results and announcing more job cuts. Its shares
dropped 1.10, or 22%, to 3.88.
Pegasus Communications, Bala Cynwyd, Pa., fell for the second consecutive day on concerns that the
outcome of a long legal battle with DirecTV may not be as favorable as hoped. Shares were down 1.05, or
4.5%, to 22.05.

Because of Japan, Fed Inflation Doves Aren't Lonesome Now
THE OUTLOOK
Because of Japan, Fed Inflation Doves Aren't Lonesome Now
By Greg Ip
830 words
4 August 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Washington -- WHEN THE FED had just begun to cut interest rates in early 2001, Federal Reserve Bank of
Dallas president Robert McTeer said inflation was under control.
The remark triggered a broadside from Northern Trust economist and frequent Fed critic Paul Kasriel. "Is
`Cowboy' Bob McTeer becoming an embarrassment to the Fed?" he asked in a report that May. Mr. McTeer
had been nicknamed the "Lonesome Dove" for twice dissenting against raising interest rates in 1999. Mr.
Kasriel thought Mr. McTeer had gone soft on inflation, a capital crime for a central banker.
A few months ago, Mr. McTeer recalled Mr. Kasriel's rebuke, then noted with satisfaction that the entire
Federal Open Market Committee had declared lower inflation to be a bigger worry than higher inflation.
"The lonesome dove is not so lonesome anymore," he crowed. Mr. Kasriel says: "I'm embarrassed now."
That inflation doves can now hold their heads high at the Fed speaks volumes about the altered mentality of
central bankers -- and it is a key reason not to expect the Fed to raise interest rates for a long time. A deep
loathing of inflation is at the top of central bankers' code of honor. The belief gives them the backbone to
resist pressure for easy money. "Only hawks get to go to central-banker heaven," Mr. McTeer remarked to
much laughter at a Fed meeting in July 1997.
Mr. McTeer, who wears cowboy boots, quotes Richard Pryor and writes limericks about economics, wasn't
always a dove. He started his career in the 1970s at the Richmond Fed, whose president, Bob Black, made
Fed Chairman Paul Volcker look soft on inflation by dissenting seven times for higher interest rates. Mr.
Black's underlings also included Mr. Kasriel and Al Broaddus, the current Richmond president who repeatedly
dissented in the 1990s in favor of higher rates.
Mr. Broaddus now worries as much about deflation as inflation. Another former hawk, San Francisco Fed
President Robert Parry, has abandoned his previous goal of zero inflation; he thought the Fed should have
cut rates by a half, not a quarter, percentage point in June. Their changes of heart are significant because
they are long-tenured reserve-bank presidents in their 60s. The Fed presidents are appointed by the
conservative boards of the 12 reserve banks and are usually more hawkish than the seven Fed governors,
including the chairman. The latter are seen as more susceptible to outside pressure because they are political
appointees. With the Fed presidents-turned-doves, Chairman Alan Greenspan will face less resistance to
keeping rates exceptionally low.
Messrs. McTeer, Broaddus and Parry claim they are neither hawks nor doves, but merely want price stability,
which means deflation and inflation are equally undesirable. Deflation is difficult to fight, however, because
the Fed can't cut rates below zero to fight falling prices.
Central bankers used to think inflation was their greatest enemy. Stopping inflation usually required rate
increases and recessions. What could be worse? Fighting deflation, meanwhile, was supposed to be as
simple as printing more money, as long as the dollar wasn't linked to gold. In 1955, the last time U.S.
experienced deflation (albeit mild), Fed Chairman William McChesney Martin raised rates, declaring:
"Inflation is a thief in the night and if we don't act promptly and decisively we will always be behind."
Even today, some central bankers play down deflation's dangers. The Cleveland Fed devoted a chunk of its
annual report to explaining why mild deflation has benefits, such as minimizing the bias against cash.
European Central Bank president Wim Duisenberg says the Netherlands' two years of deflation while he ran
its central bank were "paradise."
Many Fed watchers consider Mr. Greenspan a dove because he, like Mr. McTeer, endorsed the "New
Economy" theory that improved productivity would damp inflationary pressures. Not so. Mr. Greenspan

--- Page 13 ---
Page 13 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
actually has a deep dislike of inflation forged in the Ford administration's hapless Whip Inflation Now
campaign. He thinks flat to falling prices can be a helpful spur to further productivity.
What changed to make deflation so much scarier? In a word: Japan. Since 1999, the Bank of Japan has cut
rates to zero and printed money furiously, but prices keep falling because Japan's banks can't or won't lend
new money. This repudiation of textbook economics frightens central bankers around the world.
Mr. McTeer says the Fed has learned from Japan's mistakes, which is why it won't "nickel and dime" its
response to a stagnating, postbubble economy. Mr. Greenspan doesn't need much convincing. He may not
think deflation is as fierce as its current reputation, but he doesn't want to find out for sure. "We need a much
wider firebreak because we know so little about it," Mr. Greenspan says. Until Japan figures out how to beat
deflation, count him as a dove, too.

Currency Trading: Dollar Finishes Mostly Higher Ahead of Slew of Economic Data
Currency Trading: Dollar Finishes Mostly Higher Ahead of Slew of Economic Data
By Michael Deibert
Dow Jones Newswires
608 words
29 July 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar firmed a bit versus most other major currencies, but held to relatively narrow ranges
as investors appeared reluctant to take any bold positions at the outset of a week full of important U.S.
economic data.
"I would sum up [yesterday] as the calm before the storm," said Rebecca Patterson, global currency strategist
with J.P. Morgan Chase in New York.
With U.S. employment, gross domestic product and manufacturing data all coming out this week, the euro
tested but failed for a third straight session to remain above the $1.1500 level, while the dollar managed to
grind up to a one-month high of 119.57 yen before scaling back.
After climbing to $1.1517 earlier in the session, the euro had pulled back below the $1.1500 level early in
New York, with a weaker-than-expected euro-zone business index as well as skittishness ahead of this
week's numbers contributing to the decline, strategists said.
Late yesterday, the euro was trading at $1.1494, down from $1.1510 late Friday. The dollar was trading at
119.43 yen, up from 118.85 yen Friday in New York. Against the Swiss franc, the dollar was at 1.3459 francs,
up from 1.3446 francs late Friday. Sterling was at $1.6257, up compared with $1.6206.
The German Ifo business climate index -- viewed as an important measure of business sentiment -- rose to
89.2 from 88.8 in June, data published yesterday showed. Business sentiment improved in all areas except
construction, the Munich, Germany, Ifo institute said. The results were slightly below expectations.
Economists polled by Dow Jones Newswires had forecast business sentiment to rise to 89.5.
The dollar showed little reaction to comments by Chicago Federal Reserve President Michael Moskow that
even though the Fed has been cognizant of the risk of disinflation and the possibility of a delayed economic
recovery, a movement in the federal-funds rate (which governs overnight interbank loans) to zero would be "a
very unlikely event."
Mr. Moskow, a voting member of the policy-setting Federal Open Market Committee in 2003, also
emphasized that while an unexpected shock could derail the Fed's expectations for the economy to regain
momentum into 2004, a more likely outcome is for a pickup in growth.
"I believe in the forecast. If the forecast is accurate, I think we will not reach a zero level of a fed-funds
target," Mr. Moskow said.
The Federal Reserve has forecast gross domestic product for 2003 at 2.5% to 2.75% and at an even faster
growth rate in 2004.
North of the border, the Canadian dollar ended lower in thin trading against the U.S. currency, as the lack of
progress for major currencies against the dollar led to a modest correction for the Canadian unit after several
sessions of gains. Late yesterday, the U.S. dollar was trading at C$1.3847, down from C$1.3850 earlier in the
session but higher than C$1.3797 late Friday.
Among the commodity currencies -- so-called because of their economies' heavy dependence on
commodities such as gold and oil, the South African rand punched ahead, underpinned by gold bullion
nudging $366 an ounce as well as offshore institutional demand.
Late yesterday, the dollar was buying 7.3475 rand, down substantially from late Friday's 7.4405 rand.

In Tehran, Boutiques Stock Hot Outerwear Under the Counter --- Women Clamor to Show Off Curves in Illegal Designs; Ms. Alf's Zippered Slits
In Tehran, Boutiques Stock Hot Outerwear Under the Counter --- Women Clamor to Show Off Curves
in Illegal Designs; Ms. Alf's Zippered Slits
By Farnaz Fassihi
1,214 words
28 July 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TEHRAN, Iran -- Katayoun Alf, a designer of Islamic outerwear in one of the world's strictest Islamic states,
knows her customers. They are women such as Sepideh Karimi, a 28-year-old with golden highlights in her
hair who strolled into a boutique here recently.
The racks in this store in a suburban mall were crammed with women's clothing that would shock the
founding fathers of Iran's Islamic revolution.
Traditional Islamic women's wear is a long, loose and shapeless black or brown hijab that reaches the ankles.
The garb's main purpose is to hide curves and smother all sexuality. But Ms. Karimi, like more and more
young women in Iran's urban centers, wants something different.
"This is too loose," said Ms. Karimi as she modeled a blue coat, known as a "manteau," that hangs just below
her hips, hugging her figure. "The sleeves are too long. I'm looking for something more sexy and
see-through."
Women's wear has long been an Iranian social and political battleground, especially among the country's
educated and relatively well-off urban elite. Millions of religious Iranian women still willingly wear hijab every
day as part of their devotion to Islam. But for women who chafe at government-dictated religious rules,
stretching the dress code has become a passion. And, try as it has, the conservative government hasn't been
able to put a stop to it.
Hijab, a word that means veil, is an outgrowth of Islamic Sharia law, which requires all females over the age
of 10 to conceal their hair under a scarf and wear a long, loose cloak that extends to the ankles. Reza Shah,
founder of the Pahlavi dynasty in Iran and father of the Shah whom the Iranian revolution ousted in 1979, was
keen on making Iran a secular, modern society. Headscarves, to him, were a sign of backwardness, and he
ordered soldiers to rip them off women's heads. But when religious leader Ayatollah Khomeini came to power,
Islamic revolutionary forces reversed the edict.
The hijab law written into Iran's constitution after the Islamic Republic's victory in 1979 made many Iranian
women even more determined to bend the rules, an inch at a time, starting with the hemlines. The
government pushed back. At first, stores and restaurants shunned women who weren't properly dressed, and
a now-defunct police unit, known as "moral police," set up checkpoints to stop, fine and even arrest women
who violated the rules. That didn't work, restrictions were eased, and women went wild.
"Everyone wants tight, tight, tight," said the salesman at the mall. "Let me show you Ms. Alf's most popular
style," he told Ms. Karimi. "It's flying off the shelves."
With that, he pulled a definite no-no off the rack: a paper-thin beige tunic made of stretchy material with two
slits on each side. It comes with a matching tank-top to wear underneath and sleeves decorated with brown
Indian motifs. Ms. Karimi's eyes lit up. She quickly tried it on, then paid $35 to make the outfit her own.
Ms. Alf, a 46-year-old former computer engineer, is one of a handful of Iranian designers determined to
transform hijab from shapeless to chic. These designers have a lot to do with the surprising, and to the
conservative Iranian government, appalling, fashion display in the streets of Tehran and other big cities.
"The trend began two years ago when the government eased social restrictions. Suddenly we looked around
us and said `wow!' " said Ms. Alf, a tall, Iranian-born woman with layered brown hair and a round face, clad in
one of her own designs: a black linen, knee-length coat with matching slacks. "Women are fed up," she
continued. "They come to the shops and say, `the sexier the better,' and we meet their demands or we won't
sell a single item."

--- Page 17 ---
Page 17 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Soaring demand prompted the government, in late May, to attempt another of its periodic crackdowns on
what it considers risque women's outerwear. Its focus this time is on shop owners. For the first time, the
government issued a written warning to stores and designers against making or selling "un-Islamic" manteaux
and threatening heavy fines for shops that disobey.
A poster in the suburban mall where Ms. Karimi bought her Alf creation warns stores not to sell tight-fitting
manteaux with slits longer than six inches and hemlines above the knee. Bright colors, such as red and pink,
are also banned.
So shopkeepers are now keeping daring designs such as Ms. Alf's in out-of-the-way places and bring them
out only when customers ask for them. The crackdown has merely increased demand among young Iranians
tired of religious intrusions into their lives.
In the days after the 1979 revolution, Ms. Alf and others were already resisting. They let their hair stick out
from under their scarves, wore red lipstick when they went shopping and decorated their loose outfits with
gold applique and diamond studs. Ms. Alf, was fired from her computer job in part, she says, because the
way she dressed was deemed un-Islamic. She decided to put her sewing skills and love of design to work
making Islamic dress fashionable.
"I looked around me and saw that no one, including myself, enjoyed looking like a head-to-toe black blob. We
all wanted to maintain our individuality while observing this hijab law. Plus, it's really boring to wear the same
thing day after day," said Ms. Alf, sitting in her studio while a dozen workers cut cloth and sewed her summer
collection.
Each season she designs and produces a collection of manteaux with the styles and colors derived from the
latest European fashions. Her collection is sold at the well-known Malakouti boutique chain, which specializes
in Islamic outerwear and has branches in Tehran and other major Iranian cities.
Ms. Alf, who is divorced and has a 21-year-old son, watches the Fashion Channel on satellite television
religiously. She makes regular trips to Istanbul and Dubai in search of the latest trends. Last winter, all her
coats were made of suede and trimmed in fur. This summer, she is pushing a figure-hugging linen tunic that
hangs just below the hip and has long slits on the sides. Wide belts -- and thin ones with beads hanging from
the side -- have been popular, as well. Light colors, including powder blue, beige and white, are selling well.
Given the new restrictions, Ms. Alf is thinking about next year. More clever than confrontational, she is
planning a safari line: long, loose cloak manteaux that are stylish but technically within the restrictions. They
will have zippered slits from hemline to waist, allowing women to adjust them at a moment's notice, and long
sleeves that can be buttoned up to the shoulder.
Crackdowns just help business, Ms. Alf insists. She has been through them before. "The more they tighten
the leash, the more resolve women get. It's one fight the clerics will never win."

The Italian Job: Banker Shakes Up Secretive World Of Finance in Milan --- Mr. Profumo Says He Wants To Change Italy, but Some See Old-Style...
The Italian Job: Banker Shakes Up Secretive World Of Finance in Milan --- Mr. Profumo Says He Wants
To Change Italy, but Some See Old-Style Power Play --- Nationalism and Merrill Lynch
By Alessandra Galloni and Marcus Walker
2,274 words
22 July 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MILAN -- From a former convent behind La Scala opera house, investment bank Mediobanca SpA called the
tune of Italian finance for more than 50 years. It amassed a web of corporate shareholdings and the power to
remove chief executives, thwart takeovers and dominate some of Italy's biggest companies, often secretively
and with little apparent concern for minority investors.
Then Alessandro Profumo butted in. Mr. Profumo, chief executive of UniCredito Italiano SpA, Italy's
second-biggest commercial bank, orchestrated a shareholder revolt that ousted Mediobanca's boss in April.
Proclaiming himself the "bearer of change," the 46-year-old Mr. Profumo said the campaign was intended to
open a Byzantine financial system that propped up ailing companies against global market forces. Under new
management, Mediobanca is starting to unwind some of its stakes in corporate Italy, the world's
seventh-largest economy.
This Italian civil war underscores the tortuous path in Europe toward creating modern, open capital markets.
The close-knit elite of banks and industrial families that dominated countries such as Italy is slowly losing its
grip, as the European Union stitches together a mammoth economy, second only to that of the U.S. New
forces, such as institutional investors and international rating agencies, are gaining clout in Europe Inc.
Even though Europe's biggest economies are moving toward equal treatment of shareholders and opening
up their financial systems, barriers remain. French and German governments sometimes seek to protect
so-called national champions from foreign control. In Italy, most fund-management firms are controlled by
banks, which have cozy relationships with industry, calling into question their independence.
Old habits linger. In many countries, families control major listed companies despite investing little capital, by
using holding companies and shareholder agreements. Many companies retain stakes in each other,
insulating them from takeovers and criticism from minority shareholders. And the government is often a
shareholder or is willing to be. Italian Prime Minister Silvio Berlusconi, himself a corporate tycoon, last year
suggested that Rome invest in struggling auto maker Fiat SpA, only to back down.
Despite Mr. Profumo's stated goal of modernizing Italy, some see a more familiar power play. The
Mediobanca-UniCredito feud sucked in the Italian government, toyed with foreign investors and used Italy's
largest insurance company as a pawn. Expedience vied with principles in Mr. Profumo's quest. He invoked
nationalism while using a U.S. bank, Merrill Lynch, as a behind-the-scenes operator. The self-made financier,
whose company has been a favorite of foreign investors, argues that he had to play within the system to
change it.
Nobody practiced the clubby corporate ways better than Mediobanca. Its headquarters is a 17th-century
palazzo on a cobbled lane in Milan. There's no sign on the door. Inside, gold-lacquered ceilings decorate the
main dining hall, where a butler irons the table linen and cooks for executives. Despite a staff of just 390
people, the investment bank is Italy's mergers-and-acquisitions leader, arranging deals valued at $24.6 billion
last year, according to Dealogic, a British data firm.
Mediobanca was established in 1946 by Enrico Cuccia, a Sicilian who steered the bank until he died in 2000
at 92. A small, stooped man with sad blue eyes, Mr. Cuccia was rarely seen and never heard in public,
spending free time in his library at home reading the classics. He crafted networks of shareholdings to defend
clients, such as Fiat's Agnelli dynasty and the tire-making Pirelli clan, at a time when the Italian government
nationalized companies unable to find financing in the weak post-World War II capital markets.
As Italy prospered, companies raised money on the stock market, thus diluting the controlling families' stakes.
Mr. Cuccia helped clients keep command of their boards. His tactic was to create so-called syndicate pacts:
deals among core shareholders who own just enough of a company's stock to call the shots. Mediobanca
often bought stakes in clients and joined their syndicate pacts, giving it huge clout in corporate Italy.

--- Page 19 ---
Page 19 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mediobanca's most valuable asset was a controlling 14% stake in Assicurazioni Generali SpA, Europe's
biggest insurance company in terms of market valuation. Generali, in turn, owns a 2% stake in Mediobanca.
The investment bank also has cross-shareholdings with Fiat and Pirelli, and stakes in seven more Italian
blue-chip companies, including financial institutions and the parent company of one of Italy's biggest
newspapers, Corriere della Sera.
In the 1990s, Mediobanca's hegemony started to come under attack. Fund managers urged Italian firms to
treat all shareholders as equals, and old clients looked abroad for deals and advice. Mr. Cuccia's death
weakened the aura of invincibility. His successor, Vincenzo Maranghi, lacked Mr. Cuccia's finesse, bank
insiders say. But Mr. Maranghi did share his love of secrecy.
Declining to be interviewed for this article, he responded in a letter: "I adhere to a tradition -- or a school of
thought, if you prefer -- according to which a banker does not speak about issues directly or indirectly linked
to his profession."
The changing times created an opening for Mr. Profumo, whose salty language and back-slapping manner
are more typical of Wall Street than Milan's palazzos. Like Mediobanca's founder, Mr. Profumo was raised in
the Sicilian capital of Palermo. He moved to Milan when he was 19 and worked his way through college as a
bank clerk. After other bank jobs and a stint as a McKinsey &Co. consultant in Italy, Mr. Profumo shot to the
upper echelons of corporate Italy in 1997, when he became CEO of Credito Italiano SpA, a colossal and
sluggish former state-owned lender.
The new boss, a 6-foot-3 workholic who lists no hobbies on his resume, cut jobs and bought small but
successful regional banks. He integrated them to create UniCredito, Italy's second-biggest bank, with assets
of $245 billion. Unlike many Italian peers, Mr. Profumo pursued an international vision. He tried but failed to
merge with big Spanish and German banks, moved aggressively into Eastern Europe, acquiring banks in six
countries, and in 2000 paid $1.2 billion to buy U.S. mutual-fund company Pioneer Group, of Boston.
Though foreign investors shuddered when Mr. Profumo held merger talks in 2001 with unprofitable German
lender Commerzbank AG, they generally favored UniCredito stock because of his track record for profits.
UniCredito has a return on equity of more than 17% -- the highest in Italy. "We were the only ones with the
credibility to take on Mediobanca," Mr. Profumo says.
He didn't start out gunning for Mediobanca. In fact, he was a member of the club: UniCredito, the bank Mr.
Profumo runs, has long been part of Mediobanca's shareholder syndicate and has four seats on its
21-member board. His first clash came in 1999, when UniCredito tried to take over Banca Commerciale
Italiana SpA. BCI, too, held a stake in Mediobanca, and the investment bank feared a merger would threaten
it by creating a big shareholder on its own board. Mediobanca scuttled the deal through its control of insurer
Generali, which had a big stake in BCI and managed to divide the board. "We were quite naive then," recalls
Mr. Profumo.
Mr. Profumo started cultivating allies. One was Paolo Fresco, a former General Electric Co. vice chairman
who returned to Italy in 1998 to become chairman of Fiat. The industrial conglomerate was a longtime ally of
Mediobanca, which helped bail out Fiat twice in the past two decades. But Mr. Fresco, known as "the
American" in Italian financial circles, had no allegiance to the investment bank. As a member of Mediobanca's
board, he had even clashed with Mr. Maranghi, who he says resisted his attempts to make the bank more
transparent.
"Mediobanca had become the antithesis of the free market," says Mr. Fresco, who left Fiat earlier this year.
One deal that showed Mediobanca's determination to foil rivals, to the possible detriment of shareholders,
occurred in June 2002. Fiat had given UniCredito a mandate to take public its sports-car unit, Ferrari SpA.
But Mediobanca stepped in and bought a 34% stake for $875 million, a price that put Ferrari's value at nearly
twice that of Porsche AG, the world's most profitable car maker. The move thwarted Mr. Profumo and handed
a windfall to Fiat.
"Mediobanca showed up and played Santa Claus with its shareholders money," Mr. Profumo says.
UniCredito was the second-biggest shareholder in Mediobanca, with an 8.7% stake.
Mr. Profumo began rallying shareholders to oust Mr. Maranghi, whom he publicly criticized. The rancor split
Mediobanca's board, but Mr. Profumo couldn't muster a majority.
So he changed tack. He mounted an indirect attack via insurer Generali, which with its own tentacles
throughout corporate Italy, was the key to Mediobanca's power. Mediobanca had already dismayed foreign
investors by meddling in Generali; Mediobanca had ousted three chairmen since 1999, hobbling the
insurance giant amid growing competition, and prompting a number of international fund managers to sell
Generali.

--- Page 20 ---
Page 20 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Profumo sought to build a bigger stake in Generali than Mediobanca's 14%. UniCredito and two other
Italian banks quietly began buying shares in Generali at the end of last year, according to officials at two of
the banks. The market suspected something was brewing: Generali's share price surged 40% in February
alone.
Mr. Profumo, the self-styled financial modernizer, used old ways to outflank Mediobanca. He waited until the
end of February to show his hand, announcing that UniCredito had bought a 2% stake in Generali for $678
million. He lobbied the government, winning public backing from Italy's central bank, whose pension plan
held 4.6% of Generali.
Mr. Profumo also invoked nationalism, hinting that he was trying to neutralize French investors in
Mediobanca, to "protect the Italian nature" of Generali. "It was the best thing we could hide behind," he says.
The nationalist card was particularly ironic because Merrill Lynch emerged as a key UniCredito operator,
buying shares and options valued at nearly 5% of Generali. Some were bought on behalf of UniCredito, one
person familiar with the matter says. The U.S. bank, which declined to comment, sold its stake in late June.
By late March, UniCredito and two allied banks had spent nearly $3.4 billion, bankers say, to acquire some
10% of Generali. The support of Italy's central bank and other Generali shareholders gave Mr. Profumo and
allies a larger stake than Mediobanca's.
On April 7, over a buffet lunch of prosciutto and salad in UniCredito's second-floor executive dining room, Mr.
Profumo sealed a deal with the French investors. Afterward, a delegation drove to Mediobanca, where the
defeated Mr. Maranghi asked for no severance, "just seven days' notice," he said, according to Tarak Ben
Ammar, an aide to a French investor, who was there.
Change is coming slowly to Mediobanca. Under a new chief executive, who was chosen by Mr. Profumo and
his main allies, Mediobanca plans to strengthen investment-banking operations and gradually dispose of
some of the shareholdings that gave it influence. Last week, the bank said that only certain companies --
including Generali, Fiat and Pirelli -- are strategic investments. It has begun preparing to sell holdings in
defense, energy and some financial-services firms.
Other issues smack of old Italy, however. Conflicts of interest linger. Banks UniCredito and Capitalia SpA --
which have competing investment-banking arms -- are still large shareholders in Mediobanca. Both banks
have said they will reduce their respective stakes to 6% from around 9%, and Mr. Profumo has promised to
step down from the investment bank's executive committee.
And Generali is now controlled by an unwieldy coalition of banks. Mr. Profumo initially said that UniCredito
would sell its stake in the short-term, but last week, his board's chairman said UniCredito's stake in Generali
is a "medium-to-long term investment."
This concerns international investors, who hoped for an end to Italian banks controlling major companies.
"The worry is that Profumo may just be another power player," says Dirk Bartsch, European finance analyst
at Allianz AG's mutual-fund arm in Frankfurt.
Others are willing to give Mr. Profumo the benefit of the doubt. "When the dust settles, what he did could turn
out to be better for more people than what was there before," says Stephen Oler, an international portfolio
manager at Putnam Investments in Boston.
UniCredito and its partners have said they plan to give top Generali executives three-year terms instead of
one-year mandates currently, to free Generali from the revolving-door management that alienated foreign
investors. No steps have been taken so far, however.
Even Mr. Profumo admits that after prevailing over Mediobanca's old guard, the new order of Italian finance
still has to prove itself. "I am convinced what we did is in the interest of the market, but we'll have to see," he
says.

Money Trail: In North Korea, Secret Cash Hoard Props Up Regime --- Defectors, Intelligence Sources Say Division 39 Supplies Billions to Kim...
Money Trail: In North Korea, Secret Cash Hoard Props Up Regime --- Defectors, Intelligence Sources
Say Division 39 Supplies Billions to Kim Jong Il --- Ginseng and Counterfeit Bills
By Jay Solomon and Hae Won Choi
2,713 words
14 July 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SEOUL, South Korea -- Inside North Korea, it goes by the Orwellian name of Division 39. It is a largely
unpublicized trading network and slush fund. The money it generates is, by all accounts, the lifeblood of Kim
Jong Il's dictatorship.
According to interviews with high-level defectors, South Korean businessmen and Asian intelligence officials,
Division 39 has generated a cash hoard as large as $5 billion that is salted away in places as disparate as
Macau, Switzerland and Pyongyang. It produces a steady flow of money that Mr. Kim uses to buy political
support and loyalty. Intelligence officials have also tied it to Pyongyang's efforts to develop weapons of mass
destruction.
Concerns about North Korea's nuclear-weapons program have intensified over the past week after South
Korea's intelligence agency said it believes Pyongyang has begun reprocessing some of the 8,000 spent fuel
rods it extracted from its Yongbyon nuclear complex earlier this year. U.S. officials believe North Korea could
use this fissile material to potentially create five to six new atomic weapons in coming years.
So critical is Division 39 that defectors and North Asian security officials say cutting off its cash flow is the key
for those in the U.S. and elsewhere advocating regime change in Pyongyang. By shutting down Division 39's
businesses, "you can shut down Kim Jong Il," says Kim Dok Hong, a high-ranking defector who has worked
with Division 39 companies.
The U.S. supports such a shutdown. The Bush administration has said it is working with allies to block North
Korea's trade in illicit drugs, counterfeit dollars and weapons. Japan in recent weeks has tightened safety and
customs inspections of North Korean ships, which Tokyo has long suspected of smuggling missile parts and
narcotics between the two countries. Seoul has said that it will tie most future economic cooperation between
the two countries to Pyongyang's abandoning efforts to build nuclear weapons.
According to defectors, Division 39 was set up during the mid-1970s to fund Kim Jong Il's political career.
Headquartered on Pyongyang's main thoroughfare just down from the Russian Embassy, it has offices
throughout North Korea that funnel donations to Mr. Kim. Defectors say citizens are required to make annual
donations to Division 39 on important holidays honoring the so-called Dear Leader and his father.
Businessmen and overseas diplomats are also called on to raise cash for Division 39. The name, defectors
say, reflects the secret number the Communist Party assigned to its fund-raising offices.
Division 39 has two main arms. One is involved in illegal activities, senior-level defectors and North Asian
intelligence officials say. The other engages mostly in legitimate business under the Daesong Group, its
Daesong Bank and Vienna-based Golden Star Bank.
Song Il Hyok, a North Korean diplomat in Hong Kong, acknowledged the existence of Division 39 but said he
was "not in the position to comment" about its specific operations. He flatly denied that North Korea is
involved in any type of illicit activities, particularly allegations that it is trading in narcotics and counterfeit U.S.
currency. "I can say that this is a cynical campaign . . . and not true at all," Mr. Song says. "Historically, this
was used to defame our country."
A Golden Star bank official in Vienna, who declined to give his name, denied any suggestions that his
institution was involved in improprieties. "There is absolutely no proof and this is a pure slander," the official
said in a telephone interview from Vienna.
Tokyo authorities in April blocked a Japanese company from selling to a Daesong unit three power-control
devices that Japanese intelligence officials say could be used to enrich uranium for atomic weapons.
Executives from Daesong affiliates have been arrested by police in Macau in recent years for allegedly
trafficking in counterfeit U.S. dollar notes. Investigators in South Korea and Macau say some of these and

--- Page 23 ---
Page 23 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
other Division 39 officials also are involved in heroin and amphetamines trafficking that generates as much as
$500 million annually for Pyongyang.
Daesong Group and its subsidiaries are North Korea's largest exporters of pine mushrooms and ginseng --
both prized in Japan and China -- and other agricultural goods. Daesong holds virtual monopolies on the
country's gold, silver and magnesium mining, and on the seafood trade.
All this Division 39 business supports the patronage system that has allowed Kim Jong Il to extend his
family's rule to more than 50 years. The slush fund's money is doled out to key military men and officials of
the ruling Workers' Party officials to ensure their allegiance. It finances the Dear Leader's indulgence in
gambling, imported alcohol and Scandinavian women, according to former senior party members.
The rest of the citizenry still copes with a lingering famine that began in the mid-1990s and claimed as many
as two million lives. The United Nations World Food Program says the country needs aid donations to feed
more than six million of its 23 million people this year. The per capita gross national income in North Korea
was less than $1,000 last year, compared with more than $9,000 in the South.
Kim Jong Il formally took power in 1998, following the death of his father, Kim Il Sung, four years earlier. But
many experts say the younger Kim began running key sections of the government more than two decades
earlier, starting in 1974, after his father named him as his successor.
The son faced trouble consolidating his power in those early days. North Koreans never accorded him the
same respect they did Kim Il Sung, who was revered as the father of the nation's anticolonial and communist
movements. Recognizing the need to shore up his position, the younger Kim established Division 39 as a
secret unit within the Workers' Party that would finance a major restructuring of the party. This included
increasing the number of seats on the party's Central Committee to include men loyal to Kim Jong Il, and
intensifying the propaganda programs aimed at glorifying his achievements as he prepared to take power
formally, say defectors and North Korea analysts.
To build Division 39, defectors say Mr. Kim consolidated and brought under its control the country's key
natural resources, particularly the minerals that have fueled North Korea's economy since before the 1950-53
Korean War. He chose his closest confidants to run its operations and sent young recruits to Europe to learn
about the global financial system.
Choi Se Woong, the son of North Korea's finance minister in the early 1980s, says Mr. Kim personally sent
him to Vienna in 1984. Fluent in German, Mr. Choi boned up on foreign-exchange trading and
account-clearing procedures. He says he advised North Korean officials in establishing the Golden Star Bank
in Vienna, North Korea's only financial institution in Europe. Mr. Choi then returned to Pyongyang to advise
bankers at Daesong Bank and other institutions about Western finance.
"Kim Jong Il needed to take over the party. But to do this, he had to generate money," Mr. Choi says today
from Seoul, where he has run a currency-trading firm since his 1995 defection. "He wanted to set up more
banks" with access to outside markets.
Daesong and its subsidiaries brought in money from trading commodities and produce, primarily with
communist-bloc countries and Japan. Tokyo officials say Pyongyang had been earning as much as $58
million annually from the pine-mushroom trade, though these revenues have dropped off sharply in recent
years due to Japanese concerns about the quality of the product and North Korea's nuclear program.
Division 39 companies have also found an unlikely ally in their business ventures in South Korea's chaebol,
the family-owned conglomerates. During the 1990s, such companies as the LG and Samsung groups bought
more than $200 million of gold from North Korean companies and used the mineral in high-tech components.
Intelligence officials say they believe that virtually all of this money went into Division 39 accounts, due to its
monopoly over the minerals trade.
Lee Jong Keun, who heads LG International Corp.'s North Korean operations in Seoul, says that LG bought
the gold from Pyongyang through middlemen based in either Macau or Beijing. (Macau reverted to China's
rule in 1999.)
Mr. Lee says there was virtually no direct contact between LG and the North. He says that his company's
payments were sometimes made into accounts that belonged to individuals, not companies, due to
restrictions North Korea placed on its overseas companies' business operations. Mr. Lee says he's aware of
Division 39's operations but he doubts that his company has been dealing with it directly.
A spokesman for Samsung Corp. confirmed the company purchased gold from North Korea, but said he
couldn't provide specific details. The Samsung team that was in charge of buying the gold has been
dissolved, he said.

--- Page 24 ---
Page 24 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Since his initial use of Division 39 to establish his political base, Kim Jong Il has continued to draw on the
slush fund to strengthen his patronage system, senior North Korean defectors say. This includes access to
the dictator's drinking parties and private audiences where gifts are showered on attendees, as well as
accompanying him on trips abroad and enjoying the best wines and food.
A high-level defector whose father served on the Central Committee during the 1980s recalls his father's
disappearing for days to attend lavish parties with high-stakes gambling and drinking contests. The defector
says his father would come home with imported Japanese stereos and deluxe beds from Europe -- courtesy
of the "Great General."
The defector Kim Dok Hong, who served for 17 years on the Central Committee, says Kim Jong Il would often
host theme parties, where the food and dress paid tribute to a foreign country. He says he attended one party
where the waiters all wore Italian clothing and were serving pasta and pizza. "The next night it might be
French," Mr. Kim says.
The number of defectors from the North has soared in recent years, to 1,140 last year from eight in 1992,
according to the South Korean Ministry of Unification. Kim Dok Hong and Choi Se Woong are among the
highest-level officials to defect.
The North Korean leader also rewarded those seen as having made exceptional contributions to his
communist state, including athletes, artists and scientists. Kim Kil Sun, who served as a journalist for a North
Korean military publication before defecting in 1997, says Mr. Kim delivered television sets to a slew of
scientists at the Yongbyon nuclear complex after they made major advances in their quest for nuclear arms in
the early 1990s. "Having a TV in North Korea is like having a private airplane" in the West, Kim Kil Sun says.
North Korea's economy, never strong, has weakened further since the early 1990s. With the collapse of the
Soviet Union in the early 1990s and China's market reforms, Pyongyang has lost the benefit of the communist
club's exclusive aid-and-trade supports. North Korea's legitimate economy has grown much less productive,
with total exports falling to just $730 million last year from $1.7 billion in 1990, according to South Korea's
central bank. As a result, Division 39's operations have increasingly turned to trade in illicit goods and
weapons systems, intelligence officials say.
Drug shipments to North Asia, which includes the Korean peninsula, Japan and Taiwan, have also been
rising in recent years, these officials say. In the past three months alone, police in Australia and South Korea
have seized drugs allegedly either produced or marketed by North Korea estimated to be worth hundreds of
millions of dollars. U.S. military officials estimate that North Korea's annual drug exports have risen to at least
$500 million from about $100 million just a few years ago.
South Korean police and some lawmakers believe hundreds of millions of dollars were illegally shipped into
Division 39's accounts from the South through Macau in recent years. A special independent investigative
team held a formal inquiry into the Hyundai Group's admission that it sent $500 million to the North just before
a historic 2000 summit between Kim Jong Il and South Korea's then-President Kim Dae Jung. A report
released this past June 25 showed that about $187 million was sent to North Korean accounts at a Macau
branch of a Chinese bank, while other money was sent to accounts in Austria and Singapore.
North Asian intelligence officials are focusing on Macau because they believe Pyongyang has used it as a
logistical and financial base for decades. North Korea's bombing of a Korean Air jetliner in 1987 was partially
plotted from Macau, according to a female North Korean agent who was eventually convicted for the
bombing. The officials say Division 39, using a local unit of Daesong called Zokwang Trading Co., has
employed Macau as a base to move counterfeit dollars and to try to procure components for its weapons
systems.
In 1994, five Zokwang officials were arrested for trying to pass $250,000 in fake U.S. dollar notes, says a
Macau police official involved in the bust. Their actions were part of a wider counterfeiting operation run by
Division 39 that introduced nearly $50 million in counterfeit U.S. currency into the global financial system, the
official says. The Zokwang officials exchanged $100,000 of the fake notes at a small commercial bank in
Macau. Emboldened, they tried to exchange a further $150,000 at the Macau bank but were apprehended
when U.S. banks in Hong Kong raised an alarm about the notes coming from the bank.
The North Korean officials were eventually released because they had diplomatic passports. Mr. Song, the
North Korean diplomat in Hong Kong, says the Zokwang officials acquired the fake notes by mistake. "North
Koreans have no choice but to carry large amounts of cash, as the U.S. freezes us out of the global financial
system," Mr. Song says.
Austrian officials have also kept a close watch on the Golden Star Bank in Vienna, fearing Division 39 might
be using it to fund illicit operations. An Austrian Interior Ministry report from 1997 noted that "this bank was
named repeatedly in connection with money laundering and distributing counterfeit money and even
involvement in the illegal trade of radioactive substances." An Austrian intelligence official said in May that he

--- Page 25 ---
Page 25 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
believed Golden Star Bank hardly engages in financial transactions anymore, but instead is a front for North
Korean spies.
Austrian regulators say they've been closely monitoring Golden Star for years, but haven't moved because of
its slackening business and no known legal violations. At the end of 2001, the bank had assets of only $19.2
million to $20.4 million, an Austrian regulator says. That was almost all loans to North Korean state
companies, such as Daesong.
Division 39's declining business prospects -- and North Korea's vulnerability to economic pressure -- are also
evident at the Macau headquarters of Zokwang Trading, which is widely viewed as Division 39's most potent
overseas tool. The offices are situated on the fifth floor of a nondescript building. The doorbell plays "Frere
Jacques."
The Zokwang executive at the office on a recent day was Park Ja Byong, the company's chief representative.
He was one of the five Zokwang officials released by police, but he is banned from leaving the territory. Mr.
Park said his company's ability to engage in business -- particularly procuring electronics components -- is
drying up as the U.S. tightens the economic pressure on his country.
"For 50 years, the U.S. has blocked all markets for North Korea and blocked us politically," the 65-year-old
man said from behind wire glasses. "Realistically, we realize this pressure is only going to increase."

Marcus Walker in Frankfurt and Miho Inada in Tokyo contributed to this article.

Currency Trading: Dollar Rises in Peripheral Arena Amid Unwinding of Rate Plays
Currency Trading: Dollar Rises in Peripheral Arena Amid Unwinding of Rate Plays
By Agnes T. Crane
Dow Jones Newswires
661 words
9 July 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar climbed steeply against many of its peripheral rivals as investors unwound positions
put in place when interest-rate differentials were the only thing that mattered in currency markets.
Those broad inroads helped the dollar consolidate its gains against the euro and yen, at one point pushing
the European currency as low as $1.1259, its weakest point since May 5, although both currencies ended
New York trading largely unchanged against the dollar.
The recent advances in global stocks and declines in bonds have put the dollar back in the limelight amid
growing optimism that the global economy is poised for a rebound.
The euro dipped below $1.13 on more than one occasion, but spent much of the day consolidating between
$1.1250 to $1.1350 after its steep fall the day before when it shed more than 1 1/2 cents.
The so-called commodity currencies weren't so lucky. The Australian, Canadian and New Zealand dollars --
referred to as commodity currencies because of the dependence of their economies on commodity prices
such as gold and oil -- sank against the U.S. dollar, becoming yet more casualties to a reversal of fortunes in
the global bond and stock markets. The price of gold also fell steeply, with the Comex August gold contract
slipping $4 to $344.40.
Nothing specific appeared to have sparked the losses in the dollar bloc currencies other than a continuation
of the move to unwind global bond positions.
"I don't think it's anything sinister or anything like that. It's just got a really, really positive tone right now for the
[U.S.] dollar, and as a result, the Canadian dollar's suffering," said Steve Butler, director of foreign exchange
at Scotia Capital in Toronto.
The correction lower for the Canadian dollar marked one of the bigger such moves since the currency
embarked on its massive rally at the beginning of the year, he said. "This is a very good correction, probably
the biggest move we've seen in terms of a pull back," he said.
In late afternoon trading, the euro was at $1.1314, up from its session low of $1.1259 but little changed from
$1.1324 late Monday. The dollar was trading at 118.23 yen, up from its session low of 117.59 yen, and up
slightly from 118.16 yen late Monday in New York. Against the Swiss franc, the dollar was at 1.3687 francs,
down from 1.3725 francs late Monday. Sterling was at $1.6357, down compared with $1.6479. The euro was
fetching 133.68 yen, nearly unchanged from 133.66 yen late Monday.
Meanwhile, the Australian dollar was fetching 66.05 U.S. cents, down from 67.90 U.S. cents in late trade
Monday while the New Zealand dollar was at 58.91 U.S. cents, down from 60.15 U.S. cents. The dollar was
at C$1.3644, up from C$1.3472 late Monday.
The reflation of economies across the globe, but most notably in the U.S. and Japan, has greatly diminished
what had been a driving force in currency markets -- the hunt for yield. When investors held a grimmer
economic view and Treasurys and Japanese government bond yields bounced around record lows, funds
gravitated toward sovereign debt in nations that offered higher returns such as in Australia, Canada, New
Zealand and the U.K.
The situation has changed during the past few weeks, however, most noticeably since the Federal Reserve
cut interest rates by 0.25 percentage point rather than a more aggressive half-percentage point that many
expected in the market. That fueled a sense that the Fed wasn't as concerned about deflation as it had
indicated, sparking a rush out of global bonds.

Currency Trading: Dollar Gains, but Then Retreats After Pallid U.S. Economic Data
Currency Trading: Dollar Gains, but Then Retreats After Pallid U.S. Economic Data
By Michael Deibert
Dow Jones Newswires
646 words
1 July 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C18
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar retreated from an initial surge, giving back gains against its major rivals after an
economic survey reduced enthusiasm for the U.S. currency.
Early in the New York session, the dollar succeeded in sailing all the way to 120.24 yen, its highest level
versus its Japanese counterpart since late April, while the euro dropped briefly to $1.1400, close to a
six-week low.
However, the dollar changed course after the Purchasing Management Association of Chicago index of area
business activity was released, with strategists suggesting that the less-than-stark gain disappointed a market
searching for traces of robust U.S. economic health.
"I think the Chicago numbers show that people have embedded a very positive third quarter [in their currency
positions] and that if the data don't live up to it, then the dollar is susceptible to downside risk," said Marcel
Kasumovich, an economist with Merrill Lynch in New York.
Late yesterday, the dollar was trading at 119.73 yen, only a touch up from 119.69 yen late Friday. The euro
was trading at $1.1515, up sharply from $1.1430 on Friday.
Against the Swiss franc the dollar was buying 1.3512 francs, little changed from 1.3506 francs on Friday.
Sterling was at $1.6558, up from $1.6490 on Friday.
Comments from the Bank for International Settlements in Switzerland, which functions as a forum for the
world's central banks, tended to cast a shadow on the dollar by noting downside risks for the U.S. currency,
analysts said.
To further cloud matters concerning the dollar's recent bout of strength with its rivals, figures released
yesterday revealed that the Bank of Japan spent far less in June to support the dollar than many had
expected, but intervention still got much of the credit for the U.S. currency's surge yesterday.
"The fact that the [Bank of Japan] came in again, though a little less than the market anticipated, shows that
they've been vigilant against yen strength, and that's put a fear factor into traders minds that below a certain
level, the [Bank of Japan] is ready to intervene," said Tim Mazanec, senior currency strategist with Investor's
Bank &Trust in Boston.
Among the commodity currencies, the South African rand managed to push the dollar down to 7.4738 rands,
down from Friday's level of 7.5301 rands. A better-than-expected trade surplus, and a rise in the bullion price
to $347.15 a troy ounce were credited with helping the rand, as South Africa is the world's biggest gold
producer.
Elsewhere, the Australian dollar was sharply up against the U.S. dollar, buttressed by news that the country's
trade deficit narrowed more than expected in May and strong household-credit data showed no urgent need
for a cut in interest rates. The Australian dollar was trading at 67.37 U.S. cents, up from 66.63 U.S. cents on
Friday.
Meanwhile, the Canadian dollar ended little changed in thin but choppy trading, with the currency's erratic
movements exaggerated by thin liquidity and the absence of many market participants due to the approach of
the Canada Day national holiday today.
Late yesterday, the U.S. dollar was trading at C$1.3475, up from C$1.3463 late Friday.

--- Page 29 ---
Page 29 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Traders attributed an early bout of weakness for the Canadian dollar mainly to cross-pressures against the
euro and pound sterling, both of which had risen strongly versus the Canadian unit in the past two trading
sessions, before giving back some of those gains later yesterday.
