Caribbean Cloud: With a Banker Facing Charges, A Nation Questions Its Success --- As the Dominican Republic Thrived, So Did Mr. Baez; Now...
Caribbean Cloud: With a Banker Facing Charges, A Nation Questions Its Success --- As the
Dominican Republic Thrived, So Did Mr. Baez; Now, Both Are Reeling --- An Alleged $2.2 Billion Fraud
By Jose de Cordoba
2,732 words
30 June 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SANTO DOMINGO, Dominican Republic -- Ramon Baez celebrated his second wedding in 1997 at the
exclusive Casa de Campo resort, as a galaxy of Latin superstars entertained more than 600 guests.
Then-President Leonel Fernandez, an official witness, danced into the night. Ill with cancer, the head of the
main opposition party, Jose Francisco Pena Gomez, rose from his sickbed to sign the matrimonial certificate.
Promoters estimated the reception cost at least $2 million.
The event was more than a showcase of extravagance and wealth in a country where per capita income is
about $2,000 a year. Mr. Baez, the 47-year-old president of one of the country's biggest banks and the
architect of a dazzling business empire, was a symbol of the Dominican Republic's emergence as the
economic star of the Caribbean region.
Then, last month, Mr. Baez was arrested by Dominican police on an array of charges, including fraud, money
laundering and tax evasion. He stands accused of using two sets of books at his Banco Intercontinental to
siphon off $2.2 billion in recent years -- a sum equal to nearly 67% of the Dominican Republic's annual
national budget and 15% of its yearly gross domestic product. As the scandal has unfolded since last
September, the fallout has rocked the Dominican economy. The peso has plummeted 50% since January,
triggering a plea by national leaders for help from the International Monetary Fund. Economists expect growth
to be flat this year, after a 4% rise in 2002.
The scandal has tarred dozens of leading figures from all sectors of national life -- from past and current
presidents, to leading journalists and justices of the Supreme Court, many of whom allegedly received lavish
gifts or regular payments from the bank. According to a report by the country's central bank, Mr. Baez and
the bank, better known as Baninter, distributed over several years a total of about $75 million in perks and
payments to government officials and other influential people.
The affair is also clouding one of Latin America's brightest recent success stories. The Dominican Republic,
whose 8.5 million residents share the island of Hispaniola with the impoverished country of Haiti, languished
under decades of autocratic rule, suffering military coups and marred elections. In the early 1990s, as part of
an agreement with the IMF, then-President Joaquin Balaguer began to implement reforms.
The economy took off after the 1996 election of President Leonel Fernandez. He sold government sugar
mills, the state-owned electric company and many industries formerly owned by Gen. Rafael Leonidas
Trujillo, the notorious dictator assassinated in 1961. Foreign investment poured into tourism developments,
telecommunications and assembly plants set up in free-trade zones. In the late 1990s, the Dominican
economy grew at an annual rate of nearly 8%, Latin America's highest.
Underscoring the financial impact of the alleged Baninter fraud, the Dominican government announced earlier
this week a rigorous austerity regime, as part of a proposed agreement with the IMF. In return for about $1
billion in financing, the government agreed, among other things, to cut public spending, freeze wages of state
employees, levy new taxes and adopt more stringent bank-examination rules.
The Dominican armed forces issued a statement last week warning citizens not to listen to "ominous and
irresponsible voices" and assuring that the military is ready to maintain order. It was a grim reminder that in
1984, after an earlier IMF deal, 112 Dominicans were killed in fighting between troops and rioters protesting
steep price increases.
The military announcement has added to the widespread anxiety here that the Baninter scandal could show
that the country's recent gains have been illusory. Many people believe that how the government handles this
crisis -- and whether Mr. Baez and his associates are fully prosecuted -- will be a key measure of how much
progress the Dominican Republic has actually made.

--- Page 31 ---
Page 31 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Baez declined a request for an interview. His lawyer, Marino Vinicio Castillo, says Mr. Baez is the
innocent victim of a government conspiracy to seize his client's media properties in advance of the next
election. "It's an odious and dangerous power play," he says.
The great-grandson of a 19th-century Dominican president, Mr. Baez graduated with a business degree from
a local university and began working with his father, a well-respected former commerce minister and part
owner of a shipping line, Baez &Rannick. Ramon Baez Sr. founded an insurance company, Intercontinental
de Seguros S.A., and then started Baninter in 1986. Within a few years, the younger Mr. Baez became
president of the bank.
The timing was perfect. Mr. Baez, and Baninter, rode the crest of the wave unleashed by the government's
economic reforms. In 1996, with the blessing of the Fernandez government and the backing of the central
bank, Baninter merged with the ailing Banco de Comercio, acquiring the most profitable parts of its portfolio.
Under the deal, the central bank absorbed Banco de Comercio's biggest liabilities. Baninter emerged as the
third largest bank in the country. "The central bank got the bones. Baninter got the steak," says a former
central bank governor.
Mr. Baez went on a spree, buying companies as varied as a gas distributor and importers of air conditioners
and appliances. In 1997, he founded his own newspaper. He gobbled up radio and television stations, and in
2000 acquired Listin Diario, the nation's most important daily publication. Earlier this year, even as Baninter
was beginning to wobble, Mr. Baez founded a 24-hour television news station and inaugurated a Dominican
financial journal, "El Financiero."
Baninter, meanwhile, attracted a steady flow of new deposits with interest rates generally four to six points
higher than other banks and heavy marketing. Mr. Baez opened branches across the country and hired
Dominican baseball superstar Sammy Sosa to tout it. In television spots, Mr. Sosa hit home runs while an
announcer gushed the bank's slogan, "Solido, Como Baninter," or "Solid, like Baninter."
Mr. Baez sponsored a famous carnival in La Vega, the fiestas of provincial towns, and local basketball and
baseball teams. The bank came to spend about $100,000 a day on advertising, investigators concluded,
making it the largest advertiser in the country. "He had turned into the great King Midas of the nation," says
former President Fernandez. "Everybody went to see him if they needed money for an event."
Mr. Baez came to be known by only one name, Ramoncito -- Little Ramon. A car was seen around the capital
with the sign: "Buy me now, because if you don't, Ramoncito will."
Mr. Baez acquired homes in Vail, Colo., Miami and the Casa de Campo resort on the southern coast of the
Dominican Republic. He commissioned a three-story, 147-foot Benetti yacht, christened "Patricia" after his
new wife. The yacht, delivered last year, was one of four luxury vessels he owned, in addition to a small
squadron of executive jets and helicopters.
As Mr. Baez's business profile rose, his influence on the government became more palpable. Current
President Hipolito Mejia, often seen in television news reports coming from or going to Mr. Baez's house for
breakfast meetings, took the banker to Washington to help lobby a trade pact in 2000. That same year, Mr.
Baez gave a speech unveiling a "10-year plan" for the nation's development. Among other things, it called for
creating a national auditor's office to avoid "the bottomless barrel of administrative corruption." Many
wondered if he was planning a presidential run.
Mr. Baez had always given generously -- and publicly -- to church charities and populist programs dear to
politicians. He paid for the toys -- bicycles for boys, dolls for girls -- that the late President Balaguer handed
out by the thousands at the door of his house every Three Kings Day.
According to his account to central bank investigators, Mr. Baez also supplied armored SUV's for President
Mejia and former President Fernandez. He claimed to have paid monthly stipends to 70 out of the about 100
Dominican army generals. He gave 10 gold Rolex watches to a colonel with close ties to President Mejia.
The central bank report says Mr. Baez also claims to have provided stipends for an unknown number of
Supreme Court justices. The Supreme Court recently published newspaper advertisements denying any such
payments.
A spokesman for President Mejia, Luis Gonzalez Fabra, says that the president acted aggressively and
properly once the fraud was discovered. Mr. Gonzalez Fabra acknowledges that Mr. Baez lent the president
his jets as many as 20 times for overseas trips and that Mr. Baez gave two sport-utility vehicles to the
presidency. But President Mejia didn't personally use the vehicles, says Mr. Gonzalez Fabra, adding: "I don't
know where they are now."
Former President Fernandez, now running for president again, confirms that after he left office Mr. Baez gave
him $100,000 to help start a think tank and also supplied him with a bullet-proof SUV he still drives. Mr.
Fernandez said many Dominican businesses have given money to his think tank, and that there was nothing

--- Page 32 ---
Page 32 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
improper about the gifts from Mr. Baez. The former president says it's not his place to say whether fraud was
committed at Baninter.
The first clear signs something was amiss at Baninter emerged in September when the bank charged Col.
Pedro "Pepe" Goico, a powerful army officer on the staff of President Mejia, and two other military men, of
running up $2 million of fraudulent expenses on a credit card provided by the bank to the president's office.
Col. Goico was arrested, but Baninter soon dropped the charges. Col. Goico was freed and received another
important command.
Reached at his new post, Col. Goico said he wasn't authorized to comment. Armed forces spokesman Col.
Puro de la Cruz says the military isn't investigating Col. Goico or the generals alleged in the central bank
report to be on Mr. Baez's payroll because no official complaint has been lodged by the president, "who is the
commander-in-chief of the armed forces." The president's spokesman said it's up to the judge investigating
Baninter to decide whether to question the generals.
The Goico scandal -- and swirling rumors that the amount of money involved might be much larger --
triggered a run on the bank. Soon the central bank, alarmed at Baninter's liquidity troubles, had pumped in
$300 million to prop up Baninter, while looking for a buyer to take over the ailing bank.
In March, Baninter and Banco del Progreso, another big Dominican bank, announced plans to merge. Then
the deal suddenly collapsed.
"When we saw what was there, we decided to get out," says a Banco del Progreso official. The central bank
took control of Baninter, and investigators rushed in. They were shocked to discover a huge portfolio of
losses and bad loans nowhere reflected in the official books, according to the central bank.
Baninter had been keeping double books since 1989, said central bank Governor Jose Lois Malkun in a
televised speech in May. Pedro O. Gamundi, a partner in the Dominican branch of Steel Hector &Davis LLP,
an international law firm hired to help locate Baninter assets, said the allegedly fraudulent bookkeeping hid a
"vampire bank" inside Baninter -- a complex system of secret accounts and corporate entities set up to spirit
away assets.
During hours when the bank was closed, a clandestine computer program streamed up to $100,000 a day out
of legitimate accounts and into the hidden coffers. Investigators say the bank's operations resembled a
pyramid scheme, in which swelling new deposits -- generated by the high profile marketing -- were used to
pay interest and support Mr. Baez's other businesses, even as the underlying accounts were being looted.
Some $70 million of this money flowed into a petty cash fund for a fictitious company called Gaperan SA. The
name was an acronym for "Gastos Personales de Ramon," which translates to "Ramon's Personal
Expenses," according to Manuel Rubio, the coordinator of the Central Bank's legal team.
Central Bank investigators say Mr. Baez also used an arm of the bank in the Cayman Islands to attract
deposits from wealthy Dominicans. The unit supposedly held $152 million, but the funds actually had been
shifted into the secret accounts at Baninter, investigators say. In a TV interview last week, President Mejia
said no one had yet claimed about two-thirds of that money, raising questions about the provenance and
legality of the funds. He added that U.S. and international police agencies have taken interest in that part of
the case.
The lion's share of Baninter's losses allegedly took place during the last three years and swelled last fall after
the Central Bank intervened. Investigators say most of the $300 million in pesos the Central Bank poured in
before the scandal became public was quickly converted to dollars by Mr. Baez and friends, and shifted
outside the country.
Another close associate of Mr. Baez, Luis Alvarez Renta, was fired from his post as ambassador to France in
May and later arrested for allegedly helping to launder embezzled funds from the bank. Central Bank
investigators say that at least $176 million was transferred abroad from Baninter through a company run by
Mr. Alvarez Renta early this year. Mr. Alvarez Renta declined to comment, but in a recent newspaper
advertisement denied any wrongoing and said he would "defend his honor." The ad said Mr. Alvarez Renta
never held a position in Baninter that could "directly or indirectly" link him to the charges, and that in 2001 he
sold his interest in the company allegedly involved in the transfers.
The swift flow of funds out of the Dominican Republic accelerated the devaluation of the peso, said Mr. Lois
Malkun in a televised speech in May. Between January and March of this year, Mr. Baez simply erased from
the bank's books about $680 million in loans taken out by his companies and friends, Mr. Lois Malkun said in
the speech.
Since then, in addition to the bank, the government has taken control of Baninter's insurance company and
media properties, some of which have already closed. The Central Bank has had to continue subsidizing

--- Page 33 ---
Page 33 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
many of the money-losing media companies as it awaits a judicial decision that would allow their sale. Two of
the newspapers and a handful of small radio stations have already closed.
The regulators and auditors charged with overseeing Baninter during the period of the most egregious
irregularities have denied responsibility. The Superintendent of Banks and the Central Bank president have
been promoted since the revelations. Both are now in President Mejia's cabinet. Mr. Gonzalez Fabra, the
presidential spokesman, says the two officials couldn't have detected a fraud so complex. Auditing firm
PricewaterhouseCoopers says it didn't know about the double books.
Whatever failings there were, Julio Cross, the country's newly-appointed top bank regulator says that Mr.
Baez will pay for the alleged embezzlement. "He won't have a jet, helicopter or yacht left," says Mr. Cross.
"We'll do whatever we have to do, but justice will be done."
Many Dominicans don't believe that. "All of society's leaders are implicated," says Marino Zapete, one of the
the first journalists to write about the scandal. "Reason tells one that obtaining justice will be very difficult."
Indeed, Mr. Zapete was detained for eight hours on June 11 and interrogated by the head of the Dominican
Republic's intelligence agency about stories on Baninter and on two mansions that President Mejia currently
is building. During a televised appearance two days later, President Mejia lashed out at Mr. Zapete by name.
"I won't accept that my honesty be disrespected," President Mejia said.
Meanwhile, Mr. Baez sits in an air-conditioned holding cell in the shabby Justice Building in downtown Santo
Domingo, awaiting trial and chatting with a steady trickle of rich and powerful visitors. Friends of the banker
say Mr. Baez recently assured them in a phone call that he expects to be back deep-sea fishing soon.

Thinking Things Over: World Money at the Palazzo Mundell
Thinking Things Over: World Money at the Palazzo Mundell
By Robert L. Bartley
989 words
30 June 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A17
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SANTA COLOMBA, Italy -- At this rural crossroads a short cab ride outside Siena's medieval gates, the
question of the day is: "Does the Global Economy Need a Global Currency?"
"Yes," declares former Federal Reserve Chairman Paul Volcker, adding that the 15 or so onlookers here are
maybe half the people in the world willing to entertain the question seriously. They include former Israeli
central bank head Jacob Frenkel, former Argentine Finance Minister Domingo Cavallo and currency-board
champion Steve Hanke. They've been gathered by Nobel Prize economist Robert Mundell for his 10th Santa
Colomba Conference.
The first conference was held in 1971, three weeks after President Nixon's Aug. 15 announcement severing
the link between the dollar and gold and breaking up the Bretton Woods international monetary system. This
was also two years after young Professor Mundell spent $20,000 to buy the five-story limestone castle once
owned by Pandolfo "Il Magnifico" Petrucci, who ruled Siena from 1487 until his death in 1512. Filled with
endless shelves of books, it's now Palazzo Mundell, home and personal conference center for a man who's
influenced the world not by Petrucci's notorious treachery and violence, but by the force of ideas. Mundell
ideas motivated both the Reagan administration economic policies in the U.S. and the advent of the euro on
this side of the Atlantic.
And if the euro can replace the franc, mark and lira, why can't a new world currency merge the dollar, euro
and yen? The euro's recent recovery against the dollar almost certainly establishes its credibility as a
permanent currency. While major eurozone economies remain troubled, practically no one so far is blaming
the European Central Bank.
This suggests success for the grandest reform of all, a supra-national central bank. The ECB Executive
Board and Governing Council could yet become political targets, of course, especially if much-discussed
deflation actually sets in. But even with strikes in Germany and France, few politicians seek a way out in a
little more inflation or currency depreciation; few complain about the loss of "monetary sovereignty."
World money, with a world central bank, seems a next logical step. The small band of dreamers decamped
to the hills of Tuscany has always been skeptical of prevailing wisdom that the answer to all exchange-rate
problems is "float." The euro was introduced at $1.18 to the dollar on Jan. 1, 1999, floated to below 83 cents
in October 2000, and floated back to $1.18 this spring. In four years, that is, down 31% and back up 44%,
with no obvious economic fundamentals to explain the float, er, gyrations.
In theory such monetary gyrations will disturb the real economy, and for whatever reason the years
1999-2003 have indeed been peculiar ones, with a tech-stock "bubble," a stock-market crash and a grudging
world recovery. Over this time, too, U.S. monetary policy has tended toward the hyperactive, with the Fed
tightening in 1999, easing dramatically in 2001 and just now cutting its open-market interest rate to an all-time
low.
It's not easy to tease out the skein of causation connecting these events, but we do know from history that
changes in the international monetary order have powerful effects, economic and often political. The Bretton
Woods system of fixed exchange rates provided an international monetary stability that helped rebuild the
world after World War II, for example. Its breakdown led to a decade of world inflation, an "energy crisis" and
war in the Middle East.
With the advent of the euro, the world has evolved a system of currency blocs. The dollar and euro zones are
perhaps each large enough not to be overwhelmed by currency changes -- or at least, say, to delay the
inflationary impact of the recent decline in the dollar. But around the world small and open economies --
Argentina, for example -- suffer profound economic and political shocks when the two currencies in which
they trade gyrate by 30% or 40%.

--- Page 35 ---
Page 35 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
For such nations in particular, the Mundell view stresses that floating exchange rates, recently a standard
prescription of the International Monetary Fund, are not a policy but the lack of a policy. Under fixed rates, the
central bank of a small nation devotes monetary policy to targeting the exchange rate with a larger
neighbor; this is a policy. But a "float" simply takes away the anchor; for a policy you need another target --
historically the price of gold, more recently some measure of the domestic inflation rate.
Under the second Bush administration, the United States has set out to establish itself as a positive force for
order in the world, but it has been uninterested in the non-military side of world power, in particular the
monetary leadership exercised so successfully by the U.S. during Bretton Woods or Great Britain during the
19th century. A world money would be an extraordinary boon to international stability.
When the U.S. gets ready to seize world leadership, Bob Mundell has a plan, based on the euro and looking
toward the year 2040. To wit, all currencies convertible into an international money, the dey (dollar, euro, yen)
or perhaps the intor. The supply of this currency under supervision of an international board; monetary gains
from its issue split along IMF quotas.
As the Palazzo Mundell finale, he gave a banquet suitable for "Il Magnifico," with a suckling pig turning on an
open spit and diners seated at a table guarded by twin suits of armor. He'll take his latest Santa Colomba
conclusions to the IMF meeting in Dubai in September. Could it happen that his ideas will reach beyond a few
dreamers in Tuscany to the real world? Well, with supply-side tax cuts and the euro, I've seen it happen twice
before.

Just Say No to Rate Cuts
Just Say No to Rate Cuts
By Brian S. Wesbury
1,291 words
23 June 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Federal Reserve is poised to cut interest rates even further this week. But, instead of asking by how
much, the markets should be asking, why?
There has been no ground swell of complaints about high interest rates or tight money. In fact, corporate
bond issuance is booming, and mortgage-refinancing applications exceed the capacity available to handle
them. Total commercial bank assets have climbed by 11% in the past year and bank profits have soared -- a
clear sign that the financial system is not starved of liquidity. Private non-farm payrolls have grown for two
consecutive months, purchasing managers' indices are rising, deal-flow is improving again on Wall Street,
corporate bond spreads have narrowed sharply, and the stock market is up.
Nonetheless, Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan says that any rate cut would be cheap "insurance" against what
he seems to believe is a 1930s or Japanese-style deflationary recession. After all, the economy has grown
just 1.7% annually over the past three years, well under its potential growth rate. Fed economic models take
this as a certain sign of inadequate liquidity, no matter what else is happening. But it is non-monetary factors
that have held the economy back and easier money won't help.
In the past two years, one development after another has added uncertainty to the process of
decision-making. Terrorism, war, strange weather patterns, higher oil prices, and debate over a tax cut have
all boosted the risk of investing in the first half of 2003, reducing the velocity of money. Another rate cut might
well have as many negative effects as positive.
In 1911, before the Federal Reserve existed, Irving Fisher developed the seminal formula describing the
relationship between money and the economy. He published it in his book "The Purchasing Power of Money."
The formula has been a staple of money and banking textbooks for decades. It's simple: It says that "money x
velocity = price x quantity." Money is defined as the money supply, while velocity is the number of times that
money circulates. The right side of the equation defines nominal GDP -- the price of all goods (P), times the
quantity of all goods (Q). Typically, economists use the equation to solve for V, or velocity.
For example, in 2002, the M1 money supply, which includes currency and checking deposits, stood at $1.191
trillion, while nominal GDP or total spending equaled $10.446 trillion. Dividing GDP by M1, we find that
velocity was 8.771 -- every dollar in the M1 money supply was spent almost nine times.
In the 1960s and '70s, Beryl Sprinkel, a Milton Friedman protege and eventual Reagan cabinet member,
became a successful forecaster using this formula. But then, in the early '80s, following the deregulation of
banking, measures of the money supply changed. As a result, and by definition, velocity became unstable.
Most forecasters have lost faith in the money supply as a forecasting tool. Even Mr. Friedman recently said
that, "The use of quantity of money as a target has not been a success." Still, there is no reason to throw the
baby out with the bath water. The formula still has much to teach us -- especially in recent years. One thing
the formula says is that money-supply growth alone may not always be enough to boost GDP. For example,
in the past year, M2, which includes M1, plus savings deposits, CDs and money funds, has grown at a 6.9%
annual rate. But nominal GDP has grown just 3.8%. This means that velocity has declined by 3.1%. When
velocity falls, the impact of easier money is eroded.
In theory, a "liquidity trap" can develop in which the Fed becomes impotent. Usually this is attributed to
deflation. Consumers won't borrow or spend, because they know prices will be lower tomorrow. Alternatively,
some argue that after a bubble in equity prices, entrepreneurs have either gone bust and cannot act, or
become risk averse and will not act. According to the theory, a quagmire like that of the Great Depression or
Japan results and monetary policy becomes useless. So what the Fed must do is pump money into the
system before a "liquidity trap" can develop. Unfortunately, this argument is myopic and misses the point.
Monetary policy is not the only game in town. Fiscal policy also has an important impact.

--- Page 39 ---
Page 39 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Great Depression was preceded immediately by Smoot-Hawley, and Herbert Hoover more than doubled
marginal income tax rates in 1932. World trade imploded, and so did entrepreneurial risk-taking. Japan is the
same sad story. In 1990, the capital-gains tax rate was increased from 0% to 20%. A national sales tax was
implemented in 1989 and was increased in the mid-'90s. Japan also subsidizes failure, by supporting
bankrupt banks and companies.
Today's experience in the U.S. is in no way similar. President Bush just signed the most pro-growth tax cut
since 1981. Tariff increases last year were a huge mercantilist mistake, but now free trade is on the agenda
again. In addition, while war and terror boosted uncertainty, those ambiguities have lessened considerably.
Nonetheless, there are some negative developments. High oil and natural-gas prices and a continued desire
on the part of Congress to spend could hurt the economy. In particular, the new drug benefit in Medicare is
worrisome. But by and large, the major uncertainties of the past two years have faded, the tax cut has
realigned incentives and easy money is coursing through the system. The economy is poised for barn-burner
growth.
Regarding deflation, never in history has an economy experienced deflation when gold and commodity prices
are rising and currencies are weakening. With gold near $360 per ounce and the dollar weak, the risks of
deflation in the U.S. are nonexistent. Moreover, consumer spending has been the strongest part of the
economy -- a clear sign that consumers have not been immobilized by deflation. The market capitalization of
U.S. stocks is up by $2 trillion since last October and Wall Street deal-making has blossomed. At least a
dozen big deals have been announced in just the past few weeks. Cutting rates at this point would be overkill.
Don't forget, there are two sides to the interest-rate coin.
If the Fed cuts rates to 1.0% or 0.75%, the fees on many money-market funds would exceed the yield --
creating negative returns. Retirees, on fixed incomes, are already experiencing reduced cash flow -- another
rate cut will hurt even more. Insurance companies face lower returns on bond investments, which could drive
up the cost of premiums. Corporations will be forced to increase contributions to pension funds because of
low interest rates. Mortgages are prepaying at an alarming rate, which is great for consumers, but bad for
lenders who face contracting durations. To top it off, banks may become more reluctant to make loans,
because at low rates, recovering from a bad loan takes longer.
Finally, like 1993-94, further rate cuts will eventually lead to more drastic rate hikes, and all the damage that
they do, at some point in the near future. The economy would be better off if the Fed signaled that the rate
cutting is over. Expectations of Fed action give decision-makers another reason to hold off. It is not a lack of
money that is the problem, but a reduced desire for risk-taking. The Bush tax cuts and the end of the war
have taken care of that problem. It is not money we should worry about, but velocity.
---
Mr. Wesbury is chief economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens &Thompson in Chicago.

Going Global: With Markets Up, It's Decision Time
Going Global: With Markets Up, It's Decision Time
By Michael R. Sesit
852 words
13 June 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
PARIS -- It is time for investors to choose whether they are optimists or pessimists after the global stock
market rally of the past three months.
Since mid-March, the Dow Jones Industrial Average and the Morgan Stanley Capital International World
index have each risen 22%. That has shifted market sentiment from predictions of doom during the first
quarter to a greater focus on less-scary barometers like corporate earnings in the second.
"In the first quarter, investors were extremely risk averse -- frightened by geopolitical risk, the breakdown of
civilization as we know it" and surging oil prices, says Andrew Milligan, head of global strategy at Standard
Life Investments in Edinburgh, Scotland. Scared investors sought refuge in gold, the Swiss franc and
government bonds. During the second quarter, their focus changed: "Rather than worrying about geopolitics,
it was back to corporate earnings," Mr. Milligan says.
So how do investors feel now, and how much risk are they willing to handle?
The key considerations in making that decision are going to be judgment calls on global growth,
corporate-earnings buoyancy and how various risks could play out. About all that's certain is the next three
months won't be like the last.
"It would be extremely surprising if this straight line from mid-March continued through the summer," Mr.
Milligan says. "You should expect some profit taking, which may already have begun."
Jamie Lewin, a market economist at Gartmore Investment Management in London, warns that the risks may
have changed -- but they still are significant. Three months ago, the major fear was a collapse of global
growth. Now it's "something worse than the expected mediocre-growth environment," he says.
Companies also will need to show evidence that the profit picture is better than expected for the rally to
continue.
That might be hard. The unanticipated rise in U.S. first-quarter profits owed more to cost-cutting than a
pick-up in revenue growth. It's also a given that Europe and Japan will be drags on global growth. Thus, Mr.
Lewin warns: "If the U.S. and Asia stopped growing -- which markets don't expect -- it would be an
unexpected negative shock, and markets could fall back 20% to 25% to the lows of mid-March."
In such an environment, he recommends defensive sectors, such as pharmaceuticals, beverage companies,
food producers, utilities, tobacco companies and health-care concerns -- companies with revenues that don't
necessarily jump up and down with economic growth. It also means sticking with government bonds, even if
10-year Treasurys yield just 3.21%, euro-zone equivalents 3.56% to 3.74% and comparable United Kingdom
gilts just under 4%.
"If things go wrong, interest rates will either fall where there's room for them to decline or just won't go up for
a very long time," says George Magnus, chief economist at UBS Warburg in London.
Now a moment for the optimists. Gartmore's "top down" forecast for the global economy is consistent with a
single-digit earnings growth outlook: Maybe 10% or a little bit more in Asia and parts of the U.S., and Europe
at close to zero, especially when the resurgent euro is factored in. The firm's "base case" for global growth is
below trend through next year. Still, that's better than anemic, much better than recession and a lot better
than deflation.
Portfolio managers at Standard Life also are betting reflation wins out over deflation, and their portfolios have
a pro-growth tilt. For instance, four U.S. holdings include cable company Comcast, Corp. Morgan Stanley,
Bank of America Corp. and diesel-engine maker Cummins Inc. Standard Life also is buying

--- Page 41 ---
Page 41 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
sterling-denominated bonds yielding 5.85% to 6.19% issued by insurers -- such as Britain's Prudential PLC
and Aviva PLC and Germany's Munich Re AG -- which it sees as a play on improving stock markets.
But big risks remain for the optimists.
One, geopolitical uncertainty could re-emerge in the form of Middle East violence, North Korean threats or a
major terrorist attack. For investors, it would be back to gold, Swiss francs and government bonds. Two,
earnings, especially in Europe, could disappoint.
Three, the U.S. economy could respond to fiscal and monetary stimuli and a declining dollar, "while Europe
and Japan offer only feeble responses," UBS Warburg's Mr. Magnus says. Result: The growth gap between
Uncle Sam and Europe and Japan widens, the U.S. current-account deficit balloons and the dollar goes into
free fall.
"So instead of all countries growing together, you have global convergence downward rather than upwards,"
Mr. Magnus says.
Seeking protection? Consider bonds, defensive stocks and investments denominated in euros and even yen.
Still, Gartmore's Mr. Lewin awards equal odds to an optimistic scenario as to a more pessimistic one:
Corporate spending zooms; profits jump; employment rises; and consumption climbs. In that case, look at
auto makers, chemicals and engineering companies, information technology concerns and media companies
plus small-capitalization stocks, he says.

Greenspan Doesn't See Gas Prices Falling Soon
Greenspan Doesn't See Gas Prices Falling Soon
By Russell Gold and Greg Ip
840 words
11 June 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
This year's jump in natural-gas prices looks likely to be protracted, and yesterday, Federal Reserve Chairman
Alan Greenspan raised the possibility that those prices could become an increasing drag on the U.S.
economy.
Natural gas is used to generate electricity, heat homes and manufacture a variety of chemicals, plastics and
fertilizers that in turn are used to make thousands of other products. Mr. Greenspan, appearing before the
House energy and commerce committee, said higher natural-gas prices have knocked "a couple tenths [of a
percentage point] off profit margins" for U.S. companies, which are a key determinant of business investment,
currently the economy's weakest spot. At the same time, the costlier fuel is likely to mean higher bills for
consumers, taking spending money out of their pockets.
Mr. Greenspan said there is little prospect for natural-gas prices to fall much in coming months. "If we stay at
these very elevated prices, we are going to see some erosion in a number of macroeconomic variables."
The Fed is already worried about the sluggish pace of economic growth and the risk that low inflation,
excluding energy prices, could turn into deflation. It may trim short-term interest rates later this month as
insurance against further weakness and deflation.
The price of natural gas for July delivery rose 1.6 cents to $6.33 per million British thermal units on the New
York Mercantile Exchange, more than twice the typical summer price.
Mr. Greenspan also warned that high gas prices could do permanent damage to industries whose operations
are built around natural-gas prices of $2 to $3 per million BTU. Industries that depend on natural gas as a
feedstock, such as petrochemicals and plastics, are feeling the impact.
"Our guys aren't going to fail -- they are going to leave. They will pack up and go where gas prices are
competitive," says Greg Lebedev, president and chief executive of the American Chemistry Council.
For consumers, higher natural-gas prices will drive up electricity bills during the next few months, especially if
it is a warmer-than-normal summer.
"We can anticipate upward pressure on prices of electricity this summer in markets where gas is a
predominant fuel" in power plants, says Charles Gray, executive director of the National Association of
Regulatory Utility Commissioners. And when summer is over, cold weather isn't likely to bring much relief
because the prices will also raise winter heating bills.
After a particularly cold winter in the Northeast, inventories of natural gas in storage are unusually low and the
U.S. natural-gas industry has been slow to ramp up production.
In mid-April, utilities reported their lowest inventories since 1993, when the federal Energy Information
Administration began to keep records. Cooler weather during the past couple of weeks has allowed an
aggressive rebuilding of storage levels, but stocks are still 38.7% below a year ago.
In the past, natural-gas-fired utilities have managed seasonal price fluctuations by purchasing gas in the
summer, when demand and prices are low, and storing it for use in the winter, when prices are typically
higher.
But this pricing scenario started to crumble in the late 1990s, when hundreds of power plants were built.
During the summer, when consumers turn on their air conditioners, power plants now use natural gas to meet
electricity demand.
"What used to be a winter peaking fuel is now a winter and summer peaking fuel," says Rob Black, president
of Waterville Gas &Oil Co., which serves 3,800 homes in suburban Toledo, Ohio. "This year is going to be the

--- Page 43 ---
Page 43 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
most expensive storage gas we've encountered so far," he says. He is paying $6 per million BTUs, which
could increase his customers' heating bills next winter by 50% to 60%.
KeySpan Corp., the largest gas-distribution company in the Northeast, says customers on Long Island, who
spent an average of $1,200 last winter, should expect to see at least a 7% increase in their bills. "That is
really a best-case scenario," spokesman David J. Manning says.
Mr. Greenspan, in previous testimony, has cited high energy prices as a risk for the economy. Yesterday, he
said he saw little prospect for natural-gas prices to fall back, noting derivatives markets see a 25% probability
of gas topping $7.50 per million BTUs later this year.
The Fed chief, whose views are often sought on policy questions unrelated to banking and monetary policy,
pushed strongly to expand the U.S. infrastructure for importing liquefied natural gas, or LNG. LNG provides
only 1% of current gas supply in the U.S., although analysts expect that to grow to 4% by 2005. Increased
imports would give the U.S. access to world reserves of gas much as it already has for oil, limiting the spikes
in natural-gas prices that now result from sudden increases in demand or decreases in supply, Mr.
Greenspan said.

Out With the New
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Out With the New
By Sarah Robertson
2,003 words
6 June 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
W1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Love the new kitchen -- now let's change it. These days, the $170 billion remodeling industry is pushing
`serial renovators' to redo rooms all over the house. Sarah Robertson reports.]
MARIA SOWINSKI couldn't have been happier with the look of her brand-new home in Connecticut. Five
months later, she wasn't so sure. Since then, she has had renovators in five times to build cabinetry in the
guest room, add arched doorways, and hang new light fixtures. Now she's adding French doors to the back.
"If you have the cash, nothing is all that permanent," says the fine-arts teacher.
These days, it's not just sofas getting replaced before they're worn out, it's entire rooms. Thanks to low
interest rates and an industry that keeps coming up with new looks for once-permanent fixtures, more
homeowners are ripping out kitchens, redesigning bathrooms -- and making "re-renovating" the latest trend in
the $170 billion home-improvement business. According to the National Association of Home Builders,
kitchens, bathrooms and even family rooms are getting overhauled a third faster than a decade ago. Home
Depot says serial renovators now account for a surprising 40% of home-improvement spending. "They're a
gold mine -- they're never satisfied," says Jason Feldman, the company's director of design.
It's all a stepped-up version of '90s disposable decor, when retailers like Pottery Barn pushed homeowners to
think of furniture like fashion -- but now it's for stuff like windows, cabinets and doors. Window company
Andersen just launched "special collection" windows (with antique-looking mullions they go for as much as
$2,000), while the number of paint colors at Sherwin Williams has hit 1,400. Waterworks needs more than
200 pages to show off its new lines of bath and kitchen fixtures (plus a model in an evening dress). Then
there's Corian, which is encouraging homeowners not to wait to remodel the whole kitchen to install new
counters. "We want people to think of it like carpeting," says spokeswoman Susan Homan.
With Rebecca Ellsley's clients, at least, it seems to be working. The Connecticut kitchen designer says a third
of re-renovations -- triple the number five years ago. In many cases, she's taking out stuff that's practically
new. Out: side-by-side Sub-Zero fridges, Viking pro stoves, granite less than a year old. In: new "integrated"
Sub-Zeros (the ones that look like armoires), La Cornue dual-fuel stoves (better for baking, say fans), marble
counters with "more feeling" to the stone. In Minneapolis, designer Mary Jane Pappas says redoing someone
else's remodel used to be almost unheard of; now it's 10% of her business. Her latest big job? Tearing out a
brand-new kitchen that already has high-end Euro-style cabinets and those trendy warming drawers. "You'd
be totally shocked they're ripping it out," she says.
Or maybe not. Over the past five years, Elizabeth Tauber and her husband have spent so much time
renovating their Beverly Hills, Calif., home that they've only had two months without workmen in the house.
Much to her husband's initial dismay, she says, the contractors have replaced the marble in the front hall with
French limestone, gutted the master bath, and remade the kitchen with a mural over the stove, a giant
cooktop and fireplace and a whole new lighting system. That one took four months, and her husband thought
the kitchen was fine as it was -- though he went along with her plans. "It was not his room," she says.
It wasn't so long ago that people thought of their home interiors as relatively permanent. Indeed, up until the
last 15 years, remodeling "used to be driven by need," says Paul Winans, a California contractor and vice
president of the National Association of the Remodeling Industry. "Incidentally you would add aesthetic
improvements," he says. Most big-ticket items, from garage doors to bathtubs, stayed in place for 15 years or
more. And because American life was more formal then, homeowners often concentrated their fix-up
spending on living and dining rooms, investing in things like furniture, drapes and carpeting. Rather than
re-renovate if a house became too small, people tended to trade up, says Kermit Baker of the Remodeling
Futures Program at Harvard's Joint Center for Housing Studies.
But in the last couple of years, with home prices rising as much as 7% annually, that became tougher to do in
some big markets, where even starter homes can run for more than $350,000. And with home-design

--- Page 45 ---
Page 45 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
magazines, catalogs and TV shows pushing ever-more idealized images, plus an increased emphasis on
pro-style kitchens and master bath suites straight out of spas, remodeling boomed throughout much of the
decade. Giving things a boost now: the record rate of mortgage refinancing (there are expected to be 11.5
million this year), with many people taking extra money out to fix up their homes -- or to refix them up, that is.
For some people, things just snowball. When Sandy Wischmeyer started out renovating, six years ago, she
was only planning on creating a new master bath in the upstairs of her Littleton, Colo., home. But once the
new whirlpool and limestone floors were in place, the rest of the upstairs looked so shabby that the
homemaker quickly found herself replacing not only the doors and the carpet, but the banister on the stairs.
Then she moved downstairs and ripped out the kitchen and the family room -- which had already been
redone once. "It sort of seeps out," she says.
Makers, of course, keep upping the ante with new "must-haves" that can suddenly make even a newly
renovated home feel out of date, from pot-fillers in the kitchen to a "master suite" instead of a bedroom.
British maker Aga is counting on making its $12,000 stove (it's got four ovens as well as separate simmering
and boiling plates) the new gold standard for American homes, while Miele's pushing a built-in coffee maker
that goes for $1,900 and comes with something called a dregs door where the grounds get dumped
automatically. And those high-price soaking tubs that were all the rage a year ago? Fixtures makers like
Kohler are now pushing "WaterHaven" showers with multiple showerheads and aromatherapy steam.
Indeed, all the choices can get dizzying, even for the people selling them. Nuway Kitchen Showrooms, in
White Plains, N.Y., used to change its kitchen vignettes (those mock-ups with cabinets and appliances) every
three years or so. Now the turnover time is as short as 18 months. After a few years in fashion, distressed
old-world cabinets have already fallen off the hot list at Millbrook cabinets, a custom-maker. The new look: an
African hardwood called anigre and flat-fronts. Makers and retailers say they're just trying to keep up with the
clients who see more of this stuff as they surf the Web and travel more. "The consumer has become more
demanding," says Brian Gordon, president of Kitchen Expo, a New Jersey chain where the selection has
more than doubled in the past three years.
But going for the next hot look can have its drawbacks. Three years ago, Susan Moore spent $1,200 on
Fireslate countertops that were supposed to "add drama" to her kitchen. They looked great -- until she
actually started using them and found out that things like lemon juice eat away at the surface making it hard
to keep up. (The maker says they should be treated with a special oil.) Now she's planning on ripping them
out and putting in pricier granite. "We live in a throwaway society," says the Minneapolis color consultant
Another issue: A lot of these projects don't increase the value of a home anywhere near as much as they cost
-- especially when they involve the kinds of uberfixtures and appliances that can push the cost higher than
$100,000 for a kitchen. Linda Schoffman, secretary of the National Association of the Remodeling Industry,
says homeowners sometimes overimprove, creating interiors that are out of line with what the neighborhood
can bear in terms of resale. In some cases, she says, sellers can expect to lose 20% or more of the value of
a big remodel. Her advice: Paint the cabinets and call it a day.
Still, most experts say serial remodeling probably won't slow down for a while -- especially as the industry
keeps coming up with new novelties. At carpeting maker Flor of Chicago there are even wall-to-wall carpet
squares. Dubbed "room in a box," they can be pulled up and rearranged in 10 minutes. "It's as easy as
changing an accent pillow on a sofa," says Chip DeGrace, the company's vice president of marketing.

Treasure of Nimrud Is Found in Iraq, And It's Spectacular --- Fate of Assyrian Antiquities Was in Doubt Till 2 Men Pumped a Flooded Vault
Treasure of Nimrud Is Found in Iraq, And It's Spectacular --- Fate of Assyrian Antiquities Was in Doubt
Till 2 Men Pumped a Flooded Vault
By David Luhnow
1,375 words
6 June 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BAGHDAD, Iraq -- The treasure of Nimrud survived 2,800 years buried near a dusty town in northern Iraq. It
then spent 12 years tucked away in a vault. Until yesterday, it was uncertain whether it had survived Saddam
Hussein's son, a U.S. missile strike, looters, a flood and a grenade attack. But it has been found intact in the
dark, damp basement of a bombed out central bank building.
Yesterday, directors of Iraq's National Museum and a team of U.S. Customs agents and officials from the
Office of the Coalition Provisional Authority -- the Pentagon-run agency managing postwar Iraq -- cracked
open five waterlogged wooden crates, peered inside and breathed a collective sigh of relief.
There, in dozens of smaller boxes was the entire collection -- 613 pieces of gold jewelry, precious stones and
ornaments from the height of the Assyrian civilization in 800 B.C. Together, the pieces weigh well over 100
pounds.
The recovery of the artifacts, which hasn't been made public, is a great boost for the museum, which gained
the world's attention in the days after the war when U.S. forces failed to prevent looters from hauling away
thousands of artifacts from ancient civilizations that sprang up in the Tigris-Euphrates valley. Experts said it
was the worst ransacking of Iraq since Genghis Khan tore into Baghdad in the 13th century.
While initial reports talked of some 170,000 pieces stolen, it is now clear that perhaps only a few thousand
artifacts were taken, experts say. Many priceless objects from the museum are still missing, such as the
sacred Vase of Warka, a Sumerian piece from about 3000 B.C. But museum officials moved hundreds of the
most valuable items into storage rooms and secret locations only weeks before the war, including some
40,000 ancient books, Islamic manuscripts and scrolls spirited away in a bomb shelter. More than a thousand
other pieces have been recovered by U.S. officials.
Unearthed in 1988 by Iraqi archaeologists and never seen outside Iraq, the Nimrud treasure had been on
public display at Baghdad's National Museum for just a few months before Saddam Hussein invaded Kuwait
in 1990. Days after the invasion, the treasure was yanked from public view. Its whereabouts remained secret.
One man who long wondered about the treasure was Jason Williams, a British anthropologist and filmmaker,
who had tried in vain to film the Nimrud treasure in recent years. The only existing film of the pieces was
taken when Iraqi archeologists made the discovery, with grainy images of an archaeologist holding up rings
and bracelets still attached to the bones of their former owners.
"These are Iraq's crown jewels," Mr. Williams said recently as he stepped over several feet of smashed glass,
twisted metal and heaps of charred Iraqi dinars in the hull of the bank building destroyed by a U.S. missile
strike. Although the building was gutted, the missile didn't damage the basement or the vaults. But a burst
water pipe soon flooded the area.
The affable Englishman from a tiny town named Abinger Bottom found an unlikely soul mate for his search in
a New Yorker, Marine Col. Matthew Bogdanos. Mr. Bogdanos is a reservist on active duty who in civilian life
works as a homicide prosecutor at the New York district attorney's office. His other job is on a U.S. military
counterterrorism team that has worked in hot spots such as Afghanistan. A short, muscular man, he also has
a master's degree in classical antiquities and a law degree from Columbia University.
When he read accounts of the Iraqi looting, Mr. Williams, who lives in Washington, called the National
Geographic Society and pitched the idea of trying to find and film the treasure. The society quickly sent him
and a film crew, as well as five archaeologists who fanned out across Iraq to survey the damage to sites
across ancient Mesopotamia.
In the case of Col. Bogdanos, he was busy tracking down remnants of the regime when he decided he would
really rather be tracking down Iraq's stolen antiquities. He helped assemble a team of three military officers

--- Page 48 ---
Page 48 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
and seven agents from the Department of Homeland Security's Bureau of Immigration and Customs
Enforcement. The men set about their gumshoe work, helping recover thousands of stolen pieces for the
museum.
Both men were captivated by the treasure, discovered by Iraqi archeologists in four burial sites in Nimrud,
near the biblical city of Nineveh. The pieces include a queen's crown with eight winged girls and a dome of
gold leaves, white marble jars, gold plates and engraved silver pitchers.
The initial worry of both men was that the treasure was part of the haul taken by looters. In the days after the
war, however, museum curators assured U.S. officials the treasure had been moved in 1990 to the central
bank and was believed safe.
Then rumors surfaced that Saddam's youngest son, Qusay, had made an illicit withdrawal from central bank
vaults shortly before the war, making off with gold ingots and $920 million in cash. "When I heard that report,
I thought `God, please tell me he wasn't that sick to steal the treasure,' " says Mr. Williams.
His fears were put to rest when a central bank worker told Mr. Williams he was present at the vault the night
he and others were ordered to stack the money for Qusay. The man said he helped fill dozens of suitcases
with cash and nine coffins with 27.5-pound bars of gold. But, he said, none of the Nimrud treasure was taken.
The U.S. Army's Third Infantry Division quickly sent a tank to guard the area after bank officials found that
looters were trying to get inside the vaults. Several robbers had killed each other in one of the buildings, and
others were shooting AK-47s at the vault doors. One man was killed when he fired his rocket-propelled
grenade at the vault while standing less than 10 feet away.
Even after it appeared that the treasure was safe, there was still a major obstacle to getting at it: The
basement of the main central bank building was flooded. Two weeks ago, both men stared at the water
down a dark stairwell and speculated about the treasure. Mr. Williams asked for permission to buy pumps to
begin draining the basement. Col. Bogdanos gave the green light, and soon two giant irrigation pumps were
hard at work. With the vault still flooded, Mr. Williams bought two smaller pumps that could be lowered down
the stairs as the water level dropped.
Even then, progress was painfully slow. Every time Mr. Williams's team turned the pumps off, the water level
rose. Finally, one of Mr. Williams's Iraqi workers hit upon a solution. With bemused U.S. soldiers looking on,
the man lifted every manhole cover in the area until he found a valve that stopped fresh water from flowing to
the building.
It still took a week to fully drain the building. Mr. Williams, now back in Washington, estimates his team of
amateur hydrologists drained 640,000 gallons of water.
There were still some hiccups along the way. When one of the first bank vaults was opened, museum officials
spotted 14 crates thought to contain the treasure only to find jewels of another kind: belonging to Iraq's first
modern king, Faisal I. "We discovered a treasure, it just wasn't the right one," says Bill Gardner, a producer
for National Geographic. Central Bank workers then told worried museum officials there were five other
crates from the museum in another vault, which was opened on Sunday.
The crates were badly damaged from the flooding and officials didn't want to haul them up the elevator shaft
in case they broke apart and damaged the treasure. So the suspense continued until the boxes could be
packed into new crates and lifted out yesterday. By then both Mr. Williams and Col. Bogdanos were stateside
again.

NEW YORK -- An unexpected rise in jobless claims and a weaker-than-expected report on factory orders
couldn't bring a halt to the current march of the small-cap stocks.
Trucking stocks skidded, however, with Roadway Corp. plunging $6.43, or 17%, to $31.16, after the
freight-hauling company warned that second-quarter earnings would be half of what it had expected. Merrill
Lynch downgraded the stock to sell from neutral.
Rival Yellow Corp. lost 1.47, or 5.5%, to 25.04, even after backing its second-quarter earnings view.
Arkansas Best shed 2.12, or 7.8%, to 25.05, as Merrill Lynch cut its stock rating to sell from neutral.
Some small-cap retailers soared on strong May sales results. Children's Place Retail Stores rose 3.12, or
19%, to 19.17. Guess climbed 95 cents, or 18%, to 6.25 and AnnTaylor Stores rose 1.24, or 4.6%, to 28.48,
both on the New York Stock Exchange.
After initial weakness on reports of an unexpected surge in jobless claims last week and a
greater-than-expected fall in U.S. factory orders in April, small stocks pushed higher as investors look ahead
to better times later in the year.
"It's the feeling about the future as opposed to the present," said Edward Riley, chief investment strategist at
State Street Global Advisors. "When investors look at the potential, they see that these smaller companies
will benefit regardless of employment trends at the moment."
The Russell 2000 Index of small stocks finished up 5.46 points, or 1.2%, to 456.69. The Standard &Poor's
SmallCap 600 Index gained 2.23 points, or 1%, to 222.15 The Nasdaq Composite Index of large- and
small-caps advanced 11.36 points, or 0.7%, to 1646.01.
Drug companies were strong, with NPS Pharmaceuticals climbing 5.21, or 22%, to 28.96, on news the Salt
Lake City company will remain independent of Enzon Pharmaceuticals, after the companies said they agreed
not to merge. Shares of Enzon fell 70 cents, or 4.7%, to 14.30.
Cytogen soared 95 cents, or nearly 19%, to 6.11, after the Princeton, N.J., biotech company said a pilot study
showed that its Quadramet drug, when combined with another drug, helps relieve pain in patients being
treated for certain cancers and attacks the cancer.
Gains in the euro, sparked by the European Central Bank's decision to cut interest rates, pushed gold
prices higher, and gold stocks followed. Meridian Gold advanced 44 cents, or 4%, to 11.48, and Stillwater
Mining added 8 cents, or 2%, to close at 4.18, both on the NYSE.
Chip companies edged higher ahead of Intel's mid-second-quarter update, with Asyst Technologies
advancing 44 cents, or 6%, to 7.93, Cirrus Logic jumping 21 cents, or 5.3%, to 4.20, and Kulicke &Soffa
climbing 76 cents, or 11%, to 7.46.
Software stocks lagged behind the market, led lower by IDX Systems, off 2.09, or 13%, to 14.41, after the
company confirmed that a major customer had canceled a contract. Some stocks bucked the trend, including
Actuate, up 27 cents, or 13%, to 2.37, a day after saying auditors completed a review of certain sales
contracts.
Tech-services stocks reversed Wednesday's gains after Smith Barney downgraded large-cap Electronic Data
Systems. Among smalls, Keynote Systems dipped 24 cents, or 2.4%, to 9.60.
Targeted Genetics, up 1.22, more than doubled to 2.41 after the Seattle firm said a midstage study of its lead
gene-therapy candidate improved cystic-fibrosis patients' lung capacity.

Are You Ready For Deflation? --- As Dow Crosses 9000, Investors Must Decide Whether They Expect Inflation or Falling Prices
Are You Ready For Deflation? --- As Dow Crosses 9000, Investors Must Decide Whether They Expect
Inflation or Falling Prices
By Jeff D. Opdyke
1,298 words
5 June 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
OK, IS IT deflation or inflation?
Investors are getting mixed signals these days on which path the economy is taking. This week, Alan
Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve Bank, again highlighted the risk that falling prices could
send the U.S. into a dangerous spiral of Japanese-style deflation. The Fed's preoccupation with deflation has
investors piling into bonds, which will increase in value if rates keep tumbling. Ten-year Treasury notes
closed yesterday at 3.29%, their lowest yield in 45 years.
At the same time, prices for many key expenditures, including health care and tuition, continue to rise. For
many consumers, these incessant price increases make inflation seem more of an immediate threat than
falling prices.
For investors, this is far more than an arcane macroeconomic debate. After the three-year bear market, many
investors are starting to wade back into the stock market again. The Dow Jones Industrial Average is up 13%
in the past two months -- it closed yesterday above 9000 for the first time since August. Investors deciding
where to deploy their funds must make a fundamental calculation on whether the economy is headed toward
falling prices or rising prices. Each scenario calls for a very different portfolio.
"This is the most important investment decision" an investor needs to make right now, says Ray Dalio,
president at Bridgewater Associates, a Westport, Conn., investment-management firm.
Deflation is usually bad news for stocks. It means companies lose their ability to raise prices, which squeezes
corporate profits. So those fearing deflation are loading up on long bonds, which would provide a steady cash
flow in a world of falling prices. They are also buying zero-coupon bonds which appreciate in value when
interest rates fall.
In contrast, the stock market generally thrives in a period of mild inflation. Such periods, like the 1990s, often
have strong economic growth and rising consumer income, both of which are good for corporate profits.
Investors betting on inflation are snapping up Treasury Inflation Protected Securities, bonds whose return
automatically rises whenever inflation does.
What follows are two competing investment strategies, one structured for deflation, the other for inflation.
Deflation
Falling prices aren't a common occurrence in the U.S. The last recorded bout of deflation was more than 50
years ago and was mild. The most infamous case occurred during the Great Depression.
In periods of deflation, cash is king. That's because the dollars you have today will buy more tomorrow as
prices fall. That favors owning U.S. government bonds with the longest maturities, since they pay the highest
rates and are locked in for the longest period of time.
In a deflationary world, zero-coupon bonds will be the rock stars. These bonds don't pay interest -- it's built
into their price at maturity, similar to a savings bond. Investors would pay about $670 today to buy a
zero-coupon bond that gives them $1,000 at maturity in 2013.
One downside: Even though you don't get interest payments from zero-coupon bonds, you're still required to
pay income taxes yearly on the internal interest your bond is earning.
To get income during deflationary times, stick to traditional bonds. The 30-year Treasurys generate the
biggest income stream and will get the biggest price pop from deflation, since when interest rates go down,
bond prices go up.

--- Page 52 ---
Page 52 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Foreign bonds should do well, too. The dollar likely would weaken in a deflationary period, so investors could
offset that risk by owning investments denominated in a foreign currency. The easiest way to buy foreign
bonds is through mutual funds such as BlackRock International Bond or American Century International
Bond.
Steer clear of corporate bonds, however, since in deflationary times, consumers put off many discretionary
purchases as they wait for even lower prices. That puts pressure on a company's sales and earnings,
increasing the likelihood of default.
Real estate would be a terrible investment, since prices would come down. That means forget the second
home or vacation property.
If there is a run of mild deflation, then investors may want to own the dividend-paying stocks of companies
that can drive sales based on volume and innovation, instead of price increases. That would include drug
companies such as Schering-Plough. Utility stocks also should be winners since their services are necessary
and they produce generally stable dividends.
In a period of more persistent deflation, however, investors should consider avoiding stocks completely. Even
dividend-paying companies will have a hard time keeping their earnings at a level that will allow them to pay
those dividends. Companies focused on commodities, such as energy stocks and gold-mining firms, also will
get hammered.
Inflation
If inflation remains mild, then a broad swath of stocks are expected to do well. The easiest way to benefit is
to own a low-cost index fund mimicking either the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index or the broader Wilshire
5000.
If inflation kicks into high gear, however, avoid stocks completely and buy hard assets, such as real estate,
gold, even collectibles like art and Persian rugs.
The safest play is Treasury Inflation Protected Securities, so-called TIPS. Their returns adjust over time to
provide a return over inflation.
TIPS "are the perfect riskless asset," says Douglas Fore, associate director of the TIAA-CREF Institute, the
research and education arm of the big provider of retirement-planning services.
Other bonds will do poorly, however. When interest rates rise, bond prices tumble. That's because today's
bonds carry low yields, and when rates rise, they will be worth less to investors.
Commodities of all sorts tend to perform strongly during inflationary periods. However, they aren't the easiest
assets to own, since they're usually structured as futures contracts that expire in time.
Real estate is often a strong performer during inflationary periods, since the price of land usually escalates.
This means owning a home or investing in a second home or vacation property might make sense. You can
play real estate more indirectly by owning a real-estate investment trust or a mutual fund such as Alpine
Realty Income &Growth.
---
Place Your Bet
Economists are debating whether deflation or
inflation is in the
cards. But investors need to make some decisions now.
-- If deflation hits, bonds of all types, particularly zero-coupon
bonds, will thrive.
-- If inflation kicks up, hard assets like real estate and gold will
be the big winners.
-- Not sure which way the economy is heading? Consider a neutral
portfolio that has a mix of bonds and stocks.
---
Which Investments Should You Be Making?
Here are the types of assets that tend to perform best during
deflationary and inflationary times. The percentages represent how
pure a play a particular asset is during that scenario. For instance,
no matter what else you own during an inflationary period, you want to
make sure you own Treasury Inflation Protected Securities.

Brazil Sees Gold in Remnants of Mines
Brazil Sees Gold in Remnants of Mines
By Gerald Jeffris
Dow Jones Newswires
728 words
4 June 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4G
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BRASILIA -- A tough economic outlook has Brazil's government scraping the bottom of the barrel, literally, in
an effort to fill the country's lean public coffers.
The country's central bank said it has decided to melt down thousands of receptacles used by migrant gold
prospectors at the deactivated Serra Pelada gold fields in northern Brazil in the 1980s to complement the
country's budget.
"When they processed the gold, this material was left impregnated in the receptacles," Central Bank
Administrative Director Joao Fleury said. "Our idea is to standardize the material that was left over and
incorporate it into the country's reserves."
The project is expected to result in about 194 kilograms (422.4 pounds) of gold, 6,727 kilograms of silver, 18
kilograms of platinum and 1,035 kilograms of palladium.
The miners' gold receptacles were purchased by the central bank after the mining operation was shut down
and have been sitting at the country's federal mint, the Casa da Moeda, ever since.
Mr. Fleury said the gold will yield about six million reals (about $2 million), which will go straight to the
country's foreign reserves. The remaining material will yield about 23 million reals, which will be directed to
the federal treasury.
The gold and precious-metal recovery effort will cost the government 1.9 million reals, according to Mr.
Fleury.
The gold-mine area was property of the then-state-owned Cia. Vale do Rio Doce, but a portion of the land
was later ceded to the migrant workers for prospecting activities.
The open pit mines, located in the Carajas region of Para state, were active in the early 1980s when
thousands of impoverished workers descended on the area. Scenes of workers climbing over each other in a
desperate rush for instant riches were captured and transmitted around the world by Brazilian documentary
photographer Sebastiao Salgado.
While the mines were open, at least 50 workers died there buried by mudslides. Hundreds of others fell ill
amid unsanitary conditions and a lack of infrastructure.
But now the government has the opportunity to turn a tragic social situation into a benefit that might give a
slight boost to social spending.
The effort to recover the gold comes as the government faces the challenge of overcoming severe
restrictions in its discretionary spending budget. The government of Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva, elected on the
promise of a bountiful distribution of social spending and income, has suffered criticism from all sides,
including the president's own Workers' Party, about its tight spending policies.
The government's austerity effort has been dubbed by some left-wing militants here as the "cursed
inheritance" from the former government of President Fernando Henrique Cardoso.
But faced with market doubts about the country's debt load, Mr. da Silva, upon taking office in January, was
obliged to continue the austerity effort.
In February, the government was forced to set aside about 14 billion reals of its 61 billion reals in
discretionary spending budget to meet a primary budget surplus target of 4.25% of gross domestic product.
The target was set in an effort to pay down the country's public-sector debt.

--- Page 55 ---
Page 55 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Meanwhile, federal spending has been hit hard, leaving cabinet ministers complaining about slim pickings.
The country's Urban Development Ministry, responsible for serving major cities that are home to more than
50 million inhabitants, got only 300 million reals this year. The Ministry of the Interior, responsible for
integrating infrastructure in more than 8.5 million square kilometers (5.3 million miles) of national territory,
was left with a budget of 188 million reals.
Those figures compare with a defense budget that is nearly 10 times larger, at three billion reals.
Meantime, other less-endowed ministries say they don't have enough resources to do their jobs.
With only 124 million reals for his programs, Culture Minister and renowned Brazilian pop singer Gilberto Gil,
for example, went to Congress earlier this month to request that his budget be increased.
"We're not making any request that is bizarre, unfitting or imprudent," Mr. Gil said. "We're asking in a realistic
manner for at least 1% of the budget for activities that according to studies generate more than one percent
of gross domestic product."
Document j000000020030604dz640002n
