Page 56 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
U.S. and Europe Signal That Rates May Be Cut Soon --- Greenspan and Duisenberg Hope to Boost Economies, Avert Threat of Deflation
U.S. and Europe Signal That Rates May Be Cut Soon --- Greenspan and Duisenberg Hope to Boost
Economies, Avert Threat of Deflation
By Greg Ip and G. Thomas Sims
1,276 words
4 June 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The chiefs of the world's largest central banks -- Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan and his
European counterpart, Wim Duisenberg -- signaled that they are contemplating interest-rate cuts soon,
moves that could help stimulate sluggish economies on both sides of the Atlantic and reduce the risk of global
deflation.
Mr. Greenspan, speaking via satellite from Washington, emphasized the importance of keeping the U.S. from
sliding into deflation while acknowledging that there is still little evidence of the economic rebound he has
been expecting. Though deflation, or generally declining prices, is unlikely, he said the Fed will "lean over
backwards" to prevent it and the issue will be "discussed in detail" when Fed policy makers meet June 24 and
25.
The Fed chief's remarks placed more emphasis on the lack of upbeat economic data and more on the need
to act pre-emptively against deflation than when he spoke to Congress two weeks ago. That suggests that
the door has opened wider to a cut in the Fed's target for its key short-term interest rate, now at a 42-year low
of 1.25%.
Deflation is dangerous because as prices fall, profit margins are squeezed, forcing firms to eventually cut
wages. Debts, whose values are fixed, become harder to repay. The central bank can't force interest rates
below inflation to stimulate demand, because rates won't go below zero.
Weak growth and the risk of deflation are now preoccupying central bankers world-wide, even the Europeans
who have been focused until recently on reducing already low inflation rates. Mr. Duisenberg, president of
European Central Bank, which sets interest rates for 12 countries that share the euro, said inflationary
pressures there have "declined significantly" and gave a bleak assessment of the European economy.
Financial markets interpreted Mr. Duisenberg's remarks as a pledge that the ECB will cut its key short-term
rate, now at 2.5%, by at least a quarter percentage point tomorrow. His comments also bolstered
expectations that the ECB may cut them by half a percentage point. The ECB has cut interest rates twice in
the past six months -- by a half percentage point in December and a quarter point in March.
The Fed's increased determination to fight deflation has added to the pressure on the ECB to cut interest
rates. That's because the Fed's announcement a month ago that it was worried about inflation falling too low
was interpreted as favoring a weaker dollar, since that would push up the prices of imports. That has helped
fuel the euro's 17% surge against the dollar since the end of last year.
"Overall, not least due to the appreciation of the euro, inflationary pressures have declined significantly over
recent months," Mr. Duisenberg said. "This assessment will be reflected in our deliberations on monetary
policy." The euro's strength is hurting European exports by making their products more costly to foreign
customers. It has, however, been welcomed by the Bush administration because it helps U.S. exporters. A
European rate cut should tend to restrain the currency's rise, but the effect could be muted if the Fed cuts
rates again.
Both men spoke on a panel at the International Monetary Conference, an annual gathering of bankers held
this year at the Hotel Adlon, located next to Berlin's historic Brandenberg Gate. The audience included chief
executives of Deutsche Bank, HSBC, JP MorganChase and Merrill Lynch. At the same event, Bank of Japan
Deputy Governor Toshiru Muto promised to "explore measures" to improve how Tokyo injects money and
credit into the economy, which has been in deflation since 1999. Also yesterday, the Bank of Canada left
interest rates unchanged, citing a bigger-than-expected drop in inflation.
The global antideflation rhetoric helped send bond prices up and yields down in Europe, Japan, Canada and
the U.S. Markets are putting the odds of a quarter-point rate cut by the Fed later this month at 80%. Some

--- Page 57 ---
Page 57 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Fed officials favor cutting rates by half a percentage point, if at all, in order to send a strong signal. The dollar
rose modestly, trading at $1.1740 to the euro.
Central bankers on both sides of the Atlantic have expected economic growth, which ground to a halt in the
runup to war with Iraq, to resume as those worries receded. But the evidence has been mixed. Euro-zone
unemployment remained at a three-year high of 8.8% in April, while producer prices dropped 0.4% from
March, thanks to lower energy costs. In the U.S., car makers reported that May light-vehicle sales eased to
an annual rate of 16.1 million from 16.5 million in April, despite heavy discounting.
Manufacturers are increasing incentives, a stark example of the deflationary pressure facing the sector. GM
yesterday raised cash discounts on some models to $4,000 from $3,000. Toyota Motor Corp.'s U.S. sales
arm, despite a record sales month, announced 2.9% financing on 60-month loans for several models,
compared with normal rates of 6% to 8%.
In the U.S., Mr. Greenspan said, "The economy did weaken in March and in April, and the data for May to
date suggest that it's stabilized. We have not yet seen any major impetus to a pickup." He still expects growth
to "quicken," in the third quarter, though to less than the 3% to 4% annual growth private economists expect.
For the first time, Mr. Greenspan praised the tax cut that Congress recently passed at President Bush's
urging. In February, he said that until it became clear whether the economy was deteriorating, new tax cuts to
stimulate growth were "premature." But yesterday, he said: "Fortuitously, this particular cut in taxes is
happening at the right time although I doubt if one could have planned that in advance."
Mr. Greenspan's comments on deflation were among his most extensive yet, and he returned repeatedly to
the point that even though deflation is unlikely, its consequences -- should it happen -- are serious enough to
merit consideration of preventive action now. That argument would let the Fed cut interest rates again
without contradicting its often-stated belief that deflation is unlikely and the economy is likely to pick up. "We
perceive [deflation] as a low probability . . . but the cost of addressing it is very small indeed," he said. He
compared the need for insurance against deflation to a "firebreak" -- a strip of cleared land used to prevent
the spread of forest fires.
Mr. Greenspan said a drop in prices by itself isn't worrisome. The real concern, he said, is "corrosive
deflation." He defined that as a self-feeding downward spiral in prices, including asset prices, that hurts the
economy by reducing household and investor wealth. The fact that investors and consumers still appear to
expect steady inflation argues against such corrosive deflation, he indicated.
Mr. Greenspan appears to consider the most worrisome aspect of deflation the Fed's inexperience with
deflation without being on the gold standard. That might reflect an awareness at the central bank after
months of research that stimulating the economy once the federal funds rate gets close to zero, is harder
than it first thought. Alternative tools, such as buying bonds to push down long-term interest rates, might
distort the economy or the financial system. The Fed is thus in no hurry to try them out.
---
Sholnn Freeman contributed to this article.

Review / Books: Around the World on Four Wheels
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Review / Books: Around the World on Four Wheels
By Holman Jenkins Jr.
1,154 words
30 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
W10
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ADVENTURE CAPITALIST
By Jim Rogers
(Random House, 357 pages, $26.95)
AMERICANS LOVE cars and driving, and they are an adventurous people -- so what could more fun than
driving around the world? The answer might be reading about some other nutjob's attempt to drive around the
world.
Jim Rogers made a name once as a Wall Street fund manager and then a second time for playing Magellan
on a motorbike, as recounted in his 1994 book "Investment Biker." Now he's back and driving a specially
modified Mercedes that looks like a bar of yellow soap on tractor tires. In "Adventure Capitalist," the doughty
Mr. Rogers spends days stuck at border crossings negotiating with pigheaded guards or hanging around
consular offices waiting for visas that never come. Getting a car from one country to the next, we sadly
discover, is seldom as easy as slipping it into drive and stepping on the gas.
Along the way Mr. Rogers is accompanied by his girlfriend (later wife), to whom he frequently prevaricates
about the dangers down the next stretch of road. Amazingly, after three years, 152,000 miles and 116
countries, she doesn't murder him. She has his baby. Go figure.
Mr. Rogers has been famous in financial circles for so long that some might be tempted to believe he was
born famous. In fact, as a youngster he helped launch George Soros's Quantum Fund, then retired in 1980
with a bundle of loot. He soon parlayed his Alabama drawl, bon vivant manner and keen powers of
observation into a second career as "Jim Rogers." He may be a little slower and a little chunkier since
"Investment Biker," and now riding on four wheels instead of two, but he's still worth riding with.
He and his long-suffering girlfriend see some crazy sights and do a lot of driving, but that's not what the book
is primarily about. Mr. Rogers is an investment guru. Lesson One resides in the car itself. He chose a
Mercedes, you see, because you can get it serviced anywhere, no matter how godforsaken. Local tinpots,
crime bosses and foreign-aid bureaucrats all drive Benzes, paid for by Western taxpayers, the World Bank
and the IMF. And these are exactly the villains Mr. Rogers blames for the ruination of many otherwise
promising Third World economies.
Like many of us, Mr. Rogers had been too sanguine, after the collapse of communism and amid the Asian
miracle, about a global conversion to sound policy principles. China is still an exception, thanks to its people's
drive and enterprise. Elsewhere he finds much of the world reverting to sclerotic ways.
In Singapore, once known for crackerjack efficiency, it takes five weeks for an investor's check, drawn on
Merrill Lynch, to clear. Mr. Rogers makes a few calls and finds out the money had been transferred in a single
week but the Singaporeans were still pushing paperwork.
He learns that Australia has made it illegal for a foreigner to own a house. Malaysia's despotic prime minister
threatens to waste a lifetime of progress on prestige projects. Even Mr. Rogers's upbeat assessment of China
is tempered by his recognition of an Asian-wide girl shortage, suggesting social strife ahead. South Korea
suffers the worst, with only 100 girls for every 120 boys at age 12.
Mr. Rogers's previous optimism about Africa has also turned to pessimism, especially about South Africa,
where he finds mismanagement in full flower a decade after the Mandela revolution. The roads are in
disrepair, and for enough cash anyone can buy a professional license (doctor, lawyer, airline pilot) from the
relevant ministries.

--- Page 64 ---
Page 64 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Rogers, to his credit, is not a population hysteric. The developing world's problem, he believes, is not too
many people but too little freedom. Governments (abetted by aid bureaucrats) stand in the way of
enterprising people who are denied the freedom -- as he puts it in a wonderfully crisp definition of how the
world ought to be -- to "come and go with their money."
Mr. Rogers, empowered by independent wealth and all the advantages that Western technology and
education can afford, may be uniquely sensitive to people telling him what he can and can't do. Freedom has
always been less than absolute, after all. And let's not forget the tendency of even the wisest people, as they
age, to confuse their own slowing down with the world going to hell in a handbasket. Yet Mr. Rogers puts
forward many small but telling evidences of societies retreating from free trade and full respect for property
rights.
On a happier note, he remains reasonably upbeat about his native country. Crossing the U.S. border even
after the terrorist attacks, the only thing that slows him down is gearhead border guards wanting to check out
his esoteric wheels. Lack of bureaucratic goo is always a key index for him. And away from New York City
and the bellyaching state capitals, he finds the economy surprisingly strong.
Still, Mr. Rogers has a blunderbuss to unload at U.S. policymakers. He wonders how current fears about
deflation can be reconciled with a falling dollar, rising commodity prices and a lake of Federal
Reserve-engineered liquidity. Industries that build too much capacity (say, telecom) are going to experience
falling prices, to be sure. But Mr. Rogers sees something else going on.
Government has been busy defining away inflation by attributing price increases to the "improved quality" of
goods rather than to the usual "too much money chasing too few goods." The Fed has so spooked itself with
this new definition of inflation that it has been engaged in a money-creation binge since the mid-1990s,
matched by similar excess in Congress and the White House on the fiscal front.
Thus Washington has been misguidedly trying to engineer a macro solution to a micro problem, namely the
need to liquidate a certain number of badly conceived business enterprises and (in cases where fraud played
a role) throw their promoters in jail. The incipient price explosion is already seen in gold, zinc and other
commodities. Mr. Rogers maintains that raw materials will remain the easiest place for investors to make
money for a time to come. In fact, he's keen on East Timor because of its huge natural-gas deposits.
Those readers who've been loading up on tech stocks recently may want to shoot themselves at this point,
but the pain is all in Mr. Rogers's message, not in the delivery. He writes with verve and blessed brevity -- a
measure of the self-discipline he cherishes in himself and others and not a virtue always apparent in the
output of the publishing industry these days.
---
Mr. Jenkins writes the Journal's Business World column, which appears on Wednesdays.

Small-Stock Focus: VaxGen Soars as Medarex Rises in Biotech Group
Small-Stock Focus: VaxGen Soars as Medarex Rises in Biotech Group
By Cynthia Schreiber
Dow Jones Newswires
880 words
29 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Stalwart optimism about a second-half recovery helped small-capitalization stocks outperform
their large-cap peers.
Two biotechnology companies with news on about anthrax rose despite a retreat in the broader sector, with
the Nasdaq Biotech Index slipping 0.7%, or 4.48, to 670.15, after hitting a 52-week high of 674.67 on
Tuesday.
VaxGen surged 68%, or $2.28, to $5.62, on word the Food and Drug Administration said the Brisbane, Calif.,
drug company could start human trials of its investigational anthrax vaccine. Medarex climbed 5.4%, or 32
cents, to 6.27, after the Princeton, N.J., drug company said its human antibody against anthrax worked on
rabbits.
Analysts said small stocks moved higher on continued optimism that the U.S. economy will rebound in the
second half of the year. The market appears to be hoping low interest rates, refinancings and tax cuts will
encourage the all-important consumer.
"The consumer is still two-thirds of the economy," said Tony Rosenthal, portfolio manager at TimesSquare
Capital Management.
The Russell 2000 Index of small stocks closed up 0.7%, or 2.77 points, to 430.48, while the Standard &Poor's
SmallCap 600 Index gained 0.9%, or 1.78, to close at 210.32. The Nasdaq Composite Index of large-cap and
small-cap stocks climbed 0.4%, or 6.55, to 1563.24.
Software-company SCO Group dropped 24%, or 2.11, to 6.60, after Novell contested SCO's assertion it owns
the copyrights and patents to the Unix operating system. SCO responded with a statement claiming it owns
the contract rights to the Unix system. Novell rose 4.3%, or 13 cents, to 3.13.
XM Satellite Radio Holdings jumped 6%, or 61 cents, to 11.16, after the Washington radio-broadcaster said
its popular portable satellite-radio product is being sold in Wal-Mart stores.
Home-builders were the session's best performers on the heels of large-cap Toll Brothers' second-quarter
profit report, with Champion Enterprises soaring 9.1%, or 25 cents, to 3, and Standard Pacific gaining 2.4%,
or 82 cents, to 34.47, both on the New York Stock Exchange.
Large-cap Costco's better-than-expected quarterly results led retailers of all sizes higher, with BJ's Wholesale
Club climbing 5%, or 73 cents, to 15.23, and Dillard's ahead 2.7%, or 36 cents, to 13.51, both on the NYSE.
Entertainment stocks moved higher, led by Avid Technology, up 6.5%, or 2.04, to 33.23, buoyed by First
Albany's upbeat comments about the technology company's new video-editing product.
Gold stocks fell as the price of the commodity finished the session lower, with Meridian Gold, on the NYSE,
skidding 4.2%, or 49 cents, to 11.06, and Stillwater Mining, also on the NYSE, falling 2.5%, or 11 cents, to
4.22.
Airline shares fell after a report showed low passenger traffic industry-wide heading into the summer travel
season. Northwest Airlines fell 3%, or 28 cents, to 8.98, while both on the NYSE, Continental Airlines dropped
4.6%, or 53 cents, to 10.97, and Alaska Air Group fell 3.5%, or 67 cents, to 18.53.
BioCryst Pharmaceuticals catapulted 28%, or 82 cents, to 3.69, after the biotech said its investigational
hepatitis-C compound will be tested against the SARS and West Nile viruses under a pact with the National
Institutes of Health and the U.S. Army.

--- Page 66 ---
Page 66 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Cyberonics sank 20%, or 4.84, to 19.16, after the Houston medical-device maker posted a fourth-quarter
profit of 17 cents a share, but forecast first-quarter earnings below analysts' target.
RealNetworks jumped 12%, or 95 cents, to 9.15, after the Seattle software company unveiled an online music
service that lets subscribers buy and download songs for 79 cents a track, compared with a similar service by
Apple for 99 cents a song without subscription fees.
Greka Energy vaulted 1.80, or 44%, to 5.85, after its chief executive agreed to buy all the shares outstanding
he doesn't already own for $6.25 each in cash.
Furniture Brands International, the St. Louis distributor of Thomasville furniture, gained 10%, or 2.15, to
24.90, on the NYSE, after an analyst said strong retail furniture sales during the Memorial Day weekend were
an encouraging sign for the industry.
Riverboat-casino owner Argosy Gaming, in NYSE trading, fell 9%, or 1.95, to 20.35, amid growing concern
over opposition to gaming expansion in Illinois, where Argosy generates significant revenue and is based.
Foster Wheeler, on the NYSE, rose 17%, or 40 cents, to 2.72, after the Bermuda-based engineering firm said
it is part of a group on a project in China.
Semtech, Camarillo, Calif., fell 9%, or 1.50, to 15.40, after a drop in first-quarter earnings. Rival Aeroflex rose
7.7%, or 55 cents, to 7.65. Spherix rose 7.5%, or 54 cents, to 7.71, after the Beltsville, Md., company said
soft-drink giant PepsiCo is exploring use of its low-calorie sweetener.

Business World: Weighing (and Ignoring) Candidate Health Plans
Business World: Weighing (and Ignoring) Candidate Health Plans
By Holman W. Jenkins Jr.
1,026 words
28 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Ask any development economist and you'll hear that barter is a hallmark of a backward stage of economic
development. Barter is when you trade a good or service directly for another, without the convenience,
liquidity and portability of cash intervening -- for instance, when a worker trades hours at a GE plant in return
for health care.
Which brings us to today's topical absurdity, a new round of labor discord at General Electric over exactly
how much health care the company will dish out to hourly workers in return for their willingness to punch the
clock. No, this isn't about labor relations, GE or the dignity of work. It's about our health-care "crisis."
Any close analysis of our predicament must begin with the fact that we don't expect our employers to give us
food in lieu of wages, pay our cable bill or supply us with hair spray. Why do we expect them to provide us
with health care? Yet both parties are keen to revise and extend this anomaly, using taxpayer dollars or
indirect tax subsidies to stimulate yet more spending on health care.
The distortion created here, ironically, goes a long way toward explaining why so many of the estimated 41
million uninsured are going without health insurance unwillingly -- i.e., they'd happily pay for a policy that met
their needs at a reasonable price. Why aren't such policies available? Because so much middle-class health
care is paid for by employers, politicians treat it as a free lunch: "We'll give you more health insurance (they
tell voters) and it won't cost you a cent. Your employer will pay for it."
Then they pass laws expanding the cornucopia of services that must be covered, driving the cost out of sight.
Workers complain of stagnant wages but the real problem is their pay raises are simply disappearing into the
health-care economy. They accuse management of trying to "shift the burden" with co-pays and deductibles.
This language plays dodge ball with reality too: Workers always paid for their own health care. The cost was
merely laundered through the company's books to qualify for a tax gimme.
We've been willing to live with this wacky system and perhaps will continue to do so indefinitely. GE's
heath-care spending on behalf of its employees is up nearly 50% in four years. But let's not lose sight of how
much trouble it costs us:
-- The system encourages more profligate consumption than if consumers were paying directly from their own
pockets with after-tax dollars (more consumption of health care is not the same thing as more health).
-- It gives the fattest subsidy to those in the highest tax bracket, tilting the entire health-care economy toward
providing gold-plated service to those with gold plated coverage.
-- It entraps most of society in a "common pool" morass -- we're all drawing from the same insurance pool, so
I better spend as much as I can because you'll be doing the same.
Complicating matters further, federal law has been tweaked to encourage companies to treat all workers the
same as far as health benefits are concerned. Thus low-skilled workers (with few or no health-care benefits)
are segregated in companies that hire only low-skilled workers and provide zero opportunity for upward
mobility.
Make no mistake: Low-skilled workers would be crazy to give up any part of their meager wages to receive
health benefits -- because such benefits are far less valuable to those in low tax brackets. Effectively, affluent
households pay $60 for $100 worth of health insurance, while low-income households pay between $85 and
$100 for the same insurance.
This year's crop of Democrats offers nothing helpful. The most honest plan is Rep. Dennis Kucinich's
unappetizing nationalization of health care, putting government in the position of controlling costs by denying
care. Guess what? It will never happen here: Politicians harp on 41 million Americans estimated to be without

--- Page 68 ---
Page 68 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
health insurance, but that leaves 240 million with coverage, few of whom would be eager to give up what
they've got today in favor of a uniform government benefit that puts them on the same footing as the
non-paying poor.
John Kerry and Howard Dean would throw more money at existing government programs and more tax
incentives at companies so yet more of the country's health spending can be channeled through third-party
payers. This is a holding operation, allowing them to contrast themselves with Republicans who want to
"spend money" on tax cuts for "the rich."
Dick Gephardt would require employers to cover all their employees and would flow tax dollars directly to
companies for 60% of the cost. Why keep employers as middlemen in a system that would effectively be
government financed? Nobody knows but it allows Mr. Gephardt to say he's "building on" the current system
and proposing nothing radical. More insidiously, channeling the money through business would allow
Washington to blame business for the resulting explosion in costs.
Not for a decade has anyone in Congress been willing to question tax favoritism for company-provided health
insurance, though it's the central engine of inefficient health-care inflation. Republicans content themselves
with proposals to extend the favoritism to out-of-pocket health-care spending, a modest redress of the
incentive to channel every hangnail through the third-party payment system.
None of this being much help, companies are improvising their own solutions. They can't pass up a big tax
subsidy for their workers, but many have become aggressive about restraining the incentive to consume
profligately. Most daring is the "defined contributions" approach. To wit, employees get a fixed company
contribution to their health-care needs and they choose themselves how to spend it.
By such means will the system be kept from blowing up for at least another election cycle.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Why Primary Health Care Is Losing Its Physicians" -- WSJ June 17,
2003)

Page 69 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Wall Street's Bulls Take Charge Again As Tax-Cut Accord Buoys Investors
Thursday's Markets
Wall Street's Bulls Take Charge Again As Tax-Cut Accord Buoys Investors
By E.S. Browning
878 words
23 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SPARKED BY NEWS of a tax-cut agreement in Washington, the bulls on Wall Street took charge again,
sending the major stock indexes up about 1% each.
Many traders had expected the slump that began one week ago to last longer, and they said they were
surprised to see the buying momentum pick up so soon. Some said the drop could resume after the Memorial
Day holiday on Monday, when stock and bond markets are closed.
But others concluded that the tax-cut deal, together with Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan's
indications Wednesday that he will keep low interest rates and other stimulative techniques to prevent
deflation, gave renewed hope to investors who are banking on an economic recovery in the second half of the
year.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 77.59 points, or 0.91%, to 8594.02. It now is up 3% since the year
began and only 1.5% below its recent high of 8726.73, hit May 12.
Plenty of jitters remained, fed in part by a report showing weekly new unemployment claims still above
400,000 last week. With investors widely expecting interest rates to remain low for some time, Treasury
bonds surged, sending their yields down yet again. In late New York trading, the yield on the 10-year
Treasury note fell to 3.326%, yet another 45-year low. The buying was fueled by speculation that, if rate cuts
alone aren't enough, the Fed could wind up buying back longer-term bonds in order to stimulate the
economy.
The dollar fell again against the euro, nearing the four-year low it hit on Tuesday.
But stock investors viewed the day as a vote of confidence.
"I figured we would rally at the end of the week," in advance of what is expected to be a light trading day
today, "but I didn't think we would rally this strongly," said Tim Smalls, senior stock trader at New York
brokerage firm SG Cowen.
Mr. Smalls pointed out that the Dow Jones industrials were led up by manufacturing stalwarts such as Alcoa,
Caterpillar, 3M and International Paper, all dividend-paying stocks that would benefit both from a dividend tax
cut and from any recovery in the economy. "You don't buy Alcoa and Caterpillar unless you think there is an
economic recovery coming. It is a nice shift to see," he said. The Dow Jones Utility Average, full of
dividend-paying stocks, climbed 1.67%, even more than the Dow industrials.
Stocks gave back some of their early gains late in the day, as some people took profits before the weekend.
Traders also were distracted by televised reports of the progress of Annika Sorenstam, whose competition
against male golfers in the Colonial golf tournament marked the first time a woman has played in a PGA
tournament since 1945.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index gained 0.92%, or 8.45 points, to 931.87, up almost 6% on the year.
The Nasdaq Composite Index advanced 1.19%, or 17.68 points, to 1507.55, up almost 13% this year.
Gold and oil prices, which earlier in the week had been rising amid the stock declines, both fell.
Economists argued about whether tax cuts for capital gains and dividends will really boost the economy, but
investors concluded that, regardless of what else they do, they will help the stock market.
"You can debate how much stimulation it provides for the economy, but the good news is that it is good for
investors," said Andy Brooks, head of stock trading at Baltimore mutual-fund group T. Rowe Price.

--- Page 70 ---
Page 70 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Even many bullish investors say they think stocks could well decline again to consolidate following the 16%
gain the Dow Jones industrials recorded from mid-March through mid-May. But a growing number of money
managers have concluded that with interest rates low and with the government's providing some tax relief,
an economic recovery is coming, bringing a market recovery with it.
"We are very impressed with this rally," said Jon Brorson, a onetime stock skeptic who heads growth-stock
investing for money-management firm Neuberger Berman. Mr. Brorson said he expects more ups and downs
this year and thinks the overall stock gains will be muted. But he added, "The market is sniffing out a better
year next year. We think the bear market is over."
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 1.38%, or 1.48 points, to 108.99.
In major U.S. market action:

Hussein Son, Uday, Is Thinking Of Surrendering to U.S. Forces
Hussein Son, Uday, Is Thinking Of Surrendering to U.S. Forces
By Jeffrey Zaslow
471 words
23 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A8
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Saddam Hussein's eldest son, Uday, is considering surrendering to U.S. forces, but so far has been reluctant
to do so because of a tough negotiating posture by the U.S. government, according to a third party with
knowledge of the discussions.
U.S. officials in Washington had no comment.
Uday Hussein, who is hiding in a Baghdad, Iraq, suburb, wants to know what the charges against him will be,
and the process for interrogation and custody, the person familiar with the discussions said. He is working
through intermediaries. U.S. officials don't seem especially interested in cutting a deal, because they assume
Uday will be caught sooner or later, the person said.
Uday was commander of Iraq's feared paramilitary unit known as the Saddam Fedayeen, and he was also
chairman of the Iraqi Olympic Committee. He is No. 3 on the coalition's most-wanted list, after his father and
his brother, Qusay.
Uday fears that Iraqi citizens will kill him if they find him, and may instead choose the safety of a U.S. prison,
the person said, adding that Uday frequently changes his mind about surrendering. "He doesn't have good
choices," the person said.
In surrenders negotiated so far, it appears the U.S. has given no guarantees regarding prosecution and
sentencing. In some cases, it has offered to protect family members of those in custody, who fear reprisals
from Hussein loyalists. Some are being offered help in leaving Iraq.
Yesterday, U.S. forces took into custody Aziz Salih al-Numan, who was No. 8 on the most-wanted list. He
was the Iraqi governor during the 1990-91 occupation of Kuwait, and is also accused of having been involved
in the torture and killing of rebels during a 1991 Shiite uprising in the south of Iraq. He is the highest-ranking
Iraqi official now in custody, and is one of nine top leaders whom the U.S. wants to see tried for war crimes.
So far, 25 of the top 55 most-wanted Iraqis are in coalition custody.
Saddam Hussein is also alive and in suburban Baghdad, the person familiar with Uday's surrender
discussions said he has been told by a Saddam relative. He added that the deposed leader is in questionable
mental health. The U.S. government has asked intermediaries for help in finding him or negotiating a
surrender, but this person knows of no progress.
Before Tariq Aziz, Iraq's former deputy prime minister, surrendered, he told his family and others that Mr.
Hussein's sons took more than $1 billion in gold from Iraq's central bank, the person said. It has already
been reported that the sons took more than $1 billion in cash.

Money Trail: Inside Failed Bank Run by a Man U.S. Backs For Key Role in Iraq --- Before Jordanian Fraud Verdict, Chalabi Was Moving Funds...
Money Trail: Inside Failed Bank Run by a Man U.S. Backs For Key Role in Iraq --- Before Jordanian
Fraud Verdict, Chalabi Was Moving Funds Freely Among Family Firms --- Leaving in Middle of the
Night
By Robert Block
2,870 words
22 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AMMAN, Jordan -- After four decades in exile, Ahmad Chalabi is back in Baghdad, ensconced in an old
Baath Party haunt called the Iraqi Hunting Club.
The flag of his Iraqi National Congress exile group flies over buildings occupied by his militia, which the U.S.
military trained. As he angles for a big role in running Iraq, he has high-level support in at least part of the
Bush administration, the Pentagon. Yet one uncomfortable fact has long hobbled Mr. Chalabi's path to a
post-Saddam Hussein leadership role: a conviction for bank fraud.
A court in Jordan returned that verdict 11 years ago. Trying him in absentia, it sentenced him to 22 years in
prison and ordered restitution of $30 million of bank money it said he had embezzled.
Mr. Chalabi maintains the case was politically motivated, a result of Jordanian palace politics compounded by
economic pressure on the kingdom from the Hussein regime next door. He says he couldn't defend himself
because he had left Jordan, partly in fear for his life after a threat of being turned over to the Iraqis. "The
decision is by a military court. It is meaningless," he said in an interview last year.
Much about the case has always been murky, allowing Mr. Chalabi to dismiss it, even as his foes seize on it
to undermine him. But in recent weeks, some Jordanian officials and former bank managers have opened
their records and agreed to talk about the case. The files include bank records, a 1989 audit of the bank by
Arthur Andersen &Co., depositions from the trial and the court's 213-page ruling. The documents and
interviews make it possible to give the most detailed account to date of the rise and fall of a bank run by a
potential future leader of Iraq.
They portray Mr. Chalabi as, at best, always pushing the limits: exceeding Jordan's lending caps, and hiding
this by misclassifying overnight loans from other banks. They show him turning bank employees into money
couriers, dispatched on airliners with millions of dollars in endorsed checks to speed up the clearing process.
They portray him as treating other parts of a far-flung family financial empire to collateral-free loans -- one of
the ways the court determined that Mr. Chalabi had embezzled millions from the bank.
The court that made that finding was an arm of the martial-law rule Jordan was under at the time. Mr.
Chalabi, because he didn't show up for the trial, didn't have the right to defend himself. He did make an effort
to present his side of the story. He answered questions that were sent to him by a lawyer for the bank, who
passed his replies on to the prosecutor.
Though Mr. Chalabi says it was a kangaroo court, lawyers in Jordan say the court made a serious attempt to
get to the bottom of a highly complex matter. The former longtime No. 2 executive at the bank, Hassan
Abdul-Aziz, says that "the court made a huge effort to understand this case." Mr. Abdul-Aziz, who was
acquitted in the matter, remains an ardent supporter of Mr. Chalabi.
Mr. Chalabi was born in 1945 into a prominent family of Iraqi businessmen and politicians. An uncle started
the Rafidain Bank of Baghdad, once the largest bank in the Middle East. But in a bloody Iraqi coup in 1958, a
coalition of military men and Communists nationalized the bank and other Chalabi family businesses. Family
members fled into exile.
Young Ahmad attended an English boarding school, then Massachusetts Institute of Technology and the
University of Chicago. In Chicago he got a mathematics Ph.D., with a thesis in an arcane field called the
theory of knots. He took a teaching post in Beirut, where a brother had begun a bank called Middle East
Banking Corp. of Lebanon, or Mebco.
The return of a family that was famous in Middle East banking caught the eye of businessmen in nearby
Jordan, which in the 1970s was on the cusp of a financial boom. Oil-flush Gulf states had agreed to pour

--- Page 75 ---
Page 75 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
$1.25 billion into the kingdom because of its front-line position next to Israel. Other cash flowed in from
Palestinian laborers abroad, who saw Jordanians as natural custodians of their interests after Israel occupied
the West Bank in 1967. A group close to Jordan's royal family, concerned that Palestinians were beginning to
dominate banking, invited the Chalabis to start a competing Jordanian bank.
In 1978, Mr. Chalabi opened Petra Bank, named for ancient ruins in southern Jordan. It soon shook up the
staid banking sector. Its computer technology provided Jordanians with the novelty of itemized statements.
The bank set up lay-away plans to help young couples buy furniture and brought the kingdom its first credit
cards. Deposits poured in.
"Dr. Ahmad single-handedly modernized the banking sector in Jordan, and in the process woke up a lot of
sleeping giants. He made some big players in the field very angry," says Osama Halaseh, a former Petra
Bank projects manager.
If he irritated rivals, Mr. Chalabi won intense loyalty from underlings. "I have to admit I love the guy," says Mr.
Halaseh. "Although I was just a midlevel manager, he would meet with me personally and take an interest in
my views of the projects I was running."
Mr. Halaseh says he doesn't believe Mr. Chalabi stole from the bank. Neither does Mr. Abdul-Aziz.
What they do say is that they regularly carried out Chalabi instructions that flouted banking regulations. One
rule forbade banks to lend more than 70% of deposits. Petra Bank often lent closer to 90%, according to
documents and to Mr. Abdul-Aziz.
Often in short-term need of cash, Petra Bank was deeply involved in the interbank overnight-deposit market,
briefly holding other banks' surplus funds. Petra would frequently record these overnight deposits as
long-term deposits, in order to appear to meet the 70% limit, Mr. Abdul-Aziz says.
Bypassing Jordan's slow system of shipping endorsed checks to banks of origin for clearance, Mr. Chalabi
had his employees fly endorsed checks themselves to the U.S., Britain and Switzerland. Mr. Halaseh says he
once flew $18 million in checks to Petra Bank's sister institution in Washington, Petra International Banking
Corp., or PIBC. Jordanian authorities say the maneuver violated their foreign-exchange rules.
Mr. Halaseh also says Mr. Chalabi sometimes approved loans, with little or no collateral, to borrowers turned
away by other banks. Jordanian court records and accountants' reviews later blamed a cash squeeze at the
bank partly on its extension of credit to high-risk borrowers, including some controlled by Chalabi friends and
relatives.
The family empire included Mebco Banks in Beirut and in Geneva, Switzerland; PIBC in Washington; a
London gold-dealing company called SCF; a Geneva investment company known as Socofi; and two
Jordanian investment companies, al Remal and Abhara. Petra Bank presented these to Jordanian regulators
as separate. But they were "treated as if they were a branch of Petra Bank itself," Mr. Abdul-Aziz said in an
interview, as Zuhair Mubaideen, who was a lawyer for Petra Bank, nodded in agreement.
They said money was constantly flowing back and forth among these companies. "Loans were granted to
them without any need for checking their credit or looking for guarantees, as they were in reality part of the
bank," Mr. Abdul-Aziz says.
He adds that he repeatedly urged his boss to be more conservative. "Ahmad's biggest problem was that he
always liked to go to the edge," he says.
Mr. Chalabi says he did nothing improper. He defended the propriety of some things that others called illegal,
and he suggested that in Jordan, political motives sometimes trumped the kingdom's elaborate legal and
regulatory system. Petra Bank stuck to lending ratios, Mr. Chalabi said through a spokesman, accusing
Jordanian officials of "manipulating figures" to make it appear otherwise. He said the bank always required
borrowers to put up adequate collateral.
As for employees flying endorsed checks to banks abroad, Mr. Chalabi portrayed this as a neat way to clear
checks faster and make money in the process. In a written response through a spokesman, he said: "Banks
in Jordan traditionally offered 10-day value on U.S. dollar checks. Petra undercut the market by offering
customers 6-day value, then flew the checks to N.Y., thereby getting overnight value for itself. Petra made $2
million annually on this float."
He contended this was a legal move and one that other banks used, too.
Asked about treating related companies as an extension of the bank, Mr. Chalabi said these were companies
held in bank officials' names, but actually controlled by Petra Bank. He said the device was a way for Petra to
accumulate shares in a bank it wanted to merge with, and get around a 10% ownership limit.

--- Page 76 ---
Page 76 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Through most of the 1980s, Petra thrived, becoming the second largest bank in Jordan. But in 1988, the
kingdom faced financial crisis. A decade of boom fueled by borrowed money collapsed amid chronic
government overspending. In addition, some Palestinians abroad stopped depositing money with Jordanian
banks after King Hussein dropped Jordan's claim of sovereignty over the West Bank.
Jordan halted interest payments on its external debt. Its currency lost value. Inflation led to riots. Bedouin
women flocked to gold souks to sell 21-karat gold bangle bracelets and necklaces for food money.
In October 1988, Petra staved off a potential run on the bank by stacking $12 million in cash in the lobby of its
main branch for everyone to see.
Then Chalabi institutions elsewhere ran into trouble. Switzerland's banking commission revoked Mebco's
license in April 1989 for issuing loans without proper collateral. Socofi, the family investment firm in Geneva,
entered bankruptcy proceedings. Court-appointed liquidation attorneys instigated a criminal case against
Socofi board members, alleging false accounting and improper use of funds.
Mr. Chalabi says the Swiss Banking Commission unfairly attacked Mebco because it had the right to issue
Visa cards, while some Swiss banks didn't. Commission officials deny such a motive. The criminal case
against Socofi board members languished for years. It was eventually given a form of administrative closure,
with suspended sentences against two brothers of Mr. Chalabi. (Ahmad Chalabi wasn't charged.) The
liquidators are still trying to recover $130 million for Socofi's creditors. Authorities in Lebanon later closed the
Mebco Bank there, citing financial irregularities.
About the time that Swiss officials first took action, Jordan's King Hussein asked a former central banker to
return and try to brake the Jordanian financial system's plunge. One of the first moves by the official,
Mohammed Said Nabulsi, was to demand that banks deposit 35% of their foreign-currency holdings at the
central bank. Mr. Chalabi's Petra Bank was supposed to deposit $65 million. "He didn't come up with a
single dollar," Mr. Nabulsi says.
One reason was that the Swiss shutdown had interrupted the flow of funds among parts of the extended
Chalabi empire. For "Petra Bank [it] was like having one of its hands chopped off," Mr. Abdul-Aziz says. Mr.
Nabulsi reported his concerns about Petra Bank's solvency to a martial-law bureau called the Economic
Security Committee, which dissolved the bank's management, handing control to a new team appointed by
Mr. Nabulsi.
The central banker says he gave Mr. Chalabi a seat, hoping for his help in clearing up the mess, but "it was a
mistake -- we should have put him immediately in jail."
Mr. Chalabi maintains that Petra faced just a temporary cash squeeze that could have been worked out. He
contends that Mr. Nabulsi, with whom he had clashed in the past over plans for a bank merger, used Petra's
inability to meet the foreign-exchange demand as a pretext for the takeover.
Jordan's government took control of Petra Bank on Aug. 3, 1989. Mr. Abdul-Aziz tells a story of how three
days later, he called upon Mr. Chalabi to console him. Walking up to a vast white stone mansion on a Sunday
evening, past a row of expensive cars, Mr. Abdul-Aziz says he was met at the door by his ex-boss and still
friend -- who pressed an envelope into his hand. Written on it: "Do not open until tomorrow."
Back in his car, Mr. Abdul-Aziz says, he tore into it immediately. Inside, he says, was $5,000 in cash and a
note reading, "Spend this on your kids." A second envelope within the first held what Mr. Abdul-Aziz
describes as a "big" check and a second note reading, "For you." At the bottom was scribbled, "May God
protect us."
Mr. Abdul-Aziz says he saw the money simply as a sign of Mr. Chalabi's generosity, with no ulterior motive.
Asked about the story, Mr. Chalabi laughed and called it a "total fabrication," his spokesman says.
What isn't in doubt is that in the wee hours of the next morning, Mr. Chalabi slipped out of Jordan, never to
return.
The local press filled with reports that he had fled. He called news agencies to say he was just on holiday.
Later, press articles in London quoted him as saying he had sneaked out hidden in the trunk of a car. This
month, he said through his spokesman that the going-on-holiday tale was just a way to protect his bank
colleagues, and denied he had ever told the car-trunk version. He said he simply drove across the border to
Syria.
One reason he left: He said a Jordanian intelligence official had warned him Iraqi agents were in town looking
for him because of his opposition to Mr. Hussein, and that Jordan might turn him over.

--- Page 77 ---
Page 77 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Now in control of Petra Bank, Jordanian authorities asked for an audit. Four months later, Arthur Andersen
accountants declared the bank insolvent. Shareholder equity wasn't $50 million as reported, their 267-page
report said, but a negative $268 million. They said an additional, hard-to-explain $158 million had flowed out
but not come back. Much of that involved "transactions with parties related to the former management of the
bank," it said.
A criminal inquiry began. Mr. Chalabi faced 72 counts from a military court. Jordan asked him to return for the
trial. Mr. Chalabi said he couldn't because he had no assurances that Mr. Nabulsi, the central banker,
wouldn't have him thrown in jail.
Instead, a lawyer for the bank asked Mr. Chalabi questions, then passed the answers to the military
prosecutor, who says that he read them. Two and a half years later, the three-member court convicted Mr.
Chalabi on 48 of the counts, including embezzlement, fraud and breach of trust. It said he had made off with
$30 million of the bank's funds in various complex scams.
In one example, the judges said Mr. Chalabi and a brother had hijacked $2.6 million in management fees due
the bank for work in Sudan. The court said the brothers channeled the fee through Socofi in Geneva and
diverted it to a firm they owned, Petra Bank Management Group. Mr. Chalabi's answer: That firm, not the
bank, held the Sudan contract.
The court also found that Mr. Chalabi once had Petra Bank pay the $35,000 cost of shipping his deceased
father's body from Britain to Syria for burial. Mr. Chalabi says he paid this bill.
The court convicted 16 others in the case, all of whom had left Jordan rather than face trial. It cleared 32 who
had stayed to face the charges.
The central bank ultimately pumped $314 million into Petra Bank to rescue its depositors and cover
liabilities, Jordanian authorities say. They say they would arrest Mr. Chalabi if he returned to the kingdom,
and he would be liable for the penalty assessed. But in an odd twist of Jordanian law, because Mr. Chalabi's
absence had left him not legally entitled to a defense, upon his return he could mount a defense and in that
way challenge the court ruling.
He says he has no plans to return.
---
Hugh Pope in London, Roger Thurow in Zurich and Suha Ma'ayeh in Amman contributed to this article.

Blue Chips Gain, As Does the Dollar; Gold, Oil Show Fears
Wednesday's Markets
Blue Chips Gain, As Does the Dollar; Gold, Oil Show Fears
By E.S. Browning
466 words
22 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TAKE AN ALAN GREENSPAN speech, reports of a dividend-tax-cut agreement and more terrorist threats,
and what do you get? A sideways stock market.
Hit by those pressures, and by the debate about whether stocks have risen too fast recently, the Dow Jones
Industrial Average eked out a gain of 25.07 points, or 0.30%, to 8516.43. It is up 2.1% since the year began.
Other markets moved in conflicting directions. Gold and oil prices rose, reflecting investor nervousness. But
the yield of the benchmark 10-year Treasury note, which had been at a 45-year low on fears of deflation,
reversed course and rose. The dollar, which had been at a four-year low against the euro, also rebounded.
But many big technology stocks fell.
"There is an awful lot going on now that investors are trying to digest, not the least of which is that the stock
market came a long way very quickly," said stock trader Michael Driscoll at New York brokerage firm Bear
Stearns. The industrial average rose 16% in two months through mid-May, and now it is down 2.4% from its
May 12 high.
In testimony before a congressional committee, Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan was his usual
inscrutable self, indicating that he still expects the economy to rebound but adding that deflation remains a
risk. Analysts disagreed whether he sounded inclined to cut target short-term interest rates further.
Congress was reported during the day to have compromised on a tax cut, although that agreement fell into
question after regular trading hours ended. And the al Jazeera television network broadcast a taped threat of
more terrorist actions that it said came from Ayman al-Zawahiri, an al Qaeda leader.
The Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.08%, or 1.22 points, to 1489.87, while the Standard &Poor's 500-stock
index rose 0.40%, or 3.69 points, to 923.42.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 1.17%, or 1.27 point, to 107.51.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the Big Board, where 1.44 billion shares traded, 1,968 stocks rose and 1,295 fell.
Bonds were mixed. The 10-year Treasury note fell 9/32, or $2.8125 for each $1,000 invested. The yield,
which moves inversely to price, rose to 3.403%. The 30-year bond was up 6/32 to yield 4.355%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 117.27 yen, up from 116.71, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.1678 from $1.1707.

IMF Sees Some Deflation Risks, But Says Global Hazard Is Low
IMF Sees Some Deflation Risks, But Says Global Hazard Is Low
By David Wessel
592 words
19 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A8
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WASHINGTON -- A special International Monetary Fund deflation task force warned of a high and increasing
risk of deflation in Germany, Taiwan and Hong Kong and of worsening deflation in Japan, but concluded that
the risks of global deflation are "still low."
"In the U.S., despite the lingering effects of the equity price bubble," the IMF said in a report, "risk of deflation
appears relatively low . . . but not minimal." Serious deflation is unlikely unless unemployment, now 6%,
reaches 8% or economic growth remains below 1% over the next 18 months, the task force said.
"Deflation is seldom benign," the IMF economists warned in a bluntly worded manifesto aimed at prodding
governments and central banks into preparing for the worst. "Deflation can be costly, and is difficult to
anticipate." One or two quarters of falling prices wouldn't be worrisome, the task force said; more than that
would be. "Even mild but continuous deflation can be cause for concern."
"Policies can be effective in warding off deflation, but only if pre-emptive, forceful and sometimes
unconventional steps are taken," the task force said. "It is better to prevent deflation than to try to cure it, and
monetary policy must take the lead."
Examining 35 economies that comprise 90% of the global economy, the IMF described the risk of significant
deflation as "moderate" in Singapore and seven small European economies. It rated the deflation risk as
"low" in 16 countries, including the U.S and China, and "minimal" in eight others.
The task force called particular attention to Germany, where underlying inflation is below 1% already,
unemployment and unused capacity are high and rising, and banks are struggling. "The probability of mild
deflation taking hold over the next year is considerable," the task force said. Yet German fiscal policy is
turning contractionary as the government tries to keep its budget deficit below ceilings set by treaty in Europe.
And because deflation risks are lower elsewhere among countries that share the euro, the European Central
Bank has been slow to cut interest rates.
The IMF blamed falling prices in China on transitory factors, as well as that country's large pool of
underutilized workers and excess capacity. The government's antideflation fiscal and monetary policies and
"buoyant private demand and increasing real-estate prices" diminish the deflation threat, the IMF said. But it
added that severe acute respiratory syndrome, or SARS, could have an adverse -- and deflationary -- effect if
not contained quickly.
"Growing exports from China have added to price pressures in many sectors world-wide and these pressures
are set to increase," the IMF added. This is particularly evident in Taiwan and Hong Kong and, to a lesser
extent, Singapore.
The task force said it "didn't find evidence to support strong concerns of generalized global deflation."
Although deflation spread from one country to another via the gold standard in the late 1920s and 1930s, the
task force argued that exporting deflation is less likely today, partly because exchange rates are flexible.
---

Bonds Hold Steady, as Dow Industrials Push Past 8700
Thursday's Markets
Bonds Hold Steady, as Dow Industrials Push Past 8700
By E.S. Browning
710 words
16 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MANY INVESTORS CONTINUE to show signs of confidence in both stocks and bonds, as the benchmark
10-year Treasury note held steady after its milestone day Wednesday and the Nasdaq Composite Index and
the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index both reversed their two-day retreats to hit their highest levels since last
summer.
Spurred by improving earnings and tame inflation, the Dow Jones Industrial Average pushed back above
8700, after an advance of more than 65 points, although it didn't quite return to the four-month high it hit on
Monday.
The 10-year Treasury note was nearly unchanged yesterday, after Wednesday's rally that had sent its yield,
which moves opposite to price, to a 45-year low. The yield, a benchmark for many fixed-rate mortgages, rose
to 3.525% yesterday from Wednesday's thin 3.519%.
The Labor Department's report that wholesale prices fell 1.9% last month, the sharpest monthly decline since
the government began collecting the data in 1947, rekindled worries about deflation and recession. Some
investors concluded that the Federal Reserve may well cut its target rates yet again to stimulate the economy
at its next policy meeting late next month.
The more-volatile 30-year Treasury bond saw its yield plunge yet again to 4.479%, its lowest level since the
Treasury Department started issuing 30-year bonds regularly in the 1970s.
The Dow industrial average rose 65.32 points, or 0.76%, to 8713.14, up 4.5% since the year began and up
16% since March 11.
"The market is proving to be very resilient," said Tim Heekin, director of trading at San Francisco brokerage
firm Thomas Weisel Partners. Investors are shrugging off negative news, and "they are encouraged by
slightly improving fundamentals in various sectors," he said.
Stocks are trading more on momentum than on fundamental news, Mr. Heekin added. Whenever a stock
shows signs of strength, investors pile in and drive it upward, trying to get in on the rally. He said he has seen
that kind of momentum-driven buying push up stocks such as Motorola and Scientific Atlanta in recent days.
The Nasdaq Composite Index recorded its highest finish since June, 11 months ago, climbing 1.07%, or
16.48 points, to 1551.38, now up 16% this year and up 22% since March 11. The Standard &Poor's
500-stock index, now trading at its highest level since August, added 0.79%, or 7.39 points, to 946.67. That is
a gain of 7.6% this year and of 18% since March 11.
The day's news was only moderately good for stocks, and traders said shares continued to trade mainly on
hopes and on a desire by money managers to avoid missing the rally.
The wholesale-price decline was viewed as a negative because it raised recession fears. New unemployment
claims came in above 400,000 again last week, suggesting that the economy remains soft, and industrial
production fell again last month, with business capacity use hitting a 20-year low of 74%.
After regular trading hours, Dell Computer announced quarterly earnings that met Wall Street's expectations,
but warned that it still doesn't see signs of a business recovery. That comment could affect trading today, and
Dell's stock declined 2% in after-hours trading.
The dollar rebounded and oil prices declined, but gold continued its recent rebound.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.14%, or 0.15 point, to 108.90.

In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.45 billion shares traded, 2,024 stocks rose and 1,229 fell.
Bonds were mixed. The 10-year Treasury note fell 1/32, or 31.25 cents for each $1,000 invested. The yield,
which moves inversely to price, rose to 3.525%. The 30-year bond was up 17/32 to yield 4.479%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 116.54 yen, up from 116.21, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.1390 from $1.1498.

So, You Wanna Manage a Hedge Fund? Here Are Some Ways to Try It at Home
Getting Going
So, You Wanna Manage a Hedge Fund? Here Are Some Ways to Try It at Home
By Jonathan Clements
982 words
14 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS ARE taking a walk on the wild side.
Forget stocks and bonds. Today, the craze is for gold, hedge funds, real estate and other alternative
investments. My contention: Putting a little money into such investments is probably a smart idea. But there's
no need to venture beyond the comforting world of mutual funds.
If the whole notion of alternative investments has you scratching your head, there is a good reason for that. I
called around, trying to compile a list of alternative investments, and ended up with a blizzard of different
answers.
Experts mentioned venture-capital funds, gold stocks, hedge funds, smaller foreign companies, real-estate
investment trusts, natural-resource companies, emerging-market stocks and bonds, high-yield junk bonds
and even inflation-indexed Treasury bonds.
In fact, the folks I called named pretty much everything except U.S. stocks, high-quality U.S. bonds and
developed foreign-stock markets. And that, I suppose, is the essence of alternative investments. The idea is
to buy stuff that might perform well when regular stocks and bonds are struggling.
"Right now, people are desperate for returns," says William Reichenstein, an investments professor at Baylor
University in Waco, Texas. "They've been burned by stocks and they realize that bond yields are at 40-year
lows. They're looking for something else."
Lately, that something else has been hedge funds, which are often lauded as the ultimate in smart money. To
cash in on that cachet, Wall Street has been rolling out hedge funds with investment minimums of $50,000
and less. Folks, however, typically have to prove they have substantial wealth before they are allowed to
invest.
Chris Cordaro, a financial planner in Chatham, N.J., has put clients into one of these low-minimum hedge
funds. "If we get historical hedge-fund returns, we'll get 8% to 10% a year with very low volatility and no
correlation to the equity markets," he says.
But if returns prove less buoyant, the costs involved will loom large. Mr. Cordaro figures his clients will pay
2.5% or 3% of assets per year for the hedge fund, double the cost of a moderately priced stock fund.
Moreover, investors can buy into the hedge fund only once a month, and they must stay put for at least a
year. Thereafter, investors can get out once a quarter, but they have to give 60 days notice.
Despite these drawbacks, it's a good idea to own investments that perform well when conventional stocks
and bonds are suffering. But you don't have to purchase a hedge fund to get this sort of portfolio protection.
For proof, check out the four mutual funds in the accompanying chart. According to Chicago's Morningstar
Inc., all four have an extremely low correlation with both the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index and the
Lehman Brothers Aggregate Bond Index.
In addition, there's none of the sticker shock associated with hedge funds. You could open accounts at the
four funds for a total investment of just $10,500. All four can be bought without paying a sales commission.
The most costly, the Merger Fund, levies a reasonable 1.34% in annual expenses.
Together, the four funds offer a diverse mix of investment strategies. Merger Fund invests in pending
takeovers, thus giving you exposure to the merger arbitrage that many hedge funds favor. Vanguard Energy
could be your natural-resource play. Vanguard REIT Index would give you exposure to the property market
through its investment in real-estate investment trusts. Finally, American Century Global Gold lets you bet on
the yellow metal, which is famous for faring well when everything else isn't.

--- Page 84 ---
Page 84 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
This basket of four funds offers two other key advantages over hedge funds. First, there is less worry,
because mutual funds are heavily regulated.
Second, unlike hedge funds, you can buy and sell mutual funds every trading day. Why does that matter? If
you own investments that post gains when regular stocks and bonds are suffering, you will enjoy a certain
psychological solace.
But to convert that solace into superior investment returns, you need to rebalance. The idea is to set target
percentages for all of your funds and thereafter regularly rebalance back to your targets, thus cutting back on
the funds that are performing well and adding to those that are suffering. This rebalancing, which would be
much more difficult with a hedge fund, forces you to buy low and sell high and thus tends to boost long-run
returns.
"If you own a precious-metals fund and you don't rebalance, you will get the fund's return, which might be
three percentage points a year above inflation," says William Bernstein, author of "The Four Pillars of
Investing." "But if you do rebalance, you could increase your return by about five percentage points a year
over the fund's return."
What percentage of your portfolio should you stash in alternative assets? I wouldn't put more than 20% of
your stock portfolio in this basket of four funds, with maybe 8% in the REIT fund and the remaining 12%
spread equally among the other three.
"I might even cut the total to 15%, rather than 20%," says Russel Kinnel, Morningstar's director of fund
analysis. "These funds are good to round out your portfolio. But you don't want them to take over your
portfolio."

Blue Chips Top The 8700 Level; Bonds Advance
Monday's Markets
Blue Chips Top The 8700 Level; Bonds Advance
By E.S. Browning
687 words
13 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MORE INVESTORS JUMPED on the stock bandwagon, pushing the Dow Jones Industrial Average through
the 8700 level and sending the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index to its highest finish in more than eight
months.
Traders cited a variety of explanations for the powerful gain, but added that the stock strength surprised them
since it came despite the dollar's slump to a new four-year low against the euro, and despite another jump in
Treasury-bond prices. The yield of the 10-year Treasury bond, which declines when the bond's price rises, fell
to 3.625%, only slightly above the 44-year low of 3.568% at which it traded on March 10.
Money appeared to be flowing both toward stocks and bonds, stimulated by the Federal Reserve's signal last
week that it will keep interest rates low for the foreseeable future. The hope seems to be that the Fed will
create another "Goldilocks" scenario, under which the economy grows and profits improve, but inflation and
interest rates stay low, helping bonds.
That kind of hope helped push the Dow up 122.13 points, or 1.42%, to 8726.73, the first close above 8700
since January. The industrials are now are up 4.6% since the year began.
The S&P 500 broke through the much-watched level of 935, which had served as a sort of upper limit in
recent months. It rose 1.25%, or 11.70 points, to 945.11, its highest finish since Aug. 26 of last year. It is up
7.4% this year.
After pulling money out of stock mutual funds for most of the year, investors have been starting to funnel it
back, and money managers also are moving assets out of money funds and into stocks.
"You are seeing more money flowing into the equity markets," said Matthew Johnson, head of U.S. stock
trading at Lehman Brothers. "When people look out three, six or nine months into the future, they are feeling
more optimistic."
The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many technology stocks have been among the market's strongest this
year, rose 1.40%, or 21.25 points, to 1541.40, its highest level in almost 11 months, since June 18. It is up
15% this year.
The euro is up 12% against the dollar since January -- a trend likely both to hurt and to help stocks. The
falling dollar makes foreigners less inclined to invest in U.S. securities, but it boosts the value of profits that
U.S. multinationals make overseas.
For now, investors are focusing on positives. "As the euro goes up, big stocks with multinational earnings get
the benefit from the weaker dollar," said Taai Izushima, head of U.S. stock trading at Daiwa Securities
America. "That was part of the reason for the advances in the larger stocks," he said, noting that International
Business Machines finished the day near a 52-week high.
Gold rose and oil fell.
Traders said a variety of short-term factors also helped stocks, such as a boost in the rating for networking
giant Cisco Systems by a Lehman Brothers analyst. Cisco rose more than 4%.
Laszlo Birinyi, of research and money-management firm Birinyi Associates, noted that the cumulative number
of advancing stocks has exceeded the number of decliners by more than 7,000 over the past 10 days. In the
past, when stocks' momentum was that strong, they have tended to move higher. However, momentum was
almost as strong in March of last year, and stocks couldn't sustain those gains.
In major U.S. market action:

--- Page 86 ---
Page 86 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Stocks rose. On the NYSE, where 1.37 billion shares traded, 2,307 stocks rose and 989 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 16/32, or $5 for each $1,000 invested, pushing down the yield
to 3.625%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 117.03 yen, down from 117.21, while the euro
rose against the dollar to $1.1543 from $1.1495.

Small-Stock Focus: Brown Shoe, Wild Oats and Tommy Hilfiger Fall
Small-Stock Focus: Brown Shoe, Wild Oats and Tommy Hilfiger Fall
By Cynthia Schreiber
Dow Jones Newswires
875 words
9 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Small-capitalization stocks edged lower as a mixed lot of retail sales data and a poor jobs
report fueled debate about the strength of the U.S. economy.
Market observers said a rise in consumer confidence following the end of war in Iraq may be giving way to
consumer caution.
James Furey, head of small-cap strategy at Lehman Brothers, believes that the market's recovery will take
some time and that the day's economic figures served as triggers to correct the market's recent run-up. "A
consumer and a company does not immediately flip the switch as soon as a war is won," Mr. Furey said.
"This is a gradual recovery."
Mr. Furey said Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan's concerns about deflation and large-cap Cisco
Systems' cautious revenue outlook earlier this week, combined with yesterday's jobs report and retail sales
figures, made it only natural for investors to take some money off the table.
The Russell 2000 index of small-cap stocks fell 2.55, or 0.62%, to 407.68, while the S&P SmallCap 600 shed
1.33, or 0.65%, to 203.62. The Nasdaq Composite Index of large- and small-cap stocks finished the session
down 17.07, or 1.13%, to 1489.69.
A mixed batch of April retail sales reports was a major feature of the trading session with investors keeping a
close eye on earnings outlooks.
Brown Shoe sank 14%, or 4.29, to 27.10 on the NYSE, after the St. Louis retailer said same-store sales fell
5.4% during the first quarter and 1.3% in April, due to cold weather in April that depressed sales. The stock
was also discussed in yesterday's Heard on the Street column.
Men's Wearhouse (NYSE) rose 11%, or 1.69, to 17.68, as the Houston specialty retailer said U.S. same-store
sales grew 6.1% in April, well above the Thomson First Call projection of 1.9%. The company credited its
sales gains, in part, to a calendar shift that put Easter in April this year.
Tommy Hilfiger (NYSE) fell 8.9%, or 77 cents, to 7.85 after the retailer said it expects to post a fiscal
fourth-quarter net loss because of a $150 million charge related to the impairment of goodwill.
Galyan's Trading fell 9.5%, or 1.57, to 14.94, as the Plainfield, Ind., company warned investors that a 6.3%
sales slump during the first quarter and pressure from competition will cause the sporting-goods retailer to
miss first-quarter performance targets.
Natural-foods retailers took a fall after large-cap supermarket Whole Foods Market provided a disappointing
fiscal second-quarter same-store sales report and full-year outlook. Adams Harkness &Hill said industry
growth has slowed and natural-foods stores will likely experience an inventory backup that will weigh on April
and May results. The firm downgraded United Natural Foods to buy from strong buy, which helped send the
shares down 12%, or 3.53, to 25.53.
Meanwhile, supermarket Wild Oats Markets of Boulder, Colo., fell 14%, or 1.46, to 9.19, while organic-milk
distributor Horizon Organic Holding, also of Boulder, fell 2.8%, or 38 cents, to 13.02.
Precious-metals stocks were the best performers of the session as weakness in the U.S. dollar along with
mixed economic data spurred a rally in gold prices. Among small-caps, Randgold &Exploration of
Johannesburg advanced 7.7%, or 92 cents, to 12.82, while Kinross Gold (NYSE) of Toronto jumped 3.3%, or
22 cents, to 6.83.

Internet-services providers were also strong with EarthLink shares up 2.8%, or 17 cents, to 6.25. A federal
judge awarded the Atlanta company $16.4 million and ordered an injunction against Howard Carmack, a
Buffalo, N.Y., resident who allegedly sent millions of e-mails from illegally obtained EarthLink Internet
accounts.
Home-construction stocks edged higher after Credit Suisse First Boston changed its ratings on some
companies based on the strong performance of the group and Mr. Greenspan's comments that interest rates
should remain near historic lows, at least for the short term. Among small-caps, the firm upgraded M.D.C.
Holdings to outperform from neutral, and the shares rose 5.1%, or 2.20, to 44.99, as a result.
But CTI Molecular Imaging fell 17%, or 3.47, to 16.50, after US Bancorp downgraded the stock to market
perform from outperform, based on valuation and its belief that the Knoxville, Tenn., company's 2003 revenue
guidance has softened. Meanwhile, the maker of cancer-detection systems' fiscal second-quarter earnings of
10 cents a share met analysts' average target.
Software provider webMethods lost 7.6%, or 80 cents, to 9.70, after RBC Capital downgraded the stock to
sector perform from outperform.
Omega Healthcare Investors (NYSE) advanced 13%, or 40 cents, to 3.38, after the Timonium, Md.,
nursing-home real-estate investment trust said first-quarter funds from operations rose to 25 cents a share
from 15 cents a share a year earlier.
