Investors Don't Get a Rate Trim -- for Now
Tuesday's Markets
Investors Don't Get a Rate Trim -- for Now
By E.S. Browning
924 words
7 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE FEDERAL RESERVE jolted investors by suggesting it was concerned about deflation, but, after a series
of gyrations, stock indexes finished the day with more gains.
Still, there were clear signs of concern, as bond prices rose sharply and the dollar fell to another four-year low
against the euro.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average swung in a range of about 110 points after the Fed's announcement,
which, as usual, came at 2:15 pm EDT following the Fed's regular interest-rate-policy meeting. The Dow
finished up 56.79 points, or 0.67%, at 8588.36, leaving it with a gain of 3% this year.
For a second consecutive day, trading volume on the Nasdaq Stock Market was the heaviest of the year, this
time hitting 2.11 billion shares, according to WSJ Market Data Group. On the New York Stock Exchange,
1.61 billion shares changed hands, making it one of the 10 heaviest days of the year.
What sparked the concern and the stock moves was the Fed's mention, in its written comments, of the risk of
"an unwelcome substantial fall in inflation."
Translated from Fedspeak, that was taken to mean that monetary policy makers are worried about deflation,
or a decline in prices, which could help push the economy back into recession. The Fed stressed that it sees
the deflation risk as "minor," and it decided to leave its target short-term interest rate unchanged at 1.25%, a
42-year low. But it indicated that it fears deflation more than inflation at this point.
After reading and rereading the Fed comments, many investors concluded that, if the economy doesn't pick
up soon, the Fed will cut its target rate, possibly as soon as late June, when its next interest-rate meeting is
scheduled.
"People were surprised that they brought up deflation, and there was some concern that the Fed may see
something that everybody else doesn't see," said Todd Clark, head of listed trading at Wells Fargo Securities,
a brokerage unit of Wells Fargo &Co. But investors finally concluded that the Fed "is going to be real vigilant
and be sure that rates stay down for a long time," he said.
Another support for stocks was that investors were reluctant to sell ahead of the quarterly earnings
announcement from technology bellwether Cisco Systems. Cisco did deliver better-than-expected sales and
earnings when it announced results after the end of regular trading hours, although it issued guarded
comments about the prospects for an economic recovery. Its stock fell 1.9%, or 31 cents, to $15.59 in
after-hours trading from 4 p.m. trading on the Nasdaq, and the Cisco news could affect trading today.
The main driver for stocks at the moment still appears to be the expectation that, once the effects of the Iraq
war wash through the economy, growth and business activity should improve.
Investors were able to stop worrying about the Fed and start focusing on the Cisco expectations, said Tim
Smalls, senior stock trader at brokerage firm SG Cowen, because Fed leaders have been gradually
suggesting in a series of public pronouncements that they have been working to avoid a Japanese-style
descent into deflation.
"The Fed has been very, very good about getting the message out, through a variety of people on the
public-speaking circuit," Mr. Smalls said. The allusion to the deflation risk in their public statement "caused a
little concern but it wasn't enough to derail" the stock market, he said.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many technology stocks have been leading the market on hopes that
they would benefit from an economic recovery, rose 1.31%, or 19.67 points, to 1523.71, once again hitting its

--- Page 90 ---
Page 90 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
highest level since June 2002. The index now is up 14% since the year began and up 37% since hitting a low
in October.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.85%, or 7.84 points, to 934.39, its highest finish since
November. It is up 6% since the year began.
"Deflation is an unappealing prospect for business profits and investment, and the Fed is anxious to avoid it,"
wrote UBS Warburg economist Maury Harris in a note to clients. But he added, "For our part, we continue to
believe that the economy will regain momentum as geopolitical tensions ebb completely. We expect lower
energy prices, higher federal spending, reduced geopolitical tensions, higher corporate profits and a likely tax
cut to all underpin growth over the balance of the year."
At least for now, that is the view that is carrying the day among stock investors.
Oil prices fell, but, with the dollar continuing to decline, gold prices rose.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 2.19%, or 2.34 points, to 109.09.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, 2,188 stocks rose and 1,062 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 21/32, or $6.5625 per $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, fell to 3.804%. The 30-year bond was up 18/32 to yield 4.770%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 117.52 yen, down from 118.67, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.1437 from $1.1282.

Wednesday's Markets
Blue Chips, Other Indexes Slump for Day, but Gain for April

C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average slumped below the 8500 level yet again, but it and other major indexes
still finished April with their biggest monthly gains this year.
In heavy end-of-month trading, the Dow industrials slipped 22.90 points, or 0.27%, to 8480.09, up 6.1% for
April and up 1.7% since the year began. It was their biggest monthly gain since October.
Stocks had bounced up and down throughout the day following a soft reading on manufacturing activity in the
Chicago area and an inconclusive comment on the economy from Federal Reserve Chairman Alan
Greenspan.
Speculation that the Fed might cut target interest rates as soon as next week to stimulate the economy
helped push Treasury bond prices up sharply. Stocks appeared poised for a small gain until a surge of
month-end profit-taking knocked them down just before regular trading ended.
"You would expect to see some people step in and make some sales. A lot of these stocks are up 25%, 35%,
40% since the beginning of March," said Robert Harrington, head of listed trading at brokerage firm UBS
Warburg.
Gold and oil prices rose, and the dollar fell.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.1%, or 0.92 points, to 916.92, up 8.1% in April and 4.2% since
the year began. The Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.48%, or 6.99 points, to 1464.31, up 9.2% in April and
9.6% on the year.

moves inversely to price, fell to 3.846%. The 30-year bond was up 1 3/32 to yield 4.769%.

Small-Stock Focus: Martha Stewart Falls While BearingPoint Gains
Small-Stock Focus: Martha Stewart Falls While BearingPoint Gains
By Cheryl Winokur Munk
Dow Jones Newswires
872 words
1 May 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C10
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Small-cap stocks got a slight lift but showed little conviction after Federal Reserve Chairman
Alan Greenspan's mixed review of the economy and a weaker-than-expected regional manufacturing report.
So where do we go from here?
Mellon Financial's private-wealth management group, which has been overweight on small-capitalization
stocks since early 1999, is changing its stance to neutral, said Chris Sheldon, director of investment strategy.
Although he believes small-cap stocks will continue to perform well over the long-term, he thinks large-caps
are now looking more attractive.
"The last cycle of small-cap stock outperformance ended with a Fed tightening. We feel that if rates are not at
their bottom, they're near that," he said. In addition, small-caps tend to underperform when the dollar is weak
since that tends to help the profits of larger companies more, he said.
The Russell 2000 Index of small stocks yesterday pushed up 2.90 points, or 0.73%, to 398.68, while the
Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600 Index gained 1.52, or 0.77%, to 199.65. The Nasdaq Composite Index of
large and small stocks shed 6.99, or 0.48%, to 1464.31.
Earnings continued to dominate trading. Martha Stewart Living Omnimedia (NYSE) fell $1.11, or 11%, to
$8.68 after the New York entertainment and media company reported a wider loss and 15% lower revenue
for its first quarter. The company was hurt in part by the government's investigation into alleged insider
trading in the sale of ImClone Systems' stock by its namesake chairman and chief executive; she has denied
wrongdoing.
Shares of ImClone also lost ground following news that its top two officials resigned from the embattled
pharmaceutical company amid a review of its tax payments. The stock fell 35 cents, or 1.9%, to 18.28.
BearingPoint gained 87 cents, or 12%, to 8.17 after the consulting and technology-services company, the
former KPMG Consulting, reported lower fiscal third-quarter profit but topped Wall Street's expectations.
Precious metals were among the biggest gainers, with Stillwater Mining (NYSE) up 17 cents, or 6.7%, to 2.70
and Meridian Gold (NYSE) up 16 cents, or 1.6%, to 10.18.
Full-line insurers posted gains, with small-cap Horace Mann Educators (NYSE) up nine cents, or 0.6%, to
14.77.
Wireless companies, however, had a rough day, pulled down by large-cap Nextel Communications, which
Deutsche Bank downgraded to sell from hold. Among small-caps, Nextel Partners, an affiliate of Nextel
Communications, slid 30 cents, or 4.9%, to 5.81, after reporting a wider-than-expected first-quarter loss.
Chip companies slipped, with small-cap Siliconix of Santa Clara, Calif., down 1.94, or 6.4%, to 28.25. The
company said it sees no signs of a major recovery in its primary markets, portable computers and cellular
telephones, and doesn't expect any improvement until the second half at the earliest.

The company reported first-quarter net income of nine cents a share, compared with a loss of $1.15 a share a

Stock Investors Gain Confidence --- Dow Industrials Surge 156.09, S&P Hits a Three-Month High; Earnings and Greenspan Help
Tuesday's Markets
Stock Investors Gain Confidence --- Dow Industrials Surge 156.09, S&P Hits a Three-Month High;
Earnings and Greenspan Help
By E.S. Browning
886 words
23 April 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS SURGED AHEAD again, breaking through a widely watched barrier amid hopes that the current
rally could continue, at least in the short term.
Traders said investors are gradually gaining confidence in stocks again, moving money back toward
stock-focused mutual funds after repeated fund withdrawals earlier this year. The shift toward stocks is a
response to several recent positive news developments, they said, including the end to the war in Iraq and a
series of relatively favorable earnings reports.
A comment from President Bush that Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan should have another term
also pleased investors. Although the major indexes fell immediately following the president's comment,
traders said the news probably contributed to the later market gains.
"Money is flowing back into the stock market," said Matthew Johnson, head of U.S. stock trading at New York
brokerage firm Lehman Brothers. "We are seeing stock buyers on our trading desk," he said, adding that the
buyers include traditional long-term investors such as mutual funds and pension funds, and aren't just
short-term traders.
The day's big news was that the broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, widely watched by money
managers, finally broke above the highs it reached late in March, shortly after the Iraq war began.
The S&P 500 rose 2.17%, or 19.36 points, to 911.37, its highest level in three months and first finish above
900 since January. It is up 3.6% since the year began. Financial stocks led the gains, boosted by
better-than-expected earnings news from reinsurance company Everest Re, which raised its earnings
projections for the year.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 156.09, or 1.87%, to 8484.99. It is up 1.7% this year, but, unlike the
S&P 500, hasn't yet broken above its March high.
Reflecting the increased investor interest, trading volume on the New York Stock Exchange hit 1.64 billion
shares, the heaviest trading day in a month and the fifth strongest of the year.
Technology stocks also were among the day's leaders, and the Nasdaq Composite Index rose 1.89%, or
26.99 points, to 1451.36. Tech stocks have been leading the market since late last year, and the Nasdaq
composite, dominated by big tech stocks, now is up 8.7% since the year began.
How long the rally can continue has become a major subject of debate on Wall Street. Many investors had
expected a postwar surge, especially since earnings reports, while still not strong, haven't been as weak as
many had feared. Whether this rally will be a short burst or a longer, more-gradual upswing is what many
professional investors are asking each other.
Behind the recent market strength is a bet that, with the war's end, the economy and corporate profits now
can recover. That helps explain the strength of technology and financial stocks, as well as certain industrial
stocks. Investors are hoping that such stocks will benefit from an economic recovery, in part because a better
business environment and continuing low interest rates could help to wipe away some of the credit problems
left behind by the bubble of the 1990s.
Though stock prices rose, gold prices rose again, indicating continued investor nervousness. Treasury bonds
fell, as did oil prices and the dollar.
With major indexes now up since the start of the year, even some money managers who remain skeptical
about the rally are being forced to jump into the market, to avoid being left behind.

--- Page 95 ---
Page 95 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"People are wondering: `How am I positioned? If we get a move in the market, how am I represented?' " said
Andy Brooks, head of stock trading at Baltimore mutual-fund group T. Rowe Price. He said the money moving
into mutual funds, together with the sight of the S&P 500 breaking through the 900 barrier, has impressed
some people and pulled them in. So have the low yields on high-quality bonds and on money-market funds,
which have been the alternative investments.
In addition, short sellers, investors who bet on stock declines, are being forced to cover their positions, which
has helped boost stocks. Short sellers borrow stocks and sell them, hoping to buy them back cheaper after a
decline. When stocks rally, many short sellers decide to cover positions by buying back the stocks back and
returning them to the lenders, which helps fuel rallies.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.67%, or 0.69 point, to 103.23.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, 2,436 stocks rose and 873 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 2/32, or 62.5 cents for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 3.990%. The 30-year bond was down 3/32 to yield 4.905%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 119.96 yen, down from 120.50, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.0972 from $1.0868.

In Vietnam, a New Passion for Property --- Prices Spiral as Faith Grows In Move Toward Capitalism; Few Investment Alternatives
International
In Vietnam, a New Passion for Property --- Prices Spiral as Faith Grows In Move Toward Capitalism;
Few Investment Alternatives
By Barry Wain
903 words
23 April 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A20
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
HANOI, Vietnam -- Check out one of the world's most expensive pieces of real estate: a clothes shop here
that is just over seven feet wide and not much deeper. It has bare concrete walls; the only furniture is a
couple of rough wooden floor planks stacked with bundles of unopened clothing. Two bored sales clerks, their
plastic footwear discarded, pick idly at their toes.
A Vietnamese buyer recently plunked down the equivalent of $179,000 -- almost $24,000 per square meter --
for this 7.5-square-meter gem. For his money, he got a narrow shop front on Hang Dao Street, similar to
hundreds of others in this low-rise, French colonial-era neighborhood. Prospects for redeveloping the site are
small.
While this is an extreme example of a new land fever gripping communist Vietnam, it isn't exceptional. In a
two-year frenzy, Vietnamese here and in Ho Chi Minh City have driven prices up to -- and, in some cases,
beyond -- those paid in London, New York and Tokyo. A square meter of prime residential property is
currently selling for as much as $8,700 in Ho Chi Minh City and almost $24,000 in Hanoi -- compared with
$15,000 in London and nearly $11,000 in Tokyo.
"It's crazy," says 48-year-old Tran Dang Luu, who is fighting ever-rising prices to redevelop his home in
Hanoi's Old Quarter into a minihotel.
This land grab is perhaps one of the strongest signs yet that many Vietnamese believe the capitalist-style
policies introduced piecemeal over the past 15 years are here to stay. Eager for a piece of the action, they
are pulling their savings out of mattresses and plowing them into the safest investment they know: private
property.
Few buyers seem deterred by the fact that Vietnam is run by a Communist Party with a history of hostility
toward land ownership. According to the law, only the use of land can be traded, and that itself is a fairly new
policy. While Vietnamese say they own their homes, they actually hold a government-issued land-use permit
for the plot on which the home stands.
Cash is king when Vietnamese find their castle. Typically, a buyer hands a seller several bags stuffed with
dollars, gold or -- less likely -- dong, the long-despised local currency, which currently changes hands at the
rate of 15,400 to a dollar.
Getting a grip on this property market isn't easy, even for the government. There is no central register where
transactions are made public. Numbers are frequently fudged to avoid the taxman. Sales are tracked
informally by real-estate agents and a few nosy neighbors.
The obvious question: Where is the money coming from? After all, Vietnam's per-capita income is just $438 a
year, although that figure is skewed by the millions of rural poor. Middle-level government employees get a
base salary of $50 a month.
Corruption is a vital source, say property agents and others. "They want to launder their money, so they
invest in land and houses," says Nguyen Quang Hung, director of Trung Thuc Real Estate Co. in Hanoi.
"They want to buy at any cost, so they even pay from two to five times higher than the actual value or price."
This explanation is common currency: The government-owned Vietnam Economic Times reported that a
"legion" of bureaucrats have joined the stampede to snap up apartments and houses for $100,000 or more.
But there's also a burgeoning middle class making legitimate profits from light-industrial exports, a thriving
tourist trade, and numerous small domestic businesses. An estimated $3 billion sent home annually by family
members living abroad also gives many urban Vietnamese real spending power.

--- Page 97 ---
Page 97 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
People pile into property largely because they lack investment alternatives, say economists. Vietnamese
distrust banks and the dong, a result of periodic bouts of high inflation and steep devaluations that have
wiped out savings in the past. The country's fledgling stock market remains tiny and in the tank, while
government bonds are just as unattractive. "And there is a belief that what goes up, doesn't come down,"
says Brett Ashton, the Ho Chi Minh City director for international property consultants Chesterton Petty
Vietnam.
That has certainly been the case since the government cut red tape in 2001 to encourage home purchases,
triggering the surge. Prices on average have doubled in Ho Chi Minh City and quadrupled in Hanoi, although
those gains have also been propelled in part by the rising price of gold in which Vietnamese real estate is
quoted.
The government has spent much of the past eight months trying to curb what it regards as rampant
speculation that has put housing beyond the reach of many. One measure has been to stop Vietnamese from
buying agricultural land and converting it to residential blocks. Prices have leveled off recently, prompting
officials to claim victory. They argue that strong underlying demand for housing means the boom doesn't
amount to a bubble and is therefore unlikely to burst.
Says Tran The Ngoc, first deputy director of the Land-Housing Department in Ho Chi Minh City, "I don't think
prices will go down, because we have stopped the fever in time."

Putting Junk In Your Portfolio --- To Juice Returns, Investors Pump Record Amounts Into High-Yield Bonds; Copying Warren Buffett
Putting Junk In Your Portfolio --- To Juice Returns, Investors Pump Record Amounts Into High-Yield
Bonds; Copying Warren Buffett
By Tom Herman and Jeff D. Opdyke
1,174 words
17 April 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MORE INVESTORS are trying to turn junk into gold.
With the stock market still volatile and interest rates at rock-bottom levels, Americans searching for higher
returns have piled into so-called high-yield corporate bonds -- better known as junk bonds.
Because they carry low credit ratings, junk bonds offer much higher interest payments than Treasurys and
corporate bonds. Indeed, a benchmark junk-bond index compiled by Merrill Lynch now has a yield of around
10.5%. That compares with a yield of less than 4% on a 10-year Treasury note.
Yields on some individual bonds can be eye-popping. While most issues in the Merrill index yield between 7%
and 15%, some distressed bonds are trading well above 20%.
Now is a particularly prime time to own junk bonds -- not just because of their higher yields, but because the
underlying fundamentals have the wind at their back. Unlike regular bonds, junk-bond prices tend to rise
along with the stock market as corporate balance sheets strengthen. Junk is so enticing these days that even
famed investor Warren Buffett noted in his latest letter to shareholders that he made "sensible investments in
a few `junk' bonds last year."
Junk bonds already have begun to climb. While the Dow Jones Industrial Average is down about 1% this
year, mutual funds specializing in junk bonds have gained nearly 7.5% through April 15, outpacing nearly all
other types of funds this year, according to Morningstar Inc. That helps explain why investors pumped a
record $10.5 billion into junk-bond mutual funds in the first quarter, nearly double the year-ago amount, says
AMG Data Services.
Junk bonds have been around since the early 1900s, though most folks generally associate them with the
1980s, leveraged buyouts and the king of junk bonds, Michael Milken. But the junk-bond market today is far
larger than it was during the Milken era.
Of course, junk bonds are called junk for a reason. Along with high yields, they carry a higher risk. Indeed, as
the economy sputtered last year and companies struggled, 21% of the world's junk bonds were in default, on
a dollar-volume basis, according to Moody's Investor Services.
Even junk bonds that don't slip into default see their prices drop when investors see a rocky landscape
ahead. Thus, if the economy lapses back into a funk, "junk will feel the pressure" because company earnings
likely will weaken once again, says Carolyn Gibbs, senior portfolio manager for the AIM High-Yield fund, a
junk-bond mutual fund that's up more than 9% this year.
What's more, junk bonds don't make sense for individuals with low risk tolerance. "I'm just not comfortable"
taking the risk, says New York investor Douglas E. Schoen. "I'm totally risk-phobic."
Still, in the right conditions, junk bonds can post big gains -- while kicking off fat interest payments along the
way. In 1991, a Merrill Lynch high-yield index soared 39%.
With today's economy expected to improve, "this is a good time to buy selected high-yield bonds," says David
M. Darst, a managing director of Morgan Stanley and the author of several books on bonds and asset
allocation. He says the current spreads between junk-bond yields and Treasury yields are "generous" by
historical standards, even though they have narrowed some since last year.
Such logic has made believers out of investors like Les Metz, a 74-year-old retiree in Minneapolis. He has a
portion of his portfolio in the American Funds American High Income Trust, a junk-bond fund up more than
9% this year. Mr. Metz says his fund "seems to be doing just fine, and I expect it will get better when the
economy turns up."

--- Page 99 ---
Page 99 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
That doesn't mean you should fill your portfolio with junk. Because of their volatility, high-yield bonds should
be used as a small part of a fixed-income portfolio. Junk bonds "can turn against you very quickly," says Marc
Collier, a certified financial planner in Wellesley, Mass., who began moving some of his clients into junk in
January. He is putting less than 20% of their bond allocations there.
It's generally a good idea to steer clear of owning individual junk bonds, since most investors haven't the time
or inclination to perform the necessary credit-risk analysis. Instead, stick to junk-bond mutual funds. These
funds own scores of different issues, meaning you won't be wiped out if a single issue defaults. And most
have investment minimums of $2,500 or less. By contrast, buying individual bonds would require tens of
thousands of dollars to buy enough bonds to get proper diversification.
When picking funds, don't just focus on the latest winners. Instead, look at the track record of a fund to
analyze performance during both good and bad economic times. Over five years, for example, the best
performing high-yield funds, are the Pioneer High Yield A and Neuberger Berman High Income Bond funds,
Morningstar says.
Remember, though, some funds own junkier bonds than others, and that performance and volatility can vary
widely from fund to fund. Investors also should keep tax considerations in mind. Investors in high income tax
brackets should consider investing in junk bonds through tax-favored retirement accounts, such as 401(k)
plans or individual retirement accounts.
You can get a fix on the investment style of a junk-bond mutual fund by going on the Morningstar Web site,
www.morningstar.com. It provides a composite look at the credit ratings of the junk bonds held in various
funds. The junk-bond market includes bonds with a wide variety of credit ratings. The highest-rated
speculative-grade issues carry a BB+ at Standard &Poor's and Ba1 at Moody's Investors Service.
---
Some ways to avoid getting trashed by junk bonds.
-- Don't buy individual bonds. Instead, buy mutual funds that own a
basket of different junk bonds.
-- Because of their volatility and higher risk, consider putting less
than 20% of your bond portfolio in junk.
-- Watch your portfolio like a hawk. If the economy softens, junk
prices could fall quickly as concerns of defaults rise.
---

Iraq After Hussein: Dollars Are Sent to Iraq to Replace Dinars --- Stopgap Move Is Intended For Emergency Payments In Effort to Quiet Unrest
Iraq After Hussein: Dollars Are Sent to Iraq to Replace Dinars --- Stopgap Move Is Intended For
Emergency Payments In Effort to Quiet Unrest
By Bob Davis in Washington, Chip Cummins in Kuwait City and Simeon Kerr in Basra, Iraq
1,134 words
16 April 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A8
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
As the U.S. turns from bombing Iraq to rebuilding it, the U.S. government is airlifting dollars from the Federal
Reserve Bank of New York to replace -- at least temporarily -- the discredited Iraqi dinar.
As an initial step, American officials charged with the reconstruction will use small-denomination bills to make
"emergency" payments to hundreds of thousands of Iraqi civil servants in an effort to quiet civic unrest and to
stabilize the chaotic Iraqi economy. Using U.S. dollars will make the U.S. currency the de facto currency, at
least in the interim -- a move that could prove controversial in the Arab world, but one that would give the
Iraqis a currency that will retain its value despite the uncertainties about the country's reconstruction.
American officials are working with finance- and postal-ministry officials from Saddam Hussein's government
to figure out appropriate wages, U.S. officials said. Under the Hussein regime, the central government tightly
controlled wages and prices. Setting wages too high could worsen inflation that is already running at about
70% annually.
At first, the Iraqi workers will be given $20 each -- in $1 and $5 bills. That is a large sum in Iraq, where a
midlevel oil professional with a chemical degree used to make the equivalent of $50 a month in central and
southern Iraq.
Funds for the payments will come from the $1.7 billion in Iraqi assets that the U.S. recently confiscated.
The dollar payments are occurring as the U.S. made its first step at putting together a postwar government.
Dozens of representatives from Iraqi factions, including a number of exiles, met near Ur -- the birthplace of
the biblical prophet Abraham -- to begin discussions on the shape of a new government. The group released
a 13-point statement saying that the new Iraq should be democratic and the rule of law must be respected.
The U.S. has "no interest, absolutely no interest, in ruling Iraq," White House envoy Zalmay Khalizad told the
group, which met under a gold-colored tent on one of Mr. Hussein's former military bases in southern Iraq.
Several Iraqi groups boycotted the session to express their opposition to a U.S. interim authority led by retired
Gen. Jay Garner.
Similar meetings are going to be held in other parts of the country, with the goal of choosing 40 members of
an interim advisory panel that will write a new constitution, among other duties.
Regarding the dollar, U.S. planners stress they aren't making a formal decision about what currency Iraq
should adopt. "The Iraqi people will choose their currency," a U.S. Treasury official says. That choice is one
of the most significant economic decisions a new regime can make. The bills become one of the most visible
symbols of the government, and foreign investors watch how well a government manages its currency before
making big investments in the country.
To be sure, using the American currency extensively in Iraq, even temporarily, could have political
repercussions in the Arab world, where there already is widespread fear the U.S. has wanted from the start to
dominate a postwar Iraq.
British forces also are using dollars to pay dock workers in Umm Qasr and maintenance workers in Basra
who are starting to clean up looted buildings. "You've got to put some kind of stable buying power in people's
hands," said an official with the Pentagon's office of reconstruction and humanitarian assistance, which is
overseeing Iraqi reconstruction.
Using dollars now is a stop-gap measure brought about by the looting of Iraqi banks and by large-scale
counterfeiting.

--- Page 101 ---
Page 101 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Iraq now has two currencies. A pre-Hussein dinar, nicknamed the Swiss dinar because of Switzerland's
reputation for financial probity, circulates in the northern Iraqi regions under Kurdish control. The rest of the
country uses so-called Saddam dinars, which have Mr. Hussein's portrait. Looters and counterfeiters are
dealing in Saddam dinars, causing the currency to fluctuate wildly in value.
Before the war, Saddam dinars traded at about 2,500 to the dollar. A few days ago, as looters emptied banks
of dinars, the value of some large dinar bills plunged 84% to as low as 16,000 to the dollar. Yesterday,
smaller-denomination Saddam dinars were trading at around 2,800 to the dollar in Basra, as the looting
appears to have subsided.
Even before the looting began, currency traders figured the Saddam dinar would be scrapped because it
bears Mr. Hussein's visage. "This is money of Saddam Hussein," said Salim Abu Hisham, a money changer
at the Qadhara marketplace in Basra, as he toyed with the dinars. "We know it has to go."
U.S. officials expect to start making payments in dollars later this week. They figure that local Iraqis will make
change for the bills in either Saddam dinars or other Persian Gulf currencies. In many cases, payments will
come from military civil-affairs officers, who have been working with local Iraqis.
U.S. Treasury and banking officials also are trying to figure out how to get the banking system working again,
so money can be transferred electronically around the country. In Kosovo, that task took months and led to
widespread dissatisfaction, as local merchants couldn't do business outside their area, and civil servants went
unpaid.
U.S. Treasury officials will work closely with any new Iraqi government on its currency choice. In Afghanistan,
the U.S. urged the government to use dollars for larger-scale government transactions as a way to stabilize
the economy and reassure donors that Kabul would act responsibly. But the new government turned down
the recommendation, and moved quickly to print a new currency as a symbol of national unity.
Producing a new currency could be difficult for a new Baghdad regime, which would have to trade in Saddam
dinars and Swiss dinars. U.S. officials figure that a new Iraqi government would start making payments in
dollars and then would have to decide on whether to link its currency tightly to the dollar, if not use the dollar
outright. After bitter wars, Kosovo and Bosnia largely adopted first the German mark, then the euro.
---
From Dinars to Dollars
With widespread looting and counterfeiting of the local Iraqi
currency, the U.S. plans to pay Iraqi civil servants in dollars.
-- Amount of emergency payment: $20
-- Estimated number of civil servants in Iraq: 1.5 million-2.5 million
-- Approximate salary for a midlevel oil engineer: $50 per month
Sources: Department of Defense; Banknotes.com

Leading the News: Divisions on Rebuilding Iraq May Slow Process --- Paris, Berlin, Moscow Get `Observer' Status in Talks; Coalition Partners...
Leading the News: Divisions on Rebuilding Iraq May Slow Process --- Paris, Berlin, Moscow Get
`Observer' Status in Talks; Coalition Partners Welcomed
By Bob Davis in Washington, Alan Cullison in Moscow and Farnaz Fassihi in Kirkuk, Iraq
1,587 words
14 April 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The reconstruction of Iraq moved to center stage in world politics, with the U.S. inviting its coalition partners
into dialogue on the subject but pointedly excluding the countries that objected to and still resent the U.S.
invasion.
President Bush invited Poland and Australia, which contributed troops to the war effort, to participate. No
such invitation went to Russia, France and Germany, which held their own dialogue over the weekend in St.
Petersburg, Russia. The U.S. says they can attend future meetings on rebuilding Iraq, to be hosted by Gen.
Tommy Franks, as "observers."
The widening trans-Atlantic schism over how to rebuild Iraq may make the nation-building process more
complicated. And there is much to be done: In Iraq, looters continued to rampage -- albeit at a diminished rate
-- yesterday storming the Al-Salam Presidential Palace on the western bank of the Tigris River in Baghdad.
Schools remained closed, and the capital remained without electricity or running water.
About the only common international agenda on Iraq emerged in Washington, where finance ministers with
the Group of Seven industrialized nations agreed that the International Monetary Fund and World Bank
should start technical assessment of the scope of the Iraq rebuilding efforts. The G-7 includes the U.S.,
Britain, Canada, France, Germany, Japan and Italy.
Even there, though, discord followed. U.S. Treasury Secretary John Snow issued a statement that omitted
discussion of a role for the United Nations, even though the official G-7 communique stressed the need for "a
further U.N. Security Council resolution." Mr. Snow said that the G-7 wording didn't represent a new point of
view or compromise by the U.S., but other countries pointedly seized on the importance of further U.N. action.
Deeper splits emerged among the financiers on a far more substantive issue, too: what to do about Iraq's
gargantuan foreign debt. Germany and Russia's finance ministers made clear they have no intention of
signing on to a U.S. proposal to consider forgiving Baghdad's debt. "No one has forgiven Russia's debt,
regardless of what kind of regime it was and regardless of the country's clout," Russian Finance Minister
Alexie Kudrin told Russian television. Germany's central bank president, Ernst Welteke, noted that Iraq is a
potentially rich nation because of its oil resources. Debt cancellation, he added, pointedly, is "only for very
poor countries."
On the ground in Iraq, such diplomatic wrangling seemed very far away. The U.S. Army Third Infantry
Division's Capt. Eric Murray, surveying the tatters around him about 40 miles south of Baghdad, urged his
comrades to go easier on Iraqi prisoners of war. "We're going to have to rebuild this country, and to convince
these people that we're not worse than Saddam Hussein," said the captain, who works in civil affairs. "I'm
keenly aware that the more destructive we are on this end, the more work there will be for me later on
reconstruction."
A few miles away in Nasiriyah, several dozen Iraqi teachers gathered by the looted building of the education
department, waiting for someone to tell them what and when they could teach -- and where. Iraqi schools
were closed before the hostilities, and many of them were turned into military depots.
In Kirkuk yesterday, a dozen soldiers from the U.S.'s 173rd Airborne Brigade guarded the governor's office,
vacated by the Kurdish peshmerga, or guerrilla fighters. In a careful attempt to appear neutral in the volatile
area, the American soldiers covered a Kurdish political unity sign. But it was clear to everyone who would be
in charge. The minister of peshmerga, Shirdel Hawazi, said he ordered his fighters to withdraw to
Sulaimaniyeh. "There's nothing to worry about," he said. "In a few days, the Americans will be the ones fully
in control."

--- Page 103 ---
Page 103 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
That's exactly what Russia, France and Germany are trying to avoid. At their meeting in St. Petersburg over
the weekend, the leaders of the three countries that led the anti-war coalition reiterated that they expect the
rebuilding of Iraq to be entrusted to the U.N.
"The task of restoring the political, economic and social system of Iraq is enormous," French President
Jacques Chirac told a news conference. "Only the [U.N.] has the legitimacy to do that."
Vladimir Putin issued the strongest criticism of U.S. maneuvers. The Russian president said the war had
undermined the U.N. and international law as a whole, and pointed out the U.S. failed to find weapons of
mass destruction in Iraq, which he said was the only possible justification for the conflict. "Even in the most
acute moment of the fight for its survival, the Iraqi regime did not use such means [weapons of mass
destruction]," Mr. Putin said. "If in the last moment of its existence, it did not use them, it means they do not
exist. Or they were in such condition they could not be used. That raises the question of the expediency of
the whole action."
Mr. Putin said reconstruction in Iraq should follow the model established in Afghanistan, which was supported
by Russia: First there should be an international conference organized by the U.N., then an interim
administration responsible for preparing elections, and finally the elections themselves.
The Russian leader's continued criticism is likely a sign of heightened anxiety in Moscow that the U.S.,
emboldened by its success in Iraq, could go on a binge of regime-toppling in order to install governments
more friendly to the West. Mr. Putin, who dominated a news conference held by the leaders, cautioned
against what he called "the export of capitalist, democratic revolution."
At the G-7 and IMF/World Bank meetings in Washington, the plan was to show that on economic matters, at
least, the U.S. and its Iraq-war adversaries could work together. "The IMF and World Bank will assist, under
the auspices of the U.N., so that Iraq can return to the international community," French Finance Minister
Francis Mer told reporters.
The fight over Iraq's debt soon will take center stage. Iraq may be the world's most heavily indebted nation,
compared with the size of its economy. Paying off that debt could cripple the country for years to come. The
Saddam Hussein regime simply ignored the debt, but a new government looking to establish commercial
credit arrangements around the world would be under immense pressure to pay off Baghdad's obligations.
Estimates of its foreign debt range from $60 billion to more than $130 billion. On top of that, Iraq owes $27
billion in war reparations from the first Persian Gulf War, while an additional $200 billion has yet to be
adjudicated by a U.N. commission set up to resolve Gulf War claims. Iran also may assert $100 billion in
claims against Baghdad dating from the nine-year Iran-Iraq war. Tehran hasn't made such claims yet
because it was clear that Iraq wouldn't make payment.
The G-7 agreed only that "it is important to address the debt issue" and said it was "looking forward to the
early engagement of the Paris Club." An IMF communique later echoed that language. The Paris Club is an
informal arrangement of creditor countries, which restructures government debt. Such restructuring may
entail stretching out the time for payments, deferring some payments, or reducing interest charges -- but not
cancelling debt.
The Russian and German positions are colored by the burdens placed on Moscow after the collapse of the
Soviet Union in 1991. Russia agreed to accept all of the Soviet Union's $80 billion or so in debt, and also its
assets -- Soviet embassies, gold stocks and loans made to other nations. Paris Club members restructured
the Soviet debt and Russia is still making payments on it.
Iraq owes Russia $12 billion, according to the Center for Strategic and International Studies, without
accounting for interest. And Moscow has been a very tough bargainer with debtors. Ernesto Hernandez-Cata,
a former senior IMF official, said Russia would insist on repayment from Ethiopia, Nicaragua and other
impoverished nations whose previous government had run up heavy debts to the Soviet Union. Sometimes,
Moscow would use the IMF in those efforts. "The Ethiopians would say, `It's disgusting; this was money given
to the old regime to kill us.' " Mr. Hernandez-Cata said. "We had to tell the Ethiopians that you've got to
negotiate with Russia" or the IMF couldn't approve loans to Ethiopia.
In one measure of the bizarreness of Iraq's financial situation, the U.N. last week approved payment of $864
million for war claims from the first Gulf War, paid for by earlier sales of Iraqi petroleum. The payments came
as the Saddam Hussein government was collapsing. Under the U.N.'s oil-for-food program, 25% of the
revenue from Iraqi petroleum sales made under the U.N. program are paid out in compensation for the Iraqi
invasion of Kuwait. More than half of the recent payment is earmarked for the Kuwaiti government.
---
Ken Gepfert in Washington contributed to this article.

Iraq News Isn't Enough: Stocks Fall 100.98 --- Investors Remain Squeamish But Reasons for Holding Back Have More to Do With Economy
Wednesday's Markets
Iraq News Isn't Enough: Stocks Fall 100.98 --- Investors Remain Squeamish But Reasons for Holding
Back Have More to Do With Economy
By E.S. Browning
1,017 words
10 April 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IRAQIS WERE DANCING in the streets of Baghdad, but U.S. investors, who had long been expected to join
in the celebration, continued to hold back, sending stocks to a 100.98-point decline.
Back when the war news seemed troubling, investors used that to justify their reluctance to buy stocks. Now
that the news is better, investors remain reluctant to buy -- and they are citing worries about the economy.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average did rally more than 700 points, or 10%, in the days just before the war --
and those gains remain. But since March 19, when the bombing began, the Dow industrials have made no
lasting progress, going from 8265.45 on the afternoon before the bombing began, to 8197.94 yesterday.
The contrast between the success of the war and the dour mood of the market was particularly sharp
yesterday, with television reports showing Iraqi civilians cheering as U.S. forces pulled down a huge statue of
Saddam Hussein in the middle of Baghdad. Defense Secretary Donald Rumsfeld called the scene in
Baghdad "breathtaking" and compared it to the fall of the Berlin Wall.
At the New York Stock Exchange, rapt traders watching the action briefly bid the Dow industrials up almost
90 points. And then the selling began.
As Iraqis danced on the remnants of Saddam's statue, the Dow industrials pulled back, finishing the day
down 1.22%. Other indexes fell even more, while refuge investments such as gold, Treasury bonds and oil
all advanced.
"It feels really good to have tanks rumbling through Baghdad," said Matthew Forester, a portfolio manager at
money-management firm Cumberland Advisors in Vineland, N.J. "But it would only take a little bad news on
the war to knock the market back down. There is so much anti-Americanism around the world."
Aside from the Middle East, Mr. Forester and his colleagues say they are focused on a potential housing
bubble, rising unemployment and a slow economy that the Federal Reserve appears to be having trouble
reviving.
In a strategy session, their conversation shifted away from the war, as they found themselves wondering
when the firm should start selling stocks and taking some of the paper profits it made by investing when
stocks were lower.
That kind of thought process is what is holding the market back, and it is hardly the picture that stock analysts
were painting just a few weeks ago. The expectation then was that if the war progressed quickly and
successfully, stocks would soar 20% or more in a short period. The military and the politicians delivered just
about everything the markets hoped for: speedy victories, relatively light military and civilian casualties, no
apparent blowups in the Middle East and no major terror attacks in the U.S. or elsewhere.
But so far, the big rally simply hasn't happened. From March 11 through March 19, in anticipation of a swift
war, the Dow industrials rose 10%. It has gone a little higher and a little lower in the days that followed, but
that brief rally, so far at least, has turned out to be it.
One reason: After three years of thudding market declines, ordinary investors remain frightened of stocks.
"The public isn't likely to move a substantial amount of funds back into equities until after stocks have been
doing well for many months," said Phil Roth, chief technical market analyst at New York brokerage firm Miller
Tabak, in a report to clients.

--- Page 106 ---
Page 106 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Richard Nash, chief investment strategist at Victory Capital Management, KeyCorp's money-management
arm in Cleveland, notes that after removing $371 million from domestic-stock funds in January, investors
further reduced their holdings in equity mutual funds by $11.1 billion in February. Instead, they shoveled
$32.6 billion into what they saw as the safety of bond funds in those two months, he said.
"The urge to move money into the perceived safety of fixed income may be a strong one for investors after
three years of disappointment in the equity markets," Mr. Nash said in a report. He thinks investors could be
making a big mistake, since bond prices may have peaked and yields are tiny.
Stocks still could rally belatedly, of course. But the guarded response so far indicates how skeptical many
investors remain. That skepticism could prove to be a hurdle for rallies not only now, but in the future as well.
The deepest worry is that the economy could be in for a rough period and that corporate earnings may not
turn upward as strongly as analysts hope in the year's second half.
Optimists, including Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan, have offered investors reassurance that the economy's
problems are due to short-term war worries -- suggesting that things should get better once the fighting ends.
But so far, the economic news remains gloomy. The most immediate cause of hand-wringing has been the
succession of warnings from companies that their recent sales and earnings are falling short even of analysts'
low expectations.
Several indexes fell even more than did the Dow industrials. The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many
technology stocks have proved resilient this year, gave up 1.89%, or 26.20 points, to finish at 1356.74, and is
now up only 1.6% since the year began. Meanwhile, bonds rose. The 10-year Treasury note advanced 9/32,
or $2.81 per $1,000 invested, sending the 10-year note's yield down to 3.894%. The dollar was mixed.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 1.40%, or 12.30 points, to 865.99, down 1.6% so far this
year. The Dow industrials are down 1.7% on the year.
With the war winding down, "people are saying, `Now what's next?' And they don't see a lot positive coming
out," said Todd Leone, head of listed trading at New York brokerage firm SG Cowen.

Small-Stock Focus: Vitesse, Manugistics and Tredegar Notch Gains
Small-Stock Focus: Vitesse, Manugistics and Tredegar Notch Gains
By Shaheen Pasha
Dow Jones Newswires
900 words
8 April 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Investors opted to take some profits off the table in the final hour of trading, cutting into the
sharp gains small-capitalization stocks made early in the session.
Small caps, like their large-cap brethren, couldn't hold on to early war-news gains as investors fretted that
perhaps the market had gotten a bit overextended.
"There is increasing emphasis on what peace will look like and whether it will be easy and nonviolent or not,"
said Jack Caffrey, equity strategist at J.P. Morgan Private Bank. "There are a number of people trying to
anticipate what the market will look like in terms of the operating environment" after the war.
The Russell 2000 index of small stocks rose 3.29 points, or 0.88%, to 376.57, and the S&P SmallCap 600
Index rose 1.50, or 0.8%, to 189.69.
War news dominated market action, and the early euphoria drove investors out of the relatively safe-haven
precious-metals sector and into the riskier technology group, which is expected to benefit from a speedy end
to the war. In fact, precious metals were among the poorest performers on Wall Street, with small-cap
Randgold Services dropping four cents, or 0.3%, to $12.90, while Ashanti Goldfields fell 10 cents, or 1.9%, to
5.28 on the New York Stock Exchange.
As investors rotated away from the gold sector, semiconductors managed to pick up strength. The group
helped to boost small-cap stocks, with the Philadelphia Stock Exchange Semiconductor Index gaining 3.91,
or 1.26%, to 315.35.
Among the gainers, Vitesse Semiconductor climbed 23 cents, or 9.8%, to 2.57 on word that the company's
Timestream product is being used in Lucent Technologies' Metropolis DMX access multiplexer.
Maxim Integrated Products eked out a gain despite a downgrade from JMP Securities, which cut its rating to
market underperform from market perform, citing high valuation. Maxim shares rose five cents to 37.63.
Software stocks lost some steam late in the session, although investors for the most part brushed aside a
dour profit outlook by large-cap Siebel Systems. Among small caps, rival PeopleSoft, which issued its own
profit warning Thursday, slipped two cents, or 0.1%, to 15, while Manugistics rose 18 cents, or 7.4%, to 2.61.
Aluminum stocks were the brightest performers of the session, courtesy of a better-than-expected
first-quarter report from Dow industrials component Alcoa. The news spread some good cheer to shares of
small-cap Tredegar, which rose 71 cents, or 5.5%, to 13.51.
Investors checked into the lodging sector as they became increasingly bullish that a swift resolution of the
Iraq conflict would spur travel. Extended Stay America rose 52 cents, or 5.1%, to 10.78, and Prime Hospitality
gained 14 cents, or 2.7%, to 5.33, both on the NYSE.
The U.S. advance into Baghdad, as well as the subsequent drop in oil prices, also put some lift into the
air-travel sector. Delta Air Lines gained 58 cents, or 5.8%, to 10.60, and Continental rose 31 cents, or 5%, to
6.52, both on the NYSE. AMR Corp. rose 33 cents, or 8.4%, to 4.28 on the NYSE, even as it said its
American Airlines unit plans to reduce capacity in May, joining rivals' moves.
Secondary oil companies were among the poorest performers, partly because of the drop in crude-oil prices.
Tesoro Petroleum (NYSE) fell 60 cents, or 7.2%, to 7.73 after Bear Stearns issued a bearish note saying that
Tesoro's stock was "significantly overvalued and may have no value." The firm emphasized its underperform
rating on the stock.

Quarterly Mutual Funds Review --- Value Funds Reign as Managers Avoid Mistakes With Careful Stock Picking --- Small-Cap, Midcap Choices Help...
Quarterly Mutual Funds Review --- Value Funds Reign as Managers Avoid Mistakes With Careful Stock
Picking --- Small-Cap, Midcap Choices Help Give Winners a Boost; Some Add to Cash Levels
By Theo Francis
2,141 words
7 April 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
R3
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AMID THE FOG of war, mutual-fund investors are learning that the fog of the markets can be nearly as thick.
Every category of diversified stock fund lost ground in the first quarter. The best, large growth funds,
squeaked by with a 1.29% loss, and despite posting losses, growth categories outperformed value categories
during the quarter.
Yet it's too soon to declare a sure shift in fortunes among investing styles toward growth funds. Over longer
time periods, value funds still reigned, particularly those investing in small-capitalization and midcap stocks.
And some funds did considerably better than the averages, providing solid, and even double-digit, returns in
some cases. Not bad at a time when the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index lost 3.15% for the quarter and
24.76% over 12 months.
Each quarter, The Wall Street Journal conducts a contest highlighting the best-performing stock mutual
funds. Starting with this article, the contest rules are being adjusted to highlight the best-performing funds in
the best-performing diversified U.S. stock-fund categories during the latest 12-month, three-year and
five-year periods. Previously, the contest highlighted the best-performing funds in 12-month, three-year and
10-year periods, but winners sometimes remained the same for several consecutive quarters, particularly in
the longer time periods. The new rules are intended to create more opportunities to focus on a greater variety
of managers.
As in the past, funds must retain the same portfolio manager for the entire period for which they are in
contention, and the competition will exclude index funds. If a fund wins two or more quarters in a row, an
alternate fund may be highlighted. All results -- this time for periods ended March 31 -- are based on data
provided by research firm Morningstar Inc.
12-Month Winner
Sometimes playing it safe can mean hitting it big -- at least on a relative basis.
The tactic helped RS Contrarian Fund ring up a 5.89% return for the latest 12 months. That's well above the
average for midcap value funds, which despite losing 21.59%, still took first place among diversified
stock-fund categories for the year.
Manager Andrew Pilara attributes much of his fund's success during the past 12 months to staying
conservative, and keeping about 20% of his $73 million portfolio in cash. Plus, Mr. Pilara notes, "We didn't
make any significant mistakes" during the period.
"History shows that being bold in a bear market rarely shows results," Mr. Pilara says. "We were able to
prevail in a very difficult time."
But trying to play it safe doesn't mean avoiding big bets with the RS Contrarian portfolio. The fund's top 10
holdings make up about 50% of its assets, and nearly half its assets are invested in industrial-materials
companies, though Mr. Pilara says the concentration will decline by year's end.
Indeed, Mr. Pilara's biggest bet is also one of his more successful.
China Yuchai International Ltd., a New York Stock Exchange-listed company with headquarters in Singapore
that makes diesel engines in Yulin, China, makes up 11.3% of the fund's portfolio. The stock had been
trading under the company's book value of about $6 a share, a price only about three times its earnings, Mr.
Pilara says. Since Mr. Pilara bought the stock last year, the price has risen to more than $7 a share.

--- Page 110 ---
Page 110 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
He shops for undervalued companies using discounted cash-flow analysis -- essentially analyzing a
company's business model to make predictions about the cash-flow it will generate, and then comparing the
expected returns on investment to the company's cost of capital.
What about checking earnings? "It's too late to look at the quarterly earnings," Mr. Pilara says. "When
quarterly earnings go bad, it's probably a capital decision that's been made a year or two years ago."
Lately, Mr. Pilara has been zeroing in on industries that have added little new capacity in recent years,
figuring those stand the best chance of profiting from growth as the economy improves, because the
companies will have strong pricing power. That has led him to various metals and natural-resources
companies.
Mr. Pilara sees the current stock-market doldrums as part of a long-term trend -- a "secular" bear market,
rather than a shorter-term cyclical one. The situation reminds him of the bear market he encountered when
he started in investment management in 1974.
The current downturn started with the end of what he sees as years-long bull markets in stocks and bonds
and a 20-year decline in inflation and interest rates, as well as the reversal of sustained bear markets in
gold and other commodities.
So many rapid reversals have turned the market upside down for most investors, Mr. Pilara says. "That's
what they will have to deal with in the next five years."
Three-Year Winner
If the RS Contrarian Fund concentrates on comparatively few favorite stocks, the top small-cap value fund for
the three years ended March 31 takes the opposite tack: It owns about 180 stocks.
During the three-year period, the $120 million N/I Numeric Investors Small Cap Value Fund returned an
average of 18.99% a year. As the top-performing diversified stock-fund category for the three-year period,
small-cap value funds returned an average of just 4.25% a year.
The fund's managers don't let any stock grow to more than about 2% of its portfolio, and make sure its
investments in each sector parallel the proportions of the Russell 2000 Value Index. The fund usually intends
to sell its stocks fairly rapidly, often within six months or so.
To pick the specific stocks for each sector, management-team leader Arup Datta looks for an undervalued
stock, the hallmark of any value investor. But he also wants to see a good reason to believe that the market
will rectify the mistake soon -- within six months -- to make the stock worth buying.
That helps Mr. Datta avoid stocks that look cheap, but are likely to stay cheap because they lack a catalyst
for change, he says.
To separate perennial laggards from up-and-comers, Mr. Datta and his investment team use a computer to
crunch valuation and other data, and consider whether Wall Street analysts are raising expectations for a
company's earnings, and the consistency of the company's earnings in prior quarters, among other factors.
One company that has helped Small Cap Value beat its peers is New Century Financial Corp., a subprime
mortgage lender that has returned more than 40% over the past 12 months. Trading at a price of about five
times earnings and twice book value, the company is "very cheap," Mr. Datta says. At the same time, the
company has been taking away market share from competitors and buying back shares.
Given that Mr. Datta expects interest rates to stay low for the next six months, mortgage lending remains an
attractive business, he says.
Another success has been Oshkosh Truck Corp., which has returned about 9% over the past year, and
somewhat better over the past six months. Here, Mr. Datta likes the stock not because of rock-bottom
valuations so much as the likelihood that its business will thrive despite a weak economy.
The company makes fire trucks, garbage trucks and specialized trucks for the military -- three products Mr.
Datta considers more recession-proof than many in the industry. While municipal-budget woes might keep
some local governments from buying new fire engines, military orders will likely make up for that, he reasons.
One fund bet that has turned sour is Fresh Del Monte Produce Inc., a marketer of bananas, pineapple and
other produce. "It used to be our big winner the last few years, and now it's our big loser."
The stock has lost about 40% of its value in the past six months, Mr. Datta says, under the shadow of
litigation contesting the firm's sale in 1996 to a Cayman Islands holding company that still has a large stake in
Del Monte. "When that cloud lifts, the stock should double very soon," he says.

--- Page 111 ---
Page 111 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Numeric Investors plans to close the Small Cap Value fund to new investors once the company's total
small-cap value portfolios reach a total of $300 million. Currently, the firm manages about $160 million
outside of its mutual fund, in addition to the $120 million in fund assets. The fund was closed to new
investments in June before reopening the following month, allowing Numeric Investors to absorb a large
small-cap value investment in a private account.
Five-Year Winner
The manager of the top-performing midcap value fund with a five-year record doesn't see clouds lifting from
the economy even after the fog of war is gone.
The J.P. Morgan Mid Cap Value Fund's institutional shares have posted an average annual return of 12.55%
a year over the past five years. Midcap value funds on average posted a 0.17% annual return -- anemic, but
still better than any other diversified stock-fund category during that period. (The fund's institutional shares
are mostly available through retirement plans, but the fund has other share classes with different fee
structures available to individual investors.)
Manager Jonathan Simon attributes the fund's success to old-fashioned stock picking, plus a long-term view.
He looks for companies with strong balance sheets and business models, and finds them in a variety of
industries that he might not otherwise seek out.
"We saw opportunity in financial services, not because we thought the credit environment was going to be
fantastic, not because we thought the interest-rate environment was necessarily going to be favorable," Mr.
Simon says, "but because we found companies we thought would take market share away from the big
banks."
Among the banking stocks he has favored: M&T Bank Corp., North Fork Bancorp. and Golden West Financial
Corp.
The newspaper industry is another area Mr. Simon says he has found value, particularly in Washington Post
Co., "a company that three years ago nobody cared about."
The company doesn't try to smooth out its earnings from quarter to quarter nor does it talk much with Wall
Street analysts, Mr. Simon notes, and it was one of the early companies to expense stock options. "All of a
sudden, the market came to appreciate companies that were managed in that way," he says. Over the past
year, the stock has returned about 20%.
A bet on mobile-home maker and lender Clayton Homes Inc., however, didn't fare so well. In an industry
stung by higher-than-expected defaults and the collapse of Conseco Inc., a major source of mortgage
financing for manufactured housing, Clayton's share price tumbled by 29% in 2002.
"The company's earnings will basically be flat for a couple years, maybe down 10%, in an industry that has
dropped considerably, " Mr. Simon says. But "the company has remained profitable, with a strong balance
sheet, and will do well."
One big plus for the fund over the past five years: avoiding technology stocks, Mr. Simon says. Although
some value managers have concluded that technology stocks are selling at bargain prices, Mr. Simon isn't
buying that argument.
"I'm not of the camp that there's a lot of value in technology yet," Mr. Simon says. "My technology analyst
picked one stock for me last year, and that was Lexmark [International Inc.], in the [computer] printer
business."
It has proven a rewarding pick, as Lexmark has returned about 27% over the 12 months ended March 31.
And, says Mr. Simon, "It doesn't take a genius to figure out that the printer business is all about the
outrageous prices we pay for cartridges."

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Gregory Zuckerman
365 words
4 April 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Today's Market Forecast]
Job Jitters
Investors will be scrutinizing this morning's release of March employment figures. Jobs plunged in February
and the fear is that last month's numbers could be worse than the consensus of a loss of about 60,000 jobs,
with some saying the employment rate will hit 6% from 5.8%.
If the report is a real stinker, traders figure the Federal Reserve could cut interest rates, maybe by a
whopping half point, at its next meeting May 6 or maybe even earlier.
But don't hold your breath. After all, the report only gives a snapshot of the job market before the war began.
Alan Greenspan already has sent signals that he wants to see how the battle turns out before doing anything.
And rates already are so low that a big cut may not help the economy, and could hurt banks and
money-market funds, which won't be able to earn enough on their investments to cover their costs of
operations.
"Lower rates could be toxic," warns Neal Soss, chief economist at Credit Suisse First Boston.
Junk Rules
Quick. What's the best-performing investment so far this year? Risky, reviled junk bonds.
These bonds have scored returns of 8%, trouncing stocks, other kinds of bonds, gold, baseball cards and
just about everything else. Since October, the junk market has returned gains of 15%. With stocks rocky and
other bonds sporting puny yields, everyone from mutual-fund investors to Warren Buffett is throwing money at
these low-rated bonds.
Junk-bond returns soared 46% in 1991, helped by the last victory over Iraq, bulls point out. But don't expect
anything close to that this year. A survey of about 100 high-yield companies conducted by Lehman Brothers
shows their earnings are down in the first quarter, and there are worries about the rest of 2003.
The best strategy: Stick to junk companies that are sprucing up their balance sheets and seeing improving
earnings. Among these, according to Standard &Poor's, are Lear, AdvancePCS, Caremark Rx, Millipore and
Univision.

Treasurys Falter on War's Progress --- Flight to Quality Unravels; Losses Could Be Tempered As Focus Shifts to Economy
Credit Markets
Treasurys Falter on War's Progress --- Flight to Quality Unravels; Losses Could Be Tempered As
Focus Shifts to Economy
By Michael Mackenzie
Dow Jones Newswires
729 words
3 April 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Amid renewed optimism about the war in Iraq, Treasurys are seeing an unraveling of the "flight
to safety" that had pushed yields to historic lows.
Whether growing investor confidence leads to a seismic shift in markets, with stocks benefiting at the
expense of bonds, depends on developments in the conflict, as well as on the health of the U.S. economy
and corporate earnings, analysts say.
But if Treasurys falter further as the hostilities draw to an end, losses may be tempered as attention shifts
back to the economy's weakness.
"The idea of efficient markets entails that government bond traders and investors are looking beyond [Iraq]
and focusing on the outlook for the economy," said Jim Caron, a Merrill Lynch bond strategist.
With risk aversion having ruled the mind-set of investors for many months, the initial reaction of markets to
the war's ending in Iraq could well be dramatic.
"There is no doubt that financial markets are poised for an aggressive reaction in the wake of a cessation of
hostilities and the transition to a democratic government in Iraq," said William Sullivan, an economist at
Morgan Stanley.
He expects to see solid buying of equities and the dollar, and selling of gold, oil and Treasurys. Corporate
bonds will outperform Treasurys, and some investors will seek more risk in junk bonds, he said.
Michael Kastner, a portfolio manager at Deutsche Bank Private Wealth Management, which manages $10
billion in funds, said crossover investors -- who had tilted their portfolios in favor of bonds -- are likely to return
to equities. "We have seen two rallies in stocks now on hopes that the war is resolved, and that should
encourage investors to start buying stocks on dips," Mr. Kastner said.
Developments in the war yesterday suggesting progress by the U.S.-led coalition sparked strong gains in
equities and pronounced weakness in Treasurys.
At 4 p.m., the benchmark 10-year note was down 25/32 point, or $7.8125 per $1,000 face value, at 99 19/32.
Its yield rose to 3.923% from 3.828% Tuesday, as yields move inversely to prices. The 30-year bond was
down 1 11/32 points at 106 27/32 to yield 4.920%, up from 4.837% Tuesday.
"Treasurys have been so overbought for the last several months, and we are now starting to see the
unraveling of the flight-to-quality sentiment," said Jim Cusser, a portfolio manager at Waddell &Reed in
Kansas City, Mo., which manages $1.7 billion in fixed-income funds.
Still, concerns about the economy raise doubts about whether the bull run in Treasurys will end soon.
Mr. Sullivan of Morgan Stanley said a big rally in equities might lift Treasury yields a quarter to a half
percentage point above current levels. After that, negative bond-market sentiment "would then be replaced by
an assessment of the economic realities," he added.
Complicating the Treasury market's outlook is the possible role of investors in mortgage-backed securities.
They sometimes sell Treasurys when rates are rising, helping push yields even higher.
Mr. Kastner of Deutsche Bank said a sustained rise above 4.26% in the 10-year Treasury yield would lead to
hefty mortgage selling of Treasurys.

--- Page 115 ---
Page 115 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Yet, a sharp rise in interest rates also would pose a threat to the economy. Sharply higher rates would choke
off the mortgage- and debt-refinancing boom that has kept consumer spending ticking along and allowed
corporations to refinance debt at lower rates.
However, for now, the Treasury selloff remains restrained, awaiting additional news from Iraq. "There are a lot
of moving parts, and until there is a definitive resolution in Iraq, the 10-year note will not push through 4.26%,"
Mr. Kastner said.

Commodities Report: Oil and Gold Slide as Optimism Breaks Through Earlier Gloom
Commodities Report: Oil and Gold Slide as Optimism Breaks Through Earlier Gloom
By Masood Farivar
Dow Jones Newswires
529 words
3 April 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Both crude-oil prices and gold fell sharply Wednesday as investors grew more optimistic
about the progress of the U.S.-led military operation in Iraq, and amid weekly reports that showed
larger-than-expected growth in U.S. inventories of crude oil.
"Most of the selling pressure continued to come from hope that Saddam Hussein's regime is going to fall
sometime soon," said Michael Rothman, director of energy-market research at Merrill Lynch in New York. "If
the regime is ousted sooner rather than later, Iraqi exports will return sooner than later."
The market's mood swing undid the gloom that settled on traders last week after initial expectations for a
quick war proved too optimistic. Oil prices rose 12% in New York last week as traders bet on a long war and a
prolonged halt to Iraqi oil output, but prices have fallen 9% this week.
May light sweet crude-oil futures at the New York Mercantile Exchange fell as much as $1.53, or 5%, to a low
of $28.18 a barrel. They closed at $28.56 a barrel, down $1.22.
Meanwhile, gold futures for April delivery on the New York Merc's Comex division slid $4.70, to $329.60 per
ounce. Sharp gains in the U.S. dollar coupled with a buoyant tone across the stock markets put pressure on
gold, which had risen in recent months as a "safe haven" amid war jitters.
As for oil, the rapid progress in the U.S.-led campaign to overthrow the Iraqi regime, which traders once
feared could cause a massive disruption in Persian Gulf oil supplies, comes at a time when a deluge of oil
imports has helped U.S. commercial inventories of crude oil recover sharply from their recent lows.
The statistical arm of the U.S. Department of Energy reported Wednesday that U.S. crude-oil inventories
grew by 6.8 million barrels, to 280.7 million barrels, last week, as imports surged to a record 10.4 million
barrels a day.
The increase in imports comes at a time when the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries has sharply
increased its output in response to the loss of Iraqi oil. Some OPEC officials and analysts believe a quick
resumption of Iraqi oil exports could lead to a supply glut in the second quarter. Fearing a price collapse,
some OPEC officials have indicated the group may act to cut output when Iraqi oil returns to the market.
But others warn that Iraqi oil may stay off the market longer than many expect. And with Nigerian crude oil
output sharply reduced by ethnic unrest, OPEC may not have to reduce supply.
In other commodity markets:
SOYBEANS: Futures at the Chicago Board of Trade closed with sharp gains Wednesday as fresh U.S. export
demand surfaced. The U.S. Department of Agriculture announced that Spain had purchased 110,000 metric
tons of U.S. soybeans. Chart-related technical buying added to the bullish enthusiasm as prices reached a
fresh 6.5-month high.

Going Global: Investment Firms Go on Defensive
Going Global: Investment Firms Go on Defensive
By Michael R. Sesit
565 words
28 March 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- As the war-rally fizzles and prospects dim for a brief Iraq conflict, some institutional investors
are firmly back on the defensive.
After a huge run-up in global stock markets, many analysts had initially argued that the war rally will last.
Some still do. "This rally has legs," says Teun Fraaisma, a strategist at Morgan Stanley in London.
But at Credit Suisse Asset Management's London office, Deputy Chairman Robert Parker warns, "Investors
shouldn't chase the rally we had last week."
Rather, the Credit Suisse Group unit and several other prominent investment firms are hunkering down in
preparation for a drab few months. Despite stocks' recent run-up, their asset-allocation strategies are to be
underweight stocks and overweight bonds -- a defensive posture -- as they remain jittery about the global
economy's weak outlook regardless of how and when the Iraq war plays out.
Still, investment firms are hardly sticking cash under the metaphorical mattress. Instead they are scouring the
globe for investments to match their pessimistic view. So what are they looking for?
The answer: Not necessarily the traditional super-safe havens such as U.S. Treasury, gold and Swiss francs,
but a wide range of bond and equity investments in some far-flung parts of the world.
Among bonds, some recommend euro-denominated to dollar-denominated securities. "The prospects of
inflation in Europe are very benign," says Alan Brown, group chief investment officer at State Street Global
Advisors in London, which also is overweight bonds and underweight stocks.
Money managers even say some developed-country bonds aren't as attractive as more-risky sovereign debt.
"We think [developed] country government bonds are still too expensive," says Michael Karagianis, head
global strategist at Aberdeen Asset Management, an investment firm based in Aberdeen, Scotland.
Instead, Aberdeen managers favor a U.S. dollar-denominated Brazilian government bond that matures in
2040, is rated single-B plus and currently yields 14.6% to maturity. Part of its attractiveness: Brazilian
President Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva's economic turnaround strategy and market-friendly policies.
Another favorite: a 2012, double-B-rated dollar-denominated bond that yields 12% issued by Philippine Long
Distance Telephone, which is majority government-owned.
In addition to emerging-market securities, investment-grade and high-yield corporate bonds also are seen as
a way to eke out higher, but relatively safe, returns than most major industrial-country debt now pays. France
Telecom's bonds, for instance, have lagged behind the recent rally in investment-grade credit even though its
mobile division is performing well. Aberdeen managers like the French company's 2005, triple-B-minus,
sterling issue, which yields 1.75 percentage points more than comparable British government bonds.
Among equities, Credit Suisse Asset Management is leaning toward stocks with high dividend yields in
defensive sectors -- energy, pharmaceuticals, utilities and consumer staples -- where cash flows are strong.
Oil companies also represent a good hedge against the situation worsening significantly in the Middle East.
The firm's top holdings include United Kingdom oil firm BP, drug makers Novartis and Pfizer, Germany's E.On
and Britain's Severn Trent in the utility field, and German retailer Metro and U.S. consumer-products concern
Gillette among consumer-staples companies. Aberdeen's petroleum picks include Italy's ENI and Brazil's
Petroleo Brasileiro.
