The War Economy -- The Outlook: Path of U.S. Economy Isn't Easy After End Of War, History Shows
The War Economy -- The Outlook: Path of U.S. Economy Isn't Easy After End Of War, History Shows
By Jon E. Hilsenrath
781 words
24 March 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
New York -- WILL THE U.S. ECONOMY bounce back after the war with Iraq is resolved?
Many economists and top economic policy makers are counting on it. But a look at the history of U.S. military
conflicts in the 20th century suggests that the path of the economy isn't necessarily an easy one after a war is
concluded.
The first Persian Gulf War in 1991 gave way to an anemic economic recovery, which cost the first President
Bush his job. By the time U.S. troops pulled out of Vietnam in 1973, the economy was on a course to
inflation and recession. Unemployment rose sharply after the U.S. signed an armistice in July 1953 that
ended the fighting in the Korean War and cut back defense spending. And the end of the two world wars
came with doses of adjustment. Most notably, sharp inflation persisted after World War I ended in 1918, and
that led to a recession as monetary officials sought to rein in the rising prices that accompanied the financing
of the war.
"History does not seem to show that the period immediately following a war, whether it is one year or two
years or three years, is a period of great prosperity," says Richard Sylla, an economic historian at New York
University's Stern School of Business.
Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan has emphasized that the pieces are in place for the economy to
begin growing more briskly once the current Gulf War is over. In a statement after the Fed decided to leave
interest rates unchanged last week, the Fed blamed "oil price premiums and other aspects of geopolitical
uncertainties" for the "hesitant" expansion. Economic forecasters seem to agree. A consensus of economists
surveyed by The Wall Street Journal earlier this month is that the economy will grow at an annual rate of
3.5% by the second half of the year.
THE REASONS ARE SIMPLE. The months of international debate that led up to the current war coincided
with rising oil prices and mounting uncertainty among business executives and consumers. That seemed to
hold back spending and investment. If the uncertainty dissipates and oil prices recede, then businesses and
consumers might be expected to start spending again.
But that isn't quite the way it played out after the first Gulf War ended in February 1991. Oil prices did recede
quickly as the war came to a conclusion and the stock market rebounded in an apparent sign of rising
confidence in the economy. But the economy was still months away from shaking off the strains of a banking
and real-estate crisis that had created a long-running credit contraction. The unemployment rate swelled from
6.6% in February 1991 to 7.4% a year later, despite the relief resulting from the end of the war.
This was a case in which underlying trends in the economy "swamped the effects of the war," says John
Wallis, an economic historian with the University of Maryland. The early 1970s served as another example of
this. U.S. troops pulled out of Vietnam in March 1973, but by then the Nixon administration had removed the
dollar from the gold standard and OPEC was on course to squeeze supplies of oil. Those trends conspired to
set off spiraling inflation and rising unemployment that had little connection to the end of the war.
OF COURSE, each of these wars was very different, and the economic backdrop from which the U.S.
emerged was also very different. This makes comparisons difficult. During the Korean conflict, for example,
defense spending amounted to almost 15% of the nation's gross domestic product; today it is less than 5%.
The Vietnam War dragged on for years, while some military experts believe the current war could end in
weeks. And inflation doesn't seem to be a concern as it was following many other wars.
Nevertheless, some economists say the U.S. isn't out of the woods even if Saddam Hussein falls quickly. In a
speech last week, Federal Reserve Bank of New York President William McDonough said investors and
lenders are still worried about the reliability of accounting statements.

--- Page 119 ---
Page 119 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
There are other concerns, from the lingering effects of the high-tech bubble to the rising federal budget deficit
to new uncertainties that would surely arise out of a potential military occupation in Iraq. "If war is resolved
quickly, it doesn't resolve the other issues," says Joseph Stiglitz, a Columbia University professor and Nobel
prize winner in economics.

Uncertainty Is Only Certainty Today
World Stock Markets
Uncertainty Is Only Certainty Today
By Michael R. Sesit
1,257 words
24 March 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
With the war under way, uncertainty has become certain. Stocks around the world have rallied; the dollar has
jumped; bond prices have fallen, and yields risen; gold has slumped.
So much for the quick returns. The big question now is whether the stock-market rebound, particularly among
global stocks, can last or whether combat-gone-wrong, continued geopolitical schisms or a lousy global
economic environment will render the rallies moot.
For now, investors appear to be setting those concerns aside. Since March 12, German stocks have jumped
22% in local-currency terms, French and Swiss shares leapt 21% and Dutch shares have soared 23%,
according to Morgan Stanley Capital International. Meanwhile, the British, Italian, Spanish and Swedish
markets have risen between 13% and 17%. Over that period, the Dow Jones Industrial Average has climbed
13%.
Labeling these and shifts in other financial markets "the coming attractions" of what will happen once a
decisive outcome becomes apparent, Nicholas Sargen, chief investment strategist at J.P. Morgan Private
Bank in New York, says, "All the assets that benefited from war uncertainty are being sold as that uncertainty
is diminished and the forgotten assets get bought."
To some, resolution in Iraq -- assuming a swift U.S. military victory and peace within the country -- could lead
to more uncertainty elsewhere. "If you get Iraq past you, do you think about reprisals, Iran, North Korea?"
asks David F. Cooley, a portfolio manager at J.&W. Seligman &Co. in New York. He worries about the high
level of consumer debt in the U.S. and how these unresolved geopolitical issues might weigh on Americans'
willingness to keep spending.
Conclusion: The latest rally in global markets "doesn't feel sustainable to me," says Mr. Cooley. "I
recommend avoiding consumer-oriented stocks that are big-ticket items -- the so-called borrow-to-buy goods,
such as cars and other expensive products. More appealing, he says, are industrial companies that have
reduced their debt loads. Four favorite investments include Austrian container-board maker Mayr-Melnhof
Karton AG, Royal Dutch Petroleum Co., Swedish telecommunications company Tele2 AB and Swiss
reinsurer Converium Holding AG.
Uri D. Landesman, a senior portfolio manager at Federated Investors in New York, also doubts stock-market
rallies will hold up in the long run if they aren't supported by improved corporate earnings. "The whole
problem with the world isn't the war," he says. "You're going to need better profits and some topline growth;
and professional investors are going to need to see more fertile economic growth prospects than they've
seen."
Believing that U.S. stocks are stuck in a trading range and that bulging U.S. budget deficits render low bond
yields untenable, strategists at J.P. Morgan have been gradually selling bonds on rallies and buying stocks.
"We're the defensive, cautious players who are tiptoeing back into U.S. and global equities," says Mr. Sargen.
Gary Dugan, his London-based counterpart, thinks European markets could rise a further 10% to 15%,
helped by their low valuations. Noting that bank and insurance stocks have been badly beaten up, he figures
that financial-sector companies probably will rebound the most. Next comes the cyclicals sector -- chemical,
paper companies and building-materials concerns -- if investors believe that a global economic recovery will
follow the campaign in Iraq.
That's a big if, and at its center sits oil. "The price of oil is critical in assessing how far the rally can go; that's
because of its impact on economic growth," says Mr. Sargen.

--- Page 121 ---
Page 121 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ian Harnett, head European equity strategist at UBS Warburg in London, is skeptical. During and after the
1991 Gulf War, falling oil prices, declining interest rates and weakening European currencies gave European
corporate profit -- and stock -- a boost. This time, however, Mr. Harnett contends that oil prices and rates
won't fall as steeply and that the euro, instead of weakening, will be 14% higher by year end. Thus, he says:
"We are going to get a [stock-market] bounce; what I'm afraid of is that it won't be sustainable."
Federated's Mr. Landesman is "overweight" on consumer staples -- investing in companies such as Swiss
drug company Novartis AG, the U.K.'s British American Tobacco PLC and Anglo-Dutch food and
household-products firm Unilever NV -- which are generally regarded as safety plays. On a country basis, he
is underweight Japan, overweight emerging markets and underweight the 15 European Union countries. That
is based "on a belief that their lack of cooperation [in the war] is going to have a negative impact on their
prospects going forward," he says. Adding that President Bush won't forget the opposition to the war that the
U.S. has faced from France, Germany and Belgium, he says, "These Europeans are going to have to pay."
---
Friday's Market Activity
Asian shares ended mixed Friday after surging for three straight days, though Tokyo trading was closed for a
holiday. European stocks made strong gains Friday, buoyed by hopes for a short conflict in Iraq. Latin
American markets also rebounded with the U.S. rally.Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 1.75%,
or 2.39, to 138.86. Excluding the U.S., the index rose 1.11%, or 1.09, to 99.72.
In HONG KONG, the Hang Seng Index ended down 0.2%, or 15.37 points, at 9179.19. Hutchison Whampoa
said net profit rose 19% to HK$14.29 billion (US$1.83 billion) last year, defying expectations, and it assured
investors 2003 won't be hit by its third-generation mobile-telephone investments. The conglomerate fell 30
Hong Kong cents to HK$46.40. Its shares had risen 9% in the four trading days ahead of the results, and
analysts said they fell Friday on profit-taking. Property developer Cheung Kong dropped 1.7%, or 80 Hong
Kong cents, to HK$47.10, a day after reporting higher-than-forecast earnings. Pacific Century CyberWorks
fell 5%, or 25 Hong Kong cents, to HK$4.80, set back by news it won't pay a dividend in 2004.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 Index climbed 2.5%, or 95.4 points to 3861. The most heavily traded stock was
mobile-telecommunications group Vodafone, which rose 1.9% to 122 pence ($1.91). Insurers also gained.
Royal &Sun Alliance climbed 8.3% to 78 pence and Legal &General rose 7.1% to 83 pence.
In PARIS, the CAC 40 Index rose 3.4%, or 95.85 points, to 2890.68. Building-materials company
Saint-Gobain rose 8.2% to 27.21 euros ($28.66) after Merrill Lynch raised its target price on the company to
45 euros from 43 euros. Axa rose 7.5% to 12.60 euros.

Commodities Report: Active Investing: News on War Fuels Trading on Futures Marts
Commodities Report: Active Investing: News on War Fuels Trading on Futures Marts
By David Reilly
723 words
24 March 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C7
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
LONDON -- The rise in volatility seen across all markets last week may have given some investors an ulcer,
but it has also been a tonic for futures and options markets.
In both the U.S. and Europe, so-called derivatives markets -- the exchanges that trade options and futures on
commodities, stocks and stock indexes, bonds, and currencies -- have seen volumes jump before and during
the outbreak of war in Iraq.
Eurex, the world's largest derivatives exchange, had the biggest surge in trading. The market, which is based
in Germany and owned by German stock-exchange operator Deutsche Borse, twice set records last week for
the amount of futures and options contracts traded. On Wednesday, more than eight million futures and
options contracts change hands; much of the action was in the futures contract for the DJ EuroStoxx 50,
which tracks the 50-largest companies in the euro zone, and in the futures contract for German government
bonds.
(The DJ Stoxx indexes are a joint venture between Deutsche Boerse; Dow Jones &Co., which owns The Wall
Street Journal; and SWX Swiss Exchange.)
Derivatives markets are "having a field day" because of the uncertainty in markets, said Huw van Steenis, an
analyst at Morgan Stanley who follows European financial-services companies.
Euronext.Liffe, which is the successor to the London International Futures and Options Exchange and is
Eurex's main European rival, also had more activity than usual, but volumes tapered off somewhat after
spiking on March 13, when stock markets began to rally.
In the U.S., the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, which offers contracts on Eurodollars, foreign currencies and
the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, has averaged more than three million contracts a day in March -- a
first in its history. Volume this month so far peaked at 4.2 million contracts on March 13, up 75% from
average volume of 2.4 million.
At the neighboring Chicago Board of Trade, a spokeswoman said the market's exchangewide volume record
set in February is still intact, although trading in Federal Reserve funds futures set two consecutive records
Tuesday and Wednesday.
Analysts said Eurex's trading surge outpaced that seen by peers because its DJ EuroStoxx 50 contract
benefits as investors take a regional rather than a national approach to European markets.
On the debt side, Eurex benefited from fears of a fall in bond prices and changing interest-rate expectations.
While U.S. investors have a large, liquid market in 10-year Treasury notes, the European bond market is
more fragmented; there is no single bond that represents the entire European market. When traders need to
quickly adjust their portfolios, they turn to the futures market and the contract for the 10-year German
government bond, or "bund," traded on Eurex, said Christian Mundigo, head of long-term interest rates
trading at BNP Paribas in London.
That was the case last week when traders feared the surge in stock markets could lead to a collapse in bond
prices.
"There's been a lot of liquidation out of fixed income, or to hedge fixed income, so we've seen an awful lot of
volume in the bund," said Peter Osler, head of research at Man Financial.
Analysts and traders expect volumes on derivatives exchanges to remain strong for some time as investors
will have no choice but to turn to them if stock and bond markets remain volatile. "The place where everything
washes out is the futures markets," said Mr. Mundigo.

--- Page 123 ---
Page 123 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In commodity trading on Friday:
GOLD: Futures fell to their lowest level in more than three months on Comex. Gold futures had been rising
as a "safe haven" play during turmoil. April gold futures sank $6.90 to $326.10 a troy ounce. Earlier, they
fell to $325.80, the lowest since touching $325.10 on Dec. 11.
CRUDE OIL: Nymex futures tumbled as traders continued to see a quick end to the war in Iraq. May futures
declined $1.21 to $26.91 a barrel, a fresh three-month low. Nymex crude futures have lost nearly 33% since
posting a 12-year high of $39.99 on Feb. 22.

Outlook for the Markets: For Stocks, the Wait Is Over --- Reality of War Raises New Issues That May Keep Market Volatile; Will `War Rally...
Outlook for the Markets: For Stocks, the Wait Is Over --- Reality of War Raises New Issues That May
Keep Market Volatile; Will `War Rally' Gain Steam?
By Ken Brown
1,043 words
20 March 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AT LEAST THE waiting is over.
Through the buildup to the conflict with Iraq, investors fretted over every diplomatic twist and turn, and until
recently, they mainly sold stocks. Now, the reality of war raises new issues that will likely keep the market
volatile.
Early indications are that the market could rally from here -- though the big rallies of Monday and late last
week already have used up some of the "war rally" premium. Investors aren't celebrating war; rather, they
were afraid to act before, with such a big unknown hanging out there. Monday's big bounce, which marked
the market's recognition that war was imminent, attests to that. "The talk of it is almost worse than the reality,"
says Jim Paulsen, chief investment officer at Wells Capital Management.
But how the war plays out and how it impacts the economy, the national psyche and corporate balance
sheets will be the big driver in the market. Shares fell modestly in trading this morning in Tokyo, after the
launch of the war, trimming what had been a nearly 3% gain to nearly 2% by afternoon trading. Hong Kong
stocks edged up 0.1%. Oil prices fell nearly $1 a barrel in after-hours markets from the $29.36 during the day.
Gold fell modestly, and the U.S. dollar initially rose on the war news, and then eased in choppy trading.
In 4 p.m. trading yesterday, before the war officially started, U.S. stocks were up for the sixth straight day,
with the Dow Jones Industrial Average closing up 71.22 points, or 0.87%, to 8265.45, while the Nasdaq
Composite Index posted a slight decline.
In terms of what happens now, the most optimistic scenario is not only for a quick war, but a rapid elimination
of Saddam Hussein and the rest of the Iraqi leadership, all accomplished without widespread civilian deaths
and without damage to Iraqi oil fields, shipping terminals or pipelines. If any one of these factors goes the
wrong way, the already-fragile U.S. economy and stock market could take a hit.
Indeed, most economists say the war itself will have little impact on the economy or the market. It's the way
the war is perceived and the war's impact on energy prices that likely will be the biggest factors back home.
In the run-up to the war, the biggest casualty was sentiment among consumers, investors and executives.
That was a key factor in the frustrating two-month slide in stocks, the slowdown in retail sales and the slump
in business activity that raised questions about whether the economic recovery would stall out or continue at
its plodding pace. "Where I see the concern mostly is . . . when you ask somebody, `How are you doing?' "
Mr. Paulsen says.
The Conference Board's index of consumer confidence plunged in February to its lowest level in a decade,
housing starts fell to their lowest level in almost a year and auto makers are gearing down for slowing sales.
The dour mood took hold even as many economic indicators showed reasonable improvement. Spending on
durable goods has increased, inventories are down, and corporate profits have continued their climb out of
the cellar. "The market was sinking more due to the weight of emotion than to all the data points," says
Robert Morris, director of equity investments at Lord Abbett &Co.
More recent evidence suggests that negative emotions might have spread to the point that they were having
an impact on the economy, and some economic indicators have been reversing themselves recently.
Now, a clear victory could have a leveraged impact on the economy and the stock market. The most positive
scenario plays out this way:

--- Page 125 ---
Page 125 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
First investors bid up stocks and drive down oil prices on the belief that Iraqi crude will start flowing quickly
and loosen up the tight global supply situation. Then consumers do a victory lap around the mall, spending
the cash they got from their latest mortgage refinancing (which they had set aside to pay for gasoline over the
summer).
At the same time, corporate executives start getting orders and realize their nearly empty warehouses need
to be restocked, and fast. Over the next few quarters, all that spending boosts the economy and corporate
profits, justifying the stock-market rally. The economic rebound also reduces yields on corporate bonds,
relative to Treasurys, making it easier for companies to borrow and continue to shore up their balance sheets.
The only real downside to this scenario is that Treasury bonds could be hurt. That's because investors could
start to worry about inflation and decide that better returns can be had elsewhere.
The negative scenarios run the range from a temporary economic stall, as consumers sit glued to their TV
sets, to a real meltdown caused by anything from a terrorist attack to an oil-price spike driven by the torching
of Iraq's oil fields.
Any bad news likely will be magnified by the weak economy and a stock market that isn't cheap despite the
declines of the past several years. The nearly week-old stock rally could mean the market already has priced
in a quick victory, which makes it vulnerable to all but the best-case scenarios.
"What might be the surprise of surprises: The day Iraq surrenders, the market could fall 150 points," Mr.
Morris says.
The most dangerous outcome would be a prolonged, inconclusive war, which could lead to a big and
prolonged spike in oil prices. Rising oil prices themselves could cripple consumer sentiment. And consumers,
of course, have kept the economy afloat over the past two years.
One thing is certain amid the uncertainty -- markets are likely to make large moves up and down as they
digest the war and other developments.
According to money manager Bridgewater Associates, global stock markets are pricing in slightly
above-average volatility, but not as much as during the past Gulf War or the Russian debt crisis, while bond
and currency markets are saying that volatility will be less than average.

Stocks Surge on Clarity Over War With Iraq --- Blue Chips Rally 3.6% But Bonds Pull Back; Fed Panel
Meets Today
By Peter A. McKay
1,277 words
18 March 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SIGNS THAT A U.S.-led war in Iraq is only days away sent stock prices soaring both in the U.S. and abroad,
with investors scrambling to catch a widely predicted rally that assumes a quick end to the conflict.
Traders, though, noted that yesterday's 282.21-point runup in the Dow Jones Industrial Average -- on the
second-heaviest trading volume of the year on the New York Stock Exchange, behind only the rally on
Thursday of last week -- highlighted risks to the market. Any deviation to the quick-victory scenario could cost
investors dearly at a time when the economy itself still is wobbly.
The market also is looking ahead to today's announcement at 2:15 p.m. Eastern Time by the Federal
Reserve's interest-rate committee. While some economists are hoping for a quarter-point cut in interest rates,
most strategists believe the Fed is unlikely to provide further relief in the form of a cut.
But it was the apparent end of diplomatic haggling in the Iraq crisis, and the seeming inevitability of conflict,
that drove trading yesterday. The Dow industrial average's 3.6% gain, to put it back above 8000 at 8141.92,
was its biggest one-day gain since Oct. 15 of last year. It also marked the first four-day gain for the blue-chip
average in more than two months.
Strategists said they expected stocks to continue to rally in the next few days if an attack on Iraq begins, and
if those operations show early success. But even then, the market will still face question marks ranging from
underlying weakness in the world economy and corporate earnings to the threat of terrorism.
"Now that we're getting some clarity regarding Iraq, it just removes one of the obstacles for anyone who
wanted to claim that as an uncertainty facing the market," said strategist Christopher Wolfe of J.P. Morgan.
"For months now, anyone who's nervous about the war has already been selling, so in a way, there was no
where left to go but up."
Early trading in Tokyo today reflected the U.S. markets' strength. The Nikkei Stock Average, which had
sagged 134.05 points, or 1.6%, on Monday, was up more than 167 points at the close of the morning session,
back over the Nikkei's 8000 threshold.
The U.S. rally was broad. Only one of 30 stocks in the Dow industrials finished down: Altria Group Inc., the
newly named parent of Philip Morris. The rally yesterday followed strong gains earlier in Europe.
The Nasdaq Composite Index yesterday jumped nearly 3.9%, or 51.94 points, to 1392.27 -- and now is up
4% for the year, even though the Dow industrials remain in the red, down 2%. And the Standard &Poor's
500-stock index leapt to 862.79, up 3.5%, or 29.52 points, and down 2% on the year.
Both the S&P 500 and the industrial average are up nearly 10% from the trading lows hit last Wednesday,
before the pronounced "war rally" that began with Thursday's 269.68-point jump in the Dow industrials.
Volume on the Nasdaq Stock Market yesterday hit its high for the year, at 1.86 billion shares, and the NYSE's
1.69 billion shares trailed only the 1.72 billion on Thursday. Trading curbs that slow down program trading
when the industrial average moves up or down 170 points from the previous day's close (and then are
removed if the Dow average moves back to within 80 points of that close) kicked in several times during the
session's frenzied buying -- as buyers sought to join the rally and short sellers, who bet on stocks' declines,
sought to unwind their positions.
Investments in havens fell, as they historically do when stocks surge. In recent days, however, such
correlations haven't always held true. That they did yesterday was further proof of the staying power of the
stock rally, which could last at least through the start of fighting in Iraq, strategists said.

Treasury bonds were sharply lower, as was crude oil, which fell for the fifth time in six days, to $34.93 a
barrel, in New York trading. Crude futures are down more than 7.5% during that period.
Gold was up more than $8 in the morning, but most of that rally fizzled by day's end, leaving the metal up just
60 cents, at $337.20 a troy ounce, on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Before the start of yesterday's trading, the market already was digesting statements by top administration
officials on the Sunday political talk shows expressing doubt that Iraqi leader Saddam Hussein will voluntarily
disarm.
Then, early in the trading session, the U.S., Britain and Spain announced they had ended their efforts to win
United Nations support for a war and that President Bush would give Saddam Hussein an ultimatum during a
televised address to the nation.
"The three things that the market wanted clarity on are that the U.S will pursue the Iraqi situation unilaterally,
that it will begin soon and that a military option is the one we will take," said strategist Jim Russell, of Fifth
Third Bank in Cincinnati. "We got clarity on all those points today."
One nonwar factor that continues to weigh down shares is a weak corporate earnings outlook. According to
Thomson Financial/First Call, the ratio of negative corporate earnings preannouncements to positive ones for
the first-quarter so far is 2.7, higher than at any point last year.
For that reason, many market watchers are only guardedly optimistic about the length of any war-related rally.
Strategist Tobias Levkovich, of Salomon Smith Barney, said such "trading rallies" historically can push major
indexes up 20% to 25%, but "no one believes that's going to happen" now.
Strategist Ned Riley, of State Street Global Advisors, said he believes the rally will continue steadily over the
next few days or weeks, perhaps interrupted by occasional profit-taking as investors who still are nervous
jump at their first chance to pocket gains.
Beyond that, Mr. Riley said many investors are now using the last Gulf War as their guide for how to react to
geopolitical risk in the market. But he said investors will shed such comparisons once the specific
characteristics of the new conflict become apparent.
"We may have more problems, and more casualties, and a much more unusual type of warfare than we had
13 years ago," Mr. Riley said.
Outside the U.S., stocks rose in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, rose
0.37%, or 0.36 point, to 96.79.

Stocks rose. On the Big Board, 2,414 stocks rose and 893 fell.

moves opposite to price, to 3.835%. The 30-year Treasury bond fell 1 8/32, putting its yield at 4.796%.

Stocks Record Biggest Rally in Five Months --- Industrials Rise 269.68 Points, As Nasdaq Climbs Into the Black; Technology, Insurers Lead...
Thursday's Markets
Stocks Record Biggest Rally in Five Months --- Industrials Rise 269.68 Points, As Nasdaq Climbs Into
the Black; Technology, Insurers Lead Surge
By Gregory Zuckerman
1,116 words
14 March 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE STOCK MARKET's relentless punishment ended yesterday -- at least for a day.
Stocks soared in their biggest gains in five months amid hopes that a war in Iraq could be delayed, or even
avoided. Beaten-down technology and insurance stocks led the surge, which was accompanied by a drop in
oil prices and fleeting signs that there might be life coming back to the economy.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average climbed 269.68 points, or 3.57%, to 7821.75 -- the biggest rally in point
and percentage terms since Oct. 15.
The Nasdaq Composite Index jumped even higher: 4.81%, or 61.53, to 1340.77, to put the battered index
back in positive territory for the year. The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 3.45%, or 27.71 points, to
831.90.
But some were confounded by the sudden climb. The sharp gains -- and the year's heaviest trading volumes
that accompanied them -- came with as much bad news as good. Indeed, the very nature of the abrupt rise
suggested to some that the rally might be fleeting, unless there is a resolution on Iraq soon. "There was a
move out of bonds and into stocks" fueling the move, said Bob Basel, a senior trader at Salomon Smith
Barney. "But I question the sustainability of the rally."
The rally added about $300 billion in market value to the portfolios of beleaguered investors. While many
analysts have predicted a stock-market rally once a war in Iraq actually begins, yesterday investors embraced
the prospect that the standoff could be averted without military action.
The run-up began with a decline in oil prices and a rally in European stocks on reports that senior Iraqi
officers might be in talks to surrender. Though that news never was confirmed, it helped boost the dollar and
start Wall Street's day on the upswing.
As investors rushed back into stocks, they fled the investments they had plowed into seeking some measure
of safety, sending bonds and gold prices falling. Treasurys had their worst losses in almost two months.
Bonds had been soaring -- pushing down yields, which move opposite to price, to historically low levels and
helping to fuel a boom in home-buying and mortgage-refinancing. The 10-year Treasury note yesterday
dropped 1 9/32, or $12.81 for each $1,000 invested, to push up the yield to 3.744%.
Once stock prices started rallying, many investors elected to "cover" their short positions, or buy back stock
that they had borrowed and sold on a bet that prices would fall. Others flocked to stocks that might do best in
the event the Iraq crisis passes and the U.S. economy gets a lift. Semiconductor stocks, for example, jumped
8%, amid some talk by analysts that the worst is over for that sector.
"If there's strength in semiconductors that should move down the food chain to the rest of the manufacturing
sectors," said Tim Smalls, senior equity trader at SG Cowen. "I'm cautiously optimistic, as long as we make
progress toward a conclusion" of the Iraqi imbroglio.
One encouraging element was that the rally came on heated trading activity, as some large institutions
scooped up shares. Spirits rose on the trading floor of Credit Suisse First Boston in Manhattan as the rally
took off in the morning and traders rooted the market on. Indeed, investors often see big market moves that
are backed by heavy trading as more likely to continue than those based on limited trading.
The biggest percentage gainers in the Dow average were Intel, up 7.1%; Walt Disney, which rose 6.7%; and
General Motors, up 6.4%. Merck and Hewlett-Packard were the only industrial-average stocks not to
participate in the rally.

--- Page 129 ---
Page 129 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Still, there were signs that confusion still reigns in the market. For example, some traders attributed the gains
in part to a sense that a war with Iraq will be soon and will be quick; others said they were encouraged any
battle might be delayed.
And often explosive gains are the fastest to peter out. Stocks have managed some of their best one-day
gains amid the brutal bear market in the past three years, only to give the gains right back, as gloomy
economic news unfolds. Part of the reason is the short-covering, rather than out-and-out buying by bulls,
which can help to fuel rallies like yesterday's.
"There was a lot of short covering" yesterday, said Mr. Basel of Salomon Smith Barney. "That's not real
buying; these investors aren't in it for the long haul."
Just yesterday data was released showing how weak retail sales were in February, with sales falling 1.6%,
the worst one-month performance in 15 months. Some said the sales data was so disappointing that it might
encourage the Federal Reserve to cut interest rates next week.
At the same time, more weak data on first-time jobless claims underscored how the labor market has turned
weaker in just the past month or so, another problem for the market.
Bulls are hanging their hats on the argument that stock prices are so cheap, relative to the skimpy yields on
bonds, that stocks are a buy. Edward Keon, quantitative analyst at Prudential Securities, says that even
though earnings are still under pressure, and the world is a riskier place than a few years ago, "stocks are just
cheaper than bonds."
"There's no guarantee the world will be safer in six months but in the past when stocks were priced as though
they were very risky, in time that risk faded and stocks went up," he said.
Helping the stock market's spirits were heady gains in European markets. The FTSE-100 rose more than 6%,
its biggest gain since 1987, while the German DAX jumped almost 7%.
Outside the U.S., stocks rose in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, rose
1.65%, or 1.52, to 93.8.
In major U.S. market action:
Major stock indexes rose. On the Big Board, where 1.76 billion shares traded, 2,407 stocks rose and 892
declined.
Bonds fell. In addition to the 10-year note's drop, the 30-year bond dropped 2 4/32 to yield 4.741%.
The dollar rallied. Late in New York, the U.S. currency strengthened to $1.0807 per euro, from $1.1004. The
dollar fetched 118.54 yen, up from 117.29 the day before.

War Fears Send Markets Down; Treasury Yields Hit 44-Year Low
War Fears Send Markets Down; Treasury Yields Hit 44-Year Low
By E.S. Browning and Gregory Zuckerman
1,101 words
11 March 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
With investors wringing their hands over a possible war's impact on an already fragile economy, stocks
tumbled to a five-month low and the yield on 10-year Treasury notes fell to levels unseen in 44 years.
The immediate concern is that the United Nations debate over the conflict with Iraq is creating a diplomatic
morass that could weaken the U.S. internationally. The broader worry is that the lingering anxiety about
possible military action is paralyzing decision-making at U.S. corporations, postponing important spending
moves and holding back economic growth.
"Not only is this Iraq uncertainty keeping investors away from the market, but it also is hurting corporate
America," said Todd Clark, head of listed stock trading at Wells Fargo Securities, a unit of San Francisco
banking group Wells Fargo &Co. "Purchasing decisions are being postponed both in the industrial world and
on Wall Street."
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 171.85 points, or 2.22%, to 7568.18, marking a decline of 9.3% so far
this year. Volume was light. It was the lowest close for the industrials since Oct. 10, which was a day after the
blue chips hit a five-year low before beginning a rebound last fall.
As money flowed out of stocks, it poured into the relative safety of Treasury bonds, which reached levels that
many traders thought they never would see again. The yield of the benchmark 10-year Treasury note, which
declines as the bond's price rises, fell to 3.568%, its lowest level in late New York trading since the summer of
1958, according to Merrill Lynch. The bond's price rose 16/32, or $5 for each $1,000 invested.
There is one silver lining to the cloud: Mortgage interest rates are heavily influenced by the yields of
Treasury notes, meaning that the already rock-bottom rates could decline further.
But the immediate focus for investors was on the cloud, not the lining. The acrimony among Western allies
has worsened the already nasty fears investors have that something could go wrong with war plans. In
addition, if the U.S. goes to war without U.N. approval, the cost of the conflict's aftermath would largely be
borne by the U.S. and a few allies. The longer the uncertainty drags on, the more people find things to fear.
The war could go badly, for example, with Saddam Hussein using chemical or biological weapons. The U.S.
could be accused of killing civilians. Fighting could take longer than expected. The prewar debate itself could
drag on, prolonging the unease.
And that is just the immediate concern. On top of that is the fear that, even if the war is a quick success, as
many investors still expect, other problems will continue to lurk on the horizon. Those include the nuclear
programs of North Korea and Iran, and the continuing fear of terrorism.
Economic problems continue to percolate. William Poole, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of St. Louis,
said that the large size of government-sponsored mortgage-finance companies Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac
poses a potential threat to the economy. That pulled down the stocks of both Fannie and Freddie, and
weighed on financial stocks in general.
Oil prices, which have been soaring on worries that the U.S. could face a supply crunch during an Iraqi war,
pulled back somewhat after the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries -- which is already pumping
close to capacity -- asserted that the market is adequately supplied. But gold, which had fallen in recent days
after posting big gains, resumed its rise.
The risk to the economy "is definitely something that is on everybody's mind, that all this uncertainty over the
war could have a lasting economic impact, even after the war is resolved," said Taai Izushima, head of U.S.
stock trading at Daiwa Securities America. That is one reason that investors were worried by Friday's news
that the economy lost 308,000 nonfarm jobs in February, the biggest monthly drop since the period following
the terrorist attacks of Sept. 11, 2001.

--- Page 131 ---
Page 131 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Investors now fear that economists may have to start cutting their forecasts for economic growth in the first
part of the year, Mr. Izushima said.
The day's trading volume was below-average for the year, with only about 1.22 billion shares changing hands
on the New York Stock Exchange, below the year's average of more than 1.3 billion. The Nasdaq Stock
Market had its lightest trading day of the year, with only 1.1 billion shares traded. Volume has repeatedly
been light in recent weeks as many investors have taken to the sidelines as the war debate has dragged on.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 2.58%, or 21.41 points, to 807.48, its lowest level since
October. It is down 8.2% this year. The Nasdaq Composite Index declined 2.06%, or 26.92 points, to
1278.37, just above its low for this year. Its many technology stocks have held up better than most stocks
lately, and it is down 4.3% on the year.
As investors have soured on stocks they have shifted money to a wide variety of bonds, from super-safe
Treasurys to riskier "junk" bonds.
Treasurys, seen as among the safest investments available, often receive an influx of investment when global
tensions rise. While their yields are skimpy by historic standards, they remain positive, something that can't
be said for the direction of the major stock indicators.
The bond rally also reflects growing worries that the U.S. economy is bogged down. That means the Federal
Reserve is unlikely to raise interest rates any time soon, traders say, which could be a boon for bonds.
Some even think Fed policy makers could cut rates later this year, perhaps as soon as their next meeting,
one week from today.
Many investors remain hopeful for a stock rebound once the war uncertainty lifts, and some predict a sharp
rally. But yesterday, the mood was grim. The drop in the Dow industrials was the second-biggest of the year
in point or percentage terms, and the biggest since January. All 30 components of the Dow industrials fell, as
did all the companies in the Dow Jones Transportation Average and the Dow Jones Utility Average. It is only
the third time in the past three years that all these stocks fell on the same day.

Martial Plan: Is War-Linked Rally Inevitable?
Abreast of the Market
Martial Plan: Is War-Linked Rally Inevitable?
By E.S. Browning
1,707 words
10 March 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ACCORDING TO WALL STREET'S conventional wisdom, once war begins in Iraq, stocks will rise.
"When bullets fly, stocks are a buy," says Jon Brorson, who heads the growth-stock investment group at
money-management firm Neuberger Berman.
On its face, the idea sounds startling. War, after all, is a time of uncertainty and fear -- usually not a recipe for
stock-market gains. Yet many investors have bought into the idea that war gives a boost to stocks, and they
are preparing to buy shares if and when the U.S. moves against Iraq.
They paint this scenario: Before the war, the market typically sags amid hand-wringing and worry. But once
the fighting begins, patriotism and confidence replace the worry, and stocks rise.
That, more or less, is what happened after the U.S. entered Korea in 1950. The Dow Jones Industrial
Average was up 18% a year after the war began. That also is what happened during the Gulf War of
1990-1991, when the industrial average was up 24% after a year. And it is sort of what happened after the
U.S. entered World War II. Although the Dow industrials suffered during the first year of that war, they were
up 21% after two years.
But that isn't what always happens. For one thing, the hope for stock gains is based on the assumption that
the war will be quick and successful, which certainly isn't assured. With the exception of Vietnam, "we won all
the wars," notes Byron Wien, senior investment strategist at New York brokerage firm Morgan Stanley. But
victory isn't guaranteed.
And even if the U.S. emerges victorious, an examination of history suggests that things aren't as simple as
the common wisdom suggests. Following the 2001 attack on the Taliban and al Qaeda in Afghanistan, stocks
did jump, according to form. But continuing economic trouble then combined with terrorism fears to knock
stocks back down to new lows.
After the U.S. entered World War I in 1917, stocks fell sharply. It took them more than two years to recover
their losses.
Wartime experience suggests that even if the U.S. wins the war, a lot of other factors affect the market. "War
in and of itself isn't usually the major factor in how the markets perform," says Tim Hayes, global stock
strategist at Ned Davis Research in Venice, Fla. "The war's impact on employment, inflation and economic
performance, and therefore on corporate profits, is what is really important."
The period after U.S. entry into World War I coincided with mounting inflation, which also was the case
following U.S. entry into Vietnam. Because it is hard to pick a specific date for the start of the U.S.
involvement in Vietnam, it is hard to measure what the stock market did after that war began. But many
market analysts view the period from the late 1960s through the 1970s as an extended period of stock
weakness, with the market suffering from inflation, high interest rates, soaring oil prices and U.S. political
divisions.
One of the big questions for the stock market today is how the underlying factors -- inflation, business
investment, consumer confidence, investor confidence and stock valuations -- will play out. Even assuming
that stocks soar once the uncertainty over Iraq is resolved, either through military action or through a peaceful
settlement, the question is whether stocks will hold those gains. Stocks could enter a new bull market, but
they also could fall back again, prolonging the bear market.
By some measures, that extended bear market began three years ago today, when the Nasdaq Composite
Index peaked and began its decline. Last week, the hand-wringing over the looming war dominated stocks
once again, with the Dow industrials falling another 151.05 points, or 1.91%, to 7740.03, despite a gain of

--- Page 133 ---
Page 133 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
66.04 points, or 0.86%, on Friday. The Dow industrials now are down 34% from the record they hit in 2000,
while the Nasdaq is 74% off its record.
"We would love nothing more than to see this market take off," says Mr. Brorson of Neuberger. "We are
itching to get bullish. Unfortunately, we just aren't there." He, too, is betting on a big stock surge once war
begins or the conflict is resolved. "But it is going to be very, very short-lived," he says.
The broader factors just aren't there yet to permit lasting gains, he says. In the past, he notes, when stocks
were set to rebound, the broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index often was painfully cheap, based on the
earnings of its component companies. But today, even using favorable forecasts for future earnings, the S&P
500 is priced at no less than 16 times earnings, above its historic average of 15 or so, and even the most
bullish observers acknowledge that stocks can't be priced at above-average levels forever.
Mr. Brorson also worries about a demographic effect. More baby boomers are going to be retiring and taking
money out of stocks in coming years, which he thinks could hold the market back.
And, adds Mr. Wien of Morgan Stanley, "This situation is a little different because terrorism is involved." The
fear of terrorism could have as much impact on stocks as does the war's outcome.
Still, Mr. Wien is more bullish about the prospects.
"I think there might be a lasting rally," Mr. Wien says. Investors have been so gloomy that they have amassed
large amounts of cash on the sidelines, he says. A successful war could pull some of that into stocks. What's
more, inflation and interest rates, which tend to kill stock rallies, remain low. Low inflation means that
stocks may not need to fall to below-average valuations before a lasting rally can begin again.
"Stocks may not be cheap, but with relation to interest rates and inflation, stocks are attractive," he says.
His main worry is the more fundamental problems that afflict the economy: a declining dollar, a rising trade
deficit and the hunkered-down position of manufacturers.
Robert Morris, chief investment officer at Jersey City, N.J., fund-management group Lord Abbett, also thinks
stocks are overdue for a sustained recovery. But he doesn't think that a successful war in Iraq by itself will do
the trick. The U.S. needs to see consumer confidence improve, it needs to see more business investment,
and it needs to come to grips with the continuing threat of terrorism, he says.
"To the extent that the war being over, presumably with a positive outcome, lifts some of the angst," stocks
should enjoy at least a temporary rally, Mr. Morris says. "But I don't think you can say that that by itself will
change the fabric of the market. We have to see these other things."
---
Friday's Market Activity
Intel (Nasdaq) fell 69 cents, or 4.1%, to $16.01 after the chip maker said first-quarter revenue would come in
between $6.6 billion and $6.8 billion -- the lower end of the $6.5 billion to $7 billion target set in January --
because of weak first-quarter sales of its flash memory chips.
Applied Materials (Nasdaq) slipped nine cents, or 0.8%, to 11.96 while Advanced Micro Devices shed seven
cents, or 1.3%, to 5.25.

The Economy: Threat of War, High Fuel Prices Hobble Economy --- Fed Survey of Regions Finds Activity Was Subdued In January and February
The Economy: Threat of War, High Fuel Prices Hobble Economy --- Fed Survey of Regions Finds
Activity Was Subdued In January and February
By Greg Ip
485 words
6 March 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WASHINGTON -- The threat of war with Iraq and high energy prices cast a pall over the economy in January
and February, a survey prepared for Federal Reserve policy makers found.
"Growth in economic activity remained subdued in January and February," according to the Fed's latest
"beige book" report on regional economic conditions. "Consumer spending remained weak, on balance . . .
Business spending was very soft, with little change in capital spending or hiring plans."
The beige book collects anecdotes of business conditions throughout the Fed's 12 districts to help policy
makers set the course of interest rates. Under normal circumstances, the dreary tone of the report released
yesterday would seem to support a cut in the Fed's short-term interest-rate target -- now at a 41-year low of
1.25% -- when Fed policy makers meet March 18.
But Fed policy makers believe most of the economy's current weakness is due to uncertainty over a possible
war with Iraq and are counting on an imminent resolution to spur business confidence and investment.
Without such a rebound, a rate cut will probably follow soon, perhaps before the following meeting on May 7.
Futures markets put the odds of a quarter-percentage point rate cut by August at 78%.
Both consumer and business spending are slack, the survey found. One Minneapolis district dealer said auto
sales have "fallen off a cliff." Chicago district retailers said January sales fell short of "conservative
expectations." However, sales of duct tape and plastic sheeting rose because of terrorism fears.
In the Boston district, software and technology suppliers "expect flat to deteriorating demand for the next
quarter, partly reflecting increased geopolitical risk." One North Carolina plastics manufacturer said, "There
will likely be no sign of a real pickup in activity until the Iraq situation is cleared up." However, one Dallas
district technology company said that "after so little spending in the past couple of years, [businesses] feel the
need to replace old equipment."
Snowstorms hurt retail sales in many states, but boosted tourism in Miami and the upper peninsula of
Michigan. One Chicago district contact said that "frighteningly high" diesel prices could bankrupt some small
freight carriers. Other than that, inflation pressures were subdued, although the Kansas City, Mo., district
reported, "Jewelry prices edged up due to recent increases in the price of gold."
Separately, the Institute for Supply Management said service-sector activity grew at a moderate pace in
February, though more slowly than in January. Its index of nonmanufacturing activity slipped to 53.9 from
54.5 in January. Readings above 50 indicate expansion of activity while under 50 denote contraction. Its
index of new orders fell to 53 from 56.2 and its index of employment dropped to 49 from 50.3.

American Conservatism: The Presidents
American Conservatism: The Presidents
By Michael Barone
1,400 words
3 March 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[This is the 11th in an occasional series.]
Liberals have their pantheon of presidents, established by the New Deal historians. "Great presidents," in
their view, are those who expand the size and scope of the federal government in the interest of the masses
against the interests of the classes. By this criterion Franklin Roosevelt is one of the greatest presidents, in a
line that includes Washington, Jefferson, Jackson, Lincoln, Theodore Roosevelt and Wilson.
Later Democrats, alas, are not easy to classify as great. And when one looks back on the liberal pantheon,
one finds that few precisely fit the Franklin Roosevelt template. Washington was supported by the
Federalists, the one American party that identified with the classes against the masses, and that accordingly
disappeared. American politics ever since has been a struggle of the masses versus the masses.
Jackson believed in an extreme form of economic laissez-faire, and would have been aghast at FDR's
public-works projects. Lincoln was a Republican and, had he lived, this former railroad lawyer would surely
have favored economic policies the New Dealers abhorred. And history, we have learned in the last
quarter-century, does not always move left. The masses sometimes decide that in their name you have
expanded government too much and should pare it back.
---
The American presidency is by its nature not a very conservative office. The Federalist called for "energy in
the executive": Presidents do things, they don't just let things be.
But conservatives these days are tempted to build a presidential pantheon of their own. Ronald Reagan is
their template, as FDR was for the New Dealers: hence Grover Norquist's project of getting things named
after him. George W. Bush is often advanced as another Reagan, and a decaffeinated case can be made for
his father as well. But who else? Gerald Ford was opposed by Mr. Reagan. Nixon started EPA, OSHA and
racial quotas and preferences, went off gold and inflated the currency, and entered into detente with the
Soviets. Eisenhower was the quintessential liberal Republican. Hoover built great government institutions.
Coolidge's picture was on Mr. Reagan's wall, but he is not Rushmore material. A case can be made for
Harding, but never will. Taft was defeated for re-election and signed the income tax. And so on.
Washington and Lincoln can certainly be claimed as great conservative presidents. Yet Washington was also,
literally, a revolutionary, and Lincoln vastly expanded the government, levied an income tax, debased the
currency and often violated civil liberties. You can justify Washington's revolution as a response to tyranny
and Lincoln's big government as a response to rebellion, and argue that they were conservatives in a larger
sense. But they're not a perfect fit for the Reagan template, any more than Jackson was a perfect fit for the
New Dealers' FDR template.
So rather than try to construct a pantheon of conservative presidents based on today's definition of
conservatism, it is more useful to see what part presidents have played in furthering the conservative
enterprise in government -- to create a framework in which market economics can work its miracles, to pass
laws which preserve liberty and encourage virtuous behavior (and to repeal laws which encourage vicious
behavior), and to defend the nation against enemies foreign and domestic. In our decentralized system of
government and with our rich civil society, only some of the work of furthering this enterprise is done by
presidents.
Consider the question of creating a framework in which market economics can thrive. For that you need a
stable currency, enforcement of contracts, a rule of law that enables people to accumulate and invest capital.
For most of our history, these things have not been presidents' work. In Washington's administration,
Alexander Hamilton established a central bank and a funded debt. But the work of establishing a rule of law
was done by Chief Justice John Marshall and the Supreme Court, by state courts and by state legislatures

--- Page 137 ---
Page 137 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
which passed incorporation laws. Presidents have debased the currency -- Jackson by abolishing the central
bank, Lincoln by printing greenbacks, Wilson and Roosevelt through wartime inflation, Nixon by going off
gold. But stabilizing the currency and steadying financial markets have been mostly the work of others -- J. P.
Morgan for a time and later the Federal Reserve.
Presidents have played a role in passing laws which have a conservatizing effect on society. Lincoln's
Homestead Act and land-grant colleges, Franklin Roosevelt's FHA and VA home mortgage loans and the G.I.
Bill of Rights. These laws encourage, subsidize, reward and honor those who educate themselves and
accumulate wealth.
The Founders thought that one reason their republic could prosper was that almost all voters owned property;
by the early 20th century that was no longer so, as the big cities filled up with a propertyless proletariat. Laws
that transformed a nation of renters into a nation of homeowners have made America inherently more
conservative. Similarly, the securities laws which have enabled ordinary people to invest with confidence
have transformed us from a nation where most voters do not have financial wealth into a nation where most
voters do. Inconveniently for those who would construct a conservative pantheon, the presidents who signed
these laws are usually classified as liberals.
Many conservative policy successes have not been the work of presidents at all. Welfare reduction and crime
control, the great successes of the 1990s, were the product of state and local officials like Wisconsin's Gov.
Tommy Thompson and New York's Mayor Rudolph Giuliani. Bill Clinton was just an interested and
occasionally helpful bystander. The Bush education policy, similarly, just builds on the work of governors
whose ranks include Mr. Bush himself. But there is no consistent conservative position on how the state
should balance the claims of liberty and of morality. Today's conservatives opt for morality on abortion and
liberty on guns; liberals the other way around.
In the past, Stephen Douglas argued for the liberty of the slaveowner, Lincoln for the morality of restricting the
expansion of slavery -- an easy issue for us, but controversial at the time. Prohibition was another such issue:
Was it conservative to allow people to drink or to pass laws to encourage them to live virtuous lives?
Classifying presidents as conservative on such issues is not useful work.
Providing for the common defense seems a conservative cause, and yet the work of maintaining a military
establishment and mobilizing for war profoundly changes society: war, usually, is the health of the state.
Before the Spanish-American War, America was, in Walter McDougal's term, a "promised land" in which
presidents concentrated on protecting and expanding the U.S. Jefferson's Louisiana Purchase, John Quincy
Adams's aggressive diplomacy, Polk's war against Mexico and Lincoln's Civil War all contributed to this goal;
but were they conservative? Since 1898 America has been, in McDougal's words, a "crusader state"
determined to advance American interests and American ideals in the world. With increasing success: Wilson
in World War I, Roosevelt in World War II, Truman in undertaking the Cold War and Mr. Reagan and the first
Mr. Bush in concluding it.
We have come to think that the willingness to defend and advance America's interests is a conservative
enterprise. But it has often been the enterprise of liberals as well. George W. Bush's policy on Iraq is
supported not just by conservatives but also by liberals like Joseph Lieberman and Richard Gephardt.
"Put not your trust in princes," Lord Strafford said as he was facing execution, abandoned by his master
Charles I. Conservatives who rely on presidents to achieve their goals will mostly be disappointed. The
success of the conservative enterprise depends on individuals in the marketplace and the efforts of Burke's
"little platoons" in promoting virtuous behavior. George W. Bush's policies of enlarging the space for individual
choices in education, health care and Social Security, and his policy of defending the nation in the war on
terrorism, serve conservative purposes. But by themselves they do not achieve conservative goals. That is up
to all of us.
---
Mr. Barone, a senior writer at U.S. News &World Report, is a contributor to the Fox News Channel and
co-author of The Almanac of American Politics (National Journal).

Golden Years?
Golden Years?
By Lawrence Kudlow
834 words
26 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The pending war in Iraq has put an uncertainty premium on virtually all forms of investment. But it's not Iraq
that holds the key to our economic future. It's Alan Greenspan and the Federal Reserve.
The fog of war should not obscure the fact that the Fed's money policy is critical to economic recovery.
Deflation has been the major obstacle to economic and stock-market recovery. Falling prices from a shortage
of money have robbed businesses of the pricing power they need to generate profits. But today it looks like
the Fed's monetary policy has truly shifted from deflationary to reflationary, and that America's corporations
will soon be in the money again. If so, there is good reason for investors to flock back to Wall Street.
One key barometer of monetary policy is the movement of commodity prices, including gold. When gold
descended in price between early 1997 and mid-2001, it signaled that the Fed was getting tighter with the
cash. For much of the time, the Greenspan Fed was actually raising the federal funds interest rate -- even
while gold was dropping. This was the Fed at its deflationary worst. Meanwhile, businesses and the economy
-- thirsty for money -- began drying up.
In 2001 the Fed lowered its target interest rate 11 times in an attempt to reflate the economy. But these rate
cuts merely made up for lost time. It took until July 2002 for a rising gold price to indicate that Fed
money-supplying was beginning to satisfy private-sector cash demands, and until November last year for
gold to finally signal a true position of monetary ease.
The uncertainties of war could take the stock market below its October 2002 bottom. But the fact that the Fed
is indeed easing suggests that we've seen the worst. The question is, will the money keep flowing?
More than likely, Fed policy will stay easy until the economy produces stronger growth and lower
unemployment. This probably means the Fed will hold its target interest rate at 1% for the remainder of the
year. Such steadiness will be a plus for stocks.
But that doesn't mean Fed policy is on target. In the late 1990s, Mr. Greenspan obsessed about stock-market
prices and irrational exuberance. He ignored gold and commodity indicators -- which said to keep the money
coming -- and we slipped into prolonged deflation. As share prices plummeted, business wealth deflated,
causing a lengthy contraction of business investment, production, and jobs.
It could happen again.
If Mr. Greenspan once more limits stock-market wealth and economic growth, we could see a repeat of the
tight policy actions that failed so miserably only a few years ago. However, if he targets sensitive commodity
prices, including gold, stocks and the economy will be the long-run winners.
President Bush has proposed a powerful pro-investment and pro-growth tax-cut package. If enacted, his
investment-tax incentives will help the economy grow faster than the 3% to 4% rate of productivity.
Unemployment will drop. But the Bush plan requires a steady flow of new cash for true firepower. Simply put,
more money will be needed to finance the creation of more goods. If the Fed frets about a rising stock market
or economy, they will not properly accommodate the Bush tax plan. But if they watch gold and commodity
prices, they'll stay right on target.
Greenspan &Co. should also take another look at the Fed's job description. The Federal Reserve is in place
to determine the appropriate rate of money creation. Nowhere does it say they have authority over fiscal
policy. Yet while Mr. Greenspan favors ending the double taxation of corporate dividends, he is highly
skeptical of the need for other growth-oriented tax cuts. This is just what he said after 9/11, and he was
wrong. The economy has since been moving at a subpar pace precisely because stimulative tax cuts were
not put into action.

--- Page 139 ---
Page 139 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The person who has had the story right over the past two years is President Bush's top economist, Glenn
Hubbard -- the man behind the big-bang stimulus package. When Mr. Greenspan's fourth term comes to a
close next year, doesn't Mr. Hubbard deserve consideration for the post?
Meanwhile, someone from the Republican-controlled White House, Treasury, Senate, or House should be
raising the monetary issue. Although you'd never know it, Congress does in fact have supervisory authority
over the central bank. Now's the time to use it. The ability of the U.S. economy to mount another 10-year
boom, one that will greatly enhance American prestige abroad as well as prosperity at home, will depend on
the Fed targeting real-time price indicators like commodities and gold, and keeping the money spigots open.
Washington must get Alan Greenspan on board with growth.
---
Mr. Kudlow is president of Kudlow and Co. and co-host of CNBC's "Kudlow &Cramer."

Dow Industrials Test Year's Low Before Rising 51.26 Points
Tuesday's Markets
Dow Industrials Test Year's Low Before Rising 51.26 Points
By E.S. Browning
634 words
26 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER DIPPING BRIEFLY beneath its low for the year, the Dow Jones Industrial Average rebounded to
recover the day's losses and more.
Helped by a fresh rash of rumors during the trading session that a peaceful solution to the Iraq crisis might be
found, and that Saddam Hussein might seek exile, the Dow industrials finished the day up 51.26 points, or
0.65%, at 7909.50.
In the morning, the blue-chip average had been down as much as 138.60 points, or 1.76%, amid worries
about Iraq and North Korea as well as news that consumer confidence had fallen to its lowest level since
1993. The industrial average is down 5% so far this year.
The exile rumors circulated among professional investors such as hedge funds, which often make short-term
bets on stocks and trade actively during the day. There wasn't any indication the rumors were true, but the
talk appeared to help fuel the rebound.
"It shows how jittery market is," said Keith Gertsen, head of Nasdaq trading at brokerage firm Deutsche Bank
Securities. "It will rally on the slightest rumor or speculation that there will be a nonmilitary resolution to the
conflict."
Another reason for the market volatility is that trading has been light, meaning stocks will often move on
rumors. Although trading volume finished the day at an average level, it was soft before the market began to
move sharply during the afternoon.
The rally also was reinforced by the fact that the Dow industrials fell below their lowest closing level of the
year, 7749.87, the level at which they finished Feb. 13. Buyers often materialize when stocks hit a level at
which they have turned up in the past. The buyers theorize that if stocks were viewed as cheap at this level
before, they might be viewed that way again. Such bargain hunting, together with the rumors, helped stocks,
but the phenomenon left some traders skeptical.
The rebound "was good to see, but I think we are surrounded by nonbelievers," said Andy Brooks, head of
stock trading at Baltimore mutual-fund firm T. Rowe Price. "People are afraid to make a really strong
statement either way because of the potential to be whipsawed."
The day had a bad beginning, with North Korea firing a missile into the Sea of Japan. Stocks fell in Asia, and
the selling spread as European and U.S. markets opened.
Then there was the news that Mr. Hussein indicated in an interview with CBS News anchorman Dan Rather
that Iraq was resisting a United Nations demand to destroy missiles. The latest consumer-confidence
numbers, battered by war worries, came in far weaker than expected.
Existing-home sales did jump unexpectedly in January to another record, pushed ahead by low interest
rates and demand for alternative investments, but investors initially ignored that news.
Crude-oil prices moved close to the 29-month high of more than $37 a barrel they hit last week, but then fell
and finished down. Treasury bonds rose, with the 10-year note up 8/32, or $2.50 for each $1,000 invested, to
push down the yield to 3.817%. But gold fell and the dollar was mixed.
The broad S&P 500 index rose 0.72%, or 5.99 points, to 838.57, down 4.7% on the year. The Nasdaq
Composite Index rose 0.50%, or 6.60 points, to 1328.98, down 0.5% this year.

"The market appears to be trying to build a base" from which it can rise, said Alan Ackerman, market
strategist at New York brokerage firm Fahnstock. "But you've got more bears on Wall Street than in Yosemite
Park."

INVESTORS CONTINUE to worry that the looming war is holding back the economy, and yesterday a slew of

A Fed Without Reserve
Bookshelf
LEISURE &ARTS
A Fed Without Reserve
By Mary Anastasia O'Grady
906 words
20 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
D8
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A History of the Federal Reserve
By Allan H. Meltzer
(University of Chicago, 800 pages, $75)
It was "an unprecedented orgy of extravagance, a mania for speculation, overextended business in nearly all
lines and in every section of the country."
An Alan Greenspan rumination about the irrational exuberance of the late 1990s? Try the 1920 annual report
of the board of governors of the Federal Reserve. The governors had noted the Fed's failure "to check
expansion" in their 1919 annual report and even cited the chief cause: the absence of a penalty rate for
borrowing by commercial banks -- that is, a Fed lending rate higher than what commercial banks would pay
to borrow in the market. The Fed had a chance to impose such a rate and thus damp demand but failed to do
so. The boom was followed by a sharp decline. A similar failure occurred in the early days of the Great
Depression, when Fed officials noted an economic contraction but did nothing to pump money into the
system in response.
To understand why the Fed acted as it did -- at these critical moments and many others -- would require
years of study, poring over letters, the minutes of meetings and internal Fed documents. Such a task would
naturally deter most scholars of economic history but not, thank goodness, Allan Meltzer, an economist at
Carnegie Mellon University in Pittsburgh. He has now produced "A History of the Federal Reserve," the first
volume of a multivolume project, this one covering the years 1913-51. It is a masterly work bringing to the
confusing events of the period, as Mr. Greenspan writes in the preface, a "methodical illumination."
"One set of questions in particular warranted attention," Mr. Meltzer states. Why was monetary policy often
"pro-cyclical," intensifying trends instead of countering them? Why, for instance, had the Fed "failed to
respond to the Great Depression or to the deep recession of 1937-38?"
Part of the answer is that it had a limited sense of how money, international gold flows and credit worked --
and how they affected economies. What was easily observed, and painfully, was that periods of rapid growth
and seeming prosperity were often followed by panics and collapses. All banking reforms -- five in the 30
years leading up to the founding of the Fed in 1913 -- were aimed at reducing "the frequency and severity of
crises."
At the time, central banks were private. They had an implicit public responsibility to provide liquidity during a
time of crisis, but they also had a private interest in protecting themselves, to "shrink their balance sheets"
when panic hit -- i.e, to reduce lending and risk. Woodrow Wilson hoped to solve this problem when he
proposed "a public-private partnership with semiautonomous, privately funded reserve banks supervised by a
public board." Thus was born the Federal Reserve.
Its 12 banks were to represent commercial, agricultural, industrial and financial interests in each of the 12
Fed regions. Gold reserves would be pooled to make the total available in a crisis. But as Mr. Meltzer
remarks, "the structure of the system did not concentrate decision-making authority and responsibility." Thus
a struggle for power "broke out early" between the reserve banks and the board and later among regional
interests -- notably agricultural interests and eastern (New York) financial ones. There were other problems
too: confusions over economic theory, clashing personalities and pressure from the Treasury to keep the cost
of federal borrowing low.
Perhaps most important, policymakers had injected a pro-cyclical bias into the Fed when it was created, by
making the financing of the real economy -- commerce, industry, agriculture -- its primary objective. As a

--- Page 144 ---
Page 144 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
result, the Fed stimulated the economy when it was rapidly expanding (today it would try to cool it down) and
did nothing to prime the pump during recessions, when the demand for money was low. Thus the 1920-22
recession was unnecessarily harsh, and "the seven years of stability" between 1922 and 1929 were the
exception, "not the rule." Without a dominant objective of price stability, the Fed failed to avoid deflation or to
hold back inflationary expansion, a problem that persisted for many decades.
The rise in the stock market that ended in 1929, writes Mr. Meltzer, was "extraordinary by almost any
standard except 1998-2000." What brought it to an end? The "evidence suggests that deflationary monetary
policies in all the gold-standard countries induced reductions in output abroad that later spread to the United
States and other countries." Put another way: A world-wide contraction in money reduced demand. "The
stock market responded to the actual and anticipated decline in profits."
The Depression that followed was "mainly a monetary event." Rejecting the extreme positions -- that it was all
about money or not at all -- Mr. Meltzer writes that the "more plausible explanation is that the depth and
severity of the Great Depression were the consequence of a series of shocks" -- including tariff increases,
gold movements and the stock-market drop -- "that the Federal Reserve neglected or failed to offset
completely."
Had the Fed pursued price stability as a priority, it might have responded differently -- by preventing the
collapse of the money supply. The history of the 20th century might have unfolded differently too.
---
Ms. O'Grady, a senior editorial writer for the Wall Street Journal, edits the Journal's Americas column.

Blue Chips Fall 84.94 Points, Off 7% for Year
Wednesday's Markets
Blue Chips Fall 84.94 Points, Off 7% for Year
By E.S. Browning
489 words
13 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average fell to another four-month low on continuing worries about war and
terrorism.
The blue-chip average pulled back 84.94 points, or 1.08%, to 7758.17. It now is down 7% since the year
began and is 34% below its record close of 11722.98, hit on Jan. 14, 2000.
With investors reluctant to put money into stocks in advance of a possible war, trading volume was below
average.
"On the one hand, a war with Iraq could be protracted, messy and could tip the economy back into recession.
On the other, it could be quick, successful and spark a resurgence in the economy," said Paul McManus,
director of fundamental research at Boston money-management firm Independence Investments, in a report.
Yesterday, U.S. officials said that antiaircraft missiles had been deployed around Washington and that a
terror attack could occur as soon as this week. Missile experts called in by U.N. inspectors said Iraqi missile
engines had a range beyond that allowed by the U.N. Security Council.
In addition, CIA director George Tenet told the Senate Armed Services Committee that North Korea has an
untested ballistic missile capable of delivering a nuclear weapon to the Western U.S.
It all served to keep investors on edge. The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index declined 1.27%, or
10.52 points, to 818.68, down 7% this year and 46% off its record, which also dates from 2000.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, dominated by large technology stocks, gave up 1.27%, or 16.49 points, to
finish at 1278.97, off 4% this year and 75% below its record, also from 2000.
Gold fell, as investors continued to take profits after a strong run-up caused by "safe-haven" buying. Oil
continued to rise, nearing a two-year high. The dollar and Treasury bonds also rose.
Analysts also pointed to a warning from semiconductor-equipment maker Applied Materials Inc. that its
earnings for the current quarter will be in the low end of Wall Street expectations, which weighed on
technology stocks.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.82%, or 0.82 point, to 99.46.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.24 billion shares traded, 997 stocks rose and 2,222 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 13/32, or $4.0625 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, fell to 3.907%. The 30-year bond rose 6/32 to yield 4.850%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 121.29 yen, up from 120.99, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.0717 from $1.0733.
