Blue Chips Decline To Four-Month Low; Focus Remains Iraq
Tuesday's Markets
Blue Chips Decline To Four-Month Low; Focus Remains Iraq
By E.S. Browning
748 words
12 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A DAY AFTER breaking a four-session losing streak, the Dow Jones Industrial Average fell to its lowest level
in four months as investors continued to focus on Iraq and terrorism.
After being up 65.41 points amid mildly upbeat Senate testimony from Federal Reserve Chairman Alan
Greenspan, the industrials turned sharply down late in the morning, as Secretary of State Colin Powell
disclosed, elsewhere on Capitol Hill, a new tape that apparently came from terrorist leader Osama bin Laden.
Mr. Powell said the tape, in which a voice thought to be that of the al Qaeda leader declared support for the
Iraqi people and urged them to commit attacks against Americans, showed that Iraq and al Qaeda were
linked.
Commentators debated the tape's meaning throughout the day, but the Dow industrials didn't wait for the
issue to be clarified. They finished the day down 77 points, or 0.97%, at 7843.11, a decline of 6% so far this
year. That is the Dow average's lowest finish so far this year, and its lowest close since Oct. 10, the day after
stocks hit a five-year low and began a brief rally last year.
Michael Driscoll at New York brokerage firm Bear Stearns said some investors might have been "spooked" by
the tape, or at least used it as an excuse to avoid buying. "Psyches are very, very fragile, so, when in doubt,
sell," he said.
Earlier in the day, it looked as if stocks were headed up for a second consecutive day. Investors took heart at
Mr. Greenspan's comments to the Senate Banking Committee, in which the Fed chief said he thinks the
economy will turn up more strongly once the Iraq situation is resolved. If the economy didn't strengthen
sufficiently, he indicated, he would be prepared to cut interest rates or take other steps to stimulate it.
But as Mr. Greenspan spoke, Mr. Powell was testifying to the Senate Budget Committee on unrelated issues,
and used the opportunity to disclose news of the tape. Market attention shifted to Mr. Powell.
Many investors say they simply aren't prepared to commit new money to stocks until the war risks are
somehow resolved. They note that there isn't any guarantee that the war will go smoothly, and they say that
as long as the issue is up in the air, the stock market won't be able to rise. Investor nerves affected a variety
of financial markets. Stock-trading volume again was below average, and war fears helped push up oil prices
again. Gold, however, began the day down amid the stock gains, and didn't get back into the plus column.
Treasury bonds and the dollar were mixed, showing little overall change.
Investors looked past favorable profit news from the likes of Aetna, Clorox and Deere, and also shrugged off
Qualcomm's announcement that it would pay a dividend for the first time.
"We see a lot of stocks that are cheap and that would be good buys, but we aren't rushing to buy them
because we see a lot of political headwinds involving the war," explained Susan Malley, president of New
York money-management firm Malley Associates Capital Management.
Ms. Malley said her firm started the year with nearly one-third of its assets in cash, and is continuing to hold
that money out of stocks until the war situation becomes clearer.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.81%, or 6.77 points, to 829.20, while the Nasdaq
Composite Index declined 0.09%, or 1.22 points, to 1295.46.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 1.36%, or 1.35 points, to 100.28.

Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.3 billion shares traded, 1,337 stocks rose and 1,930 fell.
Bonds were mixed. The 10-year Treasury note rose 3/32, or 93.75 cents for each $1,000 invested. The yield,
which moves inversely to price, fell to 3.957%. The 30-year bond was down 5/32 to yield 4.860%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 120.99 yen, down from 121.29, while the euro fell
against the dollar to $1.0733 from $1.0739.

The Economic Fog of War
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Economic Fog of War
819 words
12 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The long and negotiated march to war -- or not war -- has generated lots of problems, including Jacques
Chirac and Kim Jong Il. But it's the impact on the economy that has us brooding today. And that's the problem
of fear and uncertainty.
While fear is a sensible and necessary reaction to war, when it combines with a prolonged period of
uncertainty, people's horizons narrow and their ability to act is impaired. As Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan
told the Senate yesterday, in his own peculiar fashion: "The heightening of geopolitical tensions has only
added to the marked uncertainties that have piled up over the past three years, creating formidable barriers to
new investment and thus to a resumption of vigorous expansion of overall economic activity."
The result is now obvious. The economy logged a dismal growth of 0.7% in the last quarter of 2002. Business
continued to restrain capital spending, and consumers, their confidence slipping, retreated. Meanwhile, the
stock market is sagging and the dollar, after dropping double-digits last year, is falling again this year. And let
us not forget the bearish rush to gold: War anxieties have driven its price to a five-year high.
Then there's oil, now more than $34 a barrel. The impact of $30-or-more oil, even over several months, can
be decidedly unpleasant: the last four recessions were associated with oil prices of more than $30 a barrel.
Some of today's price increase is due to the tightening of supply from the oil strike in Venezuela, but some is
also due to a war premium from uncertainty. Oil market uncertainty expresses itself in hoarding -- or
husbanding, as industry likes to call it. As war becomes imminent, the value of oil held in inventory increases
as companies wonder whether they can replace their stocks and at what price. This reaction is nothing more
than the classic approach of buying on the rumor and selling on the fact.
This is exactly what happened during the Gulf War when the price of oil streaked from $16 a barrel in June
1990, right before Saddam sent troops to the Kuwait border, to $23 in August when Saddam invaded Kuwait,
to peak at more than $40 by October. Prices hovered around $30 until January 1991 when the U.S. began air
strikes -- a dramatic end to uncertainty. Prices then fell to about $20 a barrel and stayed mostly below that
mark over the next four years.
While no one doubts the U.S. will win a war with Iraq, no one really knows how much damage will be done
along the way. If Saddam blows up Iraq's oil wells, prices could shoot up to $60 to $70 for a while and then
fall back to a higher level of $40 because it will take time to bring back all production.
After the first Gulf war, it took Kuwait nine months to put out the oil-well fires but two years before full
production was resumed. Ditto if Saddam damages shipping ports -- about 10% of world oil consumption
moves through Saudi export facilities every day. In the most optimistic scenario, Iraqi oil fields would not be
damaged and production would start as soon as fighting stops. In this case, oil prices might spike only to $36
or $40 a barrel and then decline to $20.
Looking at these two scenarios, it is clear how fear and uncertainty can arise. An increase in the price of oil
functions like a tax on the economy. If all goes well -- say, a three-month war with oil averaging $35 a barrel --
the tax would come to roughly $17 billion. But with a six-month war with oil averaging $55 a barrel the tax
would balloon to roughly $102.5 billion. That's a very wide range of economic impact to consider when
making business plans or spending decisions.
One way to reduce this uncertainty would be a firm declaration that the U.S. is ready to release oil from its
reserves. Currently the U.S. has 600 million barrels on tap. That could easily provide four million barrels a day
for over three months. That's more than double the amount now produced by Iraq and more than enough to
keep oil prices from rocketing.
We agree with Mr. Greenspan's remarks yesterday that right now war fear and uncertainty are eclipsing a
potentially encouraging post war economic environment. Oil prices will sink sooner or later, perhaps to as low

--- Page 149 ---
Page 149 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
as $20, a lower dollar will stimulate exports and the stock market will enjoy a traditional postwar rally. Most
important, the danger associated with having a murderous lunatic in control of weapons of mass destruction
will end.
The long march to war has brought us to a rough economic patch, but on the other side of victory lies
prosperity.

Credit Cards Swipe Sears Profits
Heard on the Street
Credit Cards Swipe Sears Profits
By Joe Hallinan and Amy Merrick
1,342 words
11 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IN THE MIDST of an ambitious turnaround plan, Sears, Roebuck &Co. is optimistic about improving its profits
this year. But it will take a lot of washing machines, power tools and Lands' End turtlenecks to overcome the
plastic problem.
Sears says it has taken action to fix its credit-card business, after stunning investors last fall with a series of
revelations about credit woes. But a closer look shows that credit problems could continue to jeopardize the
overall turnaround plan -- and hold down the price of a stock that lost nearly half its value last year, some
investors and analysts say.
Sears is the third-largest MasterCard issuer in the world, trailing behind only Citigroup Inc. and MBNA Corp.,
according to Nilson Report, an industry periodical in Oxnard, Calif. Sears launched the card just over two
years ago, part of an attempt to counter a loss of market share by its proprietary "blue" card, which is good for
use only in Sears stores. Thanks to the two cards, Sears's credit and financial-products segment provided
$1.5 billion, or 60%, of the company's operating income in the fiscal year that ended Dec. 28. Its retail
segment, by comparison, provided just $1.16 billion.
So when the company says, as it did last month, that it expects the earnings benchmark that it uses with Wall
Street to increase 5% this year, Sears will need its ailing retail division to post better than 5% earnings
growth, to make up for the sliding results in the huge credit-card business. (The earnings benchmark
excludes what Sears calls "noncomparable items," though the accounting rules generally classify those items
as ordinary gains and expenses.)
Yet Sears, still tweaking its store formats and product assortments in the wake of its acquisition of Lands'
End, suffered a 4.6% drop in comparable store sales in December from the year-earlier period, and such
same-store sales dropped 8% in January. That prompted the company on Thursday to issue a gloomy
first-quarter earnings forecast that was well below what analysts were predicting. Shares of its stock fell
nearly 9% that day and have since continued to slide. In 4 p.m. New York Stock Exchange composite trading
yesterday, Sears shares fell 36 cents to $22.88.
"Obviously, Sears was hit by the same bug as the rest of the retailing industry this holiday season," says
analyst Carol Levenson of Gimme Credit, an independent credit-research firm.
For its part, Sears, based in Hoffman Estates, Ill., says it generally isn't counting on rising store sales to lift
profits in either its retail or its credit businesses. Instead, it expects to benefit from major cost cuts made last
year. The company predicts that the better retail profits will more than offset the decline in credit-card income.
Among other steps it has taken to improve its credit-card operations, the company in October abruptly
dismissed the president of its credit division.
But by Sears's own admission, losses on its credit-card portfolio seem certain to get worse. Charge-offs
typically increase as young portfolios, such as the MasterCard one held by Sears, mature. In fact, Sears says
it expects its MasterCard charge-off rate, 3.63% of account balances as of Sept. 30, to nearly double,
peaking late this year between 6.5% and 7%.
Sears maintains it is adequately reserved against the increase, with $1.78 billion set aside. But some analysts
and investors say they would prefer to see Sears slow down its earnings growth and instead bolster its
reserve. In the fourth quarter, Sears sharply increased its provision for uncollectible accounts by 40%, but the
provision was down slightly relative to the prior quarter.
"We found the increasingly high delinquency statistics disturbing, and question whether it's prudent to cut
back on the provisioning just yet," noted Gimme Credit's Ms. Levenson, in a report after the company's Jan.
16 announcement of 2002 and fourth-quarter results. She added: "Sears desperately needs its credit

--- Page 151 ---
Page 151 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
operations to offset its rocky retailing results, but right now it appears Lands' End might be the only
profitability savior." Her conclusion for bond investors: It is "premature to recommend" Sears's
commercial-paper debt, or short-term IOUs.
Among other reasons why some analysts and investors fret: Sears's credit clientele seems more troubled
than the average American. "It's one we would expect to experience more credit challenges," says James E.
Moss, an analyst with Fitch Ratings. Bankruptcies among the retailer's credit-card holders soared 20% in the
third quarter from year-earlier levels, and vaulted 26% more in the fourth quarter. That is far higher than the
7.8% increase recorded nationwide for personal bankruptcies in the year ended Sept. 30. Sears contends its
increase is represented in dollars, not individual filers, and isn't out of line with the national statistics. (The
national statistics, from administrators of the U.S. court system, come only in the number of filings, not dollar
amounts.)
Sears says its customers are no less creditworthy than average Americans. The company says its active
MasterCard customers have an average credit score compiled by credit specialists Fair, Isaac &Co. of 720,
which roughly places them in the middle of the pack of American consumers. But Sears declines to release
the scores for its inactive MasterCard accounts or for its proprietary blue card. Sears has some 60 million
credit accounts, of which 25 million are active, meaning they have been used in the past year. Of those 25
million, roughly 16 million are Sears blue cards; the rest are gold MasterCards.
It is difficult to compare Sears's credit-card operations with those of rivals. Most credit-card issuers begin to
write off customer accounts once they are delinquent for 180 days, but Sears doesn't take action until 240
days have passed. And at Sears, a customer who is 240 days behind can become current by making two
payments, each of as little as 1/45th of the outstanding balance. Sears declines to say what percent of its
accounts has undergone this so-called re-aging process. But Sears Chairman and Chief Executive Officer
Alan J. Lacy says the number is small. "Pick one of the smallest numbers you can think of," he says. "And it's
about that."
Another potential concern for Sears is the impact of rising interest rates on the company's own debt load. At
the end of the third quarter, some 86% of Sears's domestic debt was at a variable rate, versus just 60% the
year before. Based on the $24.2 billion size of its variable-rate funding portfolio, Sears estimates a
one-percentage point increase in interest rates could cost it $242 million per year before taxes.
Sears's ability to pass such increased costs onto its customers may be limited. The chief draw of its
MasterCard is its relatively moderate annual interest rate, which generally ranges between 13.9% and 21.9%.
Bump that rate up too much, and cardholders could simply defect to a cheaper card -- or to one that offers
perks like frequent-flier miles.
But Sears says it can keep its customers happy by offering in-store discounts, as well as adding out-of-store
benefits like coupons good for discounted meals at Chili's Grill &Bar, a division of Dallas-based Brinker
International Inc.
Moody's Investors Service adds that Sears's asset-backed debt has grown to 47% of the company's capital
structure from 37% in 2001. If the level of asset-backed debt stays that high, Moody's says, it could modestly
cut Sears's unsecured debt rating.
Sears says it has a diverse funding base and back-up lines for its unsecured borrowings. But Moody's notes,
"The lack of any multiyear component to its proposed new bank facility materially weakens, in our view, the
overall quality of Sears's alternate liquidity arrangements."

A Reflationist, Deflationist or Diplomat? --- Japan Seeks Central-Bank Chief To Tackle Herculean Challenge Of Fixing Economy, Limiting Risk
A Reflationist, Deflationist or Diplomat? --- Japan Seeks Central-Bank Chief To Tackle Herculean
Challenge Of Fixing Economy, Limiting Risk
By Phred Dvorak and Jason Singer
1,205 words
11 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Tokyo -- AS THE RACE to appoint Japan's next central-bank governor enters the home stretch, only one
thing is clear: Depending on what policies he chooses, Japan's new "deflation fighter" has the potential to roil
the capital markets as never before.
Prime Minister Junichiro Koizumi has only a few weeks left to decide who should succeed Bank of Japan
governor Masaru Hayami, whose five-year term ends March 19. Analysts and members of the Japanese
media are busy second-guessing Mr. Koizumi, who has said only that he wants a "deflation fighter," and that
he will make a decision by the end of the month.
Yet no matter who wins out -- and candidates mooted so far include former BOJ policy-board member
Nobuyuki Nakahara and Toshihiko Fukui, chairman of the Fujitsu Research Institute -- the task remains one
of the most daunting in the history of modern central banking: to halt an inexorable fall in prices that has led
during the past 13 years to shrinkage throughout Japan's economy.
It also is a policy question that is likely to be more high-profile than usual in the U.S., where a number of
analysts have started to raise fears about a Japanese-style deflationary spiral at home.
Because there is still little consensus on exactly how to tame deflation, the new governor will be choosing
from a palette of policy options that range from the merely unconventional to the over-the-top. Analysts now
think a radical policy shift is unlikely. Nonetheless, investors and traders in such markets as stocks, bonds
and currency are watching carefully.
"How the Bank of Japan moves is a big focus of the foreign-exchange markets" along with possibility of war
with Iraq, says Motoshi Imura, a senior manager in the foreign-exchange section of Bank of Tokyo-Mitsubishi.
Never has the appointment of Japan's central-bank governor been fraught with this level of uncertainty, as
well as potential risk for investors. At one extreme, Mr. Hayami's successor could do nothing but maintain the
status quo of a low-interest-rate, yet cautious, monetary policy. That is what Japan's bond markets are
betting on. At the end of January, the yield on the benchmark 10-year government bond hit a record low of
0.75%, a sign that bond investors expect little change, with deflation dragging on for years.
At the other extreme, a reflationist governor would try to create inflation. That unprecedented undertaking
would entail buying massive quantities of assets, such as bonds or dollars, in order to raise prices and spur
demand. Such a move would almost certainly send the value of the yen plummeting compared with other
currencies. Depending on the assets bought, it could also send bond prices down from current record highs
and stock prices higher from a near 20-year-low now, analysts say.
A report that a noted reflationist had received the nod from Mr. Koizumi and other important politicians was
enough to rouse the markets last week, sending stocks up 2%, gold prices to a seven-year high and the yen
on its biggest fall in four months. Mr. Koizumi denied the report, and Mr. Hayami on Friday warned that
aggressive reflation "could drastically destabilize the markets and the economy" through dangerous swings in
yen or interest rates. Still, the appointment of an aggressive reflationist could cause a 2,000-point rally in the
benchmark Nikkei Stock Average in a matter of days, some capital-market executives say.
Here are some directions the new governor could take monetary policy in the months ahead.
-- Status quo: Mr. Hayami holds that the central bank has done all it can to halt deflation and boost the
economy. During his term, the Bank of Japan has driven short-term interest rates to zero and even below,
and is making sure there are trillions of yen in excess cash sitting in its accounts ready for commercial banks
to use if they need.

--- Page 153 ---
Page 153 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Normally, banks would take all that cheap money and lend it on to companies, stimulating investment and
growth. That hasn't happened in Japan, and Mr. Hayami and others like him argue it won't happen until the
government fixes structural problems in the economy.
A Hayami-style deflation fighter would mean no big changes in monetary policy unless the government
moved first.
That stance would also leave the markets, and the economy, in the weak state they are in now, investors say.
"The status quo as we've had it under Mr. Hayami is unsustainable," says Ian Burden, who manages $1.5
billion in Japanese equities as chief investment officer in Japan for Invesco Asset Management.
-- Reflation: A number of prominent economists say the Bank of Japan can stimulate growth and inflation
merely by ultra-aggressive monetary expansion. They say the central bank should show its determination by
setting a target for inflation, and a deadline for meeting it. Then, all the bank has to do is buy stuff, in the
process pumping out yen, until the target is hit.
One idea floated by Mr. Nakahara, believed to be a candidate for the governor's post, is for the central bank
to buy foreign bonds. That would weaken the yen against other currencies and make Japanese products
more competitive with those of other countries, both inside and outside Japan. Mr. Nakahara also suggests
buying as much as 30 trillion yen in bonds directly from the government during the next three years. The bank
already buys 14.4 trillion yen per year on the open market.
Takatoshi Ito, a Japanese economist mooted for one of the deputy governor's spots, has proposed buying
stocks and property as well, boosting those asset prices directly. Skeptics warn, however, that all these
measures would rock the capital markets and could hurt investors, and yet may not produce inflation. If
10-year bond yields were to rise 1%, Japanese banks could see more than 1.3 trillion yen in latent losses on
their bond holdings, estimates Barclays Capital in Tokyo.
Takehiro Sato, an economist at Morgan Stanley in Tokyo, estimates that to get Japan's core consumer price
index above zero again, the dollar would have to strengthen as far as 200 yen, from around 120 yen now.
-- Cooperation: Many analysts say Mr. Koizumi doesn't favor either policy extreme. Instead, they figure the
new governor's most important quality will be his ability to cooperate with the government on new policy
initiatives rather than drag his feet, as Mr. Hayami often did. Even this seemingly compromise choice could
lead to some big shifts in monetary policy, especially if the government decides to have the Bank of Japan
monetize, or directly underwrite, debt.
Mr. Hayami has put his foot down on buying bonds directly from the government, saying it could destroy fiscal
discipline and the credibility of the yen, and the Bank of Japan has a ceiling on the amount of government
bonds it will buy. The new governor could well be more flexible on these points, analysts say.

Commodities Report: Natural Gas Deflates on Selling, Profit-Taking After Recent Rise
Commodities Report: Natural Gas Deflates on Selling, Profit-Taking After Recent Rise
By John Edmiston
Dow Jones Newswires
598 words
6 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C11
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
HOUSTON -- Natural-gas futures fell at the New York Mercantile Exchange on a mix of outright selling and
profit-taking, after posting new contract highs during the past two days.
Those gains were a reflection of dwindling inventories due to the cold winter. The next news on inventories
comes in today's weekly storage report from the Energy Information Administration, and nerves about the
report were a factor in the price drop.
The March contract fell 11.8 cents to $5.644 per million British thermal units.
"It just looks like liquidation" of previously bought contracts ahead of today, said Kyle Cooper of Salomon
Smith Barney in Houston. "People want to protect a little bit of profits if [the EIA] report comes out on the
bearish side."
Early predictions call for the EIA report to show a draw from storage of about 190 billion cubic feet up to 210
billion cubic feet, which analysts say would be a bullish development. The most ever withdrawn during the
comparable week was 209 billion cubic feet, Mr. Cooper said.
Nymex prices fell on a combination of "panic selling," knocking out those who had bought at higher levels and
a measure of profit-taking by those previous buyers, said Tom Saal of Commercial Brokerage Corp. in Miami.
More moderating weather is seen as the market starts to shake loose from the winter season, Salomon's Mr.
Cooper said.
Near term, the market is supported by a strong cash market and cold weather, both in the Northeast and the
West. Cash prices have hovered well above $6 during the past two days, well above first-of-month index
averages.
Mr. Saal points to $5.93 as a key resistance level on price charts, as that level marks a 50% retracement of
the $10.10 highs hit in December 2000. "The market failed to take that level out," Mr. Saal said, as it moved
toward $6 this week. "With those road signs heading toward $6, just remember one of those signs might
signal a U-turn," Mr. Saal said.
In other words, a market moving straight up usually finds a reason to come down, another veteran trader
said.
For now, added Gerry Saccente of ABN Amro in New York, the market has moved into "a crazy period," in
which "we're moving up very, very fast."
Inventories are getting low and sustained cold weather is digging into what is left, Mr. Saccente said.
End-of-season levels have fallen toward lows seen in 1996 and 2000. Utilities are worried there may not be
enough gas and they are buying supply wherever they can find it, he noted.
In other commodity markets:
SILVER: Futures fell sharply on the Comex division of Nymex as Secretary of State Colin Powell's
presentation to the United Nations of evidence of Iraqi deception of U.N. arms inspectors was seen as more
likely to win over other countries to supporting U.S. military action. Silver had gained with gold ahead of the
speech as uncertainty about Iraq spurred buying of "safe haven" assets such as precious metals. The March
future dropped 15 cents to $4.768.
SOYBEANS: Chicago Board of Trade futures slid as selling by speculative funds and local floor traders
triggered sell stops, or preplaced sales orders. The March future fell 7.5 cents to $5.63 a bushel. Traders

European Markets Stage Late Rally
World Stock Markets
European Markets Stage Late Rally
By Craig Karmin
1,166 words
6 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
European markets enjoyed a powerful late-day rally, rising as Secretary of State Colin Powell made his case
against Iraq to the United Nations.
Some global investors said Mr. Powell's speech, which accused Iraq of continuing programs to create
chemical, biological and nuclear weapons, contained no real surprises but was nonetheless likely to solidify
support behind the U.S. That would remove some geopolitical uncertainty by making intervention in Iraq look
increasingly likely. It would also improve the odds that the United Nations and others would back the U.S. In
fact, Turkish Prime Minister Abdullah Gul said yesterday that he would back U.S. intervention.
"I think you're going to see more backing from some of the European countries for military action, should it be
needed," said Gary Dugan, global-equity strategist for J.P. Morgan Fleming Asset Management in London.
"And that is something markets will take well."
Still, other investors pointed to midday gains by the dollar, which had been under steep selling pressure, as
the more important catalyst behind yesterday's stock rally. The dollar's recent collapse is considered a
problem for European exporters, which become less globally competitive as the euro strengthens, and
generally bearish for the European economy.
Frankfurt's Xetra DAX Index led the gains, rising 3.5%, while London's FTSE 100 Index advanced 2.5%. The
Dow Jones EuroStoxx 50 Index of blue-chip companies gained 1.9%. Asian markets turned in a mixed
performance, despite modest gains in Tokyo. Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index fell 0.11%, or 0.15,
to 136.06. Excluding the U.S., the index rose 0.4%, or 0.41, to 102.2.
For some analysts, the prospect of war and uncertainty about the timing and depth of support have thrown a
wet blanket over the stock market. Carl Weinberg, chief economist for High Frequency Economics in Valhalla,
N.Y., argues that the "war trade" has been to sell dollars and stocks and to buy euros, bonds and gold in a
manner that is inconsistent with economic fundamentals.
When the Iraq question is resolved, he wrote in a note to clients yesterday, "a sudden unwinding of the `war
trade' may well prove to be the event we remember most about 2003."
Not everyone agrees, especially investors across the Atlantic. Chris Carter, an equity strategist for Investec
Asset Management in London, suggests that European economic conditions are "deteriorating" and don't
offer a favorable backdrop for stock-market gains. "A rally may never materialize, or if it does, may prove
disappointingly lackluster," he said.
Indeed, Europe received another does of bad economic news yesterday when Germany reported that
unemployment in December rose twice as much as expected, raising the specter that Europe's largest
economy may be heading back into recession. Despite that prospect, few analysts expect the European
Central Bank to cut interest rates when it meets today.
Another blow to European economic recovery has been the continual decline in the dollar, which tumbled this
week to four-year lows against the euro and Swiss franc. Secretary Powell's speech raised hopes that the
U.S. would not have to act alone in Iraq, helping ease the euro back to $1.07, after earlier trading above
$1.09.
"The dollar exchange rate continues to be a bellwether of investor confidence," said Patrizio Merciai, chief
global strategist for Lombard Odier in Geneva.
He thinks that the dollar's rebound was a bigger factor in Europe's late-day stock-market rally than Secretary
Powell's speech. But he fears that the dollar's rebound yesterday could be short-lived. "The U.S. deficit is

--- Page 157 ---
Page 157 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
becoming more and more of a concern," Mr. Merciai said. "That means dollar weakness could be a long-term
trend."

In TOKYO, the Nikkei Stock Average rose 0.8%, or 64.95 points, to 8549.85. Japan Telecom ended the day
up 5.8% at 384,000 yen after the company said, in response to an article yesterday in the Nihon Keizai

and cost cuts.
In HONG KONG, the Hang Seng Index ended down 0.8%, or 72.24 points, at 9180.47. Conglomerate

India joint venture has bought a mobile license in Punjab state. The best blue-chip performer was Chinese

generation, supply and infrastructure businesses, despite continued weakness in wholesale power prices.
Insurers fared well in London, with Friends Provident rising 5.8% to 97 pence and Prudential, Aviva and Legal
&General all gaining 4% to 400 pence, 431 pence and 77 pence, respectively.
In PARIS, the CAC 40 Index rose 0.7%, or 21.12 points, to 2884.62, with bank Societe Generale the
second-biggest decliner, falling 3.7% to 51.15 euros. Bank BNP Paribas went against the trend, climbing
3.9% to 38.03 euros on fourth-quarter earnings that slightly beat market expectations. Specialty-chemicals
company Rhodia rose 6.8% to 6.61 euros after posting an unexpectedly strong rise in earnings before
interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization.

Powell's Speech Boosts Stocks, At Least Briefly --- Dow Industrials Finish Lower, As a 139-Point Gain Fizzles; Hope for `Certainty' Is...
Wednesday's Markets
Powell's Speech Boosts Stocks, At Least Briefly --- Dow Industrials Finish Lower, As a 139-Point Gain
Fizzles; Hope for `Certainty' Is Premature
By E.S. Browning
828 words
6 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS INITIALLY JUMPED on hopes that Secretary of State Colin Powell's strong speech might resolve
the uncertainty hanging over financial markets by creating broad international support for U.S. policy on Iraq.
But stocks gave back their gains amid speeches indicating that countries such as France and China still
aren't on board, and that Iraq remains defiant.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished the day down 28.11 points, or 0.35%, at 7985.18, leaving it down
4.3% since the year began. The blue-chip average had been up as much as 139.24 points at lunchtime,
shortly after Mr. Powell finished speaking.
"We started off with a kind of saber-rattling rally because Colin Powell was viewed as very powerful in his
presentation, and then we came back in on a realization that maybe this doesn't clarify events," said Todd
Clark, head of listed trading at Wells Fargo Securities in San Francisco, a trading arm of Wells Fargo &Co.
Short-term speculators played a big role in the gyrations, he and other traders said. Gold, for example, fell
sharply in value as Mr. Powell was speaking, an apparent indication that short-term traders were taking some
of the strong paper profits they have registered in the precious metal, which had been up more than 9% since
the start of the year.
As Mr. Powell spoke, short-sellers, who profit from stock declines, were covering their positions through
buying, taking some of the profits they have built up amid recent Iraq-related worries. To bet against stocks,
short sellers borrow stock from brokerage firms and sell it, hoping to buy it cheaper after it declines. To take
profits, they purchase stocks, which pushes them up in value.
As the day wore on, it became clear that the Iraq uncertainties would linger well after today, and some
speculators restored some of their short sales, helping knock stocks back down. Those who had bet on a
stock recovery sold their positions. Gold recovered somewhat, but never returned to the plus column, and the
dollar, which has been falling as investors move toward gold and the euro, managed to finish the day with a
gain.
"The volume is light and there were a lot of fast-money players in the stock market," said David Briggs, head
of stock trading at the Pittsburgh mutual-fund group Federated Investors. "Once it did look like gold was
selling off and stocks were doing better, there was a rush of profit-taking in gold on the part of people who
were up $25 in their gold positions."
When stocks began falling in January, he noted, a lot of people went to gold and currency markets, and they
were sitting on big unrealized gains.
Treasury bonds, like gold, declined as stocks rose, and the bonds didn't make it back into the plus column
despite the stock reversal. One reason for that, bond traders said, is that the Treasury Department
announced it will be raising money by selling $42 billion of five-year and 10-year notes this month, and will
reintroduce the three-year note in May. That drove home the fact that the government is looking for ways to
finance widening deficits, and that this will put upward pressure on interest rates. Rising rates on new bonds
are bad for existing bonds, whose rates are lower.
Although the overall stock move was minor, it did produce one significant change. The broad Standard
&Poor's 500-stock index, the benchmark for most professional fund managers, finished the day at its lowest
level yet this year. It fell 0.54%, or 4.61 points, to 843.59, down 4% so far this year. Its previous low close had
been 844.61, on Jan. 30.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, heavy in volatile technology stocks, also finished at a low for the year so far. It
fell 0.36%, or 4.65 points, to 1301.50, down 2.6% on the year.

--- Page 159 ---
Page 159 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell back below the much-watched 8000 level, but remained slightly above
its low close of 7945.13, logged Jan. 30.
Iraq worries clearly are preventing investors from focusing on the fundamental issues they normally follow,
noted Tim Morris, chief investment officer at New York money-management firm Bessemer Trust.
"We really need to get past this before investors will spend more time thinking about earnings, the economy,
where growth might be taking place, all those questions," he said. Once people look past Iraq, he said, they
will see that "the economy is on reasonably good footing here."
Added Mr. Clark of Wells Fargo, "The only real people who are playing this market right now are traders.
Investors have made their bets and are loath to make new commitments."

Bush's Tax Reform
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Bush's Tax Reform
678 words
5 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Everybody is reporting that President Bush's new budget includes a big boost for investors, but the larger
news is that taken together his proposals are a great leap forward for tax reform.
A few weeks ago Mr. Bush proposed ending the double taxation of dividends for shareholders. Last week he
topped that with a sweeping plan to benefit saving-plans. Both move the U.S. closer to a better tax system
that rewards savings over consumption.
The saving-plan idea -- and it is huge -- is to replace the current complicated network of tax-preferred saving
accounts with three simple plans: two for individual savers and one for employer-sponsored retirement
accounts. The most delicious one, for us, is called the Lifetime Savings Account where contributions can run
up to $7,500 a year and is indexed for inflation. Although contributions are taxed, all earnings accumulate
tax-free and withdrawals can be made at any time with no tax penalty.
The result will also be huge. People will be allowed to save more dollars, regardless of income. No
investment income will be taxed. And the penalty-free withdrawals will mean that those with low and
moderate incomes can contribute without worrying about what will happen if they suddenly need the money
they have committed -- a big barrier to their participation in current plans.
The second personal account is similar but for retirement savings, so tax-free withdrawals are limited to those
who are age 58 and older. The Retirement Savings Account would replace the three types of IRAs now on
offer and the $7,500 contribution limit would be indexed for inflation. The third plan, an employer-sponsored
account, would consolidate half-a-dozen such plans, like the 401(k) and 403(b), into one plan with
enormously simplified rules.
On the grounds of utter simplicity alone, the new accounts will encourage more people to save and to save
more. Ditto for business, especially for small firms, where acres of red tape and high compliance costs now
discourage employers from offering any retirement program. By itself this streamlining and clarity is important
enough to justify some cheerleading. But there is more.
The combined effect of ending dividend taxation and expanding savings accounts constitutes a major leap
toward removing the tax on income and thereby leveling the field with consumption. Because the current
system taxes income -- including income from investment and saving -- it generates incentives to consume
rather than invest and save. But by shielding more investment and saving income from taxation, the Bush
proposals tilt the tax code away from this bias.
Of course the merest hint that taxes might be lowered -- and that people might keep more of their own money
-- has infuriated income redistributionists and the budget "deficit" frightsters. They are complaining that the
Bush proposal will result in more tax revenue now (true, since contributions are not deductible) but less
revenue later (true, since withdrawals are not taxed). The critics forget that the years during which people are
consuming less, and investing and saving more, will be years of higher growth. As everybody and their dog
knows, a larger, stronger economy is the best, most efficient way to reduce deficits.
A second line of carping is that these saving-plan proposals will benefit the rich. Well, duh. The "richer" one
is, the more one is exposed to higher marginal rates of taxation and more in need of a way to shelter income
from taxation. But the critics ought to consider just where this money goes after it escapes taxation. It doesn't
buy yachts or gold coins (not that there's anything wrong with that). It goes into saving and investment where
it spurs productivity and economic growth.
It is clear from these proposals that the Bush Administration is taking the investor class seriously. As it should
since investors constitute half of all households; more than 40 million people have IRAs. But the bigger point
here is that by paying attention to investors, Mr. Bush is paying attention to the economy.

Cranky Consumer: Daring to Ask More of Your Credit-Card Company
Personal Finance
Cranky Consumer: Daring to Ask More of Your Credit-Card Company
By Peter Meyers
1,249 words
4 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Is it time for your credit-card company to make you a better offer?
With interest rates at 40-year lows, credit-card companies are mailing out more annoying solicitations than
ever -- close to four billion last year alone. And that's 10% more than a year before, says research firm
Comperemedia. Many come with goodies like 0% interest rates and bonus airline mileage. Other lures are
purely ornamental -- care for a fuchsia-colored card? -- designed, presumably, for people who don't like the
color of money.
Our own wallet was already plump with cards featuring stingy terms dating from earlier times. So we decided
to pick up the phone and ask for better treatment. We called each card company twice to see how hard we
could squeeze. In the first call, we just asked for a better deal. The next day, we called back and demanded
specifics -- lower rates, more miles and waived annual fees. Then we tallied up our booty.
Lesson: Pick up that phone and call your card company. With almost no effort we netted real savings on four
cards. In one case, we discovered an easy card switch that would save us nearly $170.
But don't be a pushover. In many instances, we found that calling more than once triggered greater gains.
Several times we weren't informed of all options on the first call.
Our biggest win came with Morgan Stanley's Discover Card. When our 0% introductory rate expired last fall,
the new rate soared to a whopping 13.49%. On the first call, the Discover customer rep offered less than a
measly one-percentage-point decrease, but we pressed a bit and, cha-ching, she agreed to drop it to 0% for
six months on future purchases.
We were treated with benign indifference at American Express, where we've been good customers with a
Gold Delta SkyMiles card. Would they reduce our annual fee? No. Cut our rate? No. Grant us bonus miles?
Anything at all? No. In response to our parting observation that many other credit-card firms seemed eager to
negotiate, all we got was chilly silence on the phone.
An American Express spokeswoman said that its variety of cards provides all the flexibility that its customers
need.
Our first call to MBNA, where we hold a Platinum Plus MasterCard with Rewards card, earned us a pat on the
back for being prompt bill payers and a four-percentage-point rate cut (down to 10.99%). This was twice the
usual two-point cut that most callers receive, according to our agent. But there was nothing he could do about
our $50 annual fee, he said. Turns out that there was, however, another option. On our second call, the rep
asked: "Are you thinking about transferring the balance?" We weren't, and we said so -- but that's when
things got interesting. Next she said she would check if there was another MBNA card we could transfer to
that would still earn us points (we had accumulated 14,000 already) but which didn't have an annual fee. She
put us on hold, subjecting us to a Muzak-style rendition of "Battle Hymn of the Republic," complete with
strains of "Glory, Glory, Hallelujah!"
Hallelujah, indeed. She returned with a suggestion that we switch cards -- from the Platinum Plus to
something called the World MasterCard, which would give us the same 10.99% rate, no annual fee, and
rewards that could be used across a much broader range of purchases than just airline miles. Total savings,
given the approximately $3,000 balance we're carrying: $170 (assuming we pay off our balance in a year).
Calls to Citigroup's Citibank about an AAdvantage Visa card generated a payoff. Our first request clipped two
percentage points off our 14.24% interest rate. But then came the sales pitch. Did we want to buy a monthly
credit-report-monitoring service for $79.90 a year? The rep pressed it pretty hard. "They also send you your
DMV record!" she told us. She said that she herself had ordered the product: "I canceled as soon as I got the
reports," she confided.

--- Page 163 ---
Page 163 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
We said no thanks. Then, the next day, we called back -- and shaved four more points off our interest rate.
Then we asked for more miles, and the rep tried to transfer us to the department that handles cancellations.
Why? People who threaten to cancel a card over rates are often offered bonus miles as a retention strategy.
We said we didn't want to cancel, so no bonus miles for us.
We got marginal gains on our First USA Platinum Visa, a product of Bank One. They agreed to upgrade us to
a 7.9% variable rate from a fixed 9.9% rate. We tried to get our new rate changed to a fixed percentage on
our second call. "At this time we don't have that available," the rep said. But "we certainly encourage you to
check back in the future."

Cranky about customer service? E-mail us at cranky@wsj.com

What we asked for: Something more than what we got on our first call
(which was a 1-point cut on our 13.49% rate).
What we got: On our second call, we scored a 0% rate for six months.
Snafu: Had to hear a scary pitch for identity-theft protection: "If
they've got your social [security number] they can do anything."
Comment: Best Rate Cut. The aggressive sales pitches were offset by
the generous cut we were granted on our interest rate.
Card Name: MBNA Platinum Plus MasterCard with Rewards
What we asked for: To cut our 14.99% interest rate in half and drop
the $50 fee.
What we got: Ended up with 10.99% -- plus a valuable tip to switch to
a similar card with no fee.
Snafu: Why didn't the first rep tip us off to the fee-saving card?
Comment: Based on questions we were asked, you might get a better
offer if you transfer your balance.

What we asked for: To cut our 9.9% rate in half and give us a 0%
balance transfer.
What we got: Our 9.9% fixed rate got cut to 7.9% variable rate.

friend

(But why don't they alert us when rates drop?)

Snafu: Agent erroneously blamed American Airlines for not being able
to waive fee. A Citibank rep says this isn't true.

Comment: Henry Ford Award. You can have any rate -- as long as it's

Latin American Stocks Get Pounded
World Stock Markets
Latin American Stocks Get Pounded
By Mike Esterl
Dow Jones Newswires
1,055 words
3 February 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Latin American stocks became world beaters in January -- on the downside.
After entering the New Year with a bang after an end-of-year rally, stocks were down 4% on the month in
dollar terms, according to Morgan Stanley's closely tracked MSCI index.
Weak economic-growth prospects and deep pockets of political uncertainty continue to plague the region. But
the biggest virus is its heavy reliance on foreign-capital investment -- which is drying up amid the heightened
likelihood of a U.S. war with Iraq.
"That increases risk aversion and Latin American economies, particularly Brazil, are more vulnerable to
shocks," said George Hoguet, an emerging-markets fund manager in Boston with State Street Global
Advisors.
In a bad month overall for global stocks, Latin America was among the worst. MSCI's world index dropped
3.1% in January, including a 2.5% decline in the U.S. and a 0.6% drop in emerging markets as a whole.
The January downturn reverses three straight months of gains in Latin American stocks, which had been
showing signs of emerging from an extended funk. MSCI's Latin American index peaked at 705.733 on Jan.
10 -- up 7.1% from the end of December and its highest level since last July -- before reversing course.
Venezuelan stocks have suffered the most bruises, down 18% in dollar terms in January, battered by a
nationwide strike aimed at unseating President Hugo Chavez that is more than two months old. The local
stock market has been closed for much of the month, and currency trading has been shuttered.
But Mexico and Brazil, which nowadays represent about 90% of stock-market liquidity in Latin America, have
been the main culprits -- and casualties.
Mexican shares were down 6.4% in dollar terms in January, with a little more than half of that decline caused
by a weak peso. "Everybody is expecting tighter monetary policy. That's no good for growth," said Robert
Berges, Latin American equities strategist in New York at Merrill Lynch, which cut its recommendation on
Mexico to underweight on Jan. 10.
Yet much of the distemper toward Mexico also is being blamed on worries about a possible double-dip
recession in the neighboring U.S., which absorbs more than 80% of Mexican exports.
Brazilian stocks have shed 6.3% this month in dollar terms, reversing a three-month rally following a stiff
selloff earlier in 2002 that had been fueled by pre-election jitters and solvency concerns. They soared 11% in
the first 10 days of January before sellers resurfaced -- even though most of the news coming out of Brazil
this month has been positive.
Not all Latin American countries are in the red. Argentine stocks have risen 7.1% in dollar terms this month,
after sinking like a stone for much of last year in the wake of the government's record debt default and messy
currency devaluation. Peruvian stocks have climbed 6.5%, boosted by relatively healthy economic growth
data and gains in gold miner Compania de Minas Buenaventura. Gold prices hit six-year highs in January as
investors sought safety from a weakening U.S. dollar.
The Argentine and Peruvian stock markets remain small, however, providing little shelter from global storms.
By sector, cement was the poorest performer in Latin America in January, plunging 15% in dollar terms,
according to Merrill Lynch. Mexico's Cemex, the world's No. 3 cement producer, is down 13% over the past
month after reporting disappointing fourth-quarter earnings.

--- Page 165 ---
Page 165 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The media sector, another cyclical group that rises and falls in tandem with economic-growth prospects, fell
14%.
Until the U.S.-Iraq cloud dissipates, Merrill Lynch's Mr. Berges is recommending that Latin America investors
stick to banks, which have been outperforming performing well in Brazil and Mexico, and export-driven
stocks. Some of the companies he likes are Mexican brewer Grupo Modelo, which exports a lot of its
recession-proof beer to the U.S. and could benefit from the weaker peso, and Brazilian mining giant
Companhia Vale do Rio Doce.
---
Friday's Market Activity
Tokyo stocks edged higher while several other Asian markets were closed for the Lunar New Year. Most
European markets moved higher. Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 0.54%, or 0.73, to 136.62.
Excluding the U.S., the index fell 0.44%, or 0.45, to 101.34.
In TOKYO, the Nikkei Stock Average gained 0.3%, or 23.13 points, to 8339.94. Sony, the most actively
traded issue, fell 1.3% to 4,710 yen after falling to a new-year low of 4,620 yen. Pioneer jumped 9.6% to
2,295 yen after raising its group net profit forecast on cost cuts and brisk sales of plasma display panels and
DVD recorders.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 Index shed 0.3%, or 11.3 points, to 3567.40. Insurer Royal &Sun Alliance fell
3.5% while the FTSE 100's only tech stock, software group Sage, sank 6.2% to 118 pence.
In PARIS, the CAC 40 Index rose 0.8%, or 23.75 points, to 2937.88, led higher by telecom-equipment maker
Alcatel, which bucked its sector's trend, rising 5.3% to 6.74 euros. Insurer AXA fell 3% to 11.42 euros.
In SAO PAULO, the Bovespa Index rose 1.8%, or 190.30 points, to 10941.09. Morgan Stanley raised it
recommendation late Thursday on Brazilian bonds, helping boost the stock market as well. Tele Norte Leste
Participacoes (Telemar) climbed 1.9% to 27 reals.

Investors Buy Bush's Speech, Eventually --- Negative Start Turns Positive After Fed Keeps Rates Unchanged; Blue Chips Finish up 0.27%
Wednesday's Markets
Investors Buy Bush's Speech, Eventually --- Negative Start Turns Positive After Fed Keeps Rates
Unchanged; Blue Chips Finish up 0.27%
By E.S. Browning
733 words
30 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER A ROCKY beginning to the day, investors decided to applaud President Bush's State of the Union
address, turning stocks around and producing a second straight day of gains.
But traders said that the volatile activity was dominated by short-term speculators, and they questioned
whether stocks can enjoy a sustained rally as long as the uncertainty of war hangs over the market.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, off almost 144 points early in the day, finished up 21.87 points, or 0.27%,
at 8110.71 -- a swing of almost 166 points. It was helped by the decision announced in the afternoon by
Federal Reserve policy makers to leave target interest rates unchanged, and by the Fed's reassuring
comment that, once the so-called geopolitical risks lift, the economy should improve.
Even so, "This was not enough of a rally to get too excited about," said Keith Gertsen, head of Nasdaq
trading at brokerage firm Deutsche Bank Securities. "There still is a lot of apprehension among investors. And
until corporations definitively begin to spend [on new business investment] I don't think you are going to see
significant, sustainable rallies."
Some of the day's early declines appeared to be fueled by sell orders from European investors, who were
alarmed at the hawkish tone of the president's speech. But many American investors reacted positively to the
speech, partly out of patriotism and partly because it offered a clearer timetable for U.S. involvement.
Once again, traders said, profit-taking by short sellers, investors who bet on stock declines, played a role in
the stock recovery. Short sellers borrow stock from brokerage firms and sell it, in hopes that it will decline and
they will be able to buy it back more cheaply. To take their profits, they buy the stock after a decline and
cover their positions, which can cause a short-term recovery. With the Iraq situation becoming clearer, and
with stocks down sharply at the start of the day, some short sellers took profits and bought stock, helping to
fuel the rebound.
"We have had such a negative market over the last two weeks, that this could just be some short-covering,"
said Terence McLaughlin, president of Lighthouse Growth Advisors, a New York money-management firm.
Oil prices continued to rise on fears of oil-supply disruptions during a war. But gold, which has been hitting
six-year highs on those same war worries, pulled back a bit. Treasury bonds also fell, as the Fed's comments
reduced the likelihood of further Fed rate cuts. In addition, some investors shifted money away from bonds
and toward stocks. The dollar resumed its retreat.
Money managers' reactions to the president's speech were a combination of patriotism and worry. Several
pointed out that this war may be quite different from the Gulf War of 12 years ago. That war involved
removing troops that had invaded another country, while this one would involve invading a country and
removing its government. Worries are widespread that this war could be longer and messier, with the risk that
Iraq could use chemical and biological weapons.
Still, many investors applauded the president's determination and the clarity of his message.
"He is willing to do this with Iraq and put the country and the world through this continuing uncertainty,
because he believes the short-term risk is outweighed by the long-term benefits, economic and otherwise,
that security from terrorism will provide," said Harvey Hirschhorn, head strategist at the Columbia
Management Group arm of FleetBoston Financial.
Computer-chip stocks and other tech shares were among the big gainers, after Merrill Lynch boosted its
rating of Novellus Systems, which makes gear used to manufacture chips.

The Nasdaq Composite Index, dominated by tech stocks, jumped 1.18%, or 15.88 points, to 1358.06. It now
is up 1.69% since the year began. The S&P 500 index rose 0.68%, or 5.82 points, to 864.36. It still is down
1.76% for the year, while the Dow industrials are down 2.77% this year.
The 10-year Treasury note fell 17/32, or $5.3125 per $1,000 invested, to push up the yield to 4.033%.

Under Review: Greeks in Troy or New York?
LEISURE &ARTS
Under Review: Greeks in Troy or New York?
By Manuela Hoelterhoff
878 words
30 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
D8
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Paris/Opera
Rolando Villazon is just what you want in a tenor but so rarely get -- lots of hair and no (visible) love handles.
He sings Gounod's Faust at the Opera Bastille in a legendary production by director Jorge Lavelli who moved
the story of the lusty old medieval magician into a luminous 19th-century space dominated by a spectacular
glass and iron cupola. Marie Mills sings the role of the doomed Marguerite, whose little cottage Faust
addresses in his aria, "Salut! Demeure chaste et pure." If only she hadn't let him in to inspect the bedroom!
-- Feb. 5, 9,13,18, 2, 25, 27; March 2, 6, 8,12. www.opera-de-paris.com.
London/Theater
No-one (except possibly Peter Shaffer) has been more successful with stage biography than Christopher
Hampton. His new play at the National/Cottesloe, "The Talking Cure," focuses on the friendship and rivalry
between the two seminal figures of psychoanalysis, Sigmund Freud and Karl Gustav Jung. Mr. Hampton sets
up Freud as the imperial conservative patriarch, clashing with Jung, the rebel child who is about to demolish
his mentor with an awareness of the world outside. In a dream, Jung sees a Europe soaked in blood.
The play fails to bring much drama to the duo. "We sane doctors have serious limitations," says Jung. So it is
with the play. Mr. Hampton's slavish adherence to the facts makes both Freud and Jung duller than Mr.
Shaffer's almost completely invented Mozart and Salieri. Ralph Fiennes as Jung is a plausibly pompous
prematurely old young man, but he fails to convince us that he is a man of science, let alone genius. Dominic
Rowan makes a respectable attempt at Freud.
-- Through Feb.5. www.nationaltheatre.org.uk.
Michael Grandage's production of "The Tempest" at the Old Vic has many delights. For starters, it clocks in at
around two hours instead of the often agonizing three. And then there's Derek Jacobi, who presents an
unusually vital Prospero. Pugnacious and punchy, he avoids the usual Gielgud melancholy and plays him as
a crafty, skilled stage manager who may be having a midlife crisis but is hardly ready for farewells. Mr.
Grandage shows a swift, sure ability to tell the tale or, in this case, the four or five competing subplots.
-- www.oldvictheatre.com.
London/Opera
Is it, or isn't it about 9/11? That was the question at the English National Opera after the first night of Richard
Jones's new production of Berlioz's "The Capture of Troy." Mr. Jones has denied any such references, but
the opera opens with a crashed jet in one corner and ends with the Trojan women about to jump from a
skyscraper as the enemy invades the building -- images that point all too painfully one way.
His direction confuses rather than clarifies the dynamics of the opera. The setting is meant to be a country on
its knees after 10 years' siege, but Mr. Jones presents a modern America with a well fed people, decently
clothed. Is America really a modern Troy?
Still, Mr. Jones offers time-transcending moments of great power: The arrival of the huge wooden horse is
handled with impressive carnivalesque pomp, and the big choral set pieces generate a fair amount of steam.
Susan Bickley as Cassandra is also outstanding. By turns anguished, neurotic and tender, and with a rich
plangent voice which spins round the auditorium like a boomerang, she pretty much carries the show.
Conductor Paul Daniel hardly probes the strange depths of Berlioz's orchestration unplumbed.
-- Jan. 31, Feb 5, 12, 15, 21, 25, 27, www.eno.org.

--- Page 169 ---
Page 169 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
At Covent Garden, David McVicar's new production of "The Magic Flute" opens with young people in simple
18th-century dress appearing throughout the auditorium holding out small luminous spheres to the audience,
creating an effect of the night sky in gentle motion. It's elegant, dreamlike and captivating. Mr. McVicar
creates a coherent universe out of Mozart's incoherent plot by rhyming the Queen of the Night's murderous
violence with the bullish misogyny of Sarastro's own followers. Colin Davis conducts with stately
gracefulness, Dorothea Roschmann (Pamina) phrased her butter-toffee voice with a recitalist's attention to
detail and Diana Damrau, as her mom from hell, employed a clutch of beautifully judged silent-movie
gestures while hitting immaculate top F's.
-- Feb. 4, 7, 10, 12, 14, 17. www.roh.org.uk.
Paris/Exhibition
A horse carried Mughal emperor Aurangzeb on his back and got a portrait out of it laced in gold leaf that is
now on view at the Institut du Monde Arabe. In all, "Chevaux et Cavaliers Arabes" has several hundred works
ranging over 1400 years. Outstandingly beautiful are 14 pages from the famed Shahnama or "King's Book of
Kings," commissioned by Persian Shah Tahmasp in the 16th century and bought in 1959 by the feckless U.S.
millionaire Arthur Houghton Jr., who ripped out and sold off individual pages.
-- Through March 30. www.imarabe.org.

Blue Chips Pull Above 8000, Ending Slide --- Selling Slows as State of the Union Looms, Sentiment Beats Forecast; Short Covering Adds to...
Tuesday's Markets
Blue Chips Pull Above 8000, Ending Slide --- Selling Slows as State of the Union Looms, Sentiment
Beats Forecast; Short Covering Adds to Bounce
By E.S. Browning
787 words
29 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average pushed back above 8000 as the sellers who had been knocking down
the market stepped aside to see what the president would say in the State of the Union address.
After falling for seven of the previous eight trading days, during which they gave up almost 10% of their value,
the Dow industrials rebounded 99.28 points, or 1.24%, to 8088.84. The blue chips still are down 3% for the
year.
The selling dried up not only because of the looming presidential speech last night, but also because of a
spate of positive economic and corporate news. On top of that came uncertainty about what Federal Reserve
officials would say after they complete a two-day meeting on interest-rate policy today. While the Fed isn't
expected to change its target rates when it makes its postmeeting announcement at about 2:15 p.m. EST
today, its comments on the prospects for the soft economy will be read closely.
With the threat of war and the continuing economic worries still hanging over the market, traders were
reluctant to extrapolate too much from the one-day rebound.
"We are still just in a very precarious position," said John O'Donoghue, a director of stock trading at New York
brokerage firm Credit Suisse First Boston. "There is a whole confluence of potential events that are out
there."
Some traders were skeptical because they saw some of the day's gains as the product of temporary actions
by short sellers, who bet on stock declines by borrowing stock from brokerage firms and selling it, hoping it
will decline in value so they can buy it back for less.
Short sellers have done well during the past two weeks, and with rumors flying about what might be included
in the president's address, some of them covered their bets and took their profits. To do that, they had to buy
stock and return it -- which temporarily drove up stock prices.
"You probably had a reasonable amount of short covering" ahead of the address, said Andy Brooks, head of
stock trading at Baltimore mutual-fund group T. Rowe Price. "That's because, if what comes out is construed
positively, you could have a knee-jerk response in the market" today and stocks could continue to rise.
At a time of political tension, traders want to be seen as supporting the government if they can, Mr. Brooks
noted. But to do that, he added, they would like the government to present more of its evidence that Iraq is
developing weapons of mass destruction -- and they also would like to be reassured that the economic
program is going to keep the economy out of recession. Traders were buoyed by reports during the day that
they would see progress on both counts, either in the president's speech or in presentations during the days
to come.
On top of that, investors learned that consumer confidence has fallen less than expected this month and that
new-home sales rose to a record in December. Merck and Procter &Gamble, components of the Dow
industrials, both forecast stronger-than-expected earnings for the coming year. Phone company SBC
Communications, also a Dow component, forecast a drop in this year's revenue, however.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 1.28%, or 16.91 points, to 1342.18. That pushed it back into positive
territory for the year, up 0.5%. The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 1.31%, or 11.06 points, to
858.54, still down 2.4% on the year.
The dollar, which has been pounded by the euro in recent weeks, recovered some ground. But war worries
sent gold to yet another six-year high, and oil prices also rose.

fell 0.4%, or 0.4 point, to 100.79.

Fears of War With Iraq Send Blue Chips Below 8000
Monday's Markets
Fears of War With Iraq Send Blue Chips Below 8000
By E.S. Browning
813 words
28 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A DRUMBEAT OF WAR talk helped to knock the Dow Jones Industrial Average down more than 141 points
to below 8000, a level it hadn't seen in nearly 3 1/2 months.
Hopes that a report from chief United Nations weapons inspector Hans Blix might somehow point to a
peaceful solution were dashed as Mr. Blix issued a tough statement noting that Iraq wasn't showing a
willingness to disarm. He said it had failed to provide important information on weapons, including nerve gas.
U.S. officials said they are willing to consult with allies, but repeated that unless Iraq shows a sudden change
of heart, they are prepared eventually to disarm Iraq by force.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 1.74%, or 141.45 points, to 7989.56. After being up as much as 6% in
the first two weeks of the new year, the Dow now has given back all of those gains and is down 4% since the
year began. The declines have been sharp, occurring in seven of the past eight trading sessions.
With foreign investors reluctant to move much money to dollar-denominated securities, Treasury bonds also
declined on the jitters. The dollar fell to another three-year low against the euro and gold hit a six-year high.
"Everyone is extremely nervous with all the saber rattling with respect to Iraq," said Tim Heekin, director of
trading at San Francisco brokerage firm Thomas Weisel Partners. "Everyone is pretty certain that we are
going in." The fear, he added, is that "it could be a long war and we could have a sustained selloff because of
it."
He said many investors still hope for a quick, surgical war, or for a peaceful solution through which Saddam
Hussein might go into exile. But few are investing based on those hopes. Because of the risk of a long,
complicated war, possibly involving chemical and biological weapons, he said, many professional investors
are nervous about committing new money at all.
"The markets were looking for some kind of an answer and they got more questions," said Taai Izushima,
head of trading at Daiwa Securities America. The tensions between the U.S. and countries such as France
and Russia make things worse, he said.
On top of that, "Some of the people at the Davos [Switzerland], economic forum, Bill Gates, Michael Dell,
John Chambers and Carly Fiorina, said that technology spending wasn't going to be so great this year. They
don't see any pickup," he said.
Another reason for jitters is that President Bush tonight delivers his State of the Union address. While he isn't
expected to disclose his plans for Iraq, investors expect more indications of his thinking.
In addition, the Federal Reserve today begins two days of monetary-policy meetings. It isn't expected to
announce any change in interest rate tomorrow, but investors worry about what it will say in tomorrow's
postmeeting statement about whether the economic recovery is sustainable.
The negative comments out of Davos helped push technology stocks down, and the Nasdaq Composite
Index followed the Dow industrials into negative territory for the year so far. The tech-laden Nasdaq fell
1.26%, or 16.87 points, to 1325.27, leaving it down 0.77% since the year began. It had been up 9.4% on Jan.
14.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 1.62%, or 13.92 points, to 847.48, down 3.7% on the year.
On the New York Stock Exchange, almost four times as many stocks fell as rose.
Despite the broadly held negative view, some investors are thinking about stepping in to do some bargain
hunting, on the theory that things may not be as bad as they seem.

Getting Ready for a Double Dip --- How to Prepare for Chance Of a Renewed Recession; `Don't Avoid the Market'
Getting Ready for a Double Dip --- How to Prepare for Chance Of a Renewed Recession; `Don't Avoid
the Market'
By Jeff D. Opdyke and Ruth Simon
1,356 words
28 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IS YOUR PORTFOLIO prepared for a double dip?
Some of the latest economic reports seem to hint that the U.S. economy, thought to be righting itself just a
few weeks ago, may in fact may be slipping into recession again. Factory orders have declined, employment
recently has shrunk and the consensus view of economists is that the nation's gross domestic product grew in
last year's fourth quarter at a scant 0.5% annual rate, substantially slower than expectations.
Meanwhile, the stock market, recoiling from the weak economic news and the mounting anxiety over war with
Iraq, has relinquished all its early gains. The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell more than 140 points
yesterday, closing below the 8,000 mark for the first time this year.
While a double dip isn't a given -- most economists are forecasting moderate growth this year -- the
increasing likelihood of another slowdown raises troubling questions for investors still smarting from three
years of bear markets. Where should you invest when nothing looks terribly appealing, when interest rates
on money-market accounts are at their lowest levels in decades, and the economy may be heading back into
the lurch?
Looking at last year's winners isn't the answer. Bonds, real estate and gold all had great runs. But returns in
all three sectors are likely to be much more modest this year.
Nor should you completely rip up your portfolio for a worst-case economic scenario. Doing so "means you'll
only be right in one case -- the worst case," says Ernie Ankrim, chief investment strategist at Frank Russell
Co., an investment-services firm.
Still, there are ways you can tweak your portfolio to build in some protection if the economy goes wobbly
again. Jim Stack, president of InvesTech Research, a market-research firm in Whitefish, Mont., has
structured his portfolio specifically to address the possibility of a double dip. Along with a sizeable 50% in
cash, he holds defensive stocks such as health-care provider Renal Care Group and financial concern
Washington Federal as well as small positions in energy and gold. Other Wall Street pros try to ride out
downturns by investing in areas such as health care or consumer products that tend to be recession-proof.
Stocks
Investing in stocks may seem a poor choice right now, but "you don't avoid the market," says David Wyss,
Standard &Poor's chief economist. If stocks return just 7% a year going forward, that likely will still beat bonds
while outpacing inflation. Even in today's tough times, a balanced portfolio should have at least 60% to 65%
in the stock market.
To buffer your stock portfolio, consider over-weighting energy stocks, consumer staples and health care.
Energy stocks serve as hedges against some of the worries about war in Iraq. If oil prices go up,
energy-company earnings benefit. Already, energy prices are up, and the pure exploration and production
companies are riding high as a result. But diversified energy companies such as Exxon Mobil and
ChevronTexaco are still trading near their yearly lows and stand to benefit from higher oil prices.
No matter what becomes of the economy, consumers must spend money on certain things, such as beer and
soft drinks, and food and pharmaceuticals. Familiar brands such as Coca-Cola, Anheuser-Busch and Kraft
Foods could be relatively untouched even if the overall market slips. Basic retailers such as Wal-Mart Stores
and drugstore chain CVS should also be affected less than upscale retailers by a tough economy.
The health-care industry, meanwhile, operates largely independently of economic cycles. "Health care is
going to be a big winner," whether the economy improves or sinks, says Edward Yardeni, chief investment

--- Page 175 ---
Page 175 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
strategist for Prudential Financial. Health-care providers, such as health-management organizations and
hospitals, have had a strong 12 months already. But many of the top pharmaceuticals can be had relatively
cheaply these days.
Bonds
Bonds have already had their bull market as interest rates have plunged in the past few years. That is
because bond prices rise when rates fall.
But the municipal-bond sector could be a big winner, regardless of the economy. Munis have taken a beating
during the past year because of heavy issuance of new debt and, more recently, because of concerns about
President Bush's tax plan for dividends. As such, "we have an extraordinarily cheap muni market right now,"
says George Friedlander, fixed-income strategist at Salomon Smith Barney.
A double dip likely would push interest rates lower, helping munis. But munis could also benefit if a
strengthening economy helps the financial underpinnings of states and of local government. According to
Morningstar, some of the better-performing muni-bond funds include the Fidelity Spartan Intermediate Muni
Income fund and the USAA Tax Exempt Intermediate-Term fund.
Don't forget foreign bonds. Interest rates on developed-nation corporate bonds are roughly equal to what
you can find at home. On top of that, if the dollar continues to weaken, Americans will see their returns juiced
on bonds that pay their coupons in pounds or euros or yen.
Some professionals are recommending keeping 10% or 15% of your bond portfolio in foreign bonds. The
easiest way to do this is a foreign bond fund such as the T. Rowe Price International Bond fund. Be sure your
fund doesn't hedge against currency risk, in which case the falling dollar won't benefit you,
Real Estate, Gold and Cash
The last time stocks fell and couldn't get up -- during the late-1960s and '70s -- real estate ruled.
But don't rush to invest in property just now. Inflation, which drove up real estate in the 1970s, is pretty much
dead these days. Moreover, real-estate prices are already high in many parts of the country. If you want to
invest in real estate, "invest in the house you live in," says Mr. Wyss, "by paying down your mortgage. Your
return will be whatever interest rate your mortgage carries."
Gold is seen as a safe haven in tumultuous times, and prices have had a strong run, surging nearly 25% last
year. It is up again this year. Mr. Stack, of InvesTech, counsels investors to have between 3% and 7% of their
portfolio tied to gold, largely as a hedge against the weakening dollar.
You can also increase your returns on cash a bit by opening a certificate of deposit denominated in a foreign
currency. Everbank (everbank.com) offers certificates of deposits in a variety of currencies. Chuck Butler,
president of Everbank World Markets, suggests investors consider the New Zealand or Norwegian CDs,
yielding 5.09% and 4.84%, respectively, over three months. You get the added benefit that your return is
even better if the dollar continues to fall.
The minimum investment is generally $10,000.
---
Warning Signs
Below are some signs that could indicate that the economy is moving
into a double-dip recession.
-- Unemployment insurance claims begin rising above 425,000.
-- Consumer confidence continues to decline.
-- Declining applications for mortgage loans by new home buyers.
-- Decline in commercial and industrial loans outstanding.
-- Negative same-store sales growth at Wal-Mart Stores.
-- Wilshire 5000 Total Market Index falls below 8,000.
Source: Economy.com
---

Investment: ExxonMobil, ChevronTexaco
Reason: Energy stocks are likely to hold up well if war breaks out.
Investment: Coca-Cola, Kraft Foods, Anheuser-Busch
Reason: People will continue to buy food and drink, regardless of the
economy.
Investment: Pfizer, Boston Scientific
