Reason: Folks will need healthcare no matter what.
Investment: Fidelity Spartan Intermediate Muni Income Fund, USAA
Tax-Exempt Intermediate-Term Fund
Reason: Municipal bonds are cheap and poised for a rebound.

International Bond Fund
Reason: Foreign bonds are a play on the falling U.S. dollar.

Reason: Many offer better yields than domestic CDs and will benefit
from a weaker dollar.

Currency Trading: After a Session of Volatile Moves, Dollar Finishes at Mixed Levels
Currency Trading: After a Session of Volatile Moves, Dollar Finishes at Mixed Levels
By John Parry
Dow Jones Newswires
630 words
28 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Struck by a fusillade of remarks from top U.N., U.S. and European officials' about the standoff
with Iraq, the dollar took a helter-skelter ride, ending mixed against major rivals.
Although currency traders still expect a U.S.-led war on Iraq to occur sometime in the next few months, the
stream of information and rhetorical fencing left them none the wiser about timing. In volatile trading, the
dollar came full circle against most rivals, as it remained a visible symbol of the "risk aversion" in global asset
markets.
While gold soared to six-year highs, reaching more than $370 per troy ounce in intraday trade, U.S. stock
markets broke below some important support levels, reflecting discomfort with riskier global assets and the
creeping effects of a dollar that continues sinking, especially against the euro. The dollar hit fresh 39-month
lows against the common currency yesterday, which briefly captured the $1.09 mark before retreating
somewhat.
"People are obviously worried about the impact of war and the pressure on the dollar is probably more of a
reason for gold climbing than anything else," said Ed Stapleton, head of foreign exchange at Fortis USA
Financial Markets in New York.
Late yesterday afternoon in New York, the euro was at $1.0857, up from $1.0831 late Friday. The dollar was
at 118.40 yen, up from 117.83 yen late Friday.
Against the Swiss franc -- a classic currency of refuge in times of global military conflict -- the dollar was at
1.3521 francs, moderately lower than 1.3530 francs late Friday. The pound was at $1.6367, up from $1.6338
late Friday.
The remark by Secretary of State Colin Powell that Iraq is in "material breach" of U.N. resolutions weighed on
the dollar. Currency ma-rkets also paid attention to comments by White House spokesman Ari Fleischer,
indicating that the administration plans to draft a second U.N. resolution aimed at authorizing war against
Iraq.
Initially, the dollar leapt across the board early in the New York morning, prompting some analysts to question
whether the Bank of Japan may have intervened to support the currency. But there was no concrete evidence
of any central bank action.
Others attributed the dollar's jump to investors betting that chief U.N. weapons inspector Hans Blix would
make the case for more time to carry out inspections. In fact, Mr. Blix's report was somewhat more hawkish, if
anything.
Even though the dollar clocked up hefty gains early in the day, it reversed course early in the afternoon, amid
signals that the U.S. may be just as disposed to seek a swift military confrontation with Iraq.
The dollar took little heart from the weapons inspectors' report, which "still leaves room for the possibility of
unilateral action by the U.S. and the U.K.," said Ashraf Laidi, chief currency strategist at MG Financial, in New
York.
There are more than purely Iraq jitters that are weighing on the greenback these days. The dollar will have to
contend with confirmation hearings for Treasury secretary nominee John Snow this morning in Washington,
followed by President Bush's State of the Union address later in the day.
That speech "is the key item we're going to be trading off for the rest of the week," said Tim Mazanec, senior
currency strategist with Investors Bank &Trust in Boston. Analysts will listen closely not just for the president's

--- Page 178 ---
Page 178 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
view of the U.S. economy, but for any indications of links between Saddam Hussein's regime and terrorist
group al Qaeda.

Prospect of War Weighs on Stocks; Even Quick Victory May Not Help
Prospect of War Weighs on Stocks; Even Quick Victory May Not Help
By E.S. Browning
1,527 words
27 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The prospect of war with Iraq is smothering the stock market, and investors increasingly fear that even a
quick end to the Iraq crisis won't put the market's broader worries to rest.
As tensions worsened last week between the U.S. and its European allies about how to deal with Iraq, the
Dow Jones Industrial Average finished the week at a three-month low. Concerns are building among
investors that history's normal pattern -- in which war typically is good for stocks -- might not apply this time.
The worry is that even if the U.S. defeats Iraq quickly, or somehow prevails without war, U.S. investors will
continue to face a host of world problems that could weigh on stocks for some time to come. In addition to
continuing economic and corporate troubles here in the U.S., investors worry about North Korea's nuclear
threat, which some consider an even bigger problem than Iraq. Others think that continuing instability in the
Middle East could keep oil prices high. And rising tensions with Europe have raised fears that the U.S. may
be going out on a limb, isolating itself from allies, which could produce bigger problems later.
Also, America's war against terrorism won't be over after an Iraq war, meaning that the risk of further attacks
will continue to hang over the stock market. "Even if you beat Saddam, do you beat terrorism?" asks Henry
Herrmann, chief investment officer at mutual-fund group Waddell & Reed in Overland Park, Kan. "Terrorism is
a weight on the market."
After soaring more than 6% in the first two weeks of the new year, the Dow industrials plunged 5.3% last
week alone. On Friday, the average was down 238.46 points, or 2.85%, at 8131.01, having given back all of
the new year's gains.
The immediate problem was the diplomatic flareup as longtime allies France and Germany criticized the U.S.
for moving too rapidly toward war. Tempers cooled somewhat after the U.S. suggested it could agree to give
United Nations inspectors a little more time to do their work. If the immediate Iraq crisis were to fade,
investors could seize upon the respite as a reason to buy beaten-down stocks, sending indexes back up.
Plenty of investors still are hoping to see stocks return to the historical pattern, in which they weaken just
before a war, but then rebound strongly soon after the gunfire begins. That is what happened during World
War II, the Korean War and the Gulf War, when conflict built patriotism and stimulated the economy.
It seemed to be happening in the fall of 2001, when stocks fell after the Sept. 11 attacks, but then rose
strongly once the U.S. began its war on Afghanistan's Taliban and al Qaeda.
But the war in Afghanistan didn't produce the same result. Instead of sparking a continuing bull market, that
fighting led to more investor disappointment. All the stock gains from that war now have unraveled, not just
because of Iraq, but also because of Enron Corp.'s meltdown, anemic corporate earnings and Wall Street
scandals. The Dow industrials now have fallen back below their level before the Afghanistan war. In fact, blue
chips are back below the low of 8235.81 hit on Sept. 21, 2001, following the terrorist attacks on New York and
Washington. However, the average is still well above the low it hit last October.
Contributing to the declines, and to the reluctance of big companies to step in and invest in the future, is a
worry that victory in Afghanistan and a potential victory in Iraq don't represent an end to the conflict. Terrorism
not only remains a concern but could grow as a result of the fallout from any U.S. move against Iraq.
Partly because of the lingering uncertainty, Mr. Herrmann says, his fund group is keeping about 10% of its
assets in cash -- more than double the normal level. He also has put more than usual in bonds and in gold.
Many smaller investors also are pulling back from stocks, withdrawing hundreds of millions of dollars from
stock mutual funds in the third week of January.

--- Page 180 ---
Page 180 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
David Briggs, head of stock trading at Pittsburgh mutual-fund group Federated Investors, says that money
managers of stock funds at his firm are required to invest fully in stocks. But even with those constraints, they
are finding ways to drag their feet about putting new money to work, and Mr. Briggs figures that they are
holding about 6% of their assets in cash, roughly double their normal level.
The diplomatic standoff between the U.S. and its allies has started to weigh down the market. Mr. Briggs says
he is seeing signs that European investors are dumping U.S. stocks. Not only are they worried about an
impending war, he says, they also are suffering from the dollar's recent decline against the euro, which
reduces the value of investments in U.S. securities.
"This whole Iraq situation has soured the world's perception of the U.S.," Mr. Briggs says. "At first they
weren't purchasing U.S. assets, and now they are dumping U.S. assets."
Mr. Briggs and other experienced investors believe that stocks initially will surge if the U.S. successfully
invades Iraq or if Saddam Hussein folds and goes into exile. Some think that an end to the Iraq uncertainty
could be all stocks need to create a powerful bull market that could last months, perhaps years. The U.S.
economy, after all, is slowly recovering, and stocks should respond if the Iraq uncertainty is lifted.
But that is where the doubts come in. Consider the World War II attack on Pearl Harbor, which has at times
been compared with Sept. 11. Stocks went down at the start of World War II, just as they did before the
Afghan attack and just as they have now. But stocks turned up shortly before the Battle of Midway in June
1942. After the Midway victory, fears of invasion and defeat began to be replaced by the expectation of
victory. The Dow industrials doubled from the Battle of Midway through the end of 1945.
Could victory over Iraq be viewed as another Midway? Many analysts are dubious.
Primarily, that's because even the end of the Hussein regime doesn't mean terrorism will go away, or that
international enmity against the U.S. will ease. Even the capture of Osama bin Laden wouldn't be seen as
ending the terrorist threat.
"This isn't just Iraq, it is much more," says market strategist Alan Ackerman of New York brokerage firm
Fahnstock &Co. "Our way of life and the growth in our economy are affected by concern about attack from
within or an external attack." Business leaders "don't want to put money to work in their businesses until they
have some clarity on what lies ahead," he says.
Some investors fear that, even if the war were brief, Iraq would damage its oil wells and prevent the U.S. from
using them. That could send oil prices up even further, jolting an already fragile U.S. economy. Others worry
about a loss of stability at friendly Arab kingdoms, whose legitimacy could come into question.
"I don't have any doubt that if we have a quick, punch-in-the-mouth war against Saddam, the stock market
[initially] will benefit," says Mr. Herrmann of Waddell. "But I have a question about whether it will provide a
lasting solution to the problem at hand."
Not all wars are good for stocks, says Tim Hayes, global stock strategist at market research firm Ned Davis
Research in Venice, Fla., who has made a study of past international crises' effects on stock markets. "If [an
Iraq invasion] just muddies things up in the Middle East and things look worse, maybe Vietnam is a better
comparison" than World War II, he says.
Mr. Hayes figures that the Vietnam War contributed to the end of a long-term bullish period that began in
1942, and which he calculates ended in 1966. Many things contributed to the onset of the long-term bear
market that began in the late 1960s and early 1970s, including soaring oil prices and mounting inflation, but
it is widely agreed that the bearish period lasted until 1982. "War itself is far less important for stock prices
than the influence that war can have on monetary and economic conditions," Mr. Hayes wrote in a recent
report.
In past crises, stocks typically have risen more than 13% one year after a surprise attack such as Pearl
Harbor or the North Korean invasion of South Korea, says Gibbons Burke of market-research firm
MarketHistory.com. Eighteen months after the event, stocks have tended to be up more than 30%, he says.
But today, more than a year after Sept. 11, stocks are down.
"History has to be used with caution, prudence and sober judgment," Mr. Burke says.

Currency Trading: Dollar Declines Against Rivals

NEW YORK -- The dollar remained on the ropes, buffeted by some hawkish remarks from the Bush
administration about the standoff with Iraq and stung by a pointed signal from Russia's central bank that the
appeal of dollar-denominated assets is waning.
Oleg Vyugin, first deputy chairman at the Russian central bank, said the bank plans to cut the share of
dollars in its foreign-exchange reserves and increase the share of other currencies. The dollar makes up
more than half of the Russian central bank's $47.9 billion in gold and foreign-exchange reserves.
The common European currency broke fresh 39-month highs against the dollar, while hitting a 3 1/2-year high
of 127.37 yen versus its Japanese counterpart. Sterling jumped to its highest level against the dollar since
January 2000, breaking through the $1.62 barrier on much better-than-expected United Kingdom retail sales
for December.
Meanwhile, gold hit six-year highs. By contrast, the Swiss franc, another classic refuge for global investors in
times of war, didn't make a sharp gain on the day, albeit remaining near four-year highs against the dollar.
Late yesterday in New York, the dollar was trading at 118.15 yen, down from 118.26 yen late Wednesday in
New York. The euro was trading at $1.0751, up from $1.0730 late Wednesday. The dollar was at 1.3628
Swiss francs, down from 1.3635 francs. Sterling was at $1.6230, up from $1.6193.
Among Latin American currencies, Venezuela's bolivar traded at 2,300 bolivars to the dollar in a parallel
market that developed soon after the government called a halt on foreign-exchange sales Wednesday, a
banking executive said. Officially, the currency stopped trading at 1,853 bolivars to the dollar Tuesday. The
government said the halt will last through next Tuesday but Domingo Maza, a director at the central bank,
indicated it may go on beyond that.
Before the trading halt, the bolivar had dropped about 25% since the beginning of the year, after falling 46%
last year, amid soaring demand for dollars as Venezuelans fled to safe harbors on fears the government may
be forced to impose strong measures because a continuing 53-day-old general strike is having a devastating
effect on the economy.

Stocks Drop, Wiping Out January's Gains
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Drop, Wiping Out January's Gains
By E.S. Browning
751 words
23 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SO MUCH FOR THE New Year's rally.
With five straight days of declines, the stock market has obliterated the year's early burst of investor optimism
-- and has wiped out January's sharp stock gains as well.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average and the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index both were up 6% in the first two
weeks of trading, and both now have given back all those gains and more in just five sessions. The Dow
industrials finished the wipeout yesterday with a decline of 1.47%, or 124.17 points, to 8318.73. After starting
the year at 8341.63, the blue chips now are off 0.27% for 2003.
Why? "I-R-A-Q," said Alfred Kugel, senior investment strategist at Chicago money-management firm Stein
Roe Investment Counsel. "This is affecting consumers' decisions about whether they are going to buy
something this week, and it is affecting business decisions about whether they are going to add to inventory
or invest in new equipment -- and it is affecting investors' decisions about whether they are going to buy
something."
The looming war, in other words, is putting a double whammy on stocks. It isn't just making investors hunker
down; it also is contributing to the gloomy outlooks being offered up by corporate leaders right now, and that
is making investors even more pessimistic.
War worries also are driving money back into Treasury bonds and even into the money market, despite the
fact that both of those investments feature some of the lowest interest rates in years. The benchmark
10-year Treasury note, for example, yesterday rose another 13/32, or $4.0625 for every $1,000 invested. The
yield falls as the note's price rises, and that pushed the yield down to just 3.924%.
Among the gloomy earnings announcements investors were digesting: Fourth-quarter performance was
disappointing at J.P. Morgan Chase and Eastman Kodak, both members of the Dow industrials. Kodak said it
also would miss first-quarter targets. Merrill Lynch said its performance targets would be hard for it to hit, as
well. Motorola posted a second consecutive profit but didn't boost earnings targets. All four stocks fell.
Motorola did project 10% growth in world-wide computer-chip sales this year, and technology stocks survived
the day better than most.
But the Nasdaq Composite Index, dominated by big tech stocks (although not including Motorola, which
trades on the New York Stock Exchange), fell 0.35%, or 4.77 points, to 1359.48. It still is up 1.8% for the
year.
The S&P 500 fell 1.04%, or 9.26 points, to 878.36, down 0.17% so far this year.
Some of the trouble began on Tuesday, when the Dow industrials broke through 8500, a key support level,
said Todd Leone, head of listed trading at New York brokerage firm SG Cowen. After that, he said, the
decline became relentless.
Trading volume, which had been lackluster most of the year, was above average yesterday on the New York
Stock Exchange, and traders said some investors turned into strong sellers, unloading stock until the Iraq
situation gets clearer.
"President Bush is pushing for a hard line on Iraq and it doesn't seem the U.N. is backing us," Mr. Leone said.
"I think people are afraid of that. It doesn't look good for Bush and it doesn't look good for us."

--- Page 183 ---
Page 183 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Kugel of Stein Roe said the Dow industrials could "run up 1,000 points in a very short period of time" once
the Iraq situation is resolved, and that the gains could begin a few weeks before any military action is
expected. But until then, the market has a cloud over it, he said.
"Everybody's worried about a war and nobody knows if it's two weeks or two months away, or if it will happen

avoid a war or if it is concluded successfully and quickly, then we will see a shift in focus to the future and to
an economic recovery. Obviously, however, we have some high hurdles to get over between now and then."
Gold moved back near its six-year high and the dollar again fell against the euro. A poll conducted by ABC
News and Money Magazine showed consumer confidence falling last week.

Currency Trading: Sliding Dollar's Fate May Be Decided in Asia --- Chinese, Japanese Top Foreign-Investor List For U.S. Securities
Currency Trading: Sliding Dollar's Fate May Be Decided in Asia --- Chinese, Japanese Top
Foreign-Investor List For U.S. Securities
By Craig Karmin and Karen Richardson
1,359 words
20 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WITH THE DOLLAR in its worst slide against major European currencies since 1987, most of the attention
has focused on European investors' jitters and what that means for the U.S. currency.
But that may be the wrong place to look for answers. Analysts now say the most important action in
determining the fate of the dollar this year may be unfolding in another corner of the globe, as Asian investors
have become crucial swing players.
Japanese, Chinese and other Asians collectively have become the biggest overseas investors in U.S.
securities, bypassing the Europeans late last year in terms of net new money pumped into U.S. stocks and
bonds. Asians last year accounted for 40% of the foreign-investment flows into the U.S., double the amount
they contributed just two years ago.
This support has provided a much-needed counterbalance to Europe's pullback. And while it hasn't been
enough to spark a recovery in the dollar, it has ensured that the decline has been gradual, even orderly.
"Asians are America's bankers right now," says Joseph Quinlan, global economist for Johns Hopkins
University's Center for Transatlantic Relations in Washington, D.C.
Following years of robust investment in U.S. securities, Europeans steadily scaled back their purchases of
U.S. stocks last year. They worried that the dollar looked overvalued and that U.S. stocks appeared
expensive compared with share prices elsewhere. The series of corporate-governance and accounting
scandals last year also spooked European investors, encouraging flight to non-U.S. assets.
Through October, European net purchases of $152 billion in U.S. securities were down 35% from the same
period a year earlier. Moreover, investors in the 12 countries participating in the euro turned net sellers over
the 10-month period for the first time since 1993.
The resulting decline for the dollar has been stark. Since the start of last year, the dollar has tumbled 16%
against the euro, 17% against the Swiss franc and 10% against the pound sterling. And a growing number of
foreign-exchange analysts predict it will get worse, with many forecasting the dollar to fall an additional 4% to
8% against the European common currency by the end of the year.
But at the same time, Asian investor appetite for U.S. securities, primarily Treasury and federal-agency
bonds, has intensified. Asians were net purchasers of $156 billion in U.S. assets for the first 10 months of
2002, after averaging only about $47 billion a year during the 1990s.
In Asia, "everybody has a fairly large portion of their portfolios in the U.S. agencies because of the large
amount of liquidity and the very efficient way they issue," says Zhu Kai, head of treasury at the Bank of China,
one of the mainland's largest banks and one of the region's largest U.S. dollar investors, with a portfolio worth
tens of billions of dollars.
Like other investors, Asians have been drawn to the relative safe haven of U.S. Treasury bonds following the
Sept. 11 terrorist attacks: South Koreans, Taiwanese and Chinese poured money into the U.S. bond market
and helped drive a rally in Treasury bonds.
Asian demand for U.S. securities has also been rooted in a more self-serving reason: The region's central
bankers have been buying U.S. assets to keep their own currencies relatively weak and help their exporters.
In Japan, the central bank was sporadically buying dollars last fall to prevent the yen from strengthening too
much against the U.S. currency. Even so -- and despite Japan's anemic economy and stock market -- the yen
rose 10% against the dollar last year.
Asia is awash with dollars to invest -- some $950 billion in excess liquidity sloshing around the region's
banking system in reserves, portfolios and deposits -- largely as a legacy of the Asian financial crisis of

--- Page 185 ---
Page 185 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
1997-98. That calamity spurred Asian governments, banks, corporate treasurers and individuals to stash their
reserves and deposits in U.S. dollars after they saw the value of their local currencies crushed. All those
dollars with nowhere to go fueled a phenomenon known as the "Asian bid" -- money that traditionally went
into high-quality, dollar-denominated bonds issued by Asian borrowers, such as local conglomerates and
regional banks.
But now, with an average of just $40 billion of Asian dollar-denominated debt being issued annually, the
Asian bid is scouring the global capital markets for more lucrative but still high-quality investments. In
Thailand and Korea in particular, local fund managers with excess dollars will buy dollar-denominated bonds
from other Asian countries and swap the debt back into their own currency to take advantage of a favorable
exchange rate.
While U.S. Treasurys remain the most popular and safest bet for most Asian investors, more are beginning to
diversify and are moving slightly lower down the credit scale. Most Asian central banks are limited to holding
only the highest-quality, triple-A-rated sovereign and corporate debt, but some Asian fund managers,
insurance companies, corporate and government treasurers and increasingly private banks are diversifying
into more obscure, and in some cases riskier, credits.
Among the big U.S. borrowers attracting interest from Asian investors are the Ford Motor Credit unit of Ford
Motor Co., General Electric Co. and Freddie Mac. Meanwhile, European borrowers are targeting Asians not
only with dollar-denominated debt, but also issuing euro and sterling bonds through private placements and
structured products, exclusively to Asian investors eager to diversify. Asian enthusiasm for European issuers
such as the European Investment Bank, Lloyds TSB Bank PLC, Standard Chartered PLC's Standard
Chartered Bank and high-yielding telecom companies has been particularly strong.
As a result, "Our portfolio is now undergoing a slow but consistent trend of lowering its proportion in U.S.
Treasurys," says Bank of China's Mr. Zhu.
The concern now is over how much longer the Asian support can last. There are anecdotal signs that Asian
central bankers are beginning to diversify their holdings into other currencies, especially the euro, at the
expense of the dollar. Analysts fear that a U.S.-led war in Iraq could further accelerate that trend.
"If Asians pull back from investing in the U.S., there isn't much else to support the dollar," says Rebecca
McCaughrin, an economist at Morgan Stanley.
In November, Japanese investors were buyers of $5.3 billion of U.S. securities while buying $9.1 billion of
securities from euro countries. That marked a notable pullback from the first 10 months of the year, when
Japanese investors favored U.S. over European assets by nearly six to one.
Foreign-exchange analysts have been predicting such moves since the euro was introduced in January 1999:
Asian central bankers will diversify their assets by adding euro-denominated securities.
Mr. Quinlan says this rotation occurs frequently when the dollar loses ground against the euro, though it
usually reverses when the dollar rebounds. Recent geopolitical events, however, seem to favor the euro. A
war in Iraq is expected to be bearish for dollar assets, especially if it appears that the U.S. economy would be
paying the bulk of the war's expenses. One hint of that is the rally in the price of gold, seen as an alternative
to more conventional U.S. investments; since Sept. 11, the price of gold has surged 31% to $356.80 an
ounce, near a six-year high on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
North Korea's saber-rattling could also weigh on the dollar if the country is the beneficiary of Asian aid that
would otherwise have been invested in the U.S. "The reunification of the Koreas could trigger a wholesale
reallocation/restructuring of Asia's massive savings pool," Mr. Quinlan wrote in a recent report.
(See related article: "Heard on the Street: European Firms Face Earnings Pressures From Weak Greenback"
-- WSJ Jan. 20, 2003)

Panel of Economists Advise Shedding Treasurys for Equities
Panel of Economists Advise Shedding Treasurys for Equities
By Ralph E. Winter
Dow Jones Newswires
737 words
15 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
No Page Citation
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
CLEVELAND -- A panel of three Midwest economists said the U.S. economy will post a sharp jump in its
growth rate once uncertainty about a war with Iraq ends, and that it is now time to move money from Treasury
bonds into equities.
"This economic soft patch will be a passing phenomenon," said Richard J. DeKaser, senior vice president
and chief economist with National City Bank, a unit of National City Corp., Cleveland. It began last August
after President Bush announced his determination to disarm Iraq, he said, and it will end when Iraq is
defeated or war no longer appears likely.
So, "lighten up on Treasurys," advises Kenneth T. Mayland, president of ClearView Economics LLC, an
economic consulting concern based in Pepper Pike, Ohio, a suburb of Cleveland. "Getting Iraq [issue] behind
us will be the catalyst for a rise in the stock market," as well as rapid economic expansion, triggering higher
interest rates that will drive down the price ofTreasury bonds.
Interest rates on Treasury bonds are at historically low levels, Mr. Mayland, and rising prices for gold and
other commodities are a signal that the markets expect higher economic activity and higher interest rates.
Investors who want to stay in fixed-income investments should consider switching to Treasury bills and other
investments comparable to cash or to higher-yielding corporate bonds, Mr. Mayland said. The shakeout in
corporate bonds is about over, he said, adding that companies likely to default on interest payments generally
have already done so.
Raj Aggarwal, who holds the Firestone Chair of Corporate Finance at Kent State University's Graduate
School of Management, Kent, Ohio, agrees investors should sell Treasury bonds, and said he expects stocks
of technology companies "with huge hoards of cash" to do well in coming months.
All three economists, who were interviewed after an annual presentation to Cleveland securities analysts last
week, believe that if there is a war with Iraq, it will be brief, and that it won't trigger widespread acts of
terrorism against the U.S. here or abroad. However, if the war is prolonged or is followed by a lengthy
occupation of Iraq, Mr. Aggarwal said, "I'm not sure how the economy would handle it."
None of the economists think President Bush's tax-cut proposals are necessary to accelerate economic
growth. They expect a rebound in growth; a reversal of the drop in capital spending, which they called a major
factor in the 2001 slowdown; a turnaround in rising unemployment, and resumption of production by
manufacturers. All would occur once the uncertainty over Iraq is resolved.
Mr. Mayland maintains the economy is actually doing much better than many people are projecting. "They
talk as though the economy still is in recession," he said, even though "gross domestic product rose at a
3.25% rate in the first nine months of last year, close to the economy's long-term growth potential of 3.5%."
In the 1991 and 2002 recoveries, the economic improvement has been questioned because the upturn hasn't
been as universal as it was in previous recoveries, Mr. Mayland said. "The business-cycle model has
changed," he said, adding that recessions aren't as deep, and they are followed by more modest recoveries.
All three economists expect the GDP expansion to continue at a 3% to 4% rate in 2003. But Mr. Aggarwal
said longer-term trends indicate the recovery in the U.S. Midwest and other manufacturing regions may be
weaker than for the rest of the country. "Manufacturers who produce goods and don't sell a lot of service with
the goods will have a tough time," he said, because rapidly growing global capacity will deprive them of the
power to raise prices or sometimes even maintain current prices.

--- Page 187 ---
Page 187 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In addition, according to Mr. Aggarwal, the quality of products manufactured in China and other low-cost
countries is rising rapidly. Some U.S. producers are moving production to Mexico or Asia to achieve higher
quality, not just because of lower costs, he said.
Mr. Aggarwal doesn't advise shunning manufacturing stocks, partly because "industrial stocks have been
severely beaten down." But he recommends buying "only the strongest and best-run companies," favoring
those with global diversification and those selling a package of product and service rather than just making
products.

The Point of a Tax Cut Is More of Them
Bookshelf
LEISURE &ARTS
The Point of a Tax Cut Is More of Them
By Todd G. Buchholz
1,049 words
9 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
D10
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
GREENBACK
By Jason Goodwin
(Henry Holt, 321 pages, $26)
Samuel Goldwyn once said that a "verbal contract isn't worth the paper it's written on." (Nor is a typed
contract, my Hollywood friends tell me.) Still, he presumably thought U.S. dollars were worth something. He
certainly collected a lot of them. He ordered his staff: "Spare no expense to make everything as economical
as possible!"
All this raises a question: What is a dollar bill but an airy verbal promise that the government will defend the
value of its currency? Tourists can no longer knock on the Treasury Department door and demand gold as
they could 100 years ago. Back then every bill declared: "The United States of America Will Pay to the Bearer
on Demand One Dollar in Gold." In the 1930s the U.S. dropped this pledge and replaced it with: "Pay to the
Bearer on Demand One Dollar of Lawful Money." A puckish man from Cleveland decided to test the
government by mailing a 10-dollar bill to Washington and asking to swap it for "lawful money." The wits at
Treasury mailed back two fives.
Though the Treasury building looks imposing, with its statue of Alexander Hamilton and its somber portraits of
past secretaries, Jason Goodwin's "Greenback" describes some madcap adventures there. During the 1860s,
Spencer Morton Clark, who ran the currency bureau like a harem, was under investigation for embezzlement,
fraud and sexual harassment. With his finger in the till and his pants down, he had the gall to put his own face
on the five-cent note. Now that's one way to try to buy yourself out of trouble! "`Treasury woman' became a
snickering euphemism for prostitute," Mr. Goodwin reports.
A travel writer and the author of a study on the Ottoman Empire, Mr. Goodwin weaves his engaging historical
anecdotes around a general theme: The U.S. accepted new-fangled paper money more willingly than
Europeans because Americans were more willing to take a flier on new things. How could you sail across
oceans, carve a country out of rocky land and then buck the paper buck?
Mr. Goodwin quotes the New England preacher Cotton Mather, who approved of paper money and argued
that "where money has not been introduced, men are brutish and savage and nothing good has been
cultivated." Three hundred years ago it was kooky to believe that paper was as good as gold. Today the
argument stands on its head. Most economists scoff at "gold bugs," thinking that only kooky people would
prefer gold to paper money.
Mr. Goodwin does a fine job taking a magnifying glass to the dollar and describing the fancy artwork. "The
dollar . . . glories in its weirdness," he says. What is that eye at the top of the pyramid? Why does George
Washington look so much like Louis Rukeyser? Ben Franklin devised the idea of leafy veins in the paper to
deter counterfeiters, but where does "E pluribus unum" come from? Cicero? The Pantheon? No, from GQ
magazine (actually its forerunner, Gentleman's Magazine). I can't wait to see what Latin phrase Maxim
magazine bestows on future money designers.
Mr. Goodwin rolls through American history, stopping whenever there's something to be said about the dollar.
He pauses in the 1800s to tell us the origin of the world "Dixie." It came from the 10-dollar bills used by
travelers along the Mississippi and marked with a prominent "Ten-Dix." The northerner who wrote the song
"Dixie" originally wished that "he wuz in the land of the dixes."
Apparently no one knows where the dollar sign came from. With about $7 trillion circulating around the globe,
the sign is the most ubiquitous brand in the world. Not even Coca-Cola or Tommy Hilfiger comes close. And

--- Page 189 ---
Page 189 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
yet Mr. Goodwin can't get to the bottom of the mystery. (I'll toss in a guess: How about the Greek letter sigma,
meaning the sum of costs?)
Whatever the design, in the late 19th century the U.S. seemed to be running out of dollars. As corn and
wheat prices tumbled, farmers, who had to pay back debts with an appreciated currency, lost their farms, and
Willie Nelson wasn't around to raise funds for them. William Jennings Bryan tried to ride to the rescue,
denouncing the gold standard and urging a looser money supply (by coining silver). His soaring words
helped to win him the 1896 Democratic nomination -- "You shall not press down upon the brow of labor this
crown of thorns, you shall not crucify mankind upon a cross of gold" -- but he lost the election.
Mr. Goodwin could sometimes use a stronger narrative thread to sew his anecdotes together. He tells us that
the word "dollar" comes from thaler, a Bohemian word named after the coins in Joachimsthal. Then we hear
that the A-bomb dropped on Hiroshima derived from uranium that Madame Curie rifled through, which
originated in, you guessed it, the mines of Joachimsthal. So the A-bomb and the dollar are related to
Bohemia, Mr. Goodwin tells us. Mmm. That stretch reminded me of the "Six Degrees of Separation From
Kevin Bacon" game. So I checked. Sure enough, Kevin Bacon starred in a movie with Pruitt Taylor Vince,
who appeared in a movie with Paul Newman, whose mother came from Bohemia. So the A-bomb, the dollar
and salad dressing are also related.
While this breezy book will no doubt entertain you, Mr. Goodwin avoids economic policy almost entirely.
Should we care how much money is circulating? Are inflation and deflation always bad? Should a country
devalue its currency to gin up exports? Mr. Goodwin never asks, preferring to think about the dollar in less
momentous terms. That's OK, I suppose, although it might have been nice to have a bit more intellectual gold
backing all that paper green.
---
Mr. Buchholz, managing director of Enso Capital Management LLC, is the author of "Market Shock" (2000).

Cracking China's Market --- Adapting to Chinese Customs, Cultural Changes, Companies From U.S., Europe Find Profit
Cracking China's Market --- Adapting to Chinese Customs, Cultural Changes, Companies From U.S.,
Europe Find Profit
By Leslie Chang and Peter Wonacott
1,878 words
9 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Beijing -- LONG-HELD perception: China is a perpetual market of tomorrow, sucking money from foreign
companies tantalized by visions of a billion new consumers, even as hope of profit recedes year by year.
Dawning reality: China has turned into a profitable market for foreigners in relatively short order.
Capitalizing on dramatic changes that include the emergence of urban consumers, more open local
governments, the spread of modern retail outlets and the entrepreneurialism of the Chinese, a critical mass of
foreign companies are now making money in China. According to an August report by the American Chamber
of Commerce, 64% of about 200 companies surveyed in China say they are profitable.
China is now Eastman Kodak Co.'s second-biggest film market after the U.S., and sales here are growing
faster than in any other major market, the film firm says. Food conglomerate Groupe Danone SA of France
has in the past six years built a $1.2 billion business in China that is profitable in all its divisions. Germany's
Siemens AG, selling everything from washing machines to high-speed railways, saw double-digit profit growth
last year in China, now its No. 3 market after the U.S. and Germany. Procter &Gamble Co. has invested $1
billion in China and says its operation here is profitable. The KFC restaurant chain, owned by Yum Brands
Inc., opens a new store every other day in China, all funded by its Chinese profits. "China is an absolute gold
mine for us," Yum's chief executive David Novak told analysts recently.
China continues to be one of the most challenging markets around, owing to brutal price wars, backstabbing
business partners, widespread counterfeiting and a slow-moving judicial system. But here is how some of the
most successful players in China are turning a profit:
Kodak: Just $12,000 to Run an Outlet
Under a tropical evening sky in Xiamen, executives from Eastman Kodak pile out of a minibus to be greeted
by hundreds of cheering workers. They climb to a stage festooned in red-and-gold Kodak colors, and a
worker reads a poem commemorating production of the 20-millionth Kodak disposable camera in this seaside
city. "Kodak, I love you," he gushes.
Kodak is loving China back. "There were a lot of people that were burned and hurt and called China a
shattered dream," says one of the executives, Ying Yeh, a vice president for Kodak. "But I never had any
doubt."
Kodak, which is based in Rochester, N.Y., has nearly 8,000 photo stores across China, one of the country's
largest retail networks in any sector. The company taps the desire of many Chinese to run their own
businesses while helping them negotiate the ins and outs of setting up shop on their own. Because of China's
vast size, foreign companies seeking national reach must rely to an unusual extent on such a far-flung
network of people, then find ways to tie their interests to the company's own.
One Kodak campaign, "99,000 Will Make You a Boss," offered all the necessary photo-development
equipment, training, and a store license for the equivalent of a one-time fee of 99,000 yuan, less than
$12,000. Kodak negotiated a deal with the Bank of China and other big banks to arrange financing for
individual operators lacking capital. As its numbers of distributors and outlets boomed, Kodak factories have
fed these "minibosses" with competitively priced cameras and film. That's thanks to a big bet Kodak made on
manufacturing in China in 1998, when it picked up three debt-laden state firms and many of their workers for
over $1 billion. In return, Beijing barred new foreign-invested film factories for four years.
The gamble helped Kodak, a distant fourth when it arrived in China in 1994, leapfrog rivals including Japan's
Fuji Photo Film Co., which relies on imports to stock its stores. Today, Fuji's market share has shrunk to 25%
compared with Kodak's 63%.

--- Page 191 ---
Page 191 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Kodak is now expanding in China's poorer west. In a country where fashion and new lifestyles spread at warp
speed, many are buying cameras for a first time to record the change. Says Paul Walrath, a plant manager in
Xiamen and a 26-year Kodak veteran, "We're counting on great performance in China to drive the company
where we want to go."
Danone: Master of Piggybacking
Like Kodak, France's Danone was a relative latecomer when it started building its China business around
1996. It decided to piggyback off the successes of domestic brands rather than to build all its businesses from
scratch, in 1996 buying a controlling stake in Hangzhou Wahaha Group Co., an enterprising maker of
vitamin-enriched milk drinks targeted at children.
Danone embarked on a massive expansion for Wahaha -- building multiple plants across the country, backed
by a huge advertising campaign -- that pushed annual sales from 800 million bottles when it bought the
company to four billion bottles within two years. It then quickly leveraged that scale, distribution network and
brand-name recognition into a new business: bottled water, for China's increasingly health-conscious
population. Through the same obsessive focus on scale and speed, Danone has built Wahaha into China's
biggest water company -- and made China into Danone's biggest water market, with $908 million in revenue
in 2001.
In launching a new drink, Danone expects that prices will collapse by 50% within three years, thanks to local
competition. Only through investing heavily upfront is it possible to achieve economies of scale and, hence,
profitability. "You need to recover your investment before the price wars start," says Simon Israel, Danone's
Asia-Pacific chairman. "I've never seen anything move so fast as it does in China."
Rare for multinationals, Danone acquires Chinese companies but continues to sell their products under their
own brands. The strategy has smoothed the way for a steady diet of acquisitions and curried favor with
Chinese executives and officials, who are loath to see national brands go under.
Today, 80% of Danone's sales here are under Chinese brands. The company has so played down its
multinational origins that it was asked by the government to help Wahaha manufacture a "domestic" cola to
take on Coke and Pepsi. Thus did Danone, which does not sell soft drinks anywhere else in the world,
become the parent of Future Cola, which holds the No. 3 spot in China and is known as "the Chinese
people's own cola."
The moves have handed Danone dominance despite its late start. The company had $1.2 billion in sales here
in 2001 and is one of China's largest food and beverage concerns. Danone has more than 50 plants and
25,000 employees around the country, all built up in the past six years.
Coke: 12-Cent Strategy
Coca-Cola Co., which has seen eight straight years of profit in China, says sales are growing faster here than
anywhere in the world. The Atlanta-based company already reaches 600 million consumers in China's large
and medium-size cities, but its latest drive focuses on reaching the other half.
A survey the company conducted in Yunnan, a largely rural province in the southwest, revealed that most
consumer offerings, from ice cream to drinks, cost between six cents and 36 cents, which meant a 30-cent
can of Coke was too expensive for many. Coke's solution: ramp up its business in returnable bottles, in which
a customer drinks a Coke on the premises of a shop or restaurant. The business, which drives down costs
because bottles and crates can be reused many times, brings the price of a Coke serving down to a single
yuan -- about 12 cents.
"We're looking for this to be a solution for our rural markets," says Nick Moore, region manager for the North
and Southwest China Region.
Such minute price distinctions are crucial in China. At an Internet cafe in Tangshan, where customers can
surf the Web for an hour for a mere 24 cents, 2,000 cases of Coke sold last year, compared with 300 cases in
2001, before Coke launched its cheaper drinks in returnable bottles. "Customers always want the cheapest
thing to drink," says the cafe's owner. "Now, the Coke is the same price as the water."
Yum Brands: Localize, Localize
When KFC set up its first China store in 1987, the venture was seen as so politically sensitive that the site
required the approval of the Beijing mayor, and foreign novelty was a big part of its appeal. Recently, it took
Yum Brands only a day to get most of the approvals it needed to set up a new Pizza Hut restaurant in the
central city of Zhengzhou.

--- Page 192 ---
Page 192 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Globalization is taking hold in China faster than anyone expected, and fast food has become part of the
Chinese landscape. Catering to national tastes, KFC offers soup, rice and Chinese breakfast porridge. It does
its own tests and product launches with minimal input from the home office in Louisville, Ky. "We're free to
create our own products. Our company is not a company that micromanages from a distance," says Sam Su,
president of Greater China for Yum Restaurants China.
KFC now has about 800 restaurants here and plans to open 200 a year for some time to come, and China is
the company's biggest source of profit after the U.S.
---

Multinational companies are expanding their operations in China and
turning profits. Some examples:

Best Route to China May Be Indirect One --- Many Turn to Asian Stocks With Mainland Exposure As Way to Invest in Economy
World Stock Markets
Best Route to China May Be Indirect One --- Many Turn to Asian Stocks With Mainland Exposure As
Way to Invest in Economy
By Craig Karmin
1,464 words
9 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IT BOASTS THE world's biggest population and one of its fastest-growing economies. But many investors are
still wondering when they are ever going to make money in China.
The question is surprising, given China's popularity as a magnet for corporate investment: In the 1990s,
China attracted more than $300 billion in foreign direct investment, second only to the U.S.
And yet for international portfolio managers, China has represented something akin to the Internet craze. As
with the tech boom, investors in China have been drawn to a grand idea full of promise, but one that has
been clouded by hype, tainted by corruption and accompanied by disappointing stock-market returns.
"It's a gigantic market, but there are few ways to play it," says Dave Linehan, an Asia-Pacific fund manager
for U.S. Trust. "I don't think you can find 20 investible names in all of China."
The result is that many investors are looking beyond China's borders as a way to gain exposure to its
locomotive economy -- investing in, say, Taiwan stocks that have mainland exposure.
The frustration over how to play the China market is widespread among fund managers, even though China's
two main markets, in Shanghai and Shenzhen, trade companies with a combined market capitalization of
more than $500 billion, leaving China trailing only Tokyo as the biggest Asian market. But many of China's
hottest initial public offerings sold abroad have given up most or even all their gains, while valuations for
Chinese stocks trading domestically are among the loftiest in the world despite questions about their quality.
Part of the problem is that China stocks are fragmented into several different categories and classes, each
with its own peculiarities. China's so-called B shares -- listings that were created for foreign investors -- have
been widely shunned even by emerging-market managers, who complain that the stocks are illiquid and the
companies are poorly run. Most are state-owned enterprises in which the government sold minority positions
in hope of bailing out faltering businesses.
China stocks have also been marked by extreme periods of boom and bust rarely seen outside the dot-com
arena: Shanghai's B-share index, for example, soared 92% in 2001, driven in large part by Chinese investors
who circumvented the restrictions. Last year, the index tumbled 33%, even as the Morgan Stanley Capital
International emerging markets index fell 8%.
Most U.S. investors interested in China have bought H-shares, or mainland companies that list in Hong Kong
or New York and comply with international accounting and governance rules. Electric utility Huaneng Power
and oil producer CNOOC, for instance, are up more than 70% since going public in 1994 and 2001,
respectively. Those success stories, however, have been the exception. The H-share index peaked in 1993,
when it was five times as high as it is today. Most H shares trade at or below their IPO price.
China Telecom, one of last year's biggest China offerings, had to cut its IPO in half and lower the price to get
the $1.4 billion deal done. Nevertheless, the shares, which also trade in Hong Kong, have slipped 2% on the
New York Stock Exchange since pricing at $18.97 in November.
Beijing announced last month that it is opening the domestic-only A-share market to foreign investors. Yet
strict requirements -- such as the regulation that money managers must have no less than $10 billion in
assets and must hold any Chinese stock for at least one year -- have dulled enthusiasm. In fact, only about
10 foreign investors have expressed interest in even applying for a license to buy these shares, according to
Shanghai Stock Exchange officials. Approvals are expected by April.
Stock valuations that tower over the rest of the world's also have been a deterrent. Years of falling interest
rates, government propaganda about the benefits of stock ownership, and reportedly widespread

--- Page 194 ---
Page 194 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
manipulation by local brokers trading on their own accounts propelled the Shenzhen A-share market seven
times higher between 1996 and mid-2001. Even after a 40% subsequent decline, notes Goldman Sachs, the
Shenzhen A-share index trades at more than 100 times trailing earnings.
All of which is why many fund managers are concluding that the best way to participate in China right now --
and perhaps for a while to come -- is indirectly: owning Taiwanese, South Korean, Singaporean and other
Asian companies that have moved facilities to the mainland to take advantage of low-cost production or
export a bulk of their sales there.
Asian companies that have moved a significant portion of their production to China have slashed their costs
and improved margins. Goldman Sachs says it has identified 388 companies "that have a meaningful
percentage of sales to China or manufacturing activities in China."
The brokerage firm recommends Johnson Electric, a Hong Kong-based electronics company with 80% of its
manufacturing in China, and Hon Hai Precision, a Taiwanese computer parts maker with 70% of
manufacturing in China. But as investors drive up the prices of the most popular outsourcing names,
investors have been turning to smaller companies recently set up on the mainland.
Fang Zheng, a portfolio manager for the New York hedge fund Neon Capital Management, points to two
outsourcers with market capitalizations of around $1 billion: Lite-On IT, a Taiwanese manufacturer of CD
burner equipment for International Business Machines whose shares rose 10% last year to 98.5 New
Taiwanese dollars; and BYD Co., a Hong Kong-listed maker of cellphone batteries that sells to Motorola and
Panasonic. Its share price has jumped 30% to 15.40 Hong Kong dollars since its IPO in July.
Marc Faber, head of his own investment company in Hong Kong and author of the book "Tomorrow's Gold:
Asia's Age of Discovery," prefers to gain exposure through companies that will service China's growing
demand for products, commodities, and travel: plywood and energy producers in Indonesia, for instance, and
hotel and leisure companies in Thailand, where Chinese have moved up to the Southeast Asian country's
second-biggest tourist group.
"One day," he says, evoking China's glorious future, "there will be an equivalent to Procter &Gamble in China.
But for now, I would play it by owning companies that are outside of China."
---
Yesterday's Market Activity
Asian markets were mixed as local factors drove trading, absent a clear direction from Wall Street. Most
European markets were lower. Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index fell 1.41 points, or 0.97%, to
143.95. Excluding the U.S., the index declined 0.46 point, or 0.43%, to 106.03.
In TOKYO, the Nikkei Stock Average fell 1.6%, or 138.70 points, to 8517.80. Mizuho Holdings ended down
4.8% at 100,000 yen after briefly slipping below that psychologically key level for the first time since Nov. 20
on heavy selling from foreign investors. Mitsubishi Tokyo Financial fell 1.6% to 621,000 yen. Sumitomo Mitsui
Financial dropped 5.3% to 341,000 yen, and UFJ Holdings declined 5.2% to 109,000 yen.
In TAIPEI, the Weighted Price Index finished up 2.9%, or 135.85 points, at 4836.93, a more than four-month
high. Sentiment was supported by an easing of restrictions on qualified foreign institutional investors by the
Securities and Futures Commission. The commission halved the asset size foreign investors need to apply
for investment status in Taiwan. The news helped to send cement, plastics and textile subindexes more than
5% higher, while the steel subindex rose 6% and construction gained 4.6%.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 Index fell 0.8%, or 32.60 points, to 3924.80. Retailer Dixons shed 30 pence, or
21%, to 117 pence after saying its Christmas sales were disappointing and pretax profit for its fiscal year will
miss market expectations by about 10%.

Tax-Cut Plans Spark a Rally -- Can It Last? --- Blue Chips Set Record For 3-Day Beginning, As They Surge 171.88
Monday's Markets
Tax-Cut Plans Spark a Rally -- Can It Last? --- Blue Chips Set Record For 3-Day Beginning, As They
Surge 171.88
By E.S. Browning
983 words
7 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
COMPLETING ITS BEST three-day start to a new year ever, the Dow Jones Industrial Average surged again,
as investors celebrated the prospect that the government's economic-stimulus package may be stronger than
expected.
The Dow industrials jumped 171.88 points, or 2%, yesterday. On top of a 3% gain on Jan. 2, the year's
opening day (followed by Friday's modest drop), the industrials are up 5.18% for the year's first three days --
surpassing the previous record of 5.1% at the start of 1988.
Gains were broad, with three stocks rising for every one that fell on the New York Stock Exchange, and with
other indexes, such as the Nasdaq Composite Index and the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, rising more
than 2% each. Treasury bonds resumed their decline as money moved toward stocks.
The gains were fueled partly by a hope that, with the stimulus package serving as a tail wind, corporate
profits will recover as the year goes on. And in part, the stock surge was caused by simple momentum. This
is the time of year when retirement programs send a slug of new cash to money managers. To avoid being
left behind and seeing their performance suffer, skeptical money managers who had held money out of the
market were forced to reverse course and shovel money into stocks.
"People are deciding to move sooner rather than later," said Matthew Johnson, head of U.S. stock trading at
New York brokerage firm Lehman Brothers. "When the market is up 5% and you aren't participating, that
becomes a problem," he said, adding that the buying "feeds on itself and becomes a self-fulfilling prophecy."
The Dow industrials' gain left the index at 8773.57 -- still well below the 9000 level the industrials reached in
July and neared in November, but much stronger than the level of less than 8400 at which the industrials
began this year, following last year's 16.8% drop.
The S&P 500 rose 2.25%, or 20.42 points, to 929.01, up 5.59% in the year's first three days. The Nasdaq
composite rose 2.47%, or 34.24 points, to 1421.32, up 6.43% this year.
Worries still hang over the market, and that was reflected in the fact that trading volume, which was below
average last week, remained only about average yesterday.
Continuing talk about possible war in Iraq helped push gold prices up during the day to their highest levels in
six years.
A report from the Institute for Supply Management on the economy's service sector, which accounts for about
two-thirds of all economic activity, showed the sector expanding less rapidly than economists had thought.
And reports on retail sales continue to suggest that holiday shopping was anemic.
Furthermore, strong gains at the start of the year don't always hold up.
Stocks surged on the second trading day of 2001 after the Federal Reserve began cutting interest rates, for
example, but they soon ran out of steam and headed down. Last year, the S&P 500 peaked early in January.
But for now, at least, hopes for improving profits together with the pressure of new investment money coming
into the marketplace were enough to restore a measure of hope to the stock market.
"The thought is that if the stimulus part can just get put through, business will be persuaded to invest more in
capital equipment," said Todd Clark, head of listed trading at Wells Fargo Securities, a securities arm of Wells
Fargo &Co. in San Francisco. "We have moved to a one-month high on the Dow, the Nasdaq and the S&P,
and that should help to bring more money into the market. I am still fairly optimistic."

--- Page 197 ---
Page 197 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Among the day's strongest perf ormers were stocks that pay dividends, helped by reports that President Bush
plans to recommend eliminating the federal tax investors pay on dividend income.
That came on top of a Wall Street Journal report that the Federal Communications Commission plans to
eliminate a requirement that Baby Bells, or local telephone companies, allow upstart competitors to buy
access to their phone lines. SBC Communications, which is a Baby Bell and also pays a dividend, jumped
8%, making it the biggest percentage gainer among the 30 stocks in the Dow industrials.
On top of that, Morgan Stanley investment strategist Byron Wien, who each year issues 10 predictions about
unexpected events that he thinks will occur in the new year, forecast that U.S. stocks will defy skeptics and
surge 25% in the first half of the year.
Now investors will be looking for some evidence of better days to come, which means they will scrutinize the
comments companies make on the profit outlook when they issue fourth-quarter earnings reports in the next
few weeks.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 1.17%, or 1.25 points, to 107.85.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.41 billion shares traded, 2,485 stocks rose and 849 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 11/32, or $3.4375 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.068%. The 30-year bond was down 17/32 to yield 4.982%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 119.16 yen, down from 119.73, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.0468 from $1.0417.

Commodities Report: Sugar Prices Rise on Outlook Of Tight Supply Outside Asia
Commodities Report: Sugar Prices Rise on Outlook Of Tight Supply Outside Asia
By Susan Buchanan OsterDowJones Commodity News
539 words
7 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- World raw-sugar prices rose on the Coffee, Sugar &Cocoa Exchange, aided by expected
tightness in supplies outside of Asia for the first quarter.
However, traders cautioned that a world-wide glut continues.
The March contract settled 0.21 cent higher at 7.61 cents a pound, but remains below the eight-cent high
touched a year-and-a-half ago.
"Northern Hemisphere raws will be tight in the first quarter [of 2003], partly because Brazil is sold out of its
center-south crop and Cuba has a small crop," said Hiroyuki Takada, broker with Tokyo General USA in New
York. But Brazil's next center-south harvest starts in April to May, and "sugar will be in oversupply for the year
as a whole," he added.
The coming harvest in center-south Brazil, the world's top producer and exporter, is expected to be another
giant and will start a little earlier to supply alcohol for local fuel.
Speculative funds bought sugar futures Monday, "influenced by strength in oil, gold and the [Commodity
Research Bureau] Futures Index, which reached another five-year high," Mr. Takada said. "Funds are
unlikely to liquidate as long as other commodities are rising."
The CRB is an index of commodity futures that is watched for signs of inflation.
Also helping the market Monday was a strike by drivers of large trucks in Thailand. That action began last
week and has slowed down movement of cane to local mills and sugar to Thai ports. Thailand is in the middle
of processing a big new crop, which heads to market in the first half this year.
Australia's 2002-03 sugar crop was also larger than expected, and East Asia will be well supplied in first half,
traders said.
"Two trade houses have been taking a lot of sugar from Thailand and other producers for Russia, and no
one's sure just how much they've taken," Mr. Takada said. Last autumn, Russian officials auctioned off import
licenses to companies for the nation's 2003 raw import quota of 3.95 million metric tons.
Russian importers have been quiet recently, ahead of Orthodox Christmas holiday today.
Open interest, or positions left open at the end of the day, in world futures set records on the CSCE last
week, reaching 226,357 lots on Thursday. Trade houses have been active buyers in recent months,
supporting prices on dips.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Oil prices dropped on the New York Mercantile Exchange Monday after reports that the
Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries is ready to increase production in an effort to curb high oil
prices. Selling hit in London and New York on the news. The February crude-oil contract fell 98 cents to
$32.10 a barrel.
PORK BELLIES: Pork bellies on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange closed down three cents, their
exchange-imposed daily limit. Market watchers cited speculative positioning ahead of a potentially bearish
weekly report on bellies in storage due out Tuesday. Pressure also came from weakness in lean hogs and
commercial selling. February bellies ended at 84.85 cents a pound.

Brazil's `Housewives' Pinch Nation's Pennies
Brazil's `Housewives' Pinch Nation's Pennies
By Miriam Jordan
1,295 words
7 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Belo Horizonte, Brazil -- LYNDA REZECK and Emilia de Moura fan out through a supermarket here in Brazil's
third-largest city to hunt down rice, sugar, laundry detergent and 90 other items. But these housewives are
pushing pencils -- not grocery carts -- in this particular price-shopping reconnaissance mission, which is key
to their ongoing battle against Brazil's economic scourge.
After years in check, inflation is climbing again, and a group known as the Housewives' and Consumers
Movement is mobilizing to contain the damage to Brazilian consumers. Led by a matron in her sixties with
silver hair, manicured nails, gold bangles and the gift of gab, this battalion of 5,000 homemakers is
pressuring supermarkets to cap prices. It is also setting an example in a country where consumer activism is
in its infancy and women are still struggling for a public voice. "Despite my surname," says Lucia Pacifico, the
movement's founder whose last name means "peaceful" in Portuguese, "I am a real fighter."
Inflation is a logical rallying point for public outcry. During the 1980s and early 1990s, Brazilians saw their
buying power eroded by hyperinflation that reached 4,000% a year. The government finally slayed the price
demon in 1994. But now annual inflation has returned to double digits, thanks to the sharp depreciation of
the country's currency last year ahead of October presidential elections. New left-leaning President Luiz
Inacio Lula da Silva has vowed to tackle inflation, as price rises of food and household goods have been the
steepest, reviving the fiscal trauma for poor and middle-class Brazilians. "We have to put the brakes on this
spiral of prices," says Mrs. Pacifico.
The Housewives' November 2002 survey found that since May of that year, the prices of cooking oil had
jumped 78%, flour 70% and sugar 69%, higher than official inflation indexes recorded. So, Mrs. Pacifico
demanded a meeting with the state supermarket association, whose members include big retailers Wal-Mart
Stores Inc. and Carrefour SA of France. The goal: a legally binding agreement to stabilize prices for a month,
hoping that by then inflationary pressures will have begun receding. The last time the housewives sought
such an accord was in 1999, when Brazil carried out a massive devaluation and then let the currency float
freely.
Mrs. Pacifico, a retired elementary-school teacher, started the Housewives' movement with a dozen friends in
1983. Whereas many American women activists find the term housewife pejorative, in Brazil "dona de casa"
connotes domestic authority, which extends over the household budget. The movement has spread to 14
states from its origin of Minas Gerais, Brazil's second most populous state. Funded by the $8 annual
membership dues, the movement publishes monthly price surveys, offers free lectures on household
budgets, legal counseling, a caller hotline and an occasional newsletter, depending on finances. Its
headquarters in Belo Horizonte comprises three rooms and a small auditorium in a skyscraper -- space
owned and donated by the municipality.
The movement gained prominence in 1988, as Brazil was drafting a constitution for the return of democracy
after two decades of military rule. Mrs. Pacifico played a key role in lobbying Brazil's Congress to pass a
consumer-protection code and a law entitling housewives to receive government retirement benefits. The
tough consumer regulations allow for binding accords between businesses and civic groups to control the
price, quality and quantity of goods -- the basis for Mrs. Pacifico's showdown with the supermarkets.
At the meeting in early December, Mrs. Pacifico is flanked by fellow housewives, a statistician (one of two
men who work for the movement), an independent market researcher and a lawyer. Cordially, the two sides
haggle over price targets for 36 items, and sometimes their proposals differ by just two U.S. cents, as in the
case for a kilogram of sugar. But the housewives don't relent.
When Adilson Rodrigues, the head of the supermarket group, requests a "force majeure" clause that would
allow retailers to breach the price controls, Mrs. Pacifico and her peers won't hear of it. "That clause would
defeat the purpose of controlling prices for 30 days," declares Monica Castro, a member of the movement's

--- Page 200 ---
Page 200 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
board. A stone-faced Mr. Rodrigues agrees to take the message to his members. "I have a lot of respect for
the housewives," he says after the meeting. "They speak for millions of anonymous consumers."
As the negotiators spill out of the movement's conference room, they are greeted by a horde of eager
reporters. With inflation making headlines, Mrs. Pacifico is a media star, and she or other Housewives'
movement members appear daily on a local radio talk show.
The housewives and supermarkets make progress over the ensuing days. But they hit a snag on chicken,
because a key input, cornmeal for chicken feed, is priced in dollars, and Brazil's exchange rate continues to
fluctuate unpredictably. Once the sides hammered out their differences, hundreds of supermarkets in Belo
Horizonte put up signs advising customers that they are taking part in the price-control campaign with the
Housewives' Movement. "Even those chains which aren't inclined to join the effort, end up joining," says Mr.
Rodrigues. "It's bad for your image to oppose."
Wal-Mart plays down the influence of the Housewives' movement, noting that the company already operates
under wafer-thin profit margins. "Any initiative to offer consumers better prices is in line with our corporate
culture," says Aprigio Rello Jr., director of corporate affairs for Wal-Mart in Brazil. Still, Wal-Mart also
participates in the price-control campaign.
Few Brazilian consumer activists have attained the high profile of Mrs. Pacifico, who was elected to the state
legislature in 2002 after serving on Belo Horizonte's local council. To acknowledge growing support among
men, she changed her organization's name from just the Housewives' Movement to the Housewives' and
Consumers Movement. She often leads demonstrations in the city's main park to protest price rises, most
recently a government-ordered increase in cooking fuel.
Mrs. Pacifico doesn't lack gumption: A few years ago she broke through the security cordon at a cattle
exhibition to hand Brazil's then president a list of consumer gripes. Last year, she dashed to Brasilia, the
federal capital, to crash a meeting between the government and civic groups to discuss electricity rationing.
She sweet-talked her way past security, but a government official barred her from the meeting, saying she
hadn't been invited. Determined that the Housewives' movement be heard, Mrs. Pacifico proclaimed to
waiting reporters, "You can't decide about energy-rationing in households without talking to housewives." In
the end, Mrs. Pacifico got a face-to-face meeting with then President Fernando Henrique Cardoso.
She kept up the pressure after energy rationing started in June 2001. Mrs. Pacifico sounded the alarm over
the fact that several companies had discreetly reduced package quantities -- say, of crackers, tuna and toilet
paper -- without reducing the price. The companies blamed the energy shortage, which forced some factories
to install expensive generators. Almost all manufacturers returned to the original quantities; those that didn't
were fined for violating consumer-protection laws.
The movement's latest victory was with the bakery association. ("The price of bread rolls is out of sight," Mrs.
Pacifico says of Brazil's breakfast staple.) After three rounds of negotiations with the association, the
Housewives' Movement brokered an agreement last month representing 5,000 bakeries in Minas Gerais state
-- a first -- to keep the price of bread rolls at the current price of about six cents each for 60 days. "If we don't
make a stink," Mrs. Pacifico says, "consumers will be abused."
