Quarterly Mutual Funds Review --- Seeking an End to the Stock Storm --- Bears Hold On Tight To Cautious Outlook; One Major Risk: Iraq
Quarterly Mutual Funds Review --- Seeking an End to the Stock Storm --- Bears Hold On Tight To
Cautious Outlook; One Major Risk: Iraq
By Aaron Lucchetti
2,625 words
6 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SO FAR this decade, it has paid to be nervous.
After losses of 0.9% in 2000 and 10.23% in 2001, the average U.S. stock fund dropped an eye-popping
22.43% in the just-completed 2002, according to Lipper Inc., marking the first three-year losing streak for
stock portfolios since the 1930s. And despite a fourth-quarter rally, only 3.8% of U.S. stock funds made
money in last year's depressed market, the smallest group on a percentage basis since 1974, according to
Morningstar Inc. Small wonder then that investors last year through November actually pulled more money
out of stock mutual funds than they put in for the first time since 1988.
In this kind of market, it's helpful for investors to look at the minority of mutual funds that were able to dodge
the worst of the stock-market bullets. A few funds chalked up gains just because they focused on the
scattered stock sectors that held up well, such as real estate. Also in the plus column were funds structured
to go up when stock-market indexes go down.
But some managers of diversified stock funds also did well -- or at least lost less than their peers -- simply by
being good at playing defense. They practiced conservative stock picking, resorted to putting a portion of their
portfolios into cash, or both. Many in this group weren't able to eke out gains in 2002, but they did avoid some
of the devastating losses that severely punctured more aggressive funds.
And even with a rebound since Oct. 9, some of these bearish professionals remain discouraged by what they
consider to be still-buoyant stock valuations, high consumer debt and low confidence in corporate executives.
Thus, they say it's not quite time to dip the toe back into the chilly stock-market waters.
Most managers still in the bear camp have something else in common -- a willingness to hold high levels of
their fund assets in cash. That goes against the grain for many investors and their advisers who expect fund
managers to keep 90% or more of their assets working in stocks nearly all the time. Nonetheless, cash has
proven to be worth its weight in gold to many managers with a bearish bent.
So will there be a fourth consecutive year of miserable stock returns? Here's a look at what three bearish
managers think is ahead for 2003.
---
For Steven Lehman, the pessimistic case for stocks starts in the nation's capital.
The forced resignation last month of Paul O'Neill as Treasury secretary was greeted as a welcome
development by many on Wall Street, but not by Mr. Lehman, manager of the Federated Market Opportunity
Fund with $455 million in assets. "I admire people who are straight speakers," the 45-year-old fund manager
says of the freewheeling Mr. O'Neill.
Perhaps unsurprisingly then, Mr. Lehman has little nice to say about one of Washington's most popular
figures -- Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan. "Maestro Greenspan has become irrelevant," he says.
Indeed, a dozen interest-rate cuts over the past two years have done little to encourage stock-market
investors.
Mr. Lehman says Mr. Greenspan "doesn't get it" and blames him for being "the foremost cheerleader during
the market mania." Specifically, he faults the Fed chairman for making positive statements about technology's
capability to increase productivity, as well as his support of tax cuts based on "rosy" budget projections.
The Federated manager's outlook is decidedly less optimistic. "I'm absolutely convinced that this is a classic
postbubble environment," he says. Consumer and business debt are too high as a percentage of the

--- Page 202 ---
Page 202 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
economy, he says, and he's concerned about the U.S. economy's reliance on foreign funding of its debt.
Further, he believes that most stock prices are too lofty relative to their earnings power.
Unwinding these "imbalances" in the U.S. stock market will look a lot like the slumping Japanese market of
the 1990s, Mr. Lehman predicts. Rallies could happen, but they will likely be overwhelmed by a continued
decline in investor psychology.
Mr. Lehman envisions a scenario in which stocks enjoy a sustained recovery "in years rather than months." In
the meantime, he's keeping about 35% of the portfolio on the sidelines in cash. Another 21% or so is in
defensive real-estate investment trusts and about 21% in foreign stocks that he believes are less pricey than
U.S. blue-chips.
Among Mr. Lehman's biggest stockholdings are the American depositary receipts of Boots Co. PLC, a British
drugstore chain with a low price-to-earnings ratio, and Norsk Hydro, a Norwegian industrial concern with a
low price-to-book-value ratio. Both stocks are out of favor, which is exactly when Mr. Lehman likes to buy.
In the U.S., the manager likes real estate because he thinks prices will hold up better in this economic slump
than they did during the early 1990s, when overbuilding battered the real-estate market. He also sees
bargains in tobacco maker UST Inc. and food distributor Bunge Ltd., which he calls his favorite pick.
He's less confident about larger stocks such as General Electric Co. and Wal-Mart Stores Inc., which he calls
good businesses that have become overpriced stocks. "My theme is that stocks of great companies were
much riskier than the underlying businesses," says Mr. Lehman. "People pay too much for obvious
greatness."
Mr. Lehman's returns have been relatively flat recently, but still solid when compared with peers. His fund,
launched by Pittsburgh-based Federated a few months after the market peak in 2000, gained 1.5% last year,
better than 96% of comparable midcap value funds, according to Lipper.
As for the economy, Mr. Lehman says he's more worried about a recession than most of his colleagues and
other stock-fund managers he knows. After a long growth boom like the 1990s, this decade is "like a
hangover," he says. "The cheerleaders are saying it's the bottom . . . but this is an environment that would
warrant people abandoning the stock market for the next five years."
Rounding out the defensive portfolio, Mr. Lehman holds gold-mining stocks such as Barrick Gold Corp.,
which he says would benefit from a falling dollar and a possible war with Iraq. "The situation in the Middle
East is just one more reason to be risk-averse," he says. "People are far too complacent with the outcome."
---
Laura Sloate has spent 34 years buying stocks for a living and would be happy to be snapping up bargains
these days as well. But right now, the manager of the Strong Value Fund isn't finding many stocks that fit the
bill.
"When we don't have enough stocks to buy, we don't buy," she explains matter-of-factly from her Manhattan
office. "If I found 10 stocks today, I'd buy them."
Instead, Ms. Sloate is patiently keeping about $11 million of the Strong fund's $43 million in assets in
money-market funds. She's tucked another $3.3 million or so in exchange-traded funds meant to diversify
risks over hundreds of different companies.
That leaves only about two-thirds of the portfolio for individual stocks.
"We're cautious and concerned," says Ms. Sloate, 57. "We want to protect assets." Indeed, the relatively
small Strong Value Fund has lost only 1.5% annually over the past three years, better than 95% of similar
multicap core funds tracked by Lipper. Last year, the fund fell 15%, better than 87% of peers.
Ms. Sloate, who has been blind since childhood, does research with the help of computer programs and
assistants that allow her to listen to the text of news articles and corporate filings. Since last summer, she has
grown more optimistic about stocks, but says she's still concerned about geopolitical issues including Iraq,
North Korea and terrorism.
She also cites a "very volatile economic situation" in the U.S. Working on several charity boards and heading
her own money-management business, Ms. Sloate says she worries about the growing negative impact of
rising health-care costs. As employers pass the health costs along to workers, consumer spending could take
a hit, she fears. Combined with climbing unemployment, the scenario could lead to slower home and auto
sales. And that doesn't include burgeoning deficits that Ms. Sloate fears could bring higher taxes and
inflation.

--- Page 203 ---
Page 203 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Worries about spending come up in many of Ms. Sloate's decisions on individual stocks. Last year, the
manager considered Sears, Roebuck & Co., but decided not to buy because she felt the retailer was too
exposed to debt-strapped consumers who might not be able to pay off their Sears credit-card balances.
Concerns about a housing bubble led Ms. Sloate to avoid Washington Mutual Inc., the large bank she
considered buying last year but then passed up on worries that mortgage foreclosures might rise.
Further, Washington Mutual has attracted too many fund managers looking for a bargain, Ms. Sloate
believes. "Every value guy in America owns the stock, but I don't understand why," she says.
Ms. Sloate also shies away from companies that have grown primarily through acquisitions. It's a red flag,
particularly in industrial companies and technology firms, she says, and was a strike against a company like
Illinois Tool Works Inc., a manufacturing company that has made dozens of small acquisitions in recent
years. "It's another popular stock and a well-run company, but it's really expensive" for a cyclical business,
trading at about 20 times 2003's estimated earnings, she says.
Even though Ms. Sloate currently holds much more cash than the 5% level she typically kept during the bull
market, she did boost her stock exposure during the second half of 2002 by adding some new names to the
fund. During the fourth quarter, she bought shares of oil-services companies Schlumberger Ltd. and Natco
Group Inc. with the belief that oil drilling will pick up in 2003.
She also bought Polo Ralph Lauren Corp., considering the apparel maker to be a cheap stock that enjoys a
well-known brand name. She also likes CIT Group Inc., a financial-services concern she believes has been
tarred unfairly by the fact that it was spun off recently by embattled conglomerate Tyco International Ltd.
More broadly, Ms. Sloate has been encouraged by the fact that there haven't been any major corporate
meltdowns since WorldCom Inc. during the summer. She discounts the chances that more blowups are
waiting to happen and instead expects the news to be dominated by continued fallout from already-known
problem cases. Another good sign: renewed interest from foreign acquirers such as British bank HSBC
Holdings PLC, which recently announced its intention to buy U.S. consumer-finance concern Household
International Inc. in a deal valued at $16 billion.
---
The stock market is headed for a rebound -- just not anytime soon, says Manu Daftary, the fast-trading
manager of Quaker Aggressive Growth Fund.
The fund, which lives up to the "aggressive" portion of its name by turning over its portfolio an average of 7
1/2 times a year, is buying and selling a lot of stocks to try to eke out gains. Part of the reason for all the
activity is that Mr. Daftary, 45, doesn't expect the stock market to have any momentum for at least the first
half of this year.
"Longer term, I'm getting more bullish," says Mr. Daftary, who started his own Boston-based firm, DG Capital
Management, in 1996 after stints at three firms that managed money for large institutions. In the next few
years, he says, pension funds, individuals, and foreign investors will decide they don't have enough exposure
to the U.S. stock market and eventually put more money there.
Over the past year, Quaker Aggressive Growth Fund, with assets of $170 million, fell 17%, and over the past
36 months, it has dropped 4.2% a year, better than the average U.S. stock fund which is down 10.4% a year
over the same period. Through much of the bear market, Mr. Daftary kept about 50% of the Quaker fund's
assets in cash. He has brought that position down to about 25% during the fourth quarter, but he says he's
likely to leave it there until he sees more signs of sales and revenue growth at bellwether companies.
In November, Mr. Daftary was encouraged when the Federal Reserve cut interest rates by 0.5 percentage
point, which he says shows policy makers are determined to see the U.S. economy grow faster. But around
the same time, Mr. Daftary called a few sales executives he knows at technology and telecommunications
companies and was told there was no end in sight to the slow economy. "Nothing is going to happen for
another year," one person told him. Another asked why he was even thinking of buying back into technology
stocks.
So, Mr. Daftary has dabbled in other sectors, looking for stocks that are temporarily undervalued and
sometimes buying and selling the same stock within the span of a few weeks. While Mr. Daftary has always
traded rapidly, he says holding for too long in this market doesn't work because skittish investors are likely to
sell stocks even after short run-ups.
"It's very hard to have sustainable moves," says the manager. With no new money coming in from individual
investors to buy stocks, the best chance for gains occurs as money rotates from one stock or industry to the
next. "We take profits very quickly," he says.

--- Page 204 ---
Page 204 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
What's the catalyst going to be for more lasting gains? Mr. Daftary says he's looking for a string of positive
profit surprises driven by better-than-expected sales. He's less encouraged by companies that are keeping
earnings afloat by restructuring or laying off employees.
Among Mr. Daftary's current picks: Darden Restaurants Inc., which operates Olive Garden and Red Lobster
chains, and Philip Morris Cos., whose hefty 6% dividend yield convinced Mr. Daftary to buy more of the stock
in the fall.
Among financial stocks, Mr. Daftary recently sold Wells Fargo &Co. because he's concerned about its
real-estate exposure. He has bought investment banks Merrill Lynch &Co. and Morgan Stanley in anticipation
of a recovery in late 2003 and beyond. He points out that the stocks are well off their highs and that investors
are overestimating the downturn in their businesses. He adds that the recent regulatory problems of the Wall
Street firms, which have resulted in large fines as a result of agreements with New York state Attorney
General Eliot Spitzer and others, are already priced into the stocks.
Mr. Daftary also made a seasonal trade recently, trimming natural-gas stock Devon Energy Corp. and selling
Anadarko Petroleum Corp. after they spiked amid colder-than-usual temperatures in much of the U.S. The
stocks were "a little overdone," he said in December. Adding to his belief that oil prices have risen too far, Mr.
Daftary says he is working under the assumption that the U.S. will wait at least several months before
attacking Iraq.

`Lula' Gives Brazil's Bourse a Lift
World Stock Markets
`Lula' Gives Brazil's Bourse a Lift
By Craig Karmin
1,274 words
6 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C11
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Investors in Brazil's turbulent stock market have found an unexpected holiday gift in the onetime radical figure
of Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva.
The benchmark Bovespa Index has surged more than 50% in dollar terms from its low point in mid-October,
shortly before President da Silva -- the Workers Party candidate and a former metalworker -- won in a runoff
election.
Foreign investors credit the new president, widely known as Lula, for reassuring the market that Brazil won't
default on its debt. They also applaud his nomination of market-friendly officials to the key positions of finance
minister and head of the central bank. Finance Minister-designate Antonio Palocci then pleasantly surprised
the market further by pledging to protect creditor rights and overhaul the ailing public pension system.
But soon, some investors warn, comes the hard part: actually passing an aggressive agenda that many
Brazilian interest groups and much of the population are certain to oppose. Mr. da Silva's effectiveness in
navigating this difficult terrain will be crucial to whether the recent stock-market gains are sustainable.
"There's probably a little more left in the rally," says Gregory Heywood, a senior Latin America analyst for
Montgomery Asset Management in San Francisco. "Maybe the market can go another 10% to 15% higher.
But then you need to see the reforms passed to address Brazil's economic imbalances. And there is some
question of how much commitment he has to stay the course."
The second quarter of the year could prove critical. From April to June, the Brazilian government has $24
billion in domestic debt due -- by far its largest amount due during any quarter this year. "If the new reform
agenda runs into trouble or gets watered down, that will become apparent by April or May," says Christian
Stracke, an emerging-markets analyst for the research group CreditSights. And if that happens, he adds,
Brazil may again have trouble finding buyers for its debt at the worst possible time.
For now, however, the honeymoon continues. The Bovespa rose Friday morning to its highest level in nearly
six months before late-day profit-taking left the index down 0.02% at 11600.23. Even so, it gained 3.3% for
the week. Last year, fears that Brazil would default helped send the Bovespa 46% lower in dollar terms.
Mr. da Silva's big lead in the polls last summer did little to assuage these concerns. In past elections, he had
advocated that Brazil declare a moratorium on its debt payments, and he had never hidden his regard for
Cuban leader Fidel Castro.
But Mr. da Silva toned down his rhetoric once the voting was over. Meanwhile, the central bank raised
interest rates seven percentage points in the fourth quarter to 25%, which was attractive enough to keep
investors interested in funding Brazil's debt and thus avoiding a default. Since their October lows, Brazil's
bond prices have climbed, and the currency strengthened to 3.44 to the dollar Friday, its best close since
September.
Investors are counting on the da Silva administration to deliver on economic measures spelled out by Mr.
Palocci. He has called for new laws to protect Brazil's creditors and autonomy for the central bank, which
reports to the Ministry of Finance.
Most important, he has indicated plans to overhaul the public workers' pension fund that has been straining
the government's finances. This was something that the previous administration under Fernando Henrique
Cardoso had tried and failed to do, in part because of opposition from Mr. da Silva's party. As a result, some
analysts are optimistic that Mr. da Silva will succeed where his predecessor couldn't.
"Not only is Lula no longer seen as a radical, but some think he's better for the economy than Cardoso," says
Mr. Stracke. "Now he's going to have to live up to those expectations," or the markets will suffer.

--- Page 206 ---
Page 206 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
---
Friday's Market Activity
Asian markets were broadly higher following Wall Street's big rally overnight, but most European markets
retreated slightly after Thursday's big gains. Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 0.48%, or 0.69,
to 144.29. Excluding the U.S., the index rose 1.15%, or 1.21, to 106.69.
In HONG KONG, the Hang Seng Index ended up 2.3%, or 218.33 points, at 9583.85. Property titan Cheung
Kong Holdings and Sun Hung Kai Properties climbed 4.5% and 2.6%, respectively, to 52.75 Hong Kong
dollars and HK$47.20, after they both tumbled 13% in December. Export- and technology-related stocks
outperformed the main board, thanks to the rebound overseas fueled by the strong U.S. manufacturing
figures. Johnson Electric rose 4.7% to HK$8.90 and Li &Fung gained 4.7% to HK$7.80.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 Index fell 0.1%, or 4.6 points, to 4004.90, following Thursday's 1.8% rise. Volume
was light. Vodafone was the most heavily traded gainer, but rose just one penny, or 0.9%, to 118 pence.
Mobile-telecom operator mmO2 rose only one penny, or 1.1%, to 47 pence after gaining almost 6%
Thursday. The banking sector gained, with Barclays rising three pence, or 0.8%, to 397 pence. Shell
Transport & Trading was among the decliners, falling four pence, or 1.1%, to 412 pence. BP declined one
penny, or 0.2%, to 427 pence. The retailing sector also weakened. Tesco fell three pence, or 1.7%, to 193
pence, and Dixons Group slumped seven pence, or 4.5%, to 143 pence.
In PARIS, the CAC 40 Index declined 0.2%, or 7.1 points, to 3187.88. Insurer AXA was among the most
heavily traded gainers, rising 21 European cents, or 1.5%, to 14.20 euros after saying it will sell its 5.3%
stake in Credit Lyonnais to Credit Agricole. The agreement removed a key area of doubt in a planned $20.48
billion merger between the two French banks. European Aeronautic Defence &Space was among the
gainers, advancing 47 cents, or 4.3%, to 11.48 euros. STMicroelectronics rose 81 cents, or 4%, to 20.92
euros. France Telecom gained 85 cents, or 4.7%, to 19.05 euros.
In TORONTO, the S&P/TSX Composite Index rose 0.5%, or 32.61 points, to 6772.66. The materials group
gained 1.1%, as the gold subgroup closed up 2.2%. Among gold stocks, Barrick Gold rose 22 Canadian
cents to C$24.92, while Placer Dome finished up 39 cents to C$18.40. The financial services group edged up
0.78%, with Royal Bank of Canada rising 78 cents to C$59.09, Canadian Imperial Bank of Commerce closing
up 21 cents to C$44.91 and Bank of Nova Scotia gaining 19 cents to C$53.64.

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2002 --- Bad News Was Good News for Commodities, Which Posted Gains --- Mideast, Argentine Unrest, Crop...
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2002 --- Bad News Was Good News for Commodities, Which
Posted Gains --- Mideast, Argentine Unrest, Crop Failures, Stock Jitters All Fueled Increases in Value
By Peter A. McKay
2,047 words
2 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
R8
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
COMMODITY PRICES changed course in 2002, rising in response to improvements in overall domestic
productivity, as well as poor growing conditions for certain crops and political uncertainty, especially the
standoff with Iraq that continues to buoy energy prices going into the New Year.
It was a gradual, yearlong rally, although the markets' heaviest gains came in the second half. The Dow
Jones-AIG Commodity Index jumped 24%, or 21.243 points, to 110.276.
The rise followed a 21% decline in 2001, when raw materials fell as the stock market plummeted and the
Sept. 11 terrorist attacks plunged the economy into uncertainty.
In 2002, investors' lingering jitters made gold one of the commodity markets' stars, as the metal reclaimed its
traditional role as a financial haven during precarious times. Gold futures skyrocketed 25%, or $69.20, to
$348.20 a troy ounce in trading at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
In the energy markets, the rally came despite weak demand in the U.S. and other industrialized markets,
fueled by the situation in Iraq and fresh turmoil in Venezuela. Even with political uncertainty in two major
oil-producing nations, the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries was so doubtful about the world
economy's demand for more crude that late in the year it declined to increase output.
Going into the New Year, many forecasters say the sheer dynamics of supply and demand should drive
energy prices down -- although a war or other nonfinancial event could disrupt that scenario.
In other markets, analysts expect modest price increases, as both the stock market and the economy
gradually recover, and consumers begin to spend more on the finished goods for which commodities are a
basic part.
"There's plenty of room for commodities to keep going up, assuming the economy continues to do well,
because prices are nowhere near the point where they've begun to signal inflation and threaten the broader
economy," says Adam de Chiara, Senior Vice President and head of Commodity Index Group at AIG
International Inc. "It's not like commodity prices are the equivalent of the Nasdaq at 5000, like a bubble."
The Dow Jones-AIG index is still about 20% off its all-time high, set in 1997, he notes.
Here is a rundown of major markets in 2002:
Energy
Talk of whether America will take military action to disarm Iraq dominated the crude-oil trade all year, and
prices for refined fuels and natural-gas prices tracked the crude market's gyrations closely. Crude-oil prices
gradually rose above $30 a barrel through the early fall, eased when the U.S. acceded to a new round of
United Nations weapons inspections, then rallied again through the end of the year as the White House
accused Iraq of failing to disclose details of its chemical and nuclear programs.
Political unrest in Venezuela, where labor unions and other dissidents are pushing for the ouster of President
Hugo Chavez, also boosted energy prices late in the year.
For the year, crude-oil contracts jumped 57%, or $11.36, to $31.20. Gasoline contracts rose 52%, or 29.72
cents, to 86.97 cents a gallon. And heating-oil contracts leapt 58%, or 31.79 cents, to 86.86 cents a gallon at
the New York Mercantile Exchange.

--- Page 208 ---
Page 208 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
An early winter blast of cold weather in the Northeast also boosted demand for heating oil and natural gas.
The latter continued its long-term price rise as gas is increasingly used to power environmentally friendly
electric power plants.
Natural-gas prices rose 86%, or $2.219, to $4.789 per million British thermal units at Nymex.
Few observers expect support for prices above $5 much longer, however, considering that the country now
has more than 2.6 trillion cubic feet of gas in reserve, according to the American Gas Association.
By contrast, reserves had almost gone to zero when gas prices were at their peak of more than $9 per million
BTUs during the California energy crisis a few years ago.
"Back then, we were calculating the date when we would run out of the stuff," says energy analyst John
Kilduff, of the brokerage Fimat USA Inc., in New York. "Now, we're trying to figure out what to do with the
stuff."
Mr. Kilduff is even less bullish on crude, which he believes may fall to $15 a barrel, if the uncertainty in Iraq
and Venezuela is resolved. He also says he expects global crude demand to remain weak in 2003, at best
remaining unchanged from the current 77 million barrels a day.
"The market lately has kind of been a tale of two worlds," Mr. Kilduff says, with a political premium in the
energy markets but weak fundamentals.
Metals
The industrial and precious-metals sectors were a mixed bag, as only platinum posted a rally that could keep
pace with gold's run-up. Platinum contracts jumped 23%, or $109.40, to $594.40 at Nymex on continued low
mine production of the geologically scarce metal.
Palladium, which is an important component of emissions-reducing car parts, continued its steady decline
from record highs in 2001. Its contracts fell 47%, or $210 a troy ounce, to $238 at Nymex. Although it often
occurs in nature with platinum, its price had soared much higher in 2001.
The silver market continued to be burdened by a glut of above-ground reserves, rose only 22.4 cents, or
4.9%, to $4.812 a troy ounce, at Comex.
Overall, the sector's performance was something of a reversal from the metals markets of recent years, when
consumers were spending more money on cars, cameras and film (which uses silver as a major component).
Prices of those metals led the way, and gold was shunned, as investors flocked to paper assets such as
stocks or the U.S. dollar.
Now, analysts say gold has the brightest outlook of any precious metal going into 2003. Frank Holmes,
chairman and chief executive officer of U.S. Global Investors, which manages several gold-oriented mutual
funds, says the metal could top $375 an ounce before mining companies can increase output.
"For about five years now, while the gold price was lagging, no one's really been starting any new [mining]
projects," Mr. Holmes says, adding that meanwhile, demand for gold has increased.
Most base metals markets were plagued with a glut of reserves to weigh down prices. Aluminum edged up
only 0.7%, or $9.50, to $1,344 a metric ton, and zinc fell 2.3%, or $18, to $749 a metric ton at the London
Metal Exchange.
Copper was a notable exception to the base-metals trend, as combined reserves of the metal at LME and
Comex warehouses fell 14% to 1.15 million metric tons. Several major mining firms also announced
production cutbacks, or programs to withhold selling metal they have already mined, pushing copper prices to
70.25 cents a pound, up 6.6%, or 4.35 cents, for the year at Comex.
John E. Gross, an independent metals consultant and publisher of the Copper Journal newsletter, says he
expects a similar trend this year, possibly pushing the metal's price as high as 80 cents.
Right now, all the base metals are being driven by speculative trading, which is good for some of them," he
says.
Agriculture
A modestly improving economy boosted beef prices late in the year, while the pork market trailed until late
August, when prices started to rally on forecasts that reduced births would result in tight supplies by year end.
Those fears seemed to be confirmed by a recent government report showing that storage of frozen pork
bellies is near a 37-year low.

--- Page 209 ---
Page 209 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
For the year, pork-belly contracts rose 5.9%, or 4.775 cents, to 86.20 cents a pound. But live-hog contracts
slipped 9.6%, or 5.45 cents, to 51.60 cents a pound. Live-cattle contracts rose 13%, or 8.925 cents, to 79.625
cents a pound at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange.
In the grain markets, poor growing conditions in parts of the U.S., Australia and Canada generally pushed
prices higher, although most crops were well off their highs by the end of the year.
At the Chicago Board of Trade, soybean contracts leapt 34%, or $1.4275, to $5.65 a bushel. Wheat contracts
rose 13%, or 36 cents, to $3.25 a bushel. Corn futures rose 13%, or 26.75 cents, to $2.3575 a bushel.
Going into 2003, however, most analysts say that only soybeans have a modestly bullish outlook. Otherwise,
a return to more normal levels of production should damp grain prices, as will a bearish export outlook that
includes rising output from low-cost producers in Eastern Europe and other regions, says Vic Lespinasse, of
brokerage firm A.G. Edwards & Sons.
He said soybeans will likely buck the trend toward lower prices because few countries besides the U.S. have
focused their agricultural efforts on producing the crop in large quantities. While consumers outside the U.S.
don't rely as heavily on soybeans as an alternative to meat as a source of protein, consumption is increasing
and some countries are using beans as animal feed .
In particular, he says several large purchases that have been attributed to anonymous buyers in recent U.S.
Department of Agriculture reports were likely sent to China, for use as livestock feed.
"They've just developed a voracious appetite for soybeans," says Mr. Lespinasse. "Historically, they've
always produced more of their own corn and wheat. Now, they have an incentive to switch some of that
production over to beans, but that will take a while."
In other agricultural markets, lumber prices fell 11%, or $27.40, to $217.70 a board foot at CME amid bloated
inventory. Cotton recovered from near record lows going into 2002 to finish at 51.60 cents a pound, up 45%,
or 16.01 cents, at the New York Cotton Exchange division of the New York Board of Trade.
Tropical Commodities
Aside from gold, one of the commodity markets' strongest sectors last year was tropical commodities, also
known as softs. Cocoa led the way, surging 54%, or $711, to $2,021 a metric ton amid political unrest in the
Ivory Coast, the world's leading cocoa-producing nation. A failed coup Sept. 19 has led to a war that has
increasingly split the former French colony along ethnic lines.
Also, the cocoa market saw its third consecutive production deficit, with a shortfall of about 133,000 metric
tons, says Judith Ganes-Chase, an independent analyst and consultant.
She estimated that trend, and an increase in prices, would continue this year. "To make up for the shortfall,
we'd probably need another 200,000 tons of production," she said. "I just don't see where that's going to
come from."
In the orange-juice market, a smaller Florida citrus crop offset a slight increase in Brazilian output. Frozen
orange-juice contracts rose 2.3%, or 2.1 cents, to 93.25 cents a pound.
Ms. Chase expects a similar uptick this year, as the two major producers flip-flop their roles and Florida is
expected to raise production while Brazil's crop slips.
By contrast, she estimates sugar traders and producers should be happy if they do as well this year as in
2002, when they pretty much broke even. Sugar futures edged up 3.0%, or 0.22 cents, to 7.61 cents a pound
at the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange, despite the industry's ninth consecutive production surplus.
The string of overruns has left a massive inventory of about 63 million metric tons, compared with total
production of an estimated 140 million metric tons in 2002. The surplus was more than four million metric tons
last year.
"I can't think of a market that has a more bearish fundamental picture," Ms. Chase says. "You would think this
market would be at three cents, not skimming eight cents."

Mining Stocks Shine After Cutbacks --- Leaner Operations Help Companies Pare Debt, Improve Efficiency
Industry Focus
Mining Stocks Shine After Cutbacks --- Leaner Operations Help Companies Pare Debt, Improve
Efficiency
By Chip Cummins
951 words
2 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
NEWMONT MINING Corp.'s net debt stood at about $1.5 billion at the end of September. Based on incorrect
information provided by the company, an Industry Focus Thursday gave the figure as $533.5 million.
(WSJ Jan. 6, 2003)
FOR MUCH OF THE past decade, North America's largest mining companies scraped their way through a
harrowing gold-price slump by closing mines, slashing costs and consolidating.
Now, miners are starting to see that discipline pay off. Gold prices have climbed to 5 1/2-year highs and
gold-mining shares were some of the best performing stocks of the past year. And miners, now leaner and
more efficient, are using increased cash flow to start paying off debt and exploring more to replenish
reserves.
Late Tuesday, gold for delivery next month was fetching $348.20 a troy ounce at the Comex division of the
New York Mercantile Exchange. Newmont Mining Corp., the world's largest gold producer, fell 21 cents to
$29.03 Tuesday in New York Stock Exchange composite trading.
Cost savings and consolidation helped boost mining companies' return
on equity even as the commodity slump wreaked havoc on profits,
according to John Dobra, an economist at the University of Nevada in
Reno and a mining-industry consultant. He estimates the industry's
return on equity -- before taxes and write-downs -- came in at about 8%
in 2001, up from 4.8% in 1997. "Even before the [
gold] price rose, we
started to see the return on equity for these companies start to rise,"
he said.
With increased cash from gold sales, Denver-based Newmont reduced
net debt by about $115 million in the third quarter. At the end of
September, net debt stood at a still-daunting $533.5 million, but
Newmont said it will be aggressively paring that down in future
quarters with excess cash flow from its mines in Nevada, Peru,
Indonesia and Australia.
After a large equity issue related to an
acquisition of two rivals early last year, Newmont's
debt-to-capitalization ratio has fallen to about 21% at the end of
September, down from about 40% at the end of 2001.
Though it hasn't yet disclosed its budget for 2003, Wayne W. Murdy, the company's chairman and chief
executive, says exploration outlays at the company will be about 10% to 15% higher in 2003 over 2002.
Mr. Murdy attributes much of the company's renewed financial health to the discipline it was forced to impose
while gold prices were low. "There's nothing like a little bit of stress" to get costs down, he said.
In the past five years, Newmont trimmed operations and bought up rivals to exploit efficiencies. Total cash
costs for mining an ounce of gold at Newmont have fallen to $184 in 2002 from $220 in 1996.
In 1997, Newmont spent $2.5 billion on Santa Fe Pacific Gold Corp., another miner with large operations in
Nevada. After the acquisition, Newmont used the two companies' combined operations to more efficiently

--- Page 211 ---
Page 211 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
match up ores and processing facilities. Newmont didn't close any mines, but it did lay off miners in Nevada,
as most companies did in the late 1990s.
Barrick Gold Corp. trimmed long-term debt to $766 million at the end of September, down from $793 million
at the end of 2001. Net debt, which was low in 2001, has been reduced to zero. The Toronto company plans
to spend about $100 million in exploration costs this year. While that is about the same as last year, much of
the 2002 budget was unplanned spending related to a big gold discovery in Peru.
Freeport-McMoRan Copper &Gold Inc. has increased the ratio of gold production at its large copper and
gold mine in Indonesia as gold prices ticked higher. The company has said it expects to reduce debt by
more than $225 million in 2002. At the end of the third quarter, the New Orleans company had net debt of
$2.53 billion.
Costs have increased for several miners more recently. Both Newmont and Barrick said higher production
costs curbed profit in the third quarter. Newmont estimates total cash costs will have grown by about 2% to
3% in 2002 over the previous year. Rising energy prices are also expected to dent fourth-quarter profits.
But miners' continued fortunes will depend largely on the volatile gold markets. Recent rallies -- including one
before the Gulf War in 1991 and another in 1999 after central banks pledged to curb gold sales -- proved
fleeting. Still, many analysts are forecasting sustained higher gold prices this time around.
A number of factors have contributed to the rally. After years of depressed prices, miners skimped on
exploration, leading to lackluster reserve replacement and predictions of falling production in coming years.
Many gold companies also have pulled back from hedging their production in derivatives markets. The
practice protected companies from falling prices in the past, but isn't attractive at today's higher gold prices
and lower interest rates. By deciding not to hedge -- or sell their gold forward -- companies have essentially
tightened supply and demand.
More recently, gold has found favor as a haven for investors ahead of possible military action in Iraq. With
U.S. interest rates low and the dollar weakening, there have been few attractive alternatives, especially for
international investors.
---

from $220 in 1996. An article yesterday incorrectly reported that cash costs were $184 in 2002.
(WSJ Jan. 3, 2003)

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2002 --- A Year of Scandals &Sorrow
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2002 --- A Year of Scandals &Sorrow
By Laura Saunders Egodigwe, John C. Long and Nima Warfield
13,598 words
2 January 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
R10
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
JACK GRUBMAN, a former analyst at Citigroup Inc.'s Salomon Smith Barney unit, has said he "invented" the
contents of an e-mail he wrote indicating that Citigroup CEO Sanford Weill pushed him to review AT&T
Corp.'s stock rating as part of Mr. Weill's power struggle with Citigroup's former co-chairman, John Reed. A
news summary in Thursday's Year-End Review reported the e-mail but not Mr. Grubman's subsequent
statement about it.
(WSJ Jan. 8, 2003)
The year began in the shadow of Sept. 11, 2001, and would soon cast shadows of its own. On Wall Street, a
parade of corporate scandals put executives in handcuffs and led companies to restate billions on their
balance sheets. The SEC made CEOs swear results were accurate. A succession of bankruptcy-court filings
made history. The stock market ended lower for a third straight year. In politics, the Democrats lost Congress,
and the Republicans lost their Senate majority leader.
Shadows fell across the international scene as well. The U.S. was spared another major terrorist attack, but
Indonesia, Kenya, Russia and Pakistan weren't, and there was no end to Palestinian suicide bombs and
Israeli gunfire. Afghanistan got a new government, but one vexed by warlords and al Qaeda. Daniel Pearl lost
his life. Osama bin Laden was apparently still at large. The U.S. worked through the U.N. to seek Iraq's
disarmament, but as the year ended, the threat of war still loomed, and North Korea flouted both the U.S. and
the U.N. by rattling nukes.
Here is a summary of the news events as chronicled on the front page of The Wall Street Journal. In most
cases, the dates reflect when the items were published.
JANUARY
2: More than 300 million Europeans start 2002 with a new currency; the euro proves popular on its first day of
official business.
The FDA rejects ImClone's application to market the cancer drug Erbitux, dealing a setback to the biotech
firm and its partner, Bristol-Myers Squibb.
Argentina's political crisis yields the nation's fifth president in two weeks, Eduardo Duhalde, a Peronist
senator.
4: The investigation of Sen. Torricelli ends as prosecutors decline to file charges in the long graft inquiry. A
Senate inquiry goes on.
An apparent anthrax hoax letter arrives in Senate Majority Leader Daschle's office.
7: Argentina says it is devaluing the peso by 29%, breaking the currency's decade-old 1-to-1 peg to the dollar
in a risky effort to shift the country out of a four-year recession.
The leaders of India and Pakistan meet and shake hands at a regional summit in Nepal as war tensions
between the two nuclear-armed neighbors ease further.
Enron agrees to let Dynegy buy a pipeline unit that was a centerpiece of the companies' failed merger deal.
9: Alcoa posts a quarterly loss for the first time in nearly a decade.
Died: Wendy's hamburger-chain founder Dave Thomas, of liver cancer, at age 69.

--- Page 213 ---
Page 213 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
10: The Justice Department confirms it will pursue a criminal probe of Enron, focusing on possible accounting
fraud.
Merrill Lynch is taking a $2.2 billion charge, partly to pay for about 9,000 job cuts, in one of the steepest
retrenchments on Wall Street in recent years.
11: Arthur Andersen says its employees destroyed many documents related to its work for Enron. Attorney
General Ashcroft recuses himself from the Enron inquiry, citing a conflict of interest.
14: The president faints, falls and hits his head after choking on a pretzel while watching football on TV,
suffering brief oxygen deprivation and a cut on his cheek.
16: An Al Qaeda PC yields possibly solid evidence of links to the shoe bomber. The computer, acquired in
Afghanistan by The Wall Street Journal, contains a file on the doings of a "brother Abdul Ra'uff" who scouted
targets in the Mideast. His travels were strikingly similar to those of Richard Reid.
The U.S. charges American Taliban fighter John Walker Lindh with conspiracy to kill U.S. citizens.
Arthur Andersen fires the partner who directed the destruction of thousands of e-mails and documents related
to the Enron audit.
17: The shoe bomber is indicted as a terrorist who was trained by al Qaeda.
Enron's board fires Arthur Andersen as auditor amid a storm of recriminations between the companies.
Kmart's president leaves, and its chairman is replaced, as the company struggles to stave off filing for
bankruptcy protection.
21: ImClone is being probed by congressional investigators amid recent setbacks at the biotechnology
company.
22: Three ex-Enron employees say documents were shredded in the accounting department of the
company's Houston headquarters after federal investigators had begun an inquiry into possible illegalities at
the energy concern.
Willamette agrees to be acquired by Weyerhaeuser for $6.11 billion, or $55.50 a share. Weyerhaeuser CEO
Steven Rogel's deal for Willamette, the helm of which he left in 1997 for Weyerhaeuser, culminates a
five-year quest to buy the rival.
23: Kmart files for Chapter 11 after efforts to secure bailout funding fizzle and outlines a plan to reduce its
size and emerge from court in 2003.
Tyco announces a plan to split into four separate firms focusing on electronics and security, health care, fire
protection and financial services.
Amazon surprises Wall Street by posting its first-ever earnings.
Died: Stanley Marcus, 96, chairman emeritus of the luxury Neiman Marcus chain, Tuesday, in Dallas.
24: Enron CEO Kenneth Lay resigns less than 24 hours after a bankruptcy-court-appointed creditors
committee seeks his removal.
Stymied anthrax investigators double to $2.5 million the reward for information on the source of the recent
mail attacks.
Merrill Lynch reports a $1.26 billion quarterly loss, its first since 1998, citing a restructuring charge and
expenses related to the terrorist attacks.
25: Former Enron Vice Chairman J. Clifford Baxter, who quit the energy firm in May, is found dead, an
apparent suicide.
28: ImClone says the SEC and Justice Department have started inquiries into whether the company misled
investors about prospects for a cancer drug.
29: Bush delivers his first formal State of the Union address, promising to defeat the twin threats of terrorism
and recession. Iran, Iraq and North Korea constitute an "axis of evil," he says. Bush plays down pro-business
rhetoric. Without mentioning Enron, he calls for greater protections for retirement plans and says corporate
America must be held to the "highest standards of conduct."

--- Page 214 ---
Page 214 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Powell speaks to Pakistan's president to spur efforts to find and free The Wall Street Journal's Daniel Pearl,
abducted while reporting on the region's conflicts.
Global Crossing files for Chapter 11 in the biggest telecom collapse so far. Global Crossing's fall is blamed by
telecom executives and analysts on a confluence of factors, ranging from aggressive accounting to a
"turnstile" of CEOs.
30: IBM names Samuel Palmisano, the company's president, to succeed Louis Gerstner as chief executive
March 1.
31: The Roman Catholic Church in Ireland agrees to pay $110 million into a compensation fund for children
sexually abused by clerics over the years; with an estimated 3,000 victims, critics say, the contribution is
insufficient.
FEBRUARY
1: The sole U.S. anthrax-vaccine maker wins approval to resume shipments after years of concern over
production quality.
Disney will no longer buy consulting services from the same firm that audits its books, a challenge to the
accounting industry's belief that a single firm can handle both roles objectively.
4: An internal Enron report finds various improper financial transactions and massive self-dealing by company
officials. Enron ex-Chairman Kenneth Lay and two other former top executives back out of testifying on
Capitol Hill, where congressional hearings will probe Enron's failure.
Tyco discloses it spent about $8 billion in its past three fiscal years on more than 700 acquisitions that were
never publicly announced.
Delta's flight attendants reject a move to unionize, in a big win for the carrier.
The New England Patriots won football's Super Bowl on Sunday, defeating the St. Louis Rams 20-17 in a
game played under heavy security.
5: Bush announces his $2.13 trillion budget as he is cheered by troops in Florida. The military is the big
winner in the fiscal 2003 spending plan, receiving a 14% increase to $379 billion for everything from pay
raises to new weapons.
Kenneth Lay says he is quitting Enron's board, and lawmakers say they will subpoena him to appear before
investigative panels.
6: The Nikkei 225 Stock Average falls to an 18-year low, and S&P downgrades the credit ratings of seven
Japanese banks.
7: Allied Irish Banks says a rogue trader at its U.S. unit incurred losses of $750 million through unauthorized
trading.
8: Bush rejects granting prisoner-of-war status to detainees from Afghanistan.
Enron's former CEO denies knowledge of improper activities by his subordinates and of many of the details of
the partnerships that brought the company down, in testimony before Congress.
Arafat names two aides he said should eventually take over the Palestinian Authority and PLO, his first hint at
a possible successor. They are Ahmed Qureia, the parliament speaker, and Mahmoud Abbas of the PLO.
WorldCom's CEO says the company stepped in to cover a personal loan of $198.7 million. The long-distance
firm cut its 2002 forecasts and said it expects a charge of $15 billion to $20 billion.
11: "Let's roll," Bush tells U.S. athletes in a pep talk before Friday's opening of the Salt Lake City Olympics.
An ex-Lehman broker, Frank Gruttadauria, under investigation over whether he bilked clients out of as much
as $300 million, turned himself in to authorities over the weekend after being on the lam for four weeks.
Died: Princess Margaret, 71, sister of Britain's Queen Elizabeth II, in London, Saturday, of a stroke.
12: Nortel's finance chief quits as the company discloses that he had sold an investment in the firm before it
released news of worse-than-expected losses last year.
Died: Victor Posner, 83, prominent 1980s corporate raider convicted of securities-law violations in 1994, in
Miami, of pneumonia.

--- Page 215 ---
Page 215 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
13: Enron's former chairman invokes the Fifth Amendment, declining to answer any questions from a Senate
panel. Separately, remarks by Kenneth Lay to an investigative committee in January indicate that on at least
two occasions he may have deliberately misled the public in order to keep the firm's problems from becoming
known. Enron announces the planned resignations of six board members, including four who served on its
audit panel.
15: A Pakistani militant arrested this week admits abducting Daniel Pearl of The Wall Street Journal. Officials
dismiss his claim that the reporter is dead.
20: CSFB fines two executives and a top broker $500,000 apiece in the aftermath of a regulatory probe of
alleged abuses in how the firm handed out hot IPOs.
21: A blaze aboard an Egyptian train kills at least 370 people in the country's worst rail disaster. The crowded
train was headed south from Cairo when it caught fire, forcing passengers to jump from speeding cars.
Investigators suspect gas containers brought on board for cooking may have been the cause.
22: Daniel Pearl was killed by his captors, The Wall Street Journal and U.S. say. A video obtained by
Pakistani investigators ends hopes of finding the 38-year-old reporter, abducted a month ago, alive. The U.S.
and Pakistan vow to find and punish the killers.
The head of the CDC, Jeffrey Koplan, resigns effective March 31. He gives no reason, but the federal health
agency had come under severe criticism for its handling of last year's anthrax attacks.
25: Cheney is sued by the GAO, which seeks a list of executives he met with while crafting Bush's energy
plan.
26: GM raises its earnings outlook and production plans for the year amid a stronger-than-expected U.S.
performance.
27: The Lyme disease vaccine is pulled off the market by its only U.S. manufacturer, which cites a lack of
demand. The move comes despite a recent government report that the disease is surging in the Northeast.
A Georgia crematory owner is hit with 100 more criminal charges for stacking hundreds of corpses on his
property for years instead of incinerating them as he was paid to do. The House votes to make that a felony.
MARCH
1: Israel storms two Palestinian refugee camps on the West Bank in an unprecedented operation that leaves
at least 12 Palestinians and one Israeli soldier dead. Israel says militants are using the camps as hideouts.
The U.S. urges Israel to show restraint.
A federal appeals court overturns the convictions of three white New York policemen in the 1997 torture of
Haitian immigrant Abner Louima. The ruling didn't affect the main culprit, but still provokes an outcry.
4: Northrop launches a formal $5.9 billion hostile bid for TRW just hours after the TRW board rejects
Northrop's unsolicited offer as inadequate.
Arthur Andersen agrees to pay $217 million to settle all pending litigation related to its audits of the Baptist
Foundation of Arizona, which filed for Chapter 11 after its collapse in 1999.
Afghan fighting reignites, with the heaviest involvement by U.S. troops yet. Hundreds of American soldiers
join 500 Afghans in an assault on a cave complex outside Gardez where al Qaeda and Taliban fighters are
believed to have regrouped.
6: Bush imposes sweeping tariffs and quotas on a wide range of steel imports for three years. The move,
which targets steel produced by the EU, Japan and South Korea, is likely to sustain U.S. firms that otherwise
would have failed and weaken incentives for industrywide consolidation. Foreign governments and
steelmakers threaten countermeasures and legal action.
Sept. 11 fund guidelines are to be revised, reportedly to boost payouts to families of victims and eliminate the
deductions for Social Security and workers' compensation benefits. The original rules were roundly reviled.
7: California Republican primary voters pick political novice Bill Simon to challenge Gov. Davis in fall
elections. Democratic Rep. Gary Condit is shown the door, still trailed by suspicions about the disappearance
of intern Chandra Levy.
National Steel files for bankruptcy-court protection, saying its problems wouldn't be offset by industry aid.
The FTC approves H-P's proposed $22 billion acquisition of Compaq.

--- Page 216 ---
Page 216 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
8: Schering-Plough plans to turn Claritin, its huge-selling prescription antihistamine, into an over-the-counter
drug available in just about any store.
Conseco's CEO discloses that he fired his chief financial officer. The statement comes amid a two-day slide
in the company's stock price.
Harvard Business Review's editor quits, but strikes a deal in which she will be back as editor at large. Suzy
Wetlaufer is caught up in a flap over her relationship with GE's former chairman.
11: Bush marks a post-Sept. 11 milepost by outlining the war on terror's next steps. New York turns on
powerful spotlights that create columns of light to memorialize the twin towers in the void their destruction tore
in the skyline.
12: Qwest and WorldCom say the SEC has launched inquiries into their accounting practices.
Kmart accepts the resignation of CEO Charles Conaway, who led the retailer into Chapter 11, and hands the
title to Chairman James Adamson.
13: A Florida flight school Monday receives student-visa paperwork for two Sept. 11 hijackers. The INS says
the formal notices of decisions made last summer just reflected the slow grinding of the bureaucratic wheel.
Bush says he was "plenty hot" to learn about it and wants an INS overhaul.
Andrea Yates is found guilty of murder by a Texas jury that rejects her claims of postpartum insanity in the
slayings of her five children at their Houston home last year. A few days later, the jury sentences her to life in
prison.
The U.S. homeland-security chief announces a color-coding system to identify terrorist-threat levels.
The Archdiocese of Boston agrees to pay up to $30 million to settle suits by dozens who say they were
abused as children by former priest John Geoghan.
15: Andersen is charged by the Justice Department with obstruction of justice over the destruction of
documents related to its Enron audit. Andersen loses some 40 clients in the wake of the Enron collapse. Lost
clients include Merck, Freddie Mac and Delta Air Lines.
Delta Air Lines says it has eliminated most of the commissions it pays to travel agents in the U.S. and
Canada, making it the first major carrier to do so. Continental and American later join the carrier. Travel
agents begin mobilizing in response.
19: Operation Anaconda ends. The Pentagon pronounces the battle outside Gardez, Afghanistan, an
"absolute success."
Donald Trump and a partner agree to sell the Empire State Building to its long-term leaseholder for $57.5
million.
20: Hewlett-Packard claims a narrow victory in its fight to win shareholder approval for its $21.4 billion
acquisition of Compaq. But dissident director Walter Hewlett, who opposed the deal, says the outcome was
too close to call and refuses to concede defeat until the votes of H-P shareholders are officially tallied.
Compaq stockholders later endorse the company's sale to H-P by a 9-to-1 margin.
22: Andersen Worldwide begins to crack, as partners in Hong Kong, China and Russia agree to merge with
rival firms, dashing efforts to forge a deal for the firm's non-U.S. operations.
Pope John Paul II breaks his silence on sex-abuse scandals rocking the Catholic Church in the U.S., Ireland,
England, France, Australia and Poland. The pontiff says they have cast a "dark cloud of suspicion" on all
priests.
25: Paul Volcker offers to take control of Arthur Andersen and oust top management. In February, Andersen
names the former Fed Chairman to head a reform panel, but by May he acknowledges the end of his role
when the panel is suspended.
PepsiCo and United Air sign a five-year accord making Pepsi the cola served on all United flights, ending the
carrier's decades-long pact with Coke. Pepsi later wins a contract for sponsorship rights with the NFL, the top
TV sport, in another setback for Coca-Cola.
Maryland Public Television pulls the plug on Louis Rukeyser, host of "Wall $treet Week," after he publicly
criticizes plans to replace him after 32 years.

--- Page 217 ---
Page 217 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
26: Shell agrees to acquire Pennzoil-Quaker State for $1.8 billion, a move that would give the firm a motor-oil
brand and network of oil-change centers to go with its chain of gas stations.
A Nigerian Muslim court overturns the sentence of death by stoning handed out to a woman convicted of
adultery. The case had drawn international attention to the imposition of the strict Shariah legal system in
some regions of Nigeria.
27: Andersen's CEO resigns, even as the accounting firm proceeds with talks to sell its nonaudit businesses
in the U.S. for over $1 billion. Joseph Berardino had come under increasing criticism from partners who
believed he mishandled the fallout from Andersen's botched audits of Enron.
BAE's CEO resigns unexpectedly, prompting speculation about the future of Europe's largest defense
contractor.
28: A suicide bomber kills 20 in Israel, shattering hopes for a cease-fire. The Hamas bomber blew himself up
in a Netanya hotel ballroom crowded with Passover diners. More than 120 are wounded. Aides to Arafat
condemn the attack, but Israel says that it holds the Palestinian leader responsible and that it would have to
reassess agreements it has made so far in U.S.-led truce talks.
29: U.S. smallpox-vaccine stocks expand suddenly as French firm Aventis Pasteur discovers as many as 90
million doses at its plant in Pennsylvania.
APRIL
1: Israel is at war, Sharon declares amid a rain of suicide bombings and counterstrikes. The prime minister,
speaking on a day when two attacks take 15 Israeli lives, vows an unrelenting offensive to smash Palestinian
militants.
Died: Britain's Queen Mother Elizabeth, 101, Saturday, near London.
2: Xerox plans to restate earnings covering four years and pay a $10 million civil penalty to settle SEC
charges that it engaged in fraudulent accounting practices. The fine would be the largest the agency has
levied against a public firm in connection with financial-reporting violations.
3: The SEC is looking into accounting methods at some of the largest U.S. companies, broadening the scope
of its inquiry beyond the issues in the Enron probe.
4: Microsoft President Rick Belluzzo is stepping down, a move some observers blame on clashes with CEO
Steve Ballmer over strategy and management style.
5: Bush raises U.S. stakes in ending Mideast violence, stung by criticism of inaction. "Enough is enough," the
president says in announcing Secretary of State Powell will go to the region. Bush calls on Israel to withdraw
from areas "occupied" in its current offensive and blasts Arafat for suicide attacks. The U.N. Security Council
demands an Israeli pullout "without delay." Israeli troops and tanks enter Nablus.
Four Pakistanis go on trial in Karachi for abduction and murder of The Wall Street Journal's Daniel Pearl.
8: An amended Enron lawsuit alleges that more than three dozen new defendants, including Merrill Lynch
and J.P. Morgan Chase, participated in a scheme with the company's top executives to defraud investors.
9: The Andersen partner fired by the firm for shredding Enron audit documents has agreed to plead guilty to
obstruction and cooperate with U.S. prosecutors. Separately, Andersen says it will eliminate about 7,000
jobs, or 27% of its work force in the U.S.
Israel announces partial pullout from the West Bank after angry prodding by President Bush. Battles continue
to rage, with about 100 Palestinians left dead in a Jenin refugee camp.
10: The SEC widens its probe of alleged accounting fraud at Xerox, telling two former executives and KPMG,
the firm's former auditor, that it may file civil charges against them.
11: Andersen's British operations agree to join with Deloitte & Touche. A week earlier, Andersen's Spanish
operations also agreed to merge with Deloitte.
15: Venezuela's Chavez returns to power following his ouster by the military last week.
16: GE will cut 7,000 jobs this year in its GE Capital Services unit in an effort to reduce costs and move more
functions to the Internet.
In Afghanistan, four U.S. soldiers die when rockets they were disarming blow up near Kandahar.

--- Page 218 ---
Page 218 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
An Air China 767 jetliner crashes on approach to Pusan, South Korea, killing 129.
18: Ford reports its fourth-straight quarterly loss. But the auto maker joins GM in saying it would boost
production, a sign that the industry is beginning to rebound.
H-P says shareholders approved its plan to buy Compaq, by a slim margin. Walter Hewlett vows to continue
challenging the results.
TRW rejects Northrop's sweetened $6.68 billion hostile offer and puts itself up for sale.
19: Andersen breaks off settlement talks with the Justice Department aimed at resolving its criminal
indictment amid signs its discussions to settle civil litigation are unraveling.
22: Enron President Jeffrey McMahon is stepping down June 1 amid rising government scrutiny of his role in
some questionable Enron-related deals.
23: The Treasury plans new federal money-laundering rules that will cover a broader spectrum of the
financial-services industry. The rules will apply to credit-card companies, mutual funds, wire-transfer firms
and commodities dealers, as well as to banks and securities firms.
Williams Communications files for Chapter 11. Ericsson warns it won't make a profit this year, while Lucent
sales plunge 40%, in signs the telecom crash is worsening.
Alfred Taubman of Sotheby's is sentenced to a year and a day in jail and fined $7.5 million for his role in a
price-fixing scheme.
24: Exxon Mobil reports that its first-quarter profit tumbled 58% as lower oil and natural-gas prices and
lackluster demand combined to create the worst business climate since the 1980s.
25: Suzy Wetlaufer quits the Harvard Business Review as her work continues to be questioned following
disclosures of a romantic relationship with GE's ex-chairman.
Andersen lawyer Nancy Temple, a pivotal figure in the document-destruction case, is under criminal
investigation.
26: Tyco abandons its plan, announced in January, to split the conglomerate into four parts. The company
also lowers its earnings forecast for the year and reports a $1.9 billion quarterly loss because of technology
write-downs. Shares tumble 20% to a 52-week low of $20.75.
Dynegy says the SEC is investigating its accounting of natural-gas trading. The news pushes the company's
shares down 30%.
29: The economy roars out of recession in the first quarter, growing at a 5.8% annualized pace, the fastest in
two years. But signs of fragility linger. Blue chips end the week down 3% at 9910.72, the first time since
February the Dow industrials close below 10000, amid bad corporate news.
30: WorldCom's CEO quits. Bernard Ebbers reaches his decision under pressure from outside directors
frustrated by the company's sinking stock price, controversy over Ebbers's $366 million personal loan from
the firm, and the SEC's investigation of the company.
Sotheby's ex-CEO Diana Brooks is sentenced to three years' probation instead of prison for her role in a
price-fixing scheme.
MAY
1: H-P's plan to buy Compaq overcomes a final hurdle as a judge dismisses a lawsuit from dissident
shareholder Walter Hewlett that sought to block the deal. After the decision, Hewlett said he would support
the $18.6 billion transaction.
2: Enron plans to reorganize itself as a small company under a new name. The mediator in Andersen's
settlement talks with Enron creditors and shareholders declares the talks dead.
Israel pulls out of Ramallah, ending Arafat's confinement.
6: France's Chirac wins a second term as president in a landslide driven by distaste for hard-right rival Le
Pen.

--- Page 219 ---
Page 219 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
7: Enron energy traders manipulated California's power system to bolster profits during the height of the
state's 2000-2001 energy crisis, documents released by federal regulators show. California officials call for a
new criminal inquiry.
8: The Fed leaves rates at a 40-year low and signals it will continue to be patient about raising them, saying
the risks of economic weakness and inflation are equally balanced.
A Chinese MD-82 jetliner crashes into the bay near the northeastern city of Dalian, killing all 112 on board. An
EgyptAir 737 crashes in Tunisia, killing 14 of the 62 people aboard.
9: The Nasdaq surges 7.8% to 1696.29, sparked by hints from Cisco about a possible business rebound. The
Dow industrials jump 3.1% to 10141.83.
KPMG signs a letter of intent to buy nearly all of Andersen Worldwide's consulting operation. The deal is the
biggest so far for a piece of Andersen.
10: Worldcom's debt is slashed to "junk" levels by Moody's and Fitch, as the telecom firm's banks asked that
it put up collateral to secure $5 billion in credit lines.
IBM plans to lay off as many as 8,000 workers, or about 2.5% of its world-wide work force, during the current
quarter.
13: Robert Hanssen is sentenced to life in prison Friday for spying for Moscow over two decades while
serving in the FBI.
14: Sears agrees to buy Lands' End, the nation's largest mail-order and Internet apparel retailer, in a $1.86
billion cash deal.
Reliant says it engaged in bogus power trades during the past three years to make its business appear
bigger. The company blames rogue traders.
The U.S. and Russia strike a deal to reduce nuclear arsenals. It will require warhead cuts of about two-thirds
by 2012, but doesn't lay out a monitoring mechanism and says nothing about missile systems.
Bush signs the farm bill he initially opposed, boosting crop subsidies as well as Republican candidates in
agricultural areas.
15: The Justice Department is investigating a group of the world's largest banks for allegedly using their
online trading service to restrict competition in the foreign-currency market.
PPG agrees to pay $2.7 billion to resolve all of its asbestos litigation through Pittsburgh Corning's bankruptcy
proceedings.
Revamped Iraq sanctions are approved by the U.N. Security Council in a victory for Bush. A ban on arms
sales will be toughened while imports of civilian goods will be made easier. Washington hopes that will help it
build support for a drive to oust Saddam Hussein.
16: CMS Energy says $4.4 billion of its electricity trading -- most of its volume for 2000 and 2001 -- resulted
from sham "round-trip" swaps with Dynegy and Reliant that had no economic value. Meanwhile, the SEC is
conducting a sweeping probe across many industries into practices that pump up revenue.
Adelphia says its founder, John Rigas, has resigned and that it has launched a probe amid new allegations
involving the Rigas family.
Bush was warned by U.S. intelligence weeks before the Sept. 11 attacks that Osama bin Laden's terrorist
network might hijack American planes. The administration notified federal agencies of the threat, but said it
lacked specificity to have prevented the tragic events.
17: Japan says it will join the EU in slapping tariffs on U.S. imports of steel, the first time Tokyo has ever
retaliated against the U.S. in a trade impasse. Bush's decision in March to impose tariffs on steel imports,
coupled with this week's approval of domestic farm subsidies, is spurring retaliation and anti-American trade
sentiment.
Adelphia is the subject of a probe by federal prosecutors into possible accounting irregularities.
Reliant and CMS announce management shake-ups, following admissions that they had engaged in bogus
power deals.

--- Page 220 ---
Page 220 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
20: Federal authorities are trying to determine whether Computer Associates wrongly booked more than $500
million in revenue in its 1998 and 1999 fiscal years in a scheme to enrich the firm's senior managers.
Schering-Plough agrees to pay $500 million to resolve manufacturing problems, in the largest penalty ever
levied by the FDA.
22: Merrill agrees to pay $100 million and change how it monitors its stock analysts in settling the New York
state attorney general's inquiry into allegations that it misled individual investors about the stock of its
investment-banking clients.
Citigroup agrees to buy Golden State for $5.8 billion in stock and cash, in a bid to expand its West Coast
branch-banking presence.
23: ImClone CEO Samuel Waksal resigns. The biotech firm has been hurt by controversy over its handling of
a failed application for its Erbitux cancer drug.
An Alabama jury convicts Bobby Frank Cherry of killing four black girls in the 1963 Birmingham church
bombing. The ex-Klansman faces life in prison.
Chandra Levy's remains are discovered in Washington, D.C. The intern had gone missing after an affair with
Rep. Gary Condit.
24: J.P. Morgan Chase announces a management shake-up in the aftermath of a series of financial-industry
setbacks.
Died: "Slammin' Sam" Snead, 89, golfing legend who won seven major championships, in Hot Springs, Va.
28: CMS's chief resigns following disclosures the energy firm's trading unit had conducted transactions that
artificially boosted volumes and revenue.
Taiwan searches for clues to what caused a China Airlines jet to break apart on Saturday and crash into the
Taiwan Strait with 225 passengers and crew on board.
Mozambique establishes a commission to investigate what caused a train crash on Saturday that killed about
200 people and injured hundreds. It was the worst rail disaster in the country's history.
29: Dynegy's chief resigns under pressure from the company's board. Chuck Watson's departure comes amid
mounting questions about some of the energy concern's financial practices. Glenn Tilton, who joined the
board last year as a representative of ChevronTexaco, was named interim chairman. Daniel Dienstbier, a
longtime director, was appointed interim CEO.
SBC agrees to pay a record $3.6 million for submitting false data to regulators weighing the firm's
long-distance applications.
Halliburton says the SEC launched a preliminary inquiry into its accounting treatment of cost overruns on
construction jobs.
Bush signs a historic accord that gives Russia a voice, but no veto, in NATO. Later, the EU says it will
formally recognize Russia as a market economy.
30: Philip Morris agrees to sell its Miller unit to South African Breweries in a $3.6 billion stock accord that
would let the U.S. firm retain a stake in the beer maker.
Nortel Networks announces plans to cut 3,500 more jobs, or about 8% of its work force, and take charges of
$600 million.
31: Analyst Jack Grubman was instrumental in making management and business decisions at Global
Crossing for two years after the firm went public in August 1998. The activities of the Salomon telecom
analyst may lend support to New York state Attorney General Eliot Spitzer's probe into conflicts of interest
among research analysts.
The FBI is given broad new powers on domestic spying. Agents now will be allowed to conduct surveillance
of public gatherings and surf the Internet as part of terrorism investigations.
Born: Adam D. Pearl, son of the late Wall Street Journal correspondent Daniel Pearl and his wife, Mariane, in
Paris.
JUNE

--- Page 221 ---
Page 221 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
3: The SEC authorizes investigators to probe the stock sales of Global Crossing's top executives.
4: Tyco announces the resignation of CEO Dennis Kozlowski.
Microsoft agrees to settle SEC civil allegations that the company misstated its earnings during certain periods
of the 1990s.
El Paso executive C. Dana Rice is found dead in an apparent suicide, further rattling an energy industry
facing growing scrutiny.
Napster files for bankruptcy protection as a prelude to a proposed sale of the Internet music service's assets
to Bertelsmann.
5: Bristol-Myers is being sued by state attorneys general, who claim it illegally delayed generic competition to
its Taxol cancer drug.
6: New York prosecutors are looking into whether former Tyco's ex-CEO Dennis Kozlowski improperly used
company funds to buy his $18 million New York apartment and whether he received interest-free loans from
the company to purchase artwork.
Currency trader John Rusnak is indicted on fraud charges in the Allfirst Financial scandal.
Israel moves against Arafat after a bombing kills at least 16 and wounds dozens of others. In response,
Israeli tanks enter the Palestinian leader's Ramallah compound and open fire.
7: Adelphia inflated the number of cable-TV subscribers by 400,000 to 500,000 and kept two sets of books to
boost the amount it spent to upgrade its systems.
R.J. Reynolds is ordered to pay a $20 million fine after a judge rules it "indirectly targeted" youths in cigarette
ads.
10: Adelphia's board dismisses auditor Deloitte &Touche, blaming it for not informing the cable company
about questionable accounting and self-dealing.
Instinet plans to buy Island ECN for about $500 million in stock, combining the Nasdaq's two biggest
electronic-trading rivals.
12: Bertelsmann is buying the stake in Zomba Music that it doesn't already own for about $3 billion, under a
longstanding option being exercised by Zomba's majority owner. The deal comes as the music industry faces
its biggest downturn in years.
13: Imclone's former CEO is arrested on charges of trying to sell ImClone stock and tipping off family
members after learning that regulators would soon reject his company's cancer drug. Samuel Waksal's arrest
creates doubts as to whether Bristol will maintain its current relationship with ImClone.
The SEC reopens a probe of Tyco as part of a broader review of possibly questionable corporate accounting
in the aftermath of the Enron meltdown. Separately, Tyco receives SEC clearance for a CIT share offer, but is
forced to take a $4.5 billion charge to reflect the unit's impaired value, after which Tyco shares surge 36% on
news of the clearance.
14: Heinz says it is spinning off its most sluggish businesses, which will become part of a new unit that will
merge with Del Monte.
17: Andersen is convicted of one felony count of obstructing the SEC's investigation into Enron's collapse.
The verdict, which the accounting firm plans to appeal, gives the Justice Department a badly needed boost in
momentum as it pursues indictments for more-serious crimes at the energy trader.
Qwest CEO Joseph Nacchio resigns at the request of the board, which has become frustrated with the
telecom company's woes. Tellabs CEO Richard Notebaert will step in.
18: Nestle is buying a controlling 67% stake in Dreyer's in a bid to compete with Unilever in the U.S. market
for premium ice cream.
Enron discloses it made $745 million of payments and stock awards to executives in the year prior to its
bankruptcy-law filing.
19: A Senate panel approves legislation creating an accounting-oversight board to set bookkeeping
standards, limit consulting work and discipline wayward auditors.

--- Page 222 ---
Page 222 of 236 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Microsoft says it would restore support for Sun's rival Java software in Windows, offering a concession on the
eve of closing arguments at its antitrust trial.
20: The IRS is bringing back its controversial practice of randomly auditing individual tax returns in an effort to
crack down on what it says is a growing wave of tax scams. IRS officials say the agency plans to conduct
about 50,000 random audits related to the 2001 tax year.
The Justice Department launches a criminal inquiry into whether the top memory-chip makers colluded to
prop up prices.
TRW agrees to sell its aeronautical-systems unit to Goodrich for $1.5 billion, a deal aimed at thwarting
Northrop's bid for TRW.
24: Three former top Rite Aid executives are charged with masterminding an illegal accounting scheme that
triggered the largest corporate-earnings restatement in U.S. history.
A Merrill Lynch sales assistant's statements about a controversial trade of ImClone stock for Martha Stewart
contradict what the home-design maven and her broker said about the sale.
Died: Ann Landers, pen name for advice columnist Esther Lederer, 83, Saturday, in Chicago, of cancer.
26: WorldCom's audit panel uncovers what could be one of the largest accounting frauds ever, with the
discovery of $3.8 billion in expenses improperly booked as capital expenditures. Without the transfers,
WorldCom would have reported a loss for 2001 and the first quarter of 2002. The company fires its longtime
chief financial officer, Scott Sullivan.
Adelphia files for bankruptcy-court protection amid probes into some of the largest self-dealing in U.S.
corporate history.
Northrop offers to raise its bid for TRW to about $7.2 billion in an effort to fend off rivals.
27: The Fed refuses to cut interest rates or even to signal that the central bank was weighing such a move
soon, despite a crisis of confidence intensified by the WorldCom scandal.
The SEC files a civil suit alleging WorldCom engaged in a fraudulent scheme to pad earnings.
